# The Prize — Part 2 Notes

## Chapter 20: The New Center of Gravity

### DeGolyer's Mission to the Middle East

- Everette DeGolyer was one of the most respected figures in the oil industry — a geologist who had discovered Mexico's great Golden Lane field as a young man. He put together the independent company Amerada on behalf of the Cowdray interests, then in the late 1930s established DeGolyer and McNaughton, the world's leading petroleum engineering consulting firm — an innovation that met the new need for independent appraisal of reserves for bank financing.
- DeGolyer was already a millionaire several times over by his mid-forties, earning $2 million a year. His interests ranged far beyond oil — he was a founder of what became Texas Instruments, a considerable historian of chili, built an extraordinary book collection, and bailed out the Saturday Review of Literature when it was about to go bust.
- As a self-made man, DeGolyer had little use for the New Deal. But when WWII broke out, he was called to Washington as a chief deputy to Harold Ickes in the Petroleum Administration for War. In 1943, he was given a special foreign mission: appraise the oil potential of Saudi Arabia and the Persian Gulf countries — the center of a contentious debate.
- In a 1940 speech, DeGolyer had prophesied: "No such galaxy of fields of the first magnitude over such a wide area has been developed previously in the history of the oil industry... I will be rash enough to prophesy that the area we have been considering will be the most important oil producing region in the world within the next score of years."
- Getting to the Middle East was arduous in wartime — military planes via Miami, Caribbean, Brazil, Africa, then the Persian Gulf. DeGolyer toured oil fields in Iraq, Iran, Kuwait, Bahrain, and Saudi Arabia. He wrote his wife: "We haven't seen anything but a pretty barren land on this whole trip... In fact Texas is a garden in comparison."
- DeGolyer mastered the art of eating sheep's eyes when ceremoniously offered. But it was the geology that captured his imagination — three structures already tapped in Saudi Arabia with reserves estimated at 750 million barrels, but similar structures suggested reserves would be far larger. For DeGolyer, the Arabian Peninsula was "an El Dorado, the stuff of legend." He had discovered a well flowing 110,000 barrels per day and had never seen anything on so vast a scale in half a century in the business.
- Upon returning to Washington in early 1944, DeGolyer reported proven and probable reserves of Iran, Iraq, Saudi Arabia, Kuwait, Bahrain, and Qatar at about 25 billion barrels. Saudi Arabia accounted for about 20% — perhaps 5 billion barrels. He was conservative, applying the same "proven" and "probable" standards he'd use for bank appraisals. He suspected reserves would be much larger — estimates sounding like lunacy, up to 300 billion barrels for the region and 100 billion for Saudi Arabia alone.
- One mission member told State Department officials: "The oil in this region is the greatest single prize in all history."
- DeGolyer's key judgment: "The center of gravity of world oil production is shifting from the Gulf-Caribbean area to the Middle East — to the Persian Gulf area, and is likely to continue to shift until it is firmly established in that area." This constituted a eulogy for America's receding place in world oil. The US had produced almost 90% of oil used by the Allies in WWII — the high-water mark. Its remaining days as an exporter would soon disappear.

### "The Allies Have the Money"

- In 1940, the Middle East (Iran, Iraq, entire Arabian Peninsula) produced less than 5% of world oil, compared to 63% for the United States. But James Terry Duce, a vice-president of Casoc (California-Arabian Standard Oil Company), wrote to DeGolyer in 1941 about "fields such as we have in that area are a totally different experience to anything in the United States — even East Texas."
- While the Axis powers were on the offensive in Russia and North Africa, the dwindling Americans in Saudi Arabia ("the hundred men") planned how to protect the wells by filling them with cement, and how to destroy them to deny them to advancing German armies. Wells in Kuwait and Iran were also plugged in coordination with British and American military authorities.
- By 1941, Ibn Saud faced a stark financial crisis — war interrupted pilgrim flow to Mecca, drought caused crop failure, traditional industries (sword and knife making, leather working) couldn't make up the losses. As he explained in 1942: "The Arabs have the religion, but the Allies have the money."
- Ibn Saud appealed to the British, to Casoc, and to its parents Standard of California and Texaco. The companies didn't want more loans but didn't want to risk losing the concession. Lend Lease was suggested, but Saudi Arabia was a kingdom, not a democracy — Ibn Saud was no constitutional monarch. Roosevelt initially decided against American assistance: "Will you tell the British... I hope they can take care of the King of Saudi Arabia. This is a little far afield for us" (July 1941).
- The British provided about $2 million in newly minted coins, and British subsidies continued. American oil men worked to convince Ibn Saud that this British aid was really American — since Britain was a Lend Lease recipient, the aid "actually came from the United States. It just came indirectly."

### "We're Running Out of Oil!"

- America's entry into the war brought a wholesale redefinition of the Middle East's importance. Oil was the critical strategic commodity — shaping the Axis military strategy, and the resource that could defeat them. The US almost single-handedly fueled the entire Allied war effort.
- From the mid-1930s onward, the discovery rate of new US oil fields fell sharply. The director of reserves for the Petroleum Administration for War warned in 1943: "The law of diminishing returns is becoming operative... the bonanza days of oil discovery, for the most part, belong to history."
- Harold Ickes published "We're Running Out of Oil!" in December 1943, warning that if there were a WWIII, "it would have to be fought with someone else's petroleum, because the United States wouldn't have it... America's crown, symbolizing supremacy as the oil empire of the world, is sliding down over one eye."
- This pessimism gave rise to the "conservation theory" — that the US government had to control and develop foreign ("extraterritorial") oil reserves to reduce the drain on domestic supplies. Even Republican Senator Henry Cabot Lodge declared "history does not give us confidence that private interest alone would adequately safeguard the national interest."
- Herbert Feis, the State Department's Economic Adviser: "In all surveys of the situation, the pencil came to an awed pause at one point and place — the Middle East."

### Anglo-American Suspicions and Rivalry

- American policymakers arrived at the same conclusion that had directed British oil policy since WWI — the centrality of the Middle East. But the two Allies viewed each other with considerable suspicion. The British feared Americans would eject them from the Middle East; the Americans feared the British would "diddle" them out of the Saudi concession.
- Navy Undersecretary William Bullitt warned that London would "diddle" the American companies "out of the concession and the British into it." When the British sent a locust-control mission to Saudi Arabia, Casoc was certain it was a cover for secret oil geology exploration.
- In truth, the Americans woefully exaggerated British designs on Saudi Arabia — Britain was hardly in a position to dislodge the Americans, on whom they depended heavily. Three alternatives emerged: acquire direct ownership (like Anglo-Persian), negotiate a settlement with the British, or leave the matter in private hands.

### The Policy of "Solidification"

- Socal and Texaco feared the British would gain a dominant position over Ibn Saud's finances. Saudi Arabia was only two decades old as a unified country — would the kingdom and the concession survive the King himself? Mexico's recent nationalization of foreign oil companies was a cautionary tale.
- Direct US government involvement became the policy of "solidification." In February 1943, the presidents of Socal, Texaco, and Casoc went to Washington seeking government aid to keep the British at bay.
- On February 16, 1943, Ickes lobbied Roosevelt over lunch: Saudi Arabia was "probably the greatest and richest oil field in the world," the British were "trying to edge their way into it." On February 18, Roosevelt reversed his earlier position and authorized Lend Lease assistance to King Ibn Saud.
- The Army Navy Petroleum Board projected a serious oil shortage for 1944, threatening military operations — pushing the US government further toward Saudi Arabia.

### The Petroleum Reserves Corporation

- Ickes created the Petroleum Reserves Corporation, backed by the Army and Navy, aiming to acquire actual ownership of foreign oil reserves. Secretary of State Hull objected, fearing "intense new disputes" — "In many conferences after the last war the atmosphere and smell of oil was almost stifling."
- In June 1943, Ickes met at the White House with War Secretary Stimson, Navy Secretary Knox, and James Byrnes (Director of the Office of War Mobilization). They agreed on the need to acquire "an interest in the highly important Saudi Arabia fields." Roosevelt confirmed the decision — the government's "interest" should be 100%.
- In August 1943, the presidents of Texaco (W.S.S. Rodgers) and Socal (Harry C. Collier) were summoned to Ickes's office. They expected to discuss aid for an option on Saudi oil. Instead, Ickes proposed the government buy the whole of Casoc. The oilmen "literally took their breath away." As one participant noted: "They had gone fishing for a cod and had caught a whale."
- Ickes eventually whittled his proposal to a 51% share, modeled on Anglo-Iranian. He even suggested naming it the "American-Arabian Oil Company."
- Then a deal was struck: the US government would acquire one-third of Casoc for $40 million to finance a new refinery at Ras Tanura, with the right to buy 51% of production in peacetime and 100% in wartime.

### Industry Opposition Kills Government Ownership

- The rest of the American oil industry rose up in "righteous and fiery indignation." Other companies feared government competition — it might champion foreign over domestic oil, be the first step toward federal control or nationalization. Opposition came from independents, Standard of New Jersey, and Socony-Vacuum (Mobil), which themselves wanted into Saudi oil.
- Ickes abruptly retreated in late 1943, repudiating the plan and blaming Texaco and Socal for being "too greedy and recalcitrant." That was the end of the US government's move into direct ownership of foreign oil.

### The Government Pipeline Plan

- In early 1944, Ickes championed a new plan: through the Petroleum Reserves Corporation, the US government would spend up to $120 million to build a pipeline carrying Saudi and Kuwaiti oil across the desert to the Mediterranean. In exchange, companies would establish a one-billion-barrel reserve for the American military at a 25% discount.
- Massive opposition mobilized. Congressmen called for abolishing the Petroleum Reserves Corporation. Independent oil men called it "a threat to our national security" and "a move towards fascism." Liberals opposed it for helping big business; isolationists for digging into Middle East sand. After D-Day, the Joint Chiefs withdrew their endorsement. The pipeline project withered and disappeared.

### "A Wrangle About Oil" — The Anglo-American Petroleum Agreement

- A partnership with Britain to manage world oil was the remaining avenue. Fears of a postwar glut of cheap Persian Gulf oil — as destabilizing as East Texas in the 1930s — drove both sides. The British feared a competitive production race that would create a glut, depress prices, deprive producing governments of royalty income, and threaten concession stability.
- The British wanted American help defending the area against "Russian pressure." The British Chiefs added: "American continental resources constitute our most secure supply in war and therefore it is in our interest to take any steps that may assist in their conservation."
- In April 1943, Basil Jackson of Anglo-Persian warned James Terry Duce: "It is the first time in history that there have been such enormous quantities of oil overhanging the markets of the world." Without agreement, there would be "a fierce competitive battle."
- Lord Beaverbrook told Churchill: "Oil is the single greatest post-war asset remaining to us. We should refuse to divide our last asset with the Americans."
- Roosevelt showed British Ambassador Lord Halifax a hand-drawn map of the Middle East: "Persian oil is yours. We share the oil of Iraq and Kuwait. As for Saudi Arabian oil, it's ours."
- Churchill fired back at Roosevelt: "A wrangle about oil would be a poor prelude for the tremendous joint enterprise and sacrifice to which we have bound ourselves." Roosevelt replied about Britain "eyeing" and trying to "horn in" on Saudi Arabia. Churchill's acid response: Britain "will not be deprived of anything which rightly belongs to her."
- The Anglo-American Petroleum Agreement was signed August 8, 1944, creating an eight-member International Petroleum Commission to estimate global demand and allocate production quotas — fundamentally a link to the market management of Achnacarry and the "As-Is" system.
- Independent American oil men bitterly opposed it, fearing international regulation of domestic production and cheap Middle Eastern oil flooding into the US. The majors feared antitrust liability. The agreement was submitted to the Senate as a treaty but was heading for "an inglorious defeat." In January 1945, the Roosevelt Administration withdrew it. Efforts to revise it were shelved as Roosevelt headed to Yalta.

### The "Twins" — Roosevelt Meets Ibn Saud

- After Yalta in February 1945, Roosevelt's plane (the Sacred Cow) flew to the Suez Canal Zone. He boarded the USS Quincy in the Great Bitter Lake. The USS Murphy brought Ibn Saud — perhaps only his second trip outside his kingdom in 45 years.
- Ibn Saud traveled with a party of 48 (originally to include 100 live sheep, reduced to 7). He spurned the commodore's cabin and slept in an improvised tent on the forecastle, furnished with Oriental carpets and his own chairs.
- Roosevelt, the chain-smoker, did not light up out of deference to the King's religious precepts. On the way to lunch, Roosevelt was taken in his wheelchair into a separate elevator — the President pushed the red emergency button to smoke two cigarettes before meeting the King again.
- The two men spent more than five intense hours together. Roosevelt's interests: a Jewish homeland in Palestine, oil, the postwar Middle East. Ibn Saud wanted continuing American interest to counterbalance British influence. On Palestine, the anti-Zionist Ibn Saud suggested displaced Jews be given a national homeland in Germany.
- Roosevelt and Ibn Saud bonded personally — the King declared himself the President's "twin" due to their close ages, national responsibilities, farming interests, and physical infirmities (Roosevelt's polio wheelchair vs. Ibn Saud's war-wounded legs). "You are luckier than I because you can still walk on your legs and I have to be wheeled wherever I go," said Roosevelt. "No, my friend, you are more fortunate," replied the King. "Your chair will take you wherever you want to go and you know you will get there. My legs are less reliable and are getting weaker every day."
- Roosevelt gave Ibn Saud one of his two wheelchairs. It was kept in the King's private apartment in Riyadh as a most valued memento, though too small for a man of the King's large stature.
- NYT correspondent C.L. Sulzberger wrote: "The immense oil deposits in Saudi Arabia alone make that country more important to American diplomacy than almost any other smaller nation." Churchill was not pleased — he "burned up the wires to all his diplomats in the area, breathing out threatening and slaughter unless appointments with him were made with the same potentates." Three days after Roosevelt, Churchill drove into the Egyptian desert to meet Ibn Saud at an oasis hotel.
- Churchill's meeting with Ibn Saud included a clash of customs: Churchill was told the King "could not allow smoking or drinking alcohol in his presence." Churchill would not be as accommodating as Roosevelt: "I was the host and I said if it was his religion that made him say such things, my religion prescribed as an absolute sacred rite smoking cigars and drinking alcohol before, after, and if need be during, all meals and the intervals between them."
- Churchill's high-handedness did little to reassure Ibn Saud about British intentions. A gift exchange went badly — Churchill gave Ibn Saud perfumes worth about 100 pounds, but the King presented Churchill and Anthony Eden with diamond-hilted jeweled swords, robes, and about 3,000 pounds' worth of diamonds and pearls "for your womanfolk." Embarrassed, Churchill promised "the finest motor car in the world" — a Rolls-Royce costing the British Treasury upwards of 6,000 pounds. The jewels were eventually sold secretly so as not to offend Ibn Saud.

### "What Do We Do Now?"

- On Roosevelt's return, his advisers were still battling over the Petroleum Agreement and antitrust issues. Roosevelt planned to meet with Ickes and Secretary of State Stettinius after resting at Warm Springs. "I shall be delighted to have the meeting that Harold proposes just as soon as I get back from Warm Springs," he told Stettinius on March 27, 1945. "Will you remind me of it?"
- Stettinius did not have the opportunity. Roosevelt died at Warm Springs on April 12, 1945.
- Under Truman, Ickes renegotiated the Petroleum Agreement in London in September 1945, but its teeth were extracted — the International Petroleum Commission was effectively precluded from touching domestic American production (which was two-thirds of world production). The British Minister of Fuel and Power told the Chancellor: "On balance, it is better that we should accept the Agreement rather than reject it."
- Meanwhile, the gloom about oil reserves was receding. J. Edgar Pew, vice-president of Sun Oil, lambasted the prospect of shortage as "a psychological rather than a geological condition." He assured Senators that domestic production would meet American needs for two decades: "Of that I am as sure as I am that the sun shall rise and set tomorrow. I am an optimist."
- With WWII victory, the crushing demand on American oil reserves eased, removing the impetus for the British agreement. In February 1946, Ickes got into a bitter scrap with Truman over the appointment of Edwin Pauley, a California oil man, as Undersecretary of the Navy. Ickes submitted a resignation letter of more than six pages, single-spaced. "It was the kind of letter sent by a man who is sure that he can have his way if he threatens to quit," Truman later said.
- But Truman was not Roosevelt — he accepted Ickes's resignation "tersely and with alacrity and delight." Ickes requested six weeks to wind up; Truman gave him two days. The Oil Czar of the New Deal and WWII left office, denouncing Truman for "lack of adherence to the strict truth" — "neither an absolute monarch nor a descendant of a putative Sun Goddess." He became a newspaper columnist.

### Forrestal Champions Middle East Oil

- Navy Secretary James Forrestal, a driving, ambitious former Dillon Read investment banker, was one of the first senior policymakers to conclude the US must organize for a protracted confrontation with the Soviet Union. Oil held a central place in his security strategy: "The Navy cannot err on the side of optimism in its estimates for what supplies might be available."
- Forrestal declared: "The prestige and hence the influence of the United States is in part related to the wealth of the Government and its nationals in terms of oil resources, foreign as well as domestic. The active expansion of such holdings is very much to be desired." The State Department should work to "substitute Middle Eastern oil for American oil" and "promote the expansion of United States oil holdings abroad."
- At Potsdam, Forrestal lectured the new Secretary of State, James Byrnes, on Saudi Arabia as "a matter of first importance." He was influenced by DeGolyer's assessment: "If we ever got into another World War it is quite possible that we would not have access to reserves held in the Middle East but in the meantime the use of those reserves would prevent the depletion of our own, a depletion which may be serious within the next fifteen years."
- But Forrestal was a minority. State Department official Claire Wilcox wrote a memo, "Oil: What Do We Do Now?" declaring the Agreement "either dangerous or useless. If employed as a cover to set up a cartel to allot quotas and fix minimum prices, it is dangerous. If not so employed, it is useless." Even local schoolteachers in Texas were mobilized against it. In 1947, the Truman Administration gave up. The Anglo-American Petroleum Agreement was dead.
- The process of "solidification" would proceed through private companies, not government action. As an Anglo-Iranian executive put it, the oil companies were working out their own "salvation" in the Middle East.

## Chapter 21: The Postwar Petroleum Order

### The Postwar Oil Boom

- Gasoline rationing was lifted in August 1945, within 24 hours of Japan's capitulation. The voice of the motorist, silenced for years, rose in a "single, deafening crescendo — 'Fill 'er up!'" Drivers tossed away rationing books and took to the streets and highways. America was in love with the automobile again.
- In 1945, 26 million cars were in service; by 1950, 40 million. Gasoline sales were 42% higher in 1950 than in 1945. By 1950, oil was meeting more of America's total energy needs than coal.
- The pessimistic predictions about postwar supplies were eviscerated. Price, after controls were lifted, proved a powerful stimulus to exploration. In 1947, Imperial (a Jersey affiliate) drilled a successful discovery well near Edmonton, Alberta, igniting the first frenetic oil boom of the postwar years. Despite growing demand, proven US reserves were 21% higher in 1950 than in 1946. The United States was not running out of oil in the ground.
- But there was a shortage of available oil in 1947-48. Crude prices more than doubled from 1945 levels. Politicians declared an energy crisis; more than twenty Congressional investigations were launched. Consumption rose with "unexpected rapidity — astonishingly," said Shell. Refineries needed redesign for civilian products (gasoline, home heating oil) rather than 100-octane aviation fuel. Steel was in short supply worldwide, slowing refinery and tanker construction. The tanker shortage worsened in early 1948 after ships broke in half at sea, and the Coast Guard ordered 288 tankers laid up for emergency reinforcing.
- Oil companies became advocates of conservation — Standard of Indiana urged motorists to avoid "jackrabbit" starts and keep tires inflated. Sun promoted "Helpful Hints" for conserving oil on newscaster Lowell Thomas's broadcasts.
- Up through 1947, American exports exceeded imports. In 1948, imports of crude oil and products exceeded exports for the first time. The US was now dependent on other countries for the marginal barrel, and "foreign oil" entered the American vocabulary.

### The Great Oil Deals: Aramco and the "Arabian Risk"

- In the developing Cold War, oil was central to American, British, and Western European security. The Middle East was the focus where foreign policy, economics, national security, and corporate interests converged.
- Aramco (the Arabian-American Oil Company, renamed from Casoc in 1944) was the joint venture of Socal and Texaco in Saudi Arabia. It was troubled — an embarrassment of riches. The enormous scale of the Saudi fields required massive capital and, above all, markets. Texaco was a famous American company (Spindletop origins, Metropolitan Opera sponsor, "the man who wears the star" ads); Socal was a regional company that had spent millions searching for oil worldwide since WWI with little to show except minor East Indies/Bahrain production and Saudi Arabia's massive potential.
- By 1946, Standard of California's investment in Aramco totaled $80 million, with tens of millions more needed. The Trans-Arabian Pipeline (Tapline) from the Persian Gulf to the Mediterranean would cost $100 million. Building a European refining and marketing system would be prohibitively expensive against established competitors. Political conditions were unstable — large communist parties in Italy and France, occupied Germany's uncertain future, Britain's Labour government nationalizing the "commanding heights."
- Saudi Arabia was only two decades old. Ibn Saud was now in his mid-sixties, blind in one eye, failing in health. He had sired upwards of 45 sons, 37 thought to be living — would they bring stability or conflict? The concession would always be in jeopardy if Aramco couldn't satisfy the Saudi government's rising expectations. The answer: spread the risk. Bring in other oil companies with capital, expertise, and especially markets. Ibn Saud insisted Aramco remain 100% American — only Standard Oil of New Jersey and Socony-Vacuum qualified.
- State Department and Navy officials had encouraged broader participation. Navy Secretary Forrestal said in 1945 he "did not care which American company or companies developed the Arabian reserves" as long as they were "American." Socal opened talks with Standard Oil of New Jersey in spring 1946.

### Internal Rebellion at Socal

- Jersey was very receptive — facing oil shortage, Europe its most vulnerable market, and getting only 9,300 barrels/day from its IPC share of Iraqi production. Socal and Texaco thrusting into European markets with cheap Arabian oil was a feared threat.
- But Socal's own people rebelled. The production department in San Francisco — "the men on the ground" who had made the barren desert bloom — didn't want to lose control to larger partners. For 13 years, there had been no return to stockholders; only now in 1946 was the concession becoming profitable. James MacPherson, a Socal engineer in charge of Saudi field operations, called the concession "a gold mine" and was intent on building Aramco into "the greatest oil company in the world." He scathingly said Aramco was about to turn itself into "an annex to Jersey's production department."
- Harry Collier, Socal's chairman (one of the "Terrible Tycoons"), was the boss. He believed Aramco would sell far more oil through Jersey's system, ending up with "much more gold." The deal would let Socal recoup all its direct investment. Collier overruled the opposition. Argument ended.

### Erasing the Red Line

- Jersey and Socony faced a massive obstacle: the Red Line Agreement from their IPC membership. Saudi Arabia was inside the red line Gulbenkian had drawn in 1928. The "self-denying" clause 10 of the IPC agreement effectively prohibited Jersey and Socony from going into Aramco unless they brought Shell, Anglo-Iranian, CFP, and Gulbenkian along.
- Jersey and Socony had wanted out of the Red Line Agreement — they were "straitjacketed in the most prolific oil basin in the world for a mere 11.875 percent each of an enterprise they did not control." The US government had helped them get into the deal in the 1920s, but Washington would not help them get out.
- The "bombshell" solution: the doctrine of "supervening illegality." During WWII, the British government had seized IPC shares held by CFP and Gulbenkian (who had gone to Vichy France) as "enemy aliens." Under this doctrine, the entire IPC agreement was "frustrated" — null and void.
- At war's end, shares reverted to CFP and Gulbenkian. But in late 1946, Jersey and Socony invoked "supervening illegality" with enthusiasm, declaring the whole IPC agreement no longer in effect. They went to London to tell the European members: the old agreement was dissolved, Red Line and all.
- Anglo-Persian and Shell indicated they could work things out on the basis of "mutual interest." The French were furious — they rejected the American contention completely. The IPC was their sole key to Middle Eastern oil, and they would not relinquish it. De Gaulle had reportedly "exploded in rage" when he discovered what small volumes CFP actually produced.
- Gulbenkian, now in residence in Lisbon, defiantly replied: "We do not acquiesce." The IPC had been his life's work, "his great personal monument." He would fight through lawyers and agents to resist efforts to overturn the Red Line Agreement. The new generation of American negotiators dismissed him: "We have no reason to buy Gulbenkian's signature," Harold Sheets of Socony said optimistically.

### Antitrust Worries and the State Department's Alternative

- The danger of litigation over the Red Line was not Jersey and Socony's only worry — would the quadripartite Aramco violate American antitrust laws? Three of the four companies had been spun out of the original Rockefeller Trust. Lawyers concluded the combination would not violate antitrust or the 1911 dissolution decree. Socony's chief counsel worried that seven companies holding "such overwhelming control of crude reserves in the Eastern Hemisphere as well as in the Western Hemisphere" couldn't go on "for any great length of time without some sort of regulation."
- The State Department, under Paul Nitze (head of Office of International Trade Policy), proposed an alternative: have Jersey sell its IPC shares to Socony, then go it alone into Aramco, creating two distinct groups with no overlapping membership. Jersey and Socony rejected this as "not a practicable plan." Undersecretary Dean Acheson scotched Nitze's idea.
- By December 1946, the four companies agreed in general principle to expand Aramco. In January 1947, the French launched a public counterattack — protests at the State Department, commercial pressure on Jersey in France, and CFP's solicitors brought suit in London charging breach of contract.
- Ibn Saud had to be consulted — told the "marriage" of four companies was "a natural" meaning more royalties. He was interested in only one point: that neither Jersey nor Socony were "British-controlled." Reassured of their purely American character, the King approved.
- The final arrangement had to account for the contingency that the French might win in court and demand Aramco participation, which Ibn Saud had made clear he would not tolerate.

### The Aramco Deal Closes

- Jersey and Socony guaranteed a loan of $102 million, convertible to equity once legally safe. They could start taking oil immediately. Jersey and Socony also became partners in Tapline. Socal and Texaco received overriding payments on every barrel for a number of years — altogether about $470 million over several years for selling 40% of Aramco, getting all their original investment back and a good deal more. As Gwin Follis of Socal observed, the sale "took the enormous investment required for Tapline off our shoulders."
- Initially Jersey and Socony planned a 50-50 split of the 40%. But Socony's president, fretful that Middle Eastern oil "was not absolutely safe" and worried about markets, argued the company "ought to put more money in Venezuela." Socony took only 10%, Jersey 30%. "It would not be very long before Socony came to regret its parsimony."
- The US Attorney General gave reassurance: "Off-hand, no legal objections to the deal. It should be a good thing for the country." But political troubles in the eastern Mediterranean threatened — communist insurrection in Greece, Soviet threat to Turkey. On March 11, 1947, Socony's directors discussed "the problems affecting the Middle East." Optimism prevailed, and the next day, March 12, 1947, officials of the four companies signed the historic documents. The concession had been "solidified."
- March 12 was also the day Truman delivered his "all-out speech" to Congress proposing aid to Greece and Turkey — the Truman Doctrine — a landmark of the Cold War. The Truman Doctrine and the sealing of the four-company Aramco deal now assured a substantial American presence stretching from the Mediterranean to the Persian Gulf.

### Gulbenkian Again

- By May 1947, a deal improved the French position in IPC, and CFP withdrew its suit. But Gulbenkian, installed in a first-floor suite at Lisbon's venerable Hotel Aviz, kept to his flint-nosed habits. He no longer kept a car and chauffeur — he hired a driver, carefully checking the odometer to make sure he wasn't being charged for someone else's trips.
- "Gulbenkian may be regarded as a man of his word once this has been given," observed a British official. "The difficulty lies in obtaining it. The ability to compromise is not one of his attributes." He escaped income taxes in France and Portugal by maintaining an appointment with the Iranian legation. To avoid property tax on his Paris mansion, he turned part of it into a picture gallery. When he sold the Ritz Hotel in Paris, he insisted on terms providing a suite permanently reserved for him so he could claim to be "in transit" and avoid French taxation.
- Though the French had dropped their suit, Gulbenkian filed suit in a British court, willing to "wash every last piece of dirty linen in public." Jersey and Socony responded with countersuits. The case received wide publicity — Gulbenkian became a tourist attraction in Lisbon, "one of the tourist 'musts' of Lisbon, along with the bullfights." Owing to his short stature, he had ordered a special platform built in the Hotel Aviz restaurant so he could eat lunch and survey the scene at the same time.
- Gulbenkian's son Nubar described his father's negotiating tactic: "It was my father's practice never to press any claim to a breakdown, but, very able negotiator that he was, to make his demands step by step, so that having obtained satisfaction on one point he would raise another and yet another, thus achieving all he wanted or, at least, much more of what he wanted than he would have obtained if he had started by putting forward all his demands at once."
- Gulbenkian had four separate representatives at negotiations, each reporting to him separately in writing, without collaborating — so he could double-check and second-guess everyone. He told a Socony director he "could not respect himself unless he drove as good a bargain as possible." To an art-loving American, he explained: money itself "did not count for very much" — he thought of himself as "an architect, even as an artist, creating beautiful structures, balancing interests, harmonizing economic forces." His art collection was the greatest ever assembled by a single person. He called the artworks his "children" and seemed to care more for them than for his actual son. But his masterpiece was the Iraq Petroleum Company — "as architecturally designed, as faultlessly composed, as Raphael's The School of Athens." He regarded the Jersey and Socony executives as "Giroloma Genga, a third-rate, mediocre, obscure imitator of the masters of the Renaissance."
- At the last minute in November 1948, with the signing in Lisbon about to take place and a celebratory dinner prepared, Gulbenkian announced at five minutes to seven that he had found "one more point." Consternation gripped the room. Telegrams went to London; a "stunned and depressing silence" descended. The dinner was somber and funereal — "only one bottle of champagne was drunk among twelve people." Around midnight, London telegraphed that Gulbenkian's final demand was acceded to. He signed at 1:30 AM. The documents were sent by chartered plane to London.
- The Group Agreement of November 1948 reconstituted the IPC. Gulbenkian got higher overall production and an extra allocation of oil. "Mr. Five Percent was no more; he was now something greater." An Anglo-Iranian executive declared: "We have now succeeded in making the Agreement completely unintelligible to anybody." One of Gulbenkian's lawyers noted: "No one will ever be able to litigate about these documents because no one will be able to understand them."
- With the Red Line Agreement dissolved, the legal threat to Jersey and Socony's participation in Aramco was removed. "If you laid all the conversations that went into this deal end to end, they would reach to the moon." In December 1948, Aramco was finalized — owned by Jersey, Socony, Socal, and Texaco. 100% American.
- Gulbenkian preserved his IPC and his position. His last display of artistry earned hundreds of millions more for the Gulbenkian interests. He lived on six more years in Lisbon, ceaselessly arguing with IPC partners and rewriting his will. When he died in 1955 at 85, he left three legacies: a vast fortune, a splendid art collection, and — most fittingly — endless litigation over his will and estate.

### Kuwait

- Gulf Oil, half owner of the Kuwait Oil Company, was constrained by its partner Anglo-Iranian from competing in India and the Middle East. Gulf needed outlets for the rapidly rising tide of Kuwaiti oil. Colonel J.F. Drake, Gulf's president, found the answer in Royal Dutch/Shell — one of the two largest marketing organizations in the Eastern Hemisphere, but with little Middle Eastern oil.
- Drake explained to the State Department: "a deal between Gulf, which is long on crude oil and short on markets, and Shell, which is long on markets and short on crude oil, made excellent sense."
- The two companies developed a unique purchase-and-sale agreement — "shadow integration" allowing Kuwaiti oil to flow into Shell's system. Initially a ten-year agreement (later extended 13 more years), it would account for a quarter of Gulf's proven Kuwaiti reserves, providing Shell with 30% of its Eastern Hemisphere needs. They innovated "netback pricing" — a 50-50 profit split defined as "final selling price" minus all costs. The profit-calculation schedules filled over half of the 170-page contract.
- The Kuwaiti Amir would insist on production increases as he saw neighbors' production rising. Shell was about the only system that could absorb so much oil. As the Foreign Office put it, Shell was "to all intents and purposes a partner in the Concession."

### Iran and the Soviet Threat

- The third great postwar oil deal involved Iran. Jersey and Socony privately raised the possibility of a long-term contract for Iranian crude with Sir William Fraser, chairman of Anglo-Iranian. "Willie" was receptive — AIOC feared being shut out of Europe by cheap Aramco oil.
- Political considerations also drove the deal: Iran was under continuing Soviet pressure. During WWII, the USSR had demanded an Iranian oil concession and occupied Azerbaijan in northern Iran. Soviet troops didn't withdraw until spring 1946 under intense US/British pressure. The Iranian Crisis of 1946 was the first major East-West confrontation of the Cold War.
- The American ambassador asked Stalin in April 1946: "What does the Soviet Union want, and how far is Russia going to go?" Stalin's reply: "We're not going much farther." He described Soviet interest in Iran as defensive — "The Baku oil fields are our major source of supply. They are close to the Iranian border and they are very vulnerable." Stalin, the former "journeyman of the Revolution" in Baku, warned that "saboteurs — even a man with a box of matches — might cause us serious damage. We are not going to risk our oil supply."
- Soviet oil production in 1945 was only 60% of 1941. Fields were seriously damaged and depleted. Stalin interrogated his petroleum minister Nikolai Baibakov (who would run the Soviet economy for two decades until Gorbachev replaced him). Stalin demanded to know what to do about the "very bad oil position." Efforts would have to be redoubled.
- In 1940, Soviet Foreign Minister Molotov had declared "the area south of Batum and Baku in the general direction of the Persian Gulf be recognized as the center of the aspirations of the Soviet Union." That area was Iran.
- After Soviet withdrawal, the communist-led Tudeh party conducted demonstrations and strikes at Anglo-Iranian's Abadan refinery, where people were killed. Iran was unstable, with the grave possibility of civil war or absorption into the Soviet bloc.
- Both American and British governments sought to preserve Iranian independence. London was categoric: Anglo-Iranian's oil position "was the company's crown jewels, and it had to be preserved at all costs." Having American companies take a direct interest in Iranian oil would help "solidify" the position. In September 1947, Anglo-Iranian, Jersey, and Socony signed a twenty-year contract.

### Europe's Energy Crisis and the Marshall Plan

- Europe in 1946 was devastated — Germany barely functioning, food and raw materials in short supply, trading patterns broken, inflation rampant, severe shortage of American dollars. By 1946, Europe was gripped by a terrible coal shortage. The winter of 1946-47 was the longest and coldest of the century — the Thames froze at Windsor, power stations shut down, electricity was cut to industry, unemployment increased six times over, and British industrial production halted for three weeks — "something German bombing had never been able to accomplish."
- This crisis drove home Britain's impoverishment. In February 1947, the Labour government referred Palestine to the UN, announced Indian independence, and told the US it could no longer afford to prop up Greece. Britain asked the US to take over broader responsibilities in the Near and Middle East.
- In June 1947 at Harvard, Secretary of State George Marshall introduced the European Recovery Program (Marshall Plan), which became central to containment of Soviet power. "Without petroleum the Marshall Plan could not have functioned," said a US government report. About 20% of total Marshall Plan aid went to oil and oil equipment imports. Oil was the largest single item in European dollar budgets.
- The "dollar oil" vs. "sterling oil" debate erupted — British Foreign Secretary Ernest Bevin told the American ambassador that "while the Americans were voting money to help Europe, the rise in oil prices nullified their efforts to a considerable extent." Middle Eastern oil prices were eventually pushed below the US Gulf Coast benchmark — ending the Achnacarry pricing convention. The last vestige of the "As-Is" system was gone.
- The Marshall Plan drove Europe's transition from coal to imported oil. Coal shortages, labor strife in mines, and communist influence in miners' unions made oil essential. "It is not a joyful thing, but it is a national necessity to import more oil," Britain's Chancellor Hugh Dalton told Marshall. Oil could effectively compete against coal in price. Central heating was the revolutionary innovation — "The Englishman began to realize that there was no value in being cold."
- In 1946, 77% of Europe's oil came from the Western Hemisphere; by 1951, 80% was expected from the Middle East.

### Tapline and the Palestine/Israel Problem

- Aramco's four parents continued to battle to build Tapline to carry Saudi oil to the Mediterranean. Steel was in short supply under US government controls. Independent oil men tried to block the steel allocation to prevent cheap foreign oil flooding America. The Truman Administration supported Tapline as essential to the Marshall Plan: "the European recovery program will be seriously handicapped" without it.
- Syria and other transit countries demanded exorbitant fees. The partition of Palestine and creation of Israel complicated everything. Ibn Saud was bitterly anti-Zionist — he said Jews "had been the enemies of Arabs since the seventh century." He told Truman that American support for a Jewish state "would be a death blow to American interests in the Arab world" and that the Arabs "will lay siege to it until it dies of famine."
- When Ibn Saud visited Aramco's Dhahran headquarters in 1947, he praised the oranges served but pointedly asked if they were from Palestine (a kibbutz). He was reassured they were from California. In opposing a Jewish state, Ibn Saud held a "trump card": he could cancel the Aramco concession.
- On May 14, 1948, Israel declared statehood, recognized almost instantly by the Soviet Union and the US. The Arab League launched a full-scale attack — the first Arab-Israeli war. James Terry Duce of Aramco warned Secretary Marshall that Ibn Saud "may be compelled, in certain circumstances, to apply sanctions against the American oil concessions." But a State Department study found the Middle East provided only 6% of free world oil supplies and "such a cut in consumption could be achieved without substantial hardship."
- Ibn Saud could have canceled the concession but at considerable risk — Aramco was his sole source of rapidly rising wealth, and the US relationship guaranteed Saudi territorial integrity. He feared the British might sponsor the Hashemites (whom he had driven from Mecca) to recapture western Saudi Arabia. He pressed for a tripartite US-British-Saudi defensive treaty. He found he could "distinguish between Aramco, a purely commercial enterprise owned by four private companies, and the policy of the U.S. government elsewhere in the region." When other Arab countries pressed him to cancel the concession, he replied that oil royalties helped make Saudi Arabia "a stronger and more powerful nation, better to assist her neighboring Arab states in resisting Jewish pretensions."
- Tapline was finished in September 1950 — 1,040 miles, with oil arriving at Sidon, Lebanon in November. It replaced 7,200 miles of sea journey via the Suez Canal, equivalent to sixty tankers in continuous operation.

### No Longer "Far Afield": The New Dimension of Security

- The British, even withdrawing from empire, could not turn their backs on the Middle East. Iran, Kuwait, and Iraq were major oil sources. Anglo-Iranian dividends were a major revenue generator. Foreign Secretary Bevin told the Cabinet Defense Committee: "Without the Middle East and its oil, I see no hope of our being able to achieve the standard of living at which we are aiming in Great Britain."
- The US shift was even more dramatic. No longer would any American President say Saudi Arabia was "a little far afield." The US was becoming an ever more petroleum-based society that could no longer supply its own needs. Soviet expansionism brought the Middle East to center stage — the oil resources were "no less vital, in its own way, than the independence of Western Europe."
- Saudi Arabia became the dominant focus. "Here was what is probably the richest economic prize in the world in the field of foreign investment," said one American official in 1948. In October 1950, Truman wrote Ibn Saud: "No threat to your Kingdom could occur which would not be a matter of immediate concern to the United States." It sounded like a guarantee.
- The special US-Saudi relationship was "an unlikely union — Bedouin Arabs and Texas oil men, a traditional Islamic autocracy allied with modern American capitalism. Yet it was one that was destined to endure."

### The End of Energy Independence

- Middle Eastern oil in wartime was "very susceptible to enemy interference" (Joint Chiefs of Staff). Some argued for importing more oil in peacetime to preserve domestic reserves for wartime. Eugene V. Rostow's book A National Policy for the Oil Industry made this case. The National Security Resources Board argued in 1948 that importing Middle Eastern oil would allow a million barrels/day of Western Hemisphere production to be shut in — "the ideal storage place for petroleum."
- Synthetic fuels were promoted as an alternative. The New York Times declared in 1948 that "The United States is on the threshold of a profound chemical revolution... gasoline will be produced from coal, air, and water." The Interior Department claimed gasoline from coal or shale could cost eleven cents a gallon (wholesale was twelve cents). But cost projections kept rising — by 1951, synthetic gasoline from coal would cost 3.5 times the market price. Cheap foreign oil killed synthetic fuels. "Imported petroleum killed synthetic fuels. And they would remain dead for three decades."
- Technology opened new domestic frontiers — greater drilling depth and offshore production. Drilling off piers near Santa Barbara dated to the mid-1890s (1-2 barrels/day). In the 1930s, drillers walked into shallow waters off Texas and Louisiana.
- Going truly offshore into the Gulf of Mexico required creating a new industry. Kerr-McGee, an Oklahoma independent, took the gamble. Technology for building platforms, drilling into the ocean floor, or servicing offshore operations barely existed. Knowledge of weather (hurricanes), tides, and currents was rudimentary.
- Kerr-McGee reasoned it couldn't compete for "real class-one" onshore acreage against majors, but offshore had hardly any competition — most companies thought it impossible. On a clear Sunday morning in October 1947, on Block 32, ten and a half miles off the Louisiana coast, Kerr-McGee struck oil. A landmark event. But offshore wells cost five times onshore wells of similar depth, and a struggle between the federal government and states over continental shelf ownership (and tax revenues) wasn't resolved until 1953.
- Natural gas was "the orphan of the oil industry" — considered a useless, inconvenient byproduct, burned off ("flared") because there was nothing else to do with it. Only a fraction was used, mostly in the Southwest. It sold for one-fifth the energy-equivalent cost of oil. The problem was transmission — pipelines had meant 150 miles maximum. But the House Armed Services Committee declared natural gas "the quickest and cheapest method immediately available to reduce domestic consumption of petroleum."
- In 1947, the Big Inch and Little Inch wartime oil pipelines were converted to natural gas. Los Angeles was connected to New Mexico and West Texas gas fields via the "Biggest Inch" pipeline (El Paso Natural Gas). By 1950, interstate natural gas movement reached 2.5 trillion cubic feet — 2.5 times the 1946 level. Without the additional natural gas, American oil demand would have been 700,000 barrels per day higher.
- By 1950, the postwar petroleum order was established, centered on the Middle East. US oil consumption jumped 12% in 1950 over 1949. A July 1950 Jersey analysis warned: "Mid-East crudes available to Jersey may exceed requirements substantially." The massive surplus would confront the industry. Bitter battles over profit division were already erupting.

## Chapter 22: Fifty-Fifty: The New Deal in Oil

### The Fight Over Rents

- In 1950, US Treasury representatives conferring with British officials mentioned "certain developments regarding Saudi Arabian oil policies, the effects of which would surely be felt throughout the Middle East." The Saudi government had made "startling demands on Aramco... They covered all possible points ever thought up by a concessionary government." All the demands came down to one thing: the Saudis wanted more money.
- In the late 1940s and early 1950s, oil companies and governments grappled continually over financial terms. The central issue was the division of "rents" — "that uneasy and important term in the economics of natural resources." The objective of producing countries was to shift revenues from oil companies and the treasuries of consuming countries to themselves. But it was not just about money — it was also about power.

### Landlord and Tenant — Ricardo's Framework

- The intellectual framework came from David Ricardo, the fantastically successful late-18th/early-19th century English stockbroker (who made a killing on Wellington's defeat of Napoleon at Waterloo). A Jew who became a Quaker, then a House of Commons member, Ricardo was one of the founding fathers of modern economics, the successor generation (with Thomas Malthus) to Adam Smith.
- Ricardo developed the concept of "rents" — the difference between the market price and the cost of production (including allowances for transport, processing, distribution, and some return on capital). Two landlords with fields of different fertility sell grain at the same price; the one with more fertile land gets not just profit but also rents — rewards from "nature's bountiful legacy," not from ingenuity or hard work.
- Applied to oil: in the late 1940s, oil sold for about $2.50/barrel. A Texas stripper-well operator might make 10 cents profit. In the Middle East, production cost only 25 cents/barrel. Deducting 50 cents for other costs and 10 cents "profit," that left $1.65 per barrel of rents on every barrel of Middle Eastern oil. The question: who — the host country, the producing company, or the consuming country — would get those rents?
- The host country had sovereignty over the oil. But the oil had no value until the foreign company risked its capital and expertise. The host country was the landlord; the company was the tenant. "A rich discovery means a dissatisfied landlord," said oil economist M.A. Adelman. "He knows that the tenant's profit is far greater than is necessary to keep him producing, and he wants some of the rent. If he gets some, he wants more."
- The battle was also political — producing countries saw foreign companies as "exploiting" the country, draining the "irreplaceable heritage," acting as the "all-too-visible embodiment of colonialism" in a "haughty, arrogant, and superior manner." The companies saw it differently: they had taken risks, invested capital, created value where none existed. Their plaintive cry was "We wuz robbed."
- For consuming countries, oil was a strategic prize vital to their economies and national strategy — and also a significant source of tax revenues. For producing countries, oil meant "power, influence, significance, and status — all of which were previously lacking." Money signified both power and pride.

### Venezuela's Ritual Cleansing — The Origin of Fifty-Fifty

- General Gomez's tyrannical dictatorship ended with his death in 1935. He had treated Venezuela as "a sole proprietorship, a personal hacienda." Much of the population was impoverished while the oil industry dominated the economy. The democratic opposition centered on the "Generation of '28" — students at the Universidad Central in Caracas who had rebelled against Gomez. They were crushed: some got sixty-pound leg irons, others were exiled or sent to road crews in disease-ridden jungles. Many perished. The survivors became the nucleus of reformers.
- With oil accounting for over 90% of Venezuela's total export value by the late 1930s, Gomez's successors reformed the oil industry's regulation and reallocated rents. The US government was the catalyst — conscious of the Mexican nationalization disaster, Washington wanted to protect access to Venezuela, the most important foreign oil source. The Roosevelt Administration made clear that Jersey could not count on US support if it failed to adapt.
- Jersey was sharply divided. Traditionalists (some Gomez cronies) wanted to stand firm. Wallace Pratt, the former chief geologist turned senior executive, argued that adaptation was "not only inevitable but essential." Better to help create the new order than be a victim. Jersey under political attack in Washington (I.G. Farben scandal, new antitrust campaign) could not risk losing Venezuela.
- Jersey installed Arthur Proudfit as top man in Venezuela — he had been part of the 1920s migration from Mexico and brought lasting memories of the disaster of government-company relations and bitter labor strife there. All sides wanted to work things out. U.S. Undersecretary of State Sumner Welles took the unprecedented step of recommending independent consultants to Venezuela, including Herbert Hoover Jr. (son of the former president, a well-known geologist).
- A settlement was hammered out based on "fifty-fifty" — a landmark. Royalties and taxes would be raised to the point where the government's take would about equal the companies' net profits in Venezuela. In exchange, the validity of concessions would be confirmed, their life extended, and new exploration opportunities opened.
- The proposed law was criticized by Accion Democratica (the liberal/socialist party of the Generation of '28 survivors). Juan Pablo Perez Alfonzo declared: "The total purification of the Venezuelan oil industry, its ritual cleansing, will remain impossible until the companies have paid adequate financial compensation to our country." Despite Accion Democratica's abstention, the Venezuelan Congress passed the new petroleum law in March 1943.
- Shell director Frederick Godber's reaction: "What they are after is money. Unless they are prompted to do so by our friends across the water, they are not likely yet to reject good money from wherever it comes." Some smaller companies were outraged — William F. Buckley (Pantepec Oil) called it "burdensome" and accepted "under compulsion."
- In 1945, Romulo Betancourt and Accion Democratica came to power via a coup. Minister of Development Juan Pablo Perez Alfonzo found the promised 50-50 split had actually worked out to about 60-40 in companies' favor. He instituted tax revisions to ensure a true 50-50. By 1948, the government's total income was six times that of 1942.
- Perez Alfonzo broke another precedent: he insisted Venezuela take some royalties in kind (in oil rather than money), then sold the royalty oil directly on the world market. "The veil of mystery over the marketing of oil — behind which the Anglo-Saxons had maintained a monopoly of rights and secrets — was removed forever," declared President Betancourt.
- Creole (Jersey's subsidiary) adapted successfully — workforce became 90% Venezuelan; Arthur Proudfit even lobbied on behalf of the Venezuelan government with the US State Department. Betancourt recognized realities: "It would have been a suicidal leap into space to nationalize the industry by decree." Venezuela received 7% more per barrel than Mexico got from its nationalized industry. Venezuelan production was six times Mexico's.
- But in November 1948, the Accion Democratica government was overthrown by the same military clique from the 1945 coup. Some operators applauded; Buckley claimed AD had "used the vast dollar resources of the country to further Russian Communistic interests." But the major companies found the coup "disheartening and disappointing" — it threatened three years of stable relationship-building. Betancourt had invited Nelson A. Rockefeller (grandson of John D.) to establish the International Basic Economy Corporation for development projects in Venezuela.

### The Neutral Zone — Precedent-Shattering Terms

- The Neutral Zone was about 2,000 square miles of desert between Kuwait and Saudi Arabia, carved out by the British in 1922 with shared sovereignty. If every system has the seeds of its own destruction, it was in the Neutral Zone that the erosion of the postwar petroleum order began.
- The State Department encouraged "new companies" to participate in Middle Eastern oil development to counterbalance the majors. Washington circularized American companies about Kuwait putting its Neutral Zone rights up for bid. Major companies feared that bidding would force them to offer terms far better than their current concessions.
- Ralph Davies, former Socal marketing executive and Ickes's wartime deputy, organized a consortium of independents (Phillips, Ashland, Sinclair) called "Aminoil" (American Independent Oil Company). Davies warned partners they were "bucking the big, big, big time."
- Aminoil had a unique entree: Jim Brooks, a Texas oil field welder returning from Saudi Arabia, had stopped at Shepheard's Hotel in Cairo. The secretary to the Amir of Kuwait was also there, tasked with finding a Texas oil man unconnected to the majors. Brooks's cowboy hat caught his attention. Brooks ended up at the Dasman Palace, where he earned lasting gratitude by adjusting the plumbing to cut water use by 90%.
- Aminoil won with a bid that stunned the industry: $7.5 million cash, $625,000 minimum annual royalty, 15% of profits, and a million-dollar yacht for the Amir.

### "The Best Hotel in Town" — J. Paul Getty

- The Saudi Arabian rights in the Neutral Zone were also up for grabs. Within eight years of winning the concession, Jean Paul Getty (or "J. Paul Getty" as he styled himself) would become the richest man in America.
- Getty was "inward, vain, and insecure" but driven by "a powerful need to make money, matched by an extraordinary talent for doing so." His philosophy: "There's always the best hotel in town and the best room in the best hotel in town, and there's always somebody in it." He was no more trusting than Gulbenkian: "One is very nearly always let down by underlings... They may be all right for 80 percent of the time, but for 20 percent, they do something quite incredible."
- Getty could not stand to lose a contest or share authority. "I had a perfect record with J. Paul Getty," said a business partner. "I had a thousand fights with him, and never won a single one." He was a gambler, but cautious: "If I wanted to gamble on typical gambling games, I'd buy a casino and have the percentages for me, rather than play."
- Getty's father was a Minnesota insurance company lawyer who went to Oklahoma to collect a bad debt and became a millionaire oil man. The father's word was his bond; the son engaged in "sharp practices" and turned them into an art. Getty was "well built, pugnacious by nature, and quick," said boxer Jack Dempsey, who had sparred with him. "I've never met anybody with such intense concentration and willpower — perhaps more than is good for him."
- Getty married five times, with "a special predilection for teenage girls." He traveled in Europe under the alias "Mr. Paul" because it was "less noticed" that he was "in transit flagrante with two or three women at a time." The only true love of his life may have been a French woman, Madame Marguerite Tallasou, with whom he had a passionate affair in Constantinople in 1913 — he lost contact with her in the turmoil of war and revolution.
- Getty wrote at least seven books (including one for Playboy on how to be rich), launched a major art collection, but never let outside interests interfere with his quest for money through oil. "A man is a business failure if he lets his family life interfere with his business record." Or: "When I'm thinking about oil, I'm not thinking about girls."
- Getty was "obsessed with value. If he thought something had value, he bought it and never sold it." In the 1920s, he decided it was cheaper to drill for oil than buy oil company shares. After the 1929 crash, he reversed — oil shares at great discounts became his target, leading to an extended takeover battle for Tidewater Oil against Standard of New Jersey. Those Depression-era stock purchases were the basis of his fortune.
- Getty was ruthless in pursuit of bargains. During the Depression, he fired all employees and rehired them at lower salaries. In 1938, he bought the Pierre Hotel in New York for $2.4 million (less than a quarter of construction cost). That same year in Vienna, after the Nazi seizure of Austria, he got himself into Baron Louis de Rothschild's home (the Baron was held prisoner by the Nazis) to look at furniture that would "soon be available." He went to Berlin to check what the SS planned, and ended up buying Rothschild pieces at a great discount.
- Getty had extensive social connections with Nazi leaders and perhaps sympathized with the Nazis. The FBI and military intelligence suspected him. His application for a naval commission "was rejected because of suspected espionage activities." He remained fascinated by dictators for life.
- During WWII, Getty managed an airplane factory in Tulsa from a concrete bunker (fearing Luftwaffe bombing). He chewed each mouthful 33 times, washed his own underwear nightly due to antipathy toward commercial detergents, and by age 55 had his second facelift and was dyeing his hair a "funny kind of reddish-brown" giving him a "wizened, embalmed look."

### Getty Wins the Saudi Neutral Zone Concession

- Getty was certain he wanted the Saudi Neutral Zone concession: "If one is to be anybody in the world oil business, one must have a footing in the Middle East." He sent Paul Walton, a young MIT-trained geologist who had worked for Socal in Saudi Arabia in the 1930s, as his point man. At the Pierre Hotel, Getty briefed Walton, who remembered Getty's "half-mad expression on his face — a sort of angry, disagreeable scowl" designed to keep people at a distance from him and his money.
- Walton went to Jidda and negotiated with Abdullah Suleiman (the same finance minister who had handled the original Socal concession in 1933). Suleiman arranged for Walton to fly low over the Neutral Zone in a DC-3. Walton saw a small mound rising from the flat expanse — it looked almost exactly like the mound in Kuwait's Burgan field, then the largest known oil field in the world. He was elated.
- Walton was extremely cautious about security — no locks on hotel rooms in Jidda, so he left no papers and sent a handwritten letter by airmail rather than wireless. He told Getty the odds of a major oil play were fifty-fifty — better than the one-in-ten or one-in-twenty odds in the Rockies.
- Negotiations were conducted mostly on Suleiman's porch. The deal: $9.5 million up front, $1 million/year guaranteed even with no oil found, and a royalty of 55 cents a barrel — far higher than anywhere else. Getty also agreed to build housing, schools, a mosque, provide free gasoline for the Saudi Army, and establish training programs. Suleiman even insisted Getty pay for a Saudi Army unit to defend the concession area. A telegram from Secretary of State Dean Acheson was required to take that demand off the table (American companies were legally prohibited from funding foreign armies).
- Getty had authorized Walton to go up to $10.5 million, but Suleiman stopped at $9.5 million. Suleiman also told Aminoil and a Wall Street firm that if they topped Getty's offer, the concession was theirs — but neither would match the price. Pacific Western was paying an unprecedentedly high price to wildcat in an unknown desert.

### The Unhappy Marriage and Five Dry Holes

- Kuwait and Saudi Arabia each held an "undivided half interest" in the Neutral Zone, so Aminoil and Getty's Pacific Western had to amalgamate operations. The result was "a totally unhappy marriage" — Getty and Ralph Davies of Aminoil could not stand each other.
- Aminoil led exploration. By early 1953, half a decade in, they had spent over $30 million and had nothing to show but five dry holes. Getty was disgusted. He wandered Europe, spent weeks researching a Rembrandt he owned, totted up his income and expenses each evening (income in thousands/millions; expenses included "newspaper — 10 centimes" and "bus fare — 5 centimes"). He bought a rare Louis XV lacquer table and enrolled in a $178 course at Arthur Murray's School of Dancing (samba, jitterbug, and leading).
- Getty insisted the sixth hole be drilled at the little mound Walton had spotted from the airplane, which the Aminoil team had resolutely ignored. Sunk costs were sunk costs — if the sixth was dry, he was pulling out. In March 1953, the Aminoil team struck oil where Walton had always thought it would be found. Fortune described it as "somewhere between colossal and history-making."

### Billionaire

- Getty made his first visit to the region only afterward, listening to a "Teach Yourself Arabic" course on records. Ralph Davies of Aminoil never made it to the Neutral Zone — he had "a pathological fear of dust, dirt and germs."
- Getty used his Neutral Zone production, especially the cheap, heavier "garbage oil," to build vast integrated oil operations in the US, Western Europe, and Japan. He reorganized all holdings under Getty Oil, making himself sole commander. By the late 1950s, he was the seventh-largest marketer of gasoline in the United States. Fortune announced in 1957 that he was America's richest man and sole billionaire. His reaction: "My bankers kept telling me that it was so, but I was hoping I wouldn't be found out." He added: "If you can count your money, you don't have a billion dollars."
- Getty became the Billionaire Miser. At his 72-room Tudor manor house, Sutton Place, in Surrey, amid his priceless art and antiques collection, he installed a pay phone for guests.
- Paul Walton, who had spotted the mound and negotiated the deal, came down with amebic dysentery in Saudi Arabia in 1948. It took three years to recover. Getty gave him a $1,200 bonus. Walton returned to Salt Lake City to work as an independent geologist. The Neutral Zone had more than a billion barrels of recoverable reserves — making Getty not only the richest American but the richest private citizen in the world. Walton continued putting together ordinary drilling deals.
- When Getty died in 1976 at 83, the Duke of Bedford eulogized: "When I think of Paul, I think of money." For Getty, "there surely could have been no higher compliment."

### Impact of Getty's Terms — Gulbenkian's Warning

- Getty's 55-cent royalty to the Saudis loomed over Aminoil's 35 cents to Kuwait, Aramco's roughly 33 cents, Anglo-Iranian's and IPC's 16.5 cents to Iran and Iraq, and the Kuwait Oil Company's 15 cents. The IPC general manager called the 55-cent royalty "completely insane, uncalled for, and responsible for the difficulties being encountered in Iran and Iraq." A British diplomat angrily denounced "the notorious Pacific Western concession."
- Gulbenkian, the veteran negotiator, prophesied: "These new groups lack the experience of developing oil concessions in the Middle East. They offer fantastic terms to the local governments who expect similar fantasies from us. The result is trouble all around." He warned: "I feel confident that the local governments, although by no means cordially disposed with each other, will come together on this question of petroleum concessions, and do their best to squeeze us. I fear that the wind of nationalization and other complications may spread to us also. I would not be tranquil."

### "Retreat Is Inevitable" — The Saudi Fifty-Fifty Deal

- In 1949, world demand for Saudi oil flattened (American recession, British economic problems). Saudi revenues fell, but the King's financial commitments kept growing. Soldiers went unpaid, tribal subsidies were withheld, debt piled up — reminiscent of the crises of the 1930s and early 1940s.
- Finance Minister Abdullah Suleiman regularly threatened to "close down the entire oil operation" unless Saudi Arabia got a greater share of "large company profits." Demands were endless: construction projects, a "welfare fund," new loans. Aramco's general counsel: "Each time the company agreed to one thing, there was always just one more."
- A Venezuelan delegation had traveled to the Middle East, promulgating the fifty-fifty concept and translating documents into Arabic. Betancourt explained their motive: "competition from low-cost, high-volume production from the Middle East was a grave threat to Venezuela." Better to get Middle Eastern costs up. The Venezuelans "decided to spread the benefits of the fifty-fifty principle to the area which was taking business away from them — the Middle East."
- The Venezuelan delegation got only as close to Saudi Arabia as Basra, Iraq (the Saudis wouldn't let them in because Venezuela had voted for Israel at the UN). But the concept crossed the border. The Saudis saw that in 1949, Aramco's profits were almost three times Saudi Arabia's own earnings from the concession. Even more striking: the US government's tax take from Aramco ($43 million) was $4 million higher than Aramco's royalty payments to Riyadh. The Saudis "weren't a darn bit happy about that."

### The Foreign Tax Credit — The Mechanism

- The Saudis, unbeknownst to Aramco, retained their own adviser on American tax law and discovered the "foreign tax credit." Under legislation dating to 1918, an American company overseas could deduct foreign income taxes (not royalties) from its US tax bill. If Saudi Arabia collected $39 million in royalties AND $39 million in taxes, that $39 million tax bill could be deducted from Aramco's $43 million US tax obligation — leaving only $4 million to the US Treasury. Saudi Arabia's take would double from $39 million to $78 million. The overall tax bite on Aramco would stay the same, but most would be collected in Riyadh, not Washington.
- In August 1950, Aramco authorized negotiations for a fundamental revision. The State Department was a strong proponent — the Korean War had begun in June 1950, and Washington worried about communist influence and Soviet expansion in the Middle East. Anti-Western nationalists had to be kept at bay. "Since company retreat is inevitable," concluded a State Department policy paper, "it would seem useful to make the retreat as beneficial and orderly as possible to all concerned."
- George McGhee, Assistant Secretary of State for Near Eastern Affairs: "The Saudis knew the Venezuelans were getting 50/50. Why wouldn't they want it too?" At a State Department meeting on September 18, 1950, McGhee told oil company representatives the time was for "rolling with the punch." He bluntly told the parent companies there was no alternative — long-term contracts created "the practical necessity of horsetrading."
- On December 30, 1950, after a month of complex negotiations, Aramco and Saudi Arabia signed the new fifty-fifty agreement.

### The Tax Credit Confirmed — Revenue Shift

- The IRS did not confirm the tax credit's eligibility until 1955 when auditing Aramco's 1950 return. In 1957, the Joint Congressional Committee on Internal Revenue Taxation approved, finding the ruling consistent with tax laws and IRS precedent.
- The revenue shift was dramatic: in 1949, the US Treasury collected $43 million from Aramco vs. $39 million in royalties to Saudi Arabia. By 1951, Saudi Arabia collected $110 million from Aramco, while Aramco paid only $6 million to the US Treasury.
- The impact spread swiftly. Kuwait insisted on fifty-fifty; Gulf overcame Anglo-Iranian chairman Sir William Fraser's "obdurate objections." Iraq followed by early 1952. Britain's Inland Revenue eventually accepted the tax credit mechanism.

### Jersey's Internal Assessment

- Inside Jersey, a working paper analyzed the significance: "We now know that the safety of our position in any country depends not alone on compliance with laws and contracts, or on the rate or amount of our payments to the government, but on whether our whole relationship is accepted at any given moment by the government and public opinion of the country — and by our own government and public opinion — as 'fair.'" Unfortunately, "'fairness' and 'unfairness' are essentially concepts of the emotions rather than fixed and measurable standards."
- The paper warned: "If we ever admit in any country that an equal division is less than 'fair,' the ground will be cut out from under our feet in every country." Fifty-fifty was where Jersey should make its stand: "'50/50' is a good position which needs no defense and is hard to attack; '55/45' or '60/40' would have no such appeal, and could be only rear-guard defense positions in an unlimited retreat."

### The Watershed

- The Saudi-Aramco agreement was described by a historian of the British empire's decline as "an economic and political watershed no less significant for the Middle East than the transfer of power for India and Pakistan." For the American government, it satisfied the urgent need to increase income to Saudi Arabia and other producing countries to maintain the postwar petroleum order and keep "friendly" regimes in power.
- Taxing oil company profits was more efficient than getting foreign aid through Congress. The fifty-fifty principle had the right psychological feel. George McGhee, quizzed at a 1974 Senate hearing about whether the tax credit was "a very ingenious way of transferring many millions by executive decision out of the public treasury and into the hands of a foreign government treasury," disagreed: "The ownership of this oil concession was a valuable asset for our country. The risk of not doing something along these lines was much too great. In essence, the threat was the loss of the concession."
- But within six months of the Saudi deal (December 1950), events in neighboring Iran would prove that the landlord-tenant relationship had by no means been satisfactorily resolved.

## Chapter 23: "Old Mossy" and the Struggle for Iran

### The Young Shah

- When Reza Pahlavi, the former Shah, died in exile in South Africa in 1944, his son Mohammed Reza Pahlavi was devastated. "My grief was immense." He had worshipped his father — the resolute, towering commander of the Persian Cossack brigade who had seized power and crowned himself Shah in the 1920s. Reza Shah had brought order, begun modernization, and subjugated the powerful mullahs.
- The son's grief was compounded by guilt — he had been part of his father's downfall. In August 1941, after Germany invaded the Soviet Union, Britain and Russia moved forces into Iran to protect the Abadan refinery and the supply line to the USSR. Alarmed by German advances in Russia and North Africa, the Allies feared a pincer converging on Iran. They deposed Reza Shah (who had shown friendliness toward the Nazis) and replaced him with his 21-year-old son.
- Mohammed Reza Pahlavi was forever haunted by his father's memory, striving to be worthy of Reza Shah. His sister Ashraf once asked "whether I was a man or a mouse." He had been entrusted to a French governess at six, sent to school in Switzerland at twelve. The American ambassador mused in 1950 that "he is a little too Westernized for an Oriental country."
- The young Shah faced treacherous circumstances that would challenge the most experienced politicians. His dynasty's legitimacy was problematic. He contended with chronic foreign intervention, direct Soviet pressure, and a highly visible British economic presence. Iran's political system was riven by every division — class, regional, religious, modern vs. traditional.
- On one side were Islamic fundamentalists led by the fiery Ayatollah Seyed Kashani, outraged by every intrusion of the modern world. On the other were the communists and the Tudeh party, a well-organized leftist party tied to Moscow. In between were reformers, nationalists, republicans, and military officers.
- Iran's political culture was "chaotic and phantasmagoric, given to wild exaggeration and violent emotion. Graft and corruption were a way of life." The British charge d'affaires summed up Majlis (parliament) rules: "Deputies expect to be bribed." Tribes and clans in the countryside hated subordination to Tehran. Virtually no part of the Shah's domain was immune to secessionist drives.
- The nation was gripped by grinding poverty and economic collapse, with pervasive hopelessness. Only one thing united the country: hatred of foreigners, especially the British. The English were regarded as "almost supernatural devils, controlling and manipulating the entire nation." Every politician was "virtually obliged to accuse his enemies and opponents of being British agents." Even droughts, crop failures, and locust plagues were blamed on "those clever Englishmen."
