- The detestation centered on Anglo-Iranian Oil Company — the largest industrial employer in Iran, the major source of foreign earnings, and "the all-too-tangible symbol of the intrusion of the modern foreign world." Between 1945 and 1950, AIOC registered £250 million profit vs. £90 million in royalties to Iran. The British government received more in taxes from Anglo-Iranian than Iran did in royalties. Dividends went to the majority owner, the British government, and it was rumored AIOC sold oil to the British Navy at a substantial discount.

### The Last Chance

- Through WWII, Iran was seen as primarily a "British show." But the Cold War shoved Iran to the fore of American foreign policy concerns. By 1949, Americans feared Iran was so decayed economically and politically that it would be easy prey for the Soviet Union.
- In February 1949, a Muslim fanatic posing as a photographer tried to kill the Shah at Tehran University, firing a half-dozen shots at point-blank range but only slightly wounding him. The Shah used the assassination attempt to impose martial law, assert authority, and exhume his father's body from South Africa (now posthumously "the Great") for a state funeral. Huge equestrian statues of Reza Shah would rise across the country.
- George McGhee, the American point man (age 37 in 1949, called "that infant prodigy" by the British), pushed the British government and Anglo-Iranian to increase royalties to Iran. McGhee was actually an Anglophile — a Rhodes Scholar who had known John Cadman's daughters at Oxford and been offered a job as a geophysicist in Iran by Anglo-Iranian. He married the daughter of Everette DeGolyer. But he thought the British needed saving from their "nineteenth-century attitude toward oil." Secretary of State Dean Acheson criticized "the unusual and persistent stupidity of the company and the British Government" on Iran.
- Despite being a 51% owner, the British government had no great affection for Anglo-Iranian. There was suspicion and rancor — "a classic case of what has been called the 'battle between Minister and Manager.'" Foreign Secretary Bevin complained in 1946 that Anglo-Iranian "is virtually a private company with state capital and anything it does reacts upon the relationships between the British Government and Persia. As Foreign Secretary, I have no power or influence, in spite of this great holding by the Government, to do anything at all."
- Anglo-Iranian, the third-largest crude producer in the world, thought Iran got a pretty good deal — not only a royalty but 20% of worldwide profits, better than any other producer received. The company saw itself as a private firm trying to carry on a complex global business. Its senior executives resented the intrusions of politicians and civil servants — "West End gentlemen" who "simply did not understand the oil business."

### Sir William Fraser's Obstinacy

- Fraser was a tough, implacable autocrat who ran Anglo-Iranian one way — his way. Dissent was not tolerated. The Gulf chairman observed that Fraser's domination was so total that Anglo-Iranian's other directors "did not dare call their souls their own." He was "a Scotsman to his fingertips" — his father had founded the leading Scottish shale oil company and then sold it to Anglo-Iranian, hence "Willie came with the shale." A Foreign Office minister said Fraser had "all the contempt of a Glasgow accountant for anything which can not be shown on a balance sheet." Another British official: "obstinate, narrow old skinflint."
- Fraser implacably resisted entreaties to negotiate with Iran and ignored the Americans. But in autumn 1950, he had an abrupt change of heart — learning about the "McGhee Bombshell" (the imminent Saudi fifty-fifty deal), he knew time had run out. He offered substantially more money, subsidies for Iranian development, and support for education. But it was too late. In December, the Saudi fifty-fifty deal was announced, forcing Premier Razmara to withdraw support for the Supplemental Agreement. Anglo-Iranian then offered its own fifty-fifty deal — but it was no longer enough.

### Mossadegh and Nationalization

- The entire opposition now focused on Anglo-Iranian. Mohammed Mossadegh, chairman of the Majlis oil committee, declared: "The source of all the misfortunes of this tortured nation is only the oil company." Another deputy thundered it would be better for the Iranian oil industry "to be destroyed by an atomic bomb than remain in the hands of Anglo-Iranian."
- Prime Minister Razmara, a lean young "soldier's soldier" (St. Cyr graduate, known for having once returned a bribe), came out against nationalization in a speech to Parliament in March 1951. Four days later, as he was about to enter Tehran's central mosque, he was assassinated by a young carpenter entrusted by Islamic terrorists with the "sacred mission" of killing "the British stooge." A week later, the minister of education was also assassinated.
- The Majlis passed a resolution nationalizing the oil industry. On April 28, 1951, the Majlis chose Mossadegh as Prime Minister with the mandate to execute the nationalization law. The Shah signed it; it went into effect May 1. In the nationalization decree, Anglo-Iranian was designated the "Former Company." As the British ambassador reported, Anglo-Iranian "has been legally abolished" and Tehran "has taken the line it has no further existence."
- Mossadegh dispatched the governor of Khuzistan to Anglo-Iranian's headquarters at Khorramshahr. The governor sacrificed a sheep in front of the building, then announced the concession was voided. Directors of the new state oil company appeared at Abadan refinery carrying stationery, rubber stamps, and a sign reading "Iranian National Oil Company." Scores more sheep were sacrificed. But though the sheep had been sacrificed, "the deed was not yet done" — for five months, the status of Anglo-Iranian's facilities remained in uncertainty.

### "Old Mossy"

- Mohammed Mossadegh was about seventy, frail, completely bald with a very long nose and bright, buttonlike eyes. He was a man of contradictions — cosmopolitan (educated in France and Switzerland), yet fiercely nationalistic and antiforeigner. Son of a high bureaucrat, great-grandson of a Shah from the preceding dynasty, he was an aristocrat with extensive landholdings including a 150-family village. Yet he took on the mantle of reform, republicanism, and rabble-rousing.
- One of the first professors at the Persian School of Political Science, he had been caught up in Iran's 1906 constitutional revolution, which remained his lodestar. He went to the Versailles Peace Conference after WWI with a rubber stamp reading "Comite resistance des nations" to plead Persia's case against the British — received no hearing and returned embittered.
- In the 1920s, he opposed Reza Shah's moves toward dictatorship and was rewarded with jail and detention on his estate, where he busied himself with "amateur medical studies and the investigation of homeopathic remedies." The 1941 expulsion of Reza Shah was the signal for Mossadegh to burst back onto the political scene. His long years of dedicated opposition established him as the "pure" man, devoted to Iran and to cleansing it of foreign domination.
- Mossadegh was "unpretentious and eccentric" — he frequently received Iranians and foreign dignitaries in pajamas, lying on his bed. He lived in perpetual fear of assassination. His bodyguards were always nearby; two doors were blocked with wardrobes to prevent access by assassins.
- Mossadegh would say whatever suited his needs, "no matter how exaggerated or fanciful. But in the next moment, there was no assertion, no matter how strongly expressed, that could not be remolded or changed or, with a joke or only a giggle, repudiated altogether." He was a master of merging theatricality with politics — bursting into tears, moaning, fainting at the climax of speeches. Once he collapsed on the floor of the Majlis; a deputy who was a medical doctor rushed up to check his pulse. "As he did so, Mossadegh opened one eye and winked at him."
- Anthony Eden dubbed him "Old Mossy" — "with his pajamas and iron bedstead, was the first real bit of meat to come the way of the cartoonists since the war." Dean Acheson: "Mossadegh was a great actor and a great gambler." Truman called Sir William Fraser "a typical nineteenth-century colonial exploiter."
- Americans initially saw Mossadegh as a rational nationalist and potential bulwark against communism. Cold War considerations shaped American policy more than British. Mossadegh's real problem centered on keeping at bay those who were "more nationalistic, more extreme, more fundamentalist, and more antiforeigner than he."
- The British saw things differently: "Mossadegh was a Moslem, and in 1951 he would not have turned to the Russians," said Peter Ramsbotham. Some British officials regarded him as "lunatic." British Ambassador Sir Francis Shepherd described him as "cunning and slippery and completely unscrupulous" — the PM "looked rather like a cab horse and diffused a slight reek of opium."

### Plan Y — Military Intervention Debate

- The British Cabinet considered Plan Y — military intervention to seize the island of Abadan and its refinery. The inland oil fields were too remote, but Abadan could be taken with surprise. The US pressed hard against armed intervention, fearing it would legitimize a Russian move into northern Iran. India had just become independent, so there was no Indian Army to call upon. Britain could expect to be "castigated all over the world for old-fashioned imperialism." Defense Minister Emmanuel Shinwell worried that if Persia got away with nationalization, "the next thing might be an attempt to nationalize the Suez Canal."
- Churchill (then Leader of the Opposition) told Attlee he was "rather shocked at the attitude of the United States" — the area from the Caspian to the Persian Gulf "was more important than Korea." Parachutists were moved to Cyprus. But Attlee's government decided against using force. Some saw this as "the real beginning of the end of Britain's credibility and position in the Middle East."

### Averell Harriman's Mission

- Washington feared British military action would push Iran into Soviet arms. Acheson arranged for his friend Averell Harriman to mediate. Harriman was a multimillionaire who had given up business for public service — FDR's Special Representative, ambassador to Moscow and London, Secretary of Commerce, Marshall Plan representative.
- In Tehran, Harriman stayed in a Shah's palace with walls covered in thousands of tiny mirrors. He found Mossadegh lying on his bed, hands crossed below his neck, two doors blocked by wardrobes. "You do not know how crafty they are," Mossadegh told Harriman of the British. "You do not know how evil they are. You do not know how they sully everything they touch." Harriman: "I assure you they are good and bad and most of all in between."
- Mossadegh mentioned his grandson was away at school. "Where?" asked Harriman. "Why, in England, of course," Mossadegh replied. "Where else?"
- Colonel Vernon Walters ordered a copy of Alice in Wonderland from Washington "to serve as a kind of unofficial guidebook." Day after day, Harriman and Walter Levy tried to educate Mossadegh about oil realities. "In his dream world," Harriman cabled Truman, "the simple passage of legislation nationalizing the oil industry creates profitable business and everyone is expected to help Iran on terms that he lays down." At one point, Mossadegh seemed to demand more revenue per barrel than the total selling price of all products derived from it. "Dr. Mossadegh, if we are going to talk intelligently about these things, we have to agree on certain principles." "Such as what?" "Such as: nothing can be larger than the sum of its parts." Mossadegh replied in French: "That is false." "Consider the fox. His tail is often much longer than he is." He put the pillow over his head and rolled back and forth, laughing uproariously.
- Mossadegh would seem to agree to a framework by evening, but by morning would say he couldn't carry through — "He would not survive." His real concern was domestic politics and the threat from Muslim extremists.
- Harriman visited Ayatollah Kashani, the religious right leader (imprisoned in WWII for Axis sympathies). Kashani declared the British "the most evil people in the world," told the story of an American in Iran who had been shot and then butchered by a mob on the operating table. "Do you understand?" asked the ayatollah. Harriman, lips tightening: "Your Eminence, you must understand that I have been in many dangerous situations in my life and I do not frighten easily." "Well," shrugged Kashani, "there was no harm in trying." Kashani accused Mossadegh of being pro-British: "If Mossadegh yields, his blood will flow like Razmara's."
- Harriman recommended the British send a special negotiator — the socialist millionaire Richard Stokes. Accompanying Stokes was Sir Donald Fergusson, the influential Permanent Undersecretary at the Ministry of Fuel and Power, a consistent critic of Fraser. But Fergusson feared any deal would threaten every other British foreign investment: "It was British enterprise, skill and effort which discovered oil under the soil of Persia, which has got the oil out, which has built the refinery, which has developed markets for Persian oil in 30 or 40 countries." He called the fifty-fifty principle "bunk."
- Fergusson recognized that Mossadegh's real objective was "not better financial terms but to get rid of this foreign Company with its predominating influence out of Persia." The negotiations failed. "Mossadegh's stock-in-trade was fighting with the British. Any settlement of the dispute would end his political power." Harriman: "I am simply not used to failure. But then he had never before tried to do business with anybody like Old Mossy."

### "Stand Firm, You Cads!" — The Farewell to Abadan

- Operations at the oil fields and refinery ground to a halt. Britain mounted an embargo, threatening tanker owners with legal action for "stolen oil." Britain also embargoed goods to Iran and the Bank of England suspended financial and trade facilities. The Majlis passed a law making "sabotage or inattention" punishable by death. Eric Drake, Anglo-Iranian's general manager in Iran, was accused of such — he fled the country in a small plane and ran operations from Basra, then from a ship in the Gulf.
- Drake was summoned to Attlee's Cabinet (entering 10 Downing Street through a secret back passage). He told the Cabinet that if Britain did nothing about Abadan, "it would eventually lose much more, including the Suez Canal." Churchill, meeting Drake privately, growled: "You've got a pistol, Drake?" Drake had turned his in. "Drake, you can finish a man with a pistol. I know because I have."
- Secret military preparations were advanced — by September 1951, Abadan could have been seized in under twelve hours. But Attlee's government decided against force. "It would be humiliating to this country if the remaining British staff at Abadan were expelled."
- On September 25, 1951, Mossadegh gave the remaining British at Abadan one week to clear out. Ayatollah Kashani declared "a day of hatred against the British Government." At the refinery, British oil men and nurses put on a farewell revue called "Stand Firm, You Cads!"
- On October 4, the oil men and families gathered at the Gymkhana Club with fishing rods, tennis rackets, golf clubs, and dogs. The guest house lady had interdicted an Iranian tank commander three days earlier using only her umbrella. The parson locked the church that held records of births, baptisms, marriages, and deaths in Abadan.
- The British cruiser Mauritius carried them upriver to Basra. In a bizarre display of protocol, the ship's band played the Iranian national anthem. As the Mauritius steamed toward Basra, the band played "Colonel Bogey" and the passengers broke into a great chorus singing unpublished and obscene versions of the venerable military march. "With this musical burst of defiance, Britain bade good-bye to its largest single overseas enterprise, and the world's largest refinery, which now had virtually ceased to operate." The first of the great Middle Eastern oil concessions was the first to be summarily canceled.

### "A Splutter of Musketry" — Churchill Returns

- The British embargo removed a substantial amount of petroleum from world commerce during the Korean War. Rationing was imposed in parts of Asia; the US Petroleum Administration for Defense estimated that without Iran, world oil demand would exceed supply by end of 1951. But the momentum of postwar oil development supported the embargo — other countries (Saudi Arabia, Kuwait, Iraq) increased production. By 1952, Iranian production had plummeted to just 20,000 barrels/day (from 666,000 in 1950), while total world production rose from 10.9 million to 13.0 million barrels/day — an increase more than three times Iran's total 1950 output.
- In October 1951, the Labour government fell. Churchill returned as PM at age seventy-seven, complaining "'my old brain' not working as it used to do." But he had firm ideas about Iran: the Labour government had been "too indecisive and too weak." Had he been in office, "there might have been a splutter of musketry but Britain would not have been kicked out of Iran." There was a grand irony — thirty-seven years earlier, as First Lord of the Admiralty, Churchill had purchased the government's stake in Anglo-Persian. Now he had lived long enough to head the government at the time of the company's greatest crisis.
- Foreign Secretary Anthony Eden had a special affinity with Iran — he had studied Oriental languages at Oxford, been a star student of Persian, and played a key role in solving the 1933 Reza Shah crisis over Anglo-Persian. He had kept up his Persian connections throughout.

### The Eisenhower Administration and the Coup — Operation Ajax

- Eden's personal connections to Iran made him keen to resolve the crisis. But Churchill's new government also considered force — Churchill told Truman that "if I had been in office it would not have come to this." The Americans counseled restraint but grew increasingly alarmed about communist infiltration. The Truman Administration tried one more diplomatic push in 1952, but Mossadegh rejected all proposals.
- The Eisenhower Administration took office in January 1953 with a harder line toward communism. The Dulles brothers — John Foster as Secretary of State and Allen as CIA Director — were receptive to British arguments that Mossadegh was leading Iran toward Soviet control. The British had developed Operation Boot; the Americans created a parallel plan, Operation Ajax, led by Kermit Roosevelt (grandson of Theodore Roosevelt), the CIA's top Middle East operative.
- The plan aimed to orchestrate a coup by rallying military officers, tribal leaders, and street mobs loyal to the Shah to topple Mossadegh. The Shah was reluctant and vacillating — his nerve had to be stiffened repeatedly. Kermit Roosevelt arrived in Tehran in mid-July 1953 under cover. The Shah finally signed two firmans (royal decrees) dismissing Mossadegh and appointing General Fazlollah Zahedi as Prime Minister.
- The first attempt at the coup on the night of August 15-16, 1953 went badly wrong. The officer sent to deliver the Shah's dismissal order to Mossadegh was himself arrested. Mossadegh's supporters and the Tudeh party took to the streets. They smashed and tore down statues of the Shah's father. The Shah himself took flight, first to Baghdad. He told the American ambassador there that "he would be looking for work shortly as he had a large family and very small means outside of Iran."
- The Shah's next stop was Rome, where he and his wife took up residence in a borrowed suite at the Excelsior Hotel. They had virtually no clothes, no retainers, and no money. The Queen wandered through shops without cash. The Royal Couple ate their meals in the hotel's public dining rooms and got their news secondhand from reporters. "Altogether, it was an excruciatingly anxious and nervous time at the Excelsior."
- On August 18, Undersecretary of State Walter Bedell Smith told Eisenhower that Operation Ajax had failed: "We now have to take a whole new look at the Iranian situation and probably have to snuggle up to Mossadeq if we're going to save anything there." But the next morning, the tide turned in Tehran. General Zahedi held a press conference distributing photostats of the Shah's dismissal order. A small pro-Shah demonstration grew into a vast, shouting crowd — "led by tumblers doing handsprings and wrestlers showing off their biceps and giant weightlifters twirling iron bars." The crowd swarmed out of the bazaars proclaiming hatred of Mossadegh and support for the Shah. Cars turned on headlights to show support.
- Key elements of the military rallied to the Shah. Soldiers and police sent to quell pro-Shah demonstrators joined them instead. Mossadegh fled over the back wall of his garden. Tehran now belonged to the Shah's supporters.
- At the Excelsior in Rome, a wire service reporter rushed up to the Shah: "Teheran: Mossadeq overthrown. Imperial troops control Teheran." The Queen burst into tears. The Shah turned white, then spoke: "I knew that they loved me." He returned in triumph. By the end of August 1953, the Shah was back on his throne, Mossadegh was under arrest, and the statues of the Shah's father were being re-erected.
- In the years following, debate continued over the real significance of Operation Ajax. Did it cost less than $100,000 or millions? Did the Western powers create the countercoup, or did they only lubricate it? Mossadegh's time was certainly running out; his base of support had greatly narrowed. What the CIA and MI6 did was play "the facilitating role, providing the financial and logistical aid, emboldening the opposition and making the critical connections, during uncertain and fluid times." Operation Ajax succeeded because it accorded with increasing popular support for the Shah and growing disillusionment with Mossadegh.
- Kermit Roosevelt reported directly to Eisenhower, who noted admiringly in his diary that Operation Ajax "seemed more like a dime novel than an historical fact."

### "A Group of Companies" — The Iranian Consortium

- With the Shah back in power, Iranian oil had to be brought back to market. Anglo-Iranian was hamstrung — taking the lead would only reignite nationalist fires. The British government was, as a Ministry of Fuel and Power official put it, "completely stumped."
- Washington would have to lead. The State Department retained Herbert Hoover Jr. as special representative to see if a new consortium of companies could take up Anglo-Iranian's interests. Hoover was the former President's son, an oil consultant who had helped devise the original fifty-fifty arrangement with Venezuela. He disliked the British, and the solution he pursued was the common American prescription: a consortium in which Anglo-Iranian was "camouflaged in the midst of a number of companies, several of them American."
- The American majors were far from enthusiastic. Their production elsewhere in the Middle East was building up rapidly. The Arab producers did not want their output reduced to make room for Iranian oil. The four Aramco partners had more than enough Saudi petroleum. A Jersey official recalled: "There was no assurance whatever that we wouldn't lose the whole thing within a few months again... It was touch and go as to whether or not the country would last." A Standard of California representative called it an "ouchy" situation.
- American officials found oil company executives difficult: "it is of critical importance to the success of any approach that oil men be handled most carefully and diplomatically. Oil officials seem overly sensitive to any indication that the industry isn't perfect... Emotion, pride, loyalty, suspicion make it difficult to penetrate to reason."
- Considerable diplomacy — and brute persuasion from Washington backed by London — went into getting the American oil men to go into Iran. Howard Page of Jersey later said: "If the U.S. and British governments hadn't really beat us on the head, we wouldn't have gone back." The State Department harped on one theme: if Iranian oil did not move, the country would collapse and fall into the Soviet camp, threatening the rest of the Middle East and all the concessions therein.
- Herbert Hoover Jr. told Sir William Fraser there was no alternative: "He who pays the fiddler gets to invite the guests to the ball." Fraser then wrote to the chairmen of the American majors, inviting them to London to discuss a consortium — a humiliating admission of defeat. A Jersey vice-president wrote to Secretary Dulles: "From the strictly commercial viewpoint, our Company has no particular interest in entering such a group but we are very conscious of the large national security interests involved."

### The Oil Cartel Case — Antitrust vs. Foreign Policy

- A massive obstacle loomed: the US government was bringing a giant antitrust case against the major oil companies — the very same companies it was seeking to push into the Iranian consortium. The Justice Department was busily gearing up a criminal suit against those companies for belonging to an "international petroleum cartel."
- Two contradictory strands of US public policy had collided: Washington would champion the companies to promote strategic and economic interests, but would also subject them to populist assaults against "big oil" for being greedy, monopolistic, and secretive. Never before had these two policies come into "such sharp and potentially paralyzing collision."
- The Federal Trade Commission had subpoenaed company documents in 1949, producing the most extensive analysis of international oil company relationships ever seen — titled The International Petroleum Cartel. One State Department specialist noted "its highly slanted and non-objective approach." It showed a fundamental blind spot: in the world of The International Petroleum Cartel, oil companies did not have to adapt to the will of governments.
- The State Department, Defense Department, and CIA were appalled by the FTC report. The White House Intelligence Advisory Committee warned it would "greatly assist Soviet propaganda." The Truman Administration classified it secret, but political pressure forced release to a Senate subcommittee. It became a subject of commentary on Radio Baku broadcasts to the Middle East.
- The Justice Department launched a criminal antitrust suit — the "As-Is conspiracy." The British government was outraged, calling it "stale bread" and the work of "witch-hunters." Britain ordered Anglo-Iranian and Shell not to cooperate. Foreign Secretary Eden called the FTC disclosures "extremely prejudicial to the national interest."
- The contradictions were stark: the State Department was trying to persuade the companies into an Iranian consortium while the Justice Department was preparing criminal conspiracy charges for exactly the kind of cooperation the State Department was promoting.
- Dean Acheson tried to call off Justice, flanked by Defense Secretary Lovett and General Omar Bradley. But Attorney General McGranery would not back off. The decision fell to President Truman, who knew something about oil — as a young man, he had been a partner in a wildcatting company that failed. A group that bought some of Truman's leases later struck a huge field; Truman sometimes mused he might have been an oil millionaire instead of President.
- Truman put his finger on Iran on a globe during a discussion about the Korean War: "Here is where they will start trouble if we aren't careful... If we just stand by, they'll move into Iran and they'll take over the whole Middle East." "They" were the Soviets. On January 12, 1953, Truman called off the criminal investigation, replacing it with a civil action.
- The Eisenhower Administration filed the civil case in April 1953. The National Security Council issued a directive that "the enforcement of the Antitrust laws of the United States against the Western oil companies operating in the Near East may be deemed secondary to the national security interest." In January 1954, both the Attorney General and the NSC provided explicit guarantees that the Iranian consortium "would not violate the anti-trust laws of the United States."

### Building the Consortium

- The consortium included Anglo-Iranian, the four Aramco partners (Jersey, Socony, Socal, Texaco), plus Gulf (Anglo-Iranian's Kuwait partner), Shell, and CFP. All seven were members of joint ventures producing oil elsewhere in the Middle East. During Iran's absence, production in neighboring countries had surged dramatically — output in the region would have to be "reined in" to make room for Iranian exports. Only by giving all seven companies a stake could their acquiescence be assured.
- Even before addressing Iran, the other producing countries had to be placated. The Aramco partners visited the aged King Ibn Saud just before his death, explaining they were joining the Iranian consortium "solely on the basis that there might be chaos out in the area if we didn't... as a political matter at the request of our government." Ibn Saud understood but warned: "In no case should you lift more than you are obligated to lift to satisfy the requirements of doing that job."
- Negotiations in Tehran were interminable — "issues and definitions and objectives were forever shifting." The Iranians could not be seen as compromising sovereignty. They feared another uprising and expulsion. At one point, the companies' team prepared to leave, though they left some members behind as "hostages."
- Howard Page of Jersey led a final push in June 1954. On September 17, 1954, Page and the Iranian Finance Minister initialed the agreement. The Shah signed it October 29, 1954 — three years after the British departure to "Colonel Bogey." Oil began flowing to waiting tankers. The first to pull away was the British Advocate, owned by Anglo-Iranian. Iran was back in the oil business.
- The consortium marked a great turning point: for the first time, the concept of foreign-owned concessions was replaced by negotiation and mutual agreement. Iran's National Iranian Oil Company would own all oil resources and facilities. But in practice, the consortium managed the industry, buying all output and marketing through their own systems.
- Anglo-Iranian held 40% of the consortium. Shell had 14%, each of the five American majors 8% initially, and CFP 6%. Soon after, each American company surrendered 1% to a new entity called "Iricon" — a "little consortium" of nine independent American companies (Phillips, Richfield, Standard of Ohio, Ashland, others). Their entry was insisted upon by the US government for political and antitrust reasons. Howard Page joked: "because people were always yacking about it, we had better put some independents in there." The British were furious: "We didn't know who the independents were... We didn't think they were reputable marketers."

### Loy Henderson's Prophecy and Anglo-Iranian's Compensation

- With the Iranian consortium established, the United States was now the major player in the volatile politics of Middle Eastern oil. Loy Henderson, American ambassador to Tehran, predicted in 1953: "It seems almost inevitable that at some time in the future... the Middle Eastern countries... will come together and decide upon unified policies which might have disastrous effects upon the operations of the companies." He warned: "Continuation and enhancement of the dependence which the West now places upon Middle Eastern oil might eventually place European consumers at their mercy."
- Anglo-Iranian came out surprisingly well from the turmoil. Fraser tenaciously pursued compensation, infuriating all other participants. The other companies paid Anglo-Iranian about $90 million up front for the 60% rights it was giving up, plus a ten-cent-a-barrel royalty on all consortium production until another $500 million had been paid. "It was a wonderful deal for Fraser, the best deal Willie Fraser ever made," said John Loudon of Shell. "After all, Anglo-Iranian actually had nothing to sell. It had already been nationalized."
- Mossadegh was put on trial, delivered impassioned speeches, spent three years in prison, then lived under house arrest for the rest of his days — "continuing his experiments with homeopathic medicines, much as he had done, three decades earlier, when the Shah's father put him under house arrest." Meanwhile, swelling oil revenues were transforming the insecurities of the young Shah into the pretensions of an assertive monarch with global aspirations.
- The antitrust case against the oil companies continued to grind on. The Attorney General's approval of the Iranian consortium had the effect of insulating joint production arrangements (like Aramco) from antitrust attack. The case was narrowed to downstream — marketing and distribution — resulting in the early 1960s breakup of Stanvac (Jersey and Socony's joint company in the Far East). Caltex (Socal and Texaco's joint European system) was dissolved for commercial reasons. It was not until 1968 that the American government finally folded its tent on the case — by then the consortium had been in business for almost a decade and a half.

## Chapter 24: The Suez Crisis

### The Canal and Its Strategic Importance

- The Suez Canal — a narrow waterway a hundred miles long, dug through the Egyptian desert to link the Red Sea to the Mediterranean — was one of the grandest achievements of the nineteenth century. It was the handiwork of Ferdinand de Lesseps, a Frenchman celebrated as "the Great Engineer." He was in fact no engineer at all, but a diplomat, entrepreneur, and promoter. At age sixty-four, he married a woman of twenty and fathered twelve children.
- Construction began in 1859; the canal was completed in 1869. The British were quick to recognize its value — it substantially reduced travel time to India, "the jewel of the empire." The Prince of Wales dubbed it "our highway to India." In 1875, Egypt's 44% ownership came on the market owing to the insolvency of the Khedive. With lightning speed and the timely financial assistance of the English branch of the Rothschilds, Prime Minister Benjamin Disraeli engineered the acquisition. He capped his efforts with a note to Queen Victoria: "You have it, Madam."
- The canal's greatest significance was strategic — the main highway and lifeline of the British Empire, linking England to India and the Far East. "Defense of the communications with India" became the fundamental rationale for Britain's security strategy. British forces were stationed permanently in the Canal Zone. At El Alamein in WWII, the British mounted their stand to defend the canal against Rommel.
- In 1948, the canal abruptly lost its traditional rationale when India became independent. But at the same moment, the canal was gaining a new role — as the highway not of empire, but of oil. The Suez Canal was the way most Persian Gulf oil got to Europe, cutting the 11,000-mile journey around the Cape of Good Hope to 6,500 miles. By 1955, petroleum accounted for two-thirds of the canal's traffic, and two-thirds of Europe's oil passed through it. Flanked by Tapline and the IPC pipelines, the canal was the critical link in the postwar oil industry.

### The Nationalist: Nasser

- Britain had exercised control over Egypt and the canal for three-quarters of a century. In 1952, a group of military officers carried out a coup, dispatching the sybaritic King Farouk to exile on the Riviera (where he acquired new renown for his many girlfriends and for being enormously fat). By 1954, Colonel Gamal Abdel Nasser had toppled General Naguib and emerged as undisputed dictator of Egypt.
- Son of a post office clerk and a born plotter, Nasser had begun anti-British maneuverings during WWII. A secret CIA profile concluded: "He gets boyish pleasure out of conspiratorial doings." He was a gifted student of Mohammed Mossadegh — he mastered the use of rhetoric and radio to inflame and mobilize the masses. He became the model for the military officer turned fiery nationalist leader in the emerging Third World.
- Nasser wanted to reach far beyond Egypt — across the entire Arab world. "The Voice of the Arabs" was his powerful radio station, beaming impassioned speeches calling for rejection of the West and threatening other Arab regimes. His program included pan-Arabism, elimination of the Israeli wedge, and the righting of what he called "the greatest international crime" — the creation of Israel.
- The Suez Canal — with ships guided by mostly foreign pilots in impeccable knee socks, shorts, and captain's hats — was an embarrassing symbol of nineteenth-century colonialism in the middle of Nasser's new Egypt. Most of the canal company's earnings from tolls went to European shareholders, including the British government. If Egypt could seize control, the tolls would open a major new source of income for a desperately poor country.
- The canal concession was due to expire in 1968, and British influence was already in retreat. In 1954, Eden negotiated the withdrawal of British troops within twenty months. In early 1955, Eden visited Cairo and startled Nasser by speaking — and telling Arabic proverbs — in Arabic.
- Nasser was viewed more tolerantly in Washington, where the Administration adopted an attitude of moral superiority toward European colonial powers. The Americans believed the relics of colonialism were an enormous handicap in the struggle with communism. The canal company chairman observed bitterly that to Americans, "the company had a certain musty, nineteenth century odor derived from that lamentable colonial period."

### The Aswan Dam and the Break

- Alarm about Nasser mounted in London and Washington in autumn 1955 when Nasser turned to the Soviet bloc for weapons. As early as February 1956, the State Department raised with oil companies the question of revising the Voluntary Agreement — originally used to manage the loss of Iranian oil — to allow cooperation in case the canal was closed. But companies found joint action unworkable because of the continuing antitrust threat. In April 1956, Standard Oil of New Jersey commissioned its own study of alternatives to the canal.
- British Foreign Secretary Selwyn Lloyd visited Nasser, who declared the Suez Canal was "an integral part of the Middle East oil complex, which was vital to Britain." Nasser replied that oil-producing countries received 50% of profits, but Egypt did not get 50% from the canal. If the canal was part of the oil complex, Egypt should have the same fifty-fifty terms as oil producers.
- At the end of 1955, the Americans, British, and World Bank had begun considering a loan to build the Aswan Dam on the Nile. But Nasser's Soviet arms deals had alienated Washington. American senators from the South were hostile (the dam would produce a competing cotton crop), and Congressmen friendly to Israel opposed aid to an antagonistic regime. Nasser had recognized "Red China." The coup de grace came when Senate Republicans told Dulles foreign aid could be approved for only one "neutralist" leader — Tito of Yugoslavia or Nasser. Dulles chose Tito. On July 19, 1956, Dulles canceled the Aswan Dam loan, taking Nasser and the World Bank by surprise.

### Code Word "de Lesseps" — Nasser Nationalizes the Canal

- Nasser was angry, humiliated, and eager for revenge. On July 26, 1956, he gave a speech in Alexandria where he had, as a boy, first demonstrated against the British. He repeatedly heaped calumny on the name of de Lesseps. "De Lesseps" was the code word to the Egyptian military to move; by the time the speech was completed, the army had seized control of the Canal Zone. The Suez Canal had been expropriated.
- Tension rose swiftly. Harold Macmillan, Chancellor of the Exchequer, wrote apprehensively: "There was last night and through today, the most violent gale I ever remember." In Cairo, Nasser slipped off to the Metro movie theater to see Cyd Charisse in Meet Me in Las Vegas.
- Three months of diplomatic circus followed. In mid-September, the British and French pilots who had guided ships through the canal were withdrawn. London and Paris assumed the Egyptians would not be able to run the canal. And indeed, considerable skill was required. But the Egyptian government had insisted for several years that Egyptians be trained as pilots, and a considerable cadre was ready. Assisted by Soviet bloc pilots, the nationalized canal continued to function more or less normally.
- The American position was confusing to the British and French. Eden and Dulles clashed in style and substance. Eden's principal private secretary wrote: "Foster talks so slowly that Master [Eden] does not want to hear what he has to say, while our man talks in so roundabout and elusive a style that the other, being a lawyer, goes away having failed to make the right guesses." Eisenhower found Dulles "not particularly persuasive in presentation and, at times, seems to have a curious lack of understanding as to how his words and manner may affect another personality."

### Eisenhower's Opposition to Force

- US policy was determined by Eisenhower. Force was neither warranted nor justified; the essence of policy was to prevent the British and French from intervening militarily. Eisenhower believed the two European countries could not establish a pliable government in Egypt that would survive. Any such attempt would arouse the entire developing world against the West and play into Soviet hands. He told Eden: "Nasser thrives on drama, and the best thing to do was to let the drama seep out of the situation." He told his own advisers that British thinking was "out of date," and that a military assault would surely turn Nasser into a hero.
- It was paramount to Eisenhower that the US not appear associated with sponsoring a return to colonial domination. After hearing one Eisenhower statement, Nasser joked to an aide: "Which side is he on?"
- Eisenhower was also up for reelection in November 1956 — he was running as a man of peace, and the last thing he wanted was a military crisis. In an appalling blunder, the British and French never really factored the American Presidential election into their calculations.

### "We Had No Intention of Being Strangled to Death"

- Both London and Paris were strongly motivated toward military intervention. The French saw Nasser as a threat to their position in North Africa — he was egging on and supplying Algerian rebels. They had already opened a military dialogue with Israel, which had its own reasons for striking at Nasser (arms buildup, guerrilla raids, blockade of Eilat).
- For Britain, oil was the jugular. Just before the canal's expropriation, Eden had told Soviet leaders Bulganin and Khrushchev: "I must be absolutely blunt about the oil... because we would fight for it." He added: "We could not live without oil and... we had no intention of being strangled to death."
- Britain's international finances were precarious — it had gone from the world's greatest creditor to greatest debtor. Gold and dollar reserves covered only three months of imports. Middle East oil holdings contributed mightily to total foreign earnings. A Nasser victory might have the same repercussions as a Mossadegh victory in Iran. Eden warned Eisenhower: "Nasser can deny oil to Western Europe and we shall all be at his mercy."
- Harold Macmillan thought exactly as Eden — convinced Britain was in a very dangerous position. In the first two weeks of the crisis, he managed to read through thousands of pages of novels (Jane Austen, Dickens, George Eliot, Thackeray, and more). "If he had not done all this reading, Macmillan later said, 'I'd have gone barmy!'" But he was as forceful as anyone in supporting action: "The truth is that we are caught in a terrible dilemma... 'If we take strong action against Egypt, and as a result the Canal is closed, the pipelines to the Levant are cut, the Persian Gulf revolts and oil production is stopped — then U.K. and Western Europe have 'had it.' Yet 'if we suffer a diplomatic defeat; if Nasser gets away with it' — and the Middle East countries 'nationalize oil'... we have equally 'had it.'"

### At the "Rhineland" Again — The 1930s Analogy

- Eden, Macmillan, French Premier Guy Mollet, and their colleagues were haunted by the 1930s. To all of them, Nasser was a resurrected Mussolini, even a nascent Hitler. Eden saw Nasser's Philosophy of Revolution as reading like Hitler's Mein Kampf — Nasser wished to carve out a great empire using the power of petroleum, "the vital nerve of civilization." Without petroleum, Nasser proclaimed, machines and tools of the industrial world are "mere pieces of iron, rusty, motionless, and lifeless."
- Eden had courageously resigned as Foreign Secretary in 1938 to protest appeasement of Mussolini and Hitler. The failure to act in time — the Rhineland in 1936, Munich in 1938 — haunted him. "We are all marked to some extent by the stamp of our generation, mine is that of the assassination of Sarajevo and all that flowed from it... Always a lap behind, the fatal lap."
- Mollet had been imprisoned at Buchenwald. Belgian Foreign Minister Paul Henri Spaak wrote to the British Foreign Secretary: "I do not wish to hide from you that I am haunted by the memory of the mistakes which were committed at the outset of the Hitler period."
- The analogies were less compelling in Washington. But contingency planning was underway — Eisenhower authorized a Middle East Emergency Committee to figure out how to supply Western Europe if the canal was blocked. The Justice Department granted limited antitrust immunity but not enough for a full joint supply operation.
- Standard Oil of New Jersey's study, commissioned in April, recommended a large-diameter pipeline from the Persian Gulf through Iraq and Turkey to the Mediterranean — cost half a billion dollars, four years to build.
- In September, Eisenhower warned Eden against "making of Nasser a much more important figure than he is." Sir Ivone Kirkpatrick, Permanent Undersecretary of the Foreign Office, sharply rejoined: "I wish the President were right. But I am convinced that he is wrong.... If we sit back while Nasser consolidates his position and gradually acquires control of the oil-bearing countries, he can, and is, according to our information, resolved to wreck us. If Middle East oil is denied to us for a year or two our gold reserves will disappear... And a country that cannot provide for its defense is finished."
- Robert Anderson, a wealthy Texas oil man and Eisenhower's personal emissary, made a secret trip to Saudi Arabia to pressure the Saudis to compromise. He warned King Saud and Prince Faisal that the US had made advances in energy "much cheaper and more efficient than oil, potentially rendering Saudi and all Middle Eastern petroleum reserves worthless." What might this substitute be? "Nuclear energy," replied Anderson. Neither King Saud nor Prince Faisal seemed impressed; they dismissed the warning.

### Force Applied — The Secret Plan at Sevres

- On October 24, 1956, senior British and French officials met secretly at a villa in Sevres, outside Paris, with top Israelis including David Ben-Gurion, Moshe Dayan, and Shimon Peres. The three nations agreed: Israel would launch a military strike across the Sinai toward the Suez Canal. Britain and France would issue an ultimatum about protecting the canal, and then invade the Canal Zone to "protect" it — with the ultimate objective of toppling Nasser.
- There was much closer understanding between Israel and France than between Israel and Britain. Eden himself, who had a fondness for Arabs, had said during WWII: "Let me murmur in your ear that I prefer Arabs to Jews." Macmillan, by contrast, thought Jews "had character." At Sevres, British Foreign Secretary Selwyn Lloyd treated the Israelis with what seemed like disdain. Britain had been considering coming to Jordan's aid against Israel — one reason the French took the lead at Sevres.
- The day before Sevres, Egypt and Syria set up a joint military command under Egyptian control. Jordan acceded the next day. On October 24 — the same day as the Sevres meeting — Red Army troops entered Budapest to put down the Hungarian revolution.
- Eden's medical condition was also a factor. In 1953, a careless surgeon had damaged his bile duct during a gall bladder operation, leaving Eden "with a largely artificial inside" and a tendency to become ill under pressure. He was on drugs for stomach pain and stimulants (apparently amphetamines) to counter the painkillers. In early October, he collapsed with a 106-degree fever. A British intelligence official confided to an American counterpart: "Chums in Downing Street tell me our old boy is feeling queer and is all nerves." Sometimes Eden would seek solace by gazing at a Degas bronze of a girl in a bath that film producer Sir Alexander Korda had given him.

### The Attack and Eisenhower's Fury

- Eisenhower had suffered a heart attack in 1955 and ileitis surgery in June 1956 — two of the three principal actors on both sides of the Atlantic were in poor health. A third would soon join them.
- On October 29, Israel launched its attack into the Sinai. On October 30, London and Paris issued their ultimatum and announced their intention to occupy the Canal Zone. The same day, Russian troops were withdrawn from Budapest with a promise of nonintervention. The next day, October 31, the British bombed Egyptian air fields, and the Egyptian Army began its retreat across the Sinai.
- The entire Suez operation came as a surprise to the Americans. Eisenhower first learned about the Israeli attack while on a campaign swing through the South. He was absolutely furious — Eden had betrayed him, his allies had deliberately deceived him. "They might inadvertently set off a far wider international crisis, involving a direct clash with the Soviet Union." And they had done so while America was preoccupied with the Presidential election, only a week away. Eisenhower was so angry that he called 10 Downing Street and personally gave Eden "unshirted hell" — or at least that is what he thought. In fact, he was in such a rage that he mistook one of Eden's aides for the Prime Minister himself, "and without waiting for the answerer to identify himself, the President dumped the diatribe on the hapless aide and then hung up before Eden could be summoned to the phone."
- On November 3, Dulles was rushed to the hospital with acute stomach cancer. With Dulles out, day-to-day control of US foreign policy rested with Undersecretary Herbert Hoover Jr. — regarded in London as antipathetic to the British.

### Nasser Blocks the Canal — Oil as Weapon

- There was a gap of several days before British and French troops could follow up their ultimatum — logistical problems, poor planning, and Eden's vacillation. Meanwhile, Nasser acted quickly: he scuttled dozens of ships filled with rocks, cement, and old beer bottles, effectively blocking the waterway and thus choking off the supply of oil — the very reason for the attack. Syrian engineers, on instructions from Nasser, sabotaged the pumping stations along the Iraq Petroleum Company pipeline, further reducing supplies.
- During months of planning, the British had always assumed the United States would step into any breach with American supplies. That assumption proved to be "a large and decisive mistake, no less a miscalculation than their inattention to the date of the Presidential election." Eisenhower refused to permit any emergency supply arrangements: "I'm inclined to think that those who began this operation should be left to work out their own oil problems — to boil in their own oil, so to speak." Petroleum would provide the way for Washington to punish and pressure its allies.

### Purgatory — The Ceasefire and Oil Crisis

- On November 6, Eisenhower won a landslide victory. That same day, the British and French agreed to a cease-fire; they had achieved only a foothold along the canal. Washington made clear that a cease-fire was not enough — they would have to withdraw, or face economic reprisals.
- Without American aid, all of Western Europe would soon be short of oil. Winter was coming, stock levels were only enough for several weeks. The normal route for three-quarters of Western Europe's oil was now interrupted by the combined loss of the canal and the Middle Eastern pipelines. Saudi Arabia instituted an embargo against Britain and France. In Kuwait, acts of sabotage shut down the supply system. When Harold Macmillan heard the US was considering oil sanctions, he threw his arms up: "Oil sanctions! That finishes it." On November 7, the British government announced consumption would have to be cut by 10%. Critics in Parliament declared that if ration coupons were issued, they should bear a portrait of Sir Anthony Eden.
- Eisenhower told the NSC on November 9 he would provide antitrust protection for a cooperative oil supply program — "Despite my stiff-necked Attorney General," he said with a smile. But he made emphatically clear that absolutely no emergency oil supply program would be put into effect until the British and French had actually begun to withdraw from Egypt. Europeans bitterly complained the US was "keeping them in purgatory."
- Britain was also economically vulnerable — an immense run on the pound started. The British strongly believed the run was carried out with the acquiescence, perhaps even instigation, of the Eisenhower Administration. The IMF, under American prodding, refused London's pleas for emergency financial aid. The British economics minister reported meeting a "brick wall at every turn" in Washington. The Americans, he added, "seem determined to treat us as naughty boys who have got to be taught that they cannot go off and act on their own without asking Nanny's permission first."
- Finally, at the end of November, London and Paris pledged swift withdrawal. Only then did Eisenhower authorize the Middle East Emergency Committee. The Americans "had earned the day." They had also added to the burden of defeat and humiliation the British and French had already suffered. Nasser was the only clear winner.
- Selwyn Lloyd visited the hospitalized Dulles at Walter Reed. A puzzling conversation ensued: "Selwyn, why did you stop? Why didn't you go through with it and get Nasser down?" Lloyd was flabbergasted — this was the same Secretary of State who had tried to head off Anglo-French action. "Well, Foster, if you had so much as winked at us we might have gone on." "He couldn't have done that," Dulles replied.

### The Oil Lift and the "Sugar Bowl"

- In December, the emergency Oil Lift finally swung into operation — a cooperative venture of governments and oil companies. Middle East oil production had not been interrupted; the problem was transportation. Tankers could transport twice as much oil from the Western Hemisphere as from the Persian Gulf via the Cape of Good Hope. The main focus was wholesale redeployment of tankers so that the Western Hemisphere would once again be the main supply source for Europe, as it had been up to the end of the 1940s.
- In Europe, the emergency supplies — known as "the sugar bowl" — were distributed equitably by the Organization for European Economic Cooperation (subsequently the OECD), which created a Petroleum Emergency Group. Belgium banned private driving on Sundays. France limited oil company sales to 70% of pre-Suez levels. Britain slapped new taxes on oil, leading to higher gasoline and fuel oil prices and a hike in London taxi fares immortalized as the "Suez sixpence." By the end of December, Britain was rationing gasoline.
- Western Hemisphere production had to increase substantially. The international companies scoured US crude markets for extra supplies. But the Texas Railroad Commission, to almost everyone's consternation, barely allowed any increase in production — basically keeping shut-in production shut in. The commission reflected the independent producers in Texas, "whose interests normally are wholly domestic." They feared a buildup of crude inventories would depress prices. Eric Drake of British Petroleum called it "nothing less than a calamity for Europe."
- Both Eden and Macmillan personally protested the Texas Railroad Commission's policies. The Daily Express declared: "No Extra Oil — Say Texas Wise Men." Commissioner Colonel E. O. Thompson replied: "We have already shipped her many barrels of crude, but we only get criticism for not going all out at her bidding. England apparently still looks on us as a province or dominion."
- Eventually, Humble (Jersey's Texas affiliate) raised the price it paid for oil by thirty-five cents a barrel. Additional crude became available and the Oil Lift surged. But soon the companies were accused of collusion to raise prices. The price increases led to Congressional hearings (2,800 pages) and a new antitrust prosecution of twenty-nine oil companies by the Justice Department. The case was eventually thrown out in 1960 — a Federal judge ruled there was "economic justification" for the price increases.
- The Oil Lift involved tremendous coordination and logistical dexterity, drawing on experience from the Allies' WWII Atlantic oil supply system. One oil company executive tried to explain how misleading it was to believe that "all you had to do is push a button and everything is all right. It was an admonition that should have been kept in mind for future crises."

### The Crisis Resolved

- By spring 1957, the oil crisis was ending. Almost 90% of lost supplies had been compensated for. Conservation measures and warm weather made up much of the rest. Overall, the European economy was not as vulnerable as it would later become — in 1956, oil was only about 20% of total energy consumption; Europe was still primarily a coal economy.
- In March 1957, the Iraq Petroleum pipelines were partly reopened. By April, the Suez Canal had been sufficiently cleared. Nasser had won — the canal now unequivocally belonged to and was operated by Egypt. The Egyptian Suez Canal pilots, while they did not dress as crisply as their British and French predecessors, were quite adequate to the navigational task. Kuwait's production had dropped by half owing to the inability to transport oil. In April, the American government suspended the Oil Lift. In mid-May, Britain terminated gasoline rationing. The Suez crisis was really over.

### The Exit of "Sir Eden"

- One American participant remembered the crisis as "a curious time, those Suez months. There was high comedy, low conspiracy, and deep tragedy — for individuals and for nations." It constituted a huge personal tragedy for Anthony Eden — whom Nasser called "Sir Eden" — who had until then amassed an extraordinary record of prescience, courage, and diplomatic skill, but whose reputation was "to be ignominiously scuttled with the ships that Nasser sent to the bottom of the canal."
- In November, while the crisis still raged, Eden's ill health forced him to take a long vacation in Jamaica, in a house loaned to him by Ian Fleming, the creator of James Bond. On his return, doctors told him his health would not allow him to function as Prime Minister. He described himself as "so unrepentant... I find it strange that so few, if any, have compared these events to 1936 — yet it is so like." In January 1957, he resigned.
- Macmillan was summoned to succeed him. Macmillan wrote in his diary of seeing Eden on that sad winter afternoon, "still looking so youthful, so gay, so debonair — the representative of all that was best of the youth that had served in the 1914-1918 War." Stunned, Macmillan walked back to the Chancellor's residence. The next morning, he sat under a portrait of Gladstone, reading Pride and Prejudice to soothe his soul, until interrupted by a phone call asking him to come to the Palace to be invested as Prime Minister.
- Suez was a watershed for Britain. It did not presage Britain's decline; rather, it made obvious what had already come to pass. Britain no longer belonged to the top echelon of world powers. Years later, the Times of London said of Eden: "He was the last prime minister to believe Britain was a great power and the first to confront a crisis which proved she was not. It was as much an epitaph for an empire and a state of mind as for a man."

### Pipelines Versus Tankers — The Supertanker Solution

- After Suez, the oil companies were no longer confident they could rely on the canal. Pipelines were also vulnerable — Syria had shown that during the crisis. The safer alternative was the route around the Cape of Good Hope, but that required much larger tankers.
- The general view in 1956 was that such tankers could not be constructed. But Japanese shipyards, taking advantage of advances in diesel engines and better steels, were soon proving otherwise. John Loudon of Shell recalled: "In 1956, the tanker people were saying larger ships would be too expensive, their fuel costs too high. The amazing thing to me was how quickly these tankers were being manufactured by the Japanese." Supertankers, along with the decline of British influence and the ascendancy of Nasser, were among the consequences of the Suez Crisis. "Tankers clearly were less subject to political risk."

### Healing the Suez Schism — The Bermuda Conference

- Much bitterness remained between the Americans and the British/French. The British ambassador in Washington described Eisenhower as having "the American boy scout views about colonialism, the United Nations and the effectiveness of phrases as act of policy." But both sides recognized the need to heal the rift.
- The new Prime Minister Harold Macmillan, known for his "unflappability" (though internally he "suffered from agonies of nervous apprehension"), had worked with Eisenhower during WWII and maintained friendship. Macmillan was a realist: after Suez, it was the "rulers in Washington, in whose hands all our destinies largely lay."
- At the Bermuda Conference in March 1957, oil and Middle Eastern security were major subjects. Macmillan called Middle Eastern oil "the biggest prize in the world" and urged cooperation for long-term peace. They discussed promoting supertankers. The British emphasized maintaining the independence of Kuwait and the Gulf states, all vulnerable to Nasserite coups.
- The Bermuda Conference helped close the schism. Eisenhower and Macmillan agreed to write privately to each other weekly. But as Suez had demonstrated, in the years ahead it would be America, not Britain, whose power would predominate.
- In 1970, fourteen years after the Suez Crisis, Edward Heath became PM and arranged a dinner at 10 Downing Street for Lord Avon (as Anthony Eden had become). Eden rose to respond impromptu and offered a special prayer for the British people — "that they might discover 'a lake of oil' under the North Sea. That was exactly what they were just beginning to do in 1970." How different things might have been in 1956 had the British known of, or even suspected, the existence of such a lake.

## Chapter 25: The Elephants

### The Era of Giant Discoveries

- In the parlance of the oil industry, a giant oil field is called an "elephant." By the early 1950s, the tally of elephants in the Middle East was lengthening rapidly. In 1953, Everette DeGolyer wrote to F. E. Wellings, IPC's chief geologist, that his company had just found three elephants in three successive months. "The Middle East is rapidly getting into a condition which has been almost chronic in the United States since the earliest days of the industry, that is, the problem becomes market rather than production." DeGolyer's firm was finishing a secret study for the Saudi government; he knew reserves would far dwarf earlier estimates. "They are so great that adding a billion barrels or so makes no real difference in the oil available."
- From the early 1950s to the end of the 1960s, the world oil market was dominated by extraordinarily rapid growth — "a tremendous surge that, like a powerful and somewhat frightening undertow, swept everyone in the industry forward with its seemingly irresistible force." Free world crude oil production increased from 8.7 million barrels/day in 1948 to 42 million barrels/day in 1972. US production grew from 5.5 to 9.5 million, but America's share slipped from 64% to 22%. The Middle East grew from 1.1 million to 18.2 million barrels/day — a 1,500% increase.
- Proven world oil reserves increased from 62 billion barrels in 1948 to 534 billion in 1972 — almost a ninefold rise. American reserves grew from 21 billion to 38 billion, but their statistical significance shrank from 34% to a mere 7% of world reserves. The Middle East had increased from 28 billion to 367 billion barrels. Out of every ten barrels added to free world reserves between 1948 and 1972, more than seven were found in the Middle East.
- In 1950, the industry estimated proven reserves at current production rates would last nineteen years. By 1972, after all the years of rapid consumption growth, the estimated reserve life was thirty-five years — reserves were being found even faster than oil was being consumed.

### The Postwar Petroleum Order — Two Foundations

- The postwar petroleum order rested on two foundations. One was the great oil deals of the 1940s, establishing relationships among companies in the Middle East — mobilizing resources for rapid development, tying production to refining and marketing systems. The second was the concessional and contractual relationships between companies and governments, at the heart of which was the fifty-fifty profit-sharing arrangement.
- Neither the major companies nor the consuming country governments wanted to budge from fifty-fifty. The British Middle East Oil Committee said in late 1954: "A reasonable basis has now been reached for a partnership between the oil companies and the Middle East Governments.... Any further encroachment... would seriously damage our oil supply system." But producing countries saw it differently — why not increase revenues if they could?

### The Shah's Ambition and the Search for an Interlocutor

- By the mid-1950s, the Shah of Iran had moved past his earlier insecurities, declaring in private that "It was Iran's destiny to become a great power." He wanted more oil income and greater independence from the consortium. But he could not afford to upset basic foreign relations. He needed an interlocutor from outside the ranks of the majors and the American independents in the consortium. The answer: a European, an Italian — Enrico Mattei.

### A New Napoleon — Enrico Mattei and ENI

- Enrico Mattei was determined to create a new major — Italy's state-owned AGIP — cast in his own image. He was a bold swashbuckler, a condottiere. The stocky hawk-featured Mattei had the look of a fervent but worldly Jesuit of the sixteenth century — dark somber eyes under high arching eyebrows, thin hair combed straight back. He was willful, ingenious, manipulative, and suspicious, with a talent for improvisation and a propensity for gambling and taking risks.
- The unruly son of a policeman from northern Italy, Mattei left school at fourteen to work in a furniture factory. By his early thirties, he ran his own chemical plant in Milan. During WWII, he became a partisan leader (Christian Democrats). After the war, his skills led to his being entrusted with AGIP — Azienda Generali Italiana Petroli — which Italy had created in the 1920s (following France's example) as a state-owned refining company. AGIP had attained a market share in Italy roughly commensurate with Esso and Shell, but outside Italy it counted not at all.
- Mattei set about transforming AGIP with "a great burst of energy and a masterly grasp of politics, Italian-style." The requisite money came from the Po Valley, where discovery and development of significant natural gas reserves generated high earnings funding both AGIP's expansion and its overseas ambitions.
- In 1953, the various state hydrocarbon companies were gathered into ENI — Ente Nazionale Idrocarburi — a sprawling conglomerate with thirty-six subsidiaries ranging from crude oil and tankers to gasoline stations, real estate, hotels, toll highways, and soaps. The US embassy in Rome reported with wonder in 1954 that ENI "has found itself in the unique position of being financially solvent, capably led, and responsible to no one other than its leader." ENI's future would be "profoundly shaped by the limitless ambition evidenced in the person of Enrico Mattei."
- Mattei became a popular hero, the most visible man in Italy — embodying antifascism, national resurrection, and the self-made "new man." He also became the most powerful man in Italy. ENI owned the newspaper Il Giorno, subsidized politicians from far left to right, and financed the Christian Democrats. "To deal with a government," Mattei would complain, "is like sucking needles." One aide recalled: "Anybody who worked with him would go into the fire for him, although you couldn't really explain why."
- Mattei came to London for a lunch with John Loudon, the senior managing director of Royal Dutch/Shell. Loudon, with apparent innocence, asked Mattei how he had gotten into oil. Mattei, flattered to be taken seriously at the heart of the major, talked nonstop through the entire lunch telling his whole life story. "Finally, when we went to dessert, he asked us for something. We couldn't do it, and that was the end of the conversation." It was not the last they would hear from Mattei.

### Mattei's Greatest Battle — Breaking the Fifty-Fifty

- Mattei's overriding objective was to ensure ENI had its own international petroleum supply, independent of the "Anglo-Saxon" companies. He loudly attacked the "cartel" — the major companies — and was credited with coining the term "Sette Sorrelle" (the Seven Sisters): Jersey (Exxon), Socony-Vacuum (Mobil), Socal (Chevron), Texaco, Gulf, Royal Dutch/Shell, and British Petroleum. Actually there was an eighth sister — CFP — but since CFP didn't fit the "Anglo-Saxon" rubric, Mattei conveniently dropped it.
- Mattei had tried to win membership in the Iranian consortium. He believed his cooperation with the embargo after Mossadegh's nationalization had earned him a place. But Italy was excluded. He was furious and looked for opportunity — and revenge.
- The 1956 Suez Crisis put the established companies on the defensive and showed the retreat of British power in the Middle East. Mattei would step forward to fill the vacuum. His anticolonial rhetoric matched the nationalistic fervor of the exporting countries.
- Mattei began talking to the Shah. If the majors used corporate intermarriages through joint ventures, Mattei would go one better — thinking dynastically, he floated the idea of marrying off an Italian princess to the Shah, who was in urgent need of a male heir. One legacy of Mossadegh's nationalization gave the Shah flexibility: the Iranian government owned all oil resources in the ground, unlike other Middle Eastern countries where the concessionaires still owned the reserves.
- Over the spring and summer of 1957, Mattei hammered out a wholly unprecedented arrangement. Under the deal, Iran's National Iranian Oil Company would be ENI's partner as well as its landlord — in practice, Iran would get 75% of profits to ENI's 25%, breaking the treasured fifty-fifty arrangement. "As J. Paul Getty and others had already found, it cost more to get into the game when you were a latecomer."
- The companies were appalled. The Americans and British protested to the Shah, warning that upsetting fifty-fifty would "seriously prejudice the stability of the Middle East" and threaten oil supplies. The Italian Foreign Ministry's own Secretary General, resentful of Mattei's power, secretly advised the British to take a tough line — any accommodation with Mattei would be seen "as a sign of weakness."
- All objections were to no avail. By August 1957, Mattei had signed the deal. The British ambassador in Tehran, who rode his horse over to the Italian summer embassy and came upon Mattei "sitting under a tree, relaxing with a whiskey and soda, happily celebrating his victory," reported that Mattei "certainly uses bold strokes and a big brush on a wide canvas."
- To his inner circle, Mattei expressed perplexity: "They've given us two tiny places in Iran, and everybody makes a great noise." The ENI-Iran partnership did not work out commercially — no significant oil was found. But Mattei achieved his larger purpose: "the fifty-fifty principle had been pierced, much weakening the foundation on which, he believed, the power of the 'Seven Sisters' had rested." The British embassy in Tehran reported: "But in fact we all know that 50/50 is about as dead as the four minute mile. And as inevitably."

### Japan Enters the Middle East

- Japan was acutely oil-sensitive, beginning its extraordinary economic climb with almost total dependence on imports. The Suez Crisis made Japan even more nervous. In spring 1957, a consortium of Japanese companies pursued a concession for the area offshore from the Saudi-Kuwaiti Neutral Zone.
- The idea had emerged by chance on a train ride in Italy, where a Japan Development Bank employee met a Japanese businessman with Middle Eastern oil contacts. Back in Japan, Taro Yamashita — an entrepreneur who had made a fortune building rental homes in Manchuria before WWII — seized on the idea, assembled the Arabian Oil Company, and won the Japanese government's backing. None of the participating companies had any significant oil industry experience.
- The Japanese agreed to take only 44% of profits, leaving the Saudis with 56%. The Saudis also had the right to acquire equity. The Kuwaitis then insisted on 57%. Arabian Oil started drilling offshore in July 1959 and made its first discovery in January 1960. Japan's MITI made refiners take the oil proportionally. By the mid-1960s, Arabian Oil was providing almost 15% of Japan's total supply.

### "Even the Americans..." — Standard of Indiana in Iran

- Others wanting to enter the Middle East would have to pay a higher price and observe the new precedents — even American companies. Standard Oil of Indiana had long regretted selling its Venezuelan production to Jersey during the Great Depression. In the late 1950s, Indiana went overseas, telling stockholders to seek "opportunities for profitable operations wherever they may exist."
- An agreement was swiftly made with Iran in 1958, along Mattei's seventy-five-twenty-five lines, plus a very large up-front bonus. The Shah opened their first meeting with a surprising lecture: "You know — we are not Arabs. We are Aryans, and we are the same race as you are, and we have a great history. We have great pride." "Oh, yes," replied Indiana's chairman, "we know, Your Majesty."
- Unlike ENI, Indiana found a great deal of oil — beginning with a great offshore field south of Kharg Island named Darius (for the ancient Persian King), to flatter the Shah. The Shah remarried soon after and had a male heir. His "continuity" now seemed assured.

### Nasser Ascendant and the Iraqi Revolution

- Nationalism was building to a crescendo across the Middle East, with Nasser as its driving force. Suez had proven a Middle Eastern country could triumph not only against "imperialistic" companies but also against the might of Western governments. A notable technological innovation — the cheap transistor radio — was carrying his rousing Voice of the Arabs to the poor masses everywhere.
- In 1958, the Soviet Union provided funding for the Aswan Dam. Syria joined Egypt to form the United Arab Republic. The apparent merger brought together the Suez Canal and the Syrian pipelines — Nasser was theoretically in a position to choke off most Middle Eastern oil transit.
- In July 1958, Nasser's propaganda war against Iraq's British-backed Hashemite monarchy came to its terrible culmination. Officers plotting a coup told their troops the far-fetched story that they had been ordered to march to Israel and surrender their weapons. The coup set off an explosion of violence and savagery. Crowds surged through the streets holding aloft huge photographs of Nasser and live squirming dogs representing the Iraqi Royal Family. King Faisal II was beheaded by troops who stormed the palace. The Crown Prince was shot, his hands and feet hacked off and carried on spikes through the streets. The pro-Western PM Nuri es-Said was recognized trying to flee disguised as a woman, was lynched, and a car was driven back and forth over his body "until it was flattened almost beyond recognition."
- The new Baghdad government demanded vast revisions in the IPC concession. The grisly coup sent shudders through every government in the region; Nasserism seemed destined to reign supreme.

### Arab Oil Experts and the Road to Organization

- Oil was a central focus of rising Arab nationalism. Since the early 1950s, meetings among "Arab Oil Experts" had been taking place. Initially the dominant subject was the oil blockade against Israel. Over time, the agenda broadened — Nasser used these meetings to shape public opinion on sovereignty, "colonialism," and his influence over Gulf oil. It was a case of a "have not" (Egypt had no oil exports) seeking to better his position with the "haves." Abdullah Tariki of Saudi Arabia declared: "Oil is the strongest of weapons the Arabs wield."
- At a 1957 conclave in Egypt, delegates proposed building domestic refining capacity, an Arab tanker fleet and pipeline, and creating an "international body" or "international consortium" to manage Middle Eastern oil production and counterbalance the power of the petroleum companies. Talk of a producers' organization remained inchoate — it needed participation from non-Arab producers, particularly Venezuela and Iran. And it would require the catalytic role of one man: Juan Pablo Perez Alfonzo.

### Juan Pablo Perez Alfonzo — The Prophet of OPEC

- In 1948, shortly after codifying fifty-fifty, the democratic government in Venezuela was overthrown. Control passed to Colonel Marcos Perez Jimenez's brutal dictatorship. Under Jimenez, oil production doubled by 1957. When the dictatorship collapsed in January 1958, Romulo Betancourt returned as President. He had been an eloquent critic of the oil companies, whose "close identification with the dictatorship" had turned Venezuela into "a petroleum factory that represented a throwback to the dark days of the Gomez dictatorship."
- Betancourt and his colleagues had learned from 1948: the need for broad democratic coalitions. They had to contend with assaults from both right and left, including communist guerrillas, and much anti-American sentiment. In 1958, VP Richard Nixon nearly was hurt or killed when an enraged mob attacked his motorcade entering Caracas. In 1960, Betancourt's own car was bombed in an assassination attempt. Despite grievances, Betancourt and his colleagues were "not impractical romantics." The man to whom Betancourt turned on oil was Juan Pablo Perez Alfonzo.
- Perez Alfonzo was "a man of iron determination... and yet soft-spoken and almost monastic." Born to a well-to-do Caracas family, he studied medicine at Johns Hopkins then law in Caracas. When the family lost its money, he became responsible for his 10 brothers — an experience that made conservation and planning central to his character.
