- Working with Betancourt, Perez Alfonzo became the opposition's expert on oil. As Minister of Development from 1945, he repaired the 1943 law to ensure Venezuela truly got 50% of profits. After the 1948 coup, he was jailed (later joking to his family that "jail was like a holiday"). In exile in Washington, D.C., he and his family scraped by on rents from their Caracas house. He became a regular at the Library of Congress, studying everything about the oil industry — from Forbes and Fortune to Oil and Gas Journal. He devoted particular time to the Texas Railroad Commission, which fascinated him as the institution that had stabilized oil production in the US.
- After several years in Washington, he moved to Mexico City (fearing his children would become too Americanized). When the dictatorship fell in 1958, Betancourt insisted Perez Alfonzo return as Minister of Mines and Hydrocarbons. He was struck by how much more affluent Caracas looked — and his response was not altogether favorable. Oil wealth, he believed, was "the gift of nature and politics, and not of hard work," with pernicious consequences.
- While in exile, the family had bought a 1950 Singer motorcar. When Perez Alfonzo returned to Venezuela, the car was shipped to him but sat at the docks rusting for two months because nobody told him. A mechanic drove it up to Caracas, but the engine burned out because nobody checked the oil. The corroded car was delivered to his villa. Perez Alfonzo viewed it all as a sign from heaven — "he had the car installed near a ping-pong table in his garden, as a corroded, overgrown shrine and symbol of what he saw as the dangers of oil wealth — laziness, the spirit of not caring, the commitment to buying and consuming and wasting."
- Once back in office, Perez Alfonzo kept a simple, parsimonious life — carrying sardine sandwiches for lunch. His objectives were clear: increase the government's share of rents, and transfer power and authority over production from the oil companies to sovereign governments. Oil was a national heritage belonging to future generations; selling it too cheaply was bad for consumers (premature exhaustion of a nonrenewable resource) and producers (discouragement of new development). "Human nature should not be allowed to squander the potential of this precious resource."
- Perez Alfonzo was also motivated by shrewd commercial judgment. Venezuela was a relatively high-cost producer at about 80 cents/barrel vs. 20 cents for Persian Gulf producers. In an out-and-out production race, Venezuela would inevitably lose market share. Venezuela thus had a very good reason to persuade Middle Eastern producers to raise their taxes — and to establish a global alliance modeled on the Texas Railroad Commission. A common front, by regulating production and allocation, would prevent Venezuela from being swamped by millions of barrels of cheap Middle Eastern oil.

### US Import Quotas and Venezuela's Fury

- The Eisenhower Administration's reluctant decision in early 1959 to impose quotas on foreign oil hit Venezuela harder than any other country — the US was the destination of 40% of its total exports. To placate neighbors, the US exempted overland oil from Canada and Mexico on "national security" grounds. To the Venezuelans, this was a convenient fiction — "The Americans are throwing us the bones," Perez Alfonzo acidly said.
- Bitterly complaining, Perez Alfonzo flew to Washington proposing a Western Hemisphere oil system run by governments, not companies — with Venezuela guaranteed a quota share of the US market (modeled on the American sugar quota system). The US was not interested; the government in Caracas was insulted. Perez Alfonzo would seek a more attentive hearing elsewhere — in Cairo.

### The "Red Sheikh" — Abdullah Tariki

- Abdullah Tariki, a Saudi Arabian, was the son of a camel owner. His intelligence was noted early; he was sent to school in Kuwait, then spent a dozen years studying in Cairo (where he imbibed Nasserism). A scholarship carried him to the University of Texas, where he studied chemistry and geology and worked as a trainee geologist with Texaco. His view of America was shaped in Texas, where on several occasions he was excluded from bars and facilities because he was taken for a Mexican.
- In 1948, he returned to Saudi Arabia — virtually the first American-educated Saudi technocrat, the first Saudi trained in geology and chemistry. He also had an American wife. In 1955, at age thirty-five, Tariki was appointed to head a newly created Directorate of Oil and Mining Affairs. He created a team of experts — including an American lawyer and a young Saudi technocrat, Hisham Nazir — and prepared to challenge not only Aramco but the Western oil companies themselves.
- Tariki was an odd combination — an ardent Nasser supporter and fervent Arab nationalist critical of the Saudi Royal Family, yet a servant of that same family in the single most important economic position in the kingdom. Known as the "Red Sheikh," Tariki's position resulted from a power struggle between King Saud (erratic, weak, spendthrift) and his younger brother Faisal (shrewd, coldly calculating). With so much energy focused on this struggle, Tariki was able to shape oil policy with considerable autonomy.
- At first, Tariki concentrated on gaining control over refining and marketing — wanting to create a Saudi company integrated "right down to the service station." He would even raise the idea of outright nationalization of Aramco. But in early 1959, his strategy abruptly changed — control over prices and production became more important than nationalization and integration. The reason: a sudden cut in the price of oil.

### Competitive Pressures — The Posted Price Gap

- While world oil demand was growing during the 1950s, production capacity was growing still more rapidly. More oil was in search of markets than there were markets for oil. Companies were forced to offer bigger and bigger discounts on the prices at which they sold their Middle Eastern oil.
- A crucial divergence was emerging between the "posted" or official price (held constant, against which the producing country's taxes and royalties were computed) and the actual market price (which was dropping). The posted price was supposed to match the market price, but as discounting spread, a gap appeared. The producing countries continued to take 50% of profits based on the posted price — meaning the companies absorbed all the effects of discounting. By the late 1950s, the posted price was a fictional price. The producing countries were actually taking 60 or 70% of the profit realized from the actual price, while Middle Eastern governments were being kept whole.
- The problem of discounting became more acute from 1958 onward. US import quotas closed off the largest oil market in the world to rapidly rising production outside the US. Those additional barrels had to fight their way into a less-than-global market.

### The Soviet Oil Offensive

- An even more important reason for discounting was the arrival of the Soviet Union as a major new entrant in the world oil market. Between 1955 and 1960, Soviet oil output actually doubled. By the end of the 1950s, the USSR had displaced Venezuela as the second-largest oil producer in the world, with output equal to about three-fifths of total Middle Eastern production.
- From 1958 onward, Soviet exports surged, becoming "a force to be reckoned with in the international petroleum field," said the CIA. The Soviets wanted whatever buyers they could obtain, cutting prices as part of what became known in Washington as the "Soviet Economic Offensive." Allen Dulles, CIA director, warned in 1958: "The free world faces a quite dangerous situation in the Soviet capacity to dislocate established markets."
- Russian oil could be picked up in Black Sea ports at about half the posted price of Middle Eastern oil. The most prominent buyer of Russian oil was none other than Enrico Mattei of ENI — the companies' number-one bete noire.
- The companies' competitive response was price cuts. But could they dare cut the posted price as well, so that producing countries would share the burden? In early 1959, British Petroleum made the first cut — eighteen cents a barrel, about a 10% reduction. It instantly set off a torrent of denunciation. Perez Alfonzo was outraged. Tariki was furious. "With a stroke of the pen, a major oil company had unilaterally slashed the national revenues of the oil producers."

### The Arab Oil Congress and the Maadi Yacht Club — The Gentlemen's Agreement

- An Arab Oil Congress had been scheduled for Cairo in April 1959. Four hundred people attended, including Tariki. Perez Alfonzo — angry about BP's price cut, the American import quotas, and Washington's rejection of his Western Hemisphere scheme — came as an "observer," with a Venezuelan delegation carrying texts of oil legislation translated into Arabic.
- Iraq notably boycotted the conference (the new Baghdad regime did not want to subordinate itself to Nasser). BP sent observers who reported the conference was "remarkably friendly" and could be "marked off as a 'plus' for the oil industry's future relations with the Arab host countries."
- But BP also tried private diplomacy through "Miss Wanda Jablonski of Petroleum Week," who arranged a meeting with Tariki. Unfortunately, BP's representative was "subjected to a diatribe on the inequity of oil production in Kuwait with its population of a few hundred thousand growing more rapidly than production in Saudi Arabia with its many millions of under-privileged."
- Wanda Jablonski was the most influential oil journalist of her time — correspondent of Petroleum Week and later editor of Petroleum Intelligence Weekly. Blonde and stylish, she was born in Czechoslovakia, daughter of a prominent botanist turned geologist. She had traveled by camel from Cairo to Jerusalem and been schooled in New Zealand, Egypt, England, Morocco, Germany, Austria, and Texas. "I have a different attitude towards the world. I can't fit in any one spot, except New York."
- In 1956, Jablonski even wrangled an invitation to interview King Saud in Riyadh — and spent an evening in the King's harem ("drinking tea with rose water, eating dinner, and having a perfectly gay 'hen party'"). She met Abdullah Tariki, whom she described as "the No. 1 man to watch in the Middle East." At a second meeting years later, she also passed him a critical piece of information: "There's another guy who's just as nuts as you — Juan Pablo Perez Alfonzo." She promised to bring them together.
- At the 1959 Arab Oil Congress in Cairo, Jablonski kept her word. She invited Perez Alfonzo to her room at the Cairo Hilton for a Coke and introduced Abdullah Tariki. "You're the one I've been hearing so many things about," said Perez Alfonzo. The two men agreed to talk secretly with representatives from other major exporters. They reconvened at an off-season, virtually deserted yacht club in Maadi, a suburb of Cairo.
- The discussions were conducted in great secrecy — "We met in a James Bond atmosphere," the Iranian participant later said. Those involved: Perez Alfonzo, Tariki, a Kuwaiti, an Iranian (who kept insisting he had no mandate and was only an observer), and an Iraqi (there as an Arab League official, since Iraq was boycotting the conference). The Iranian was so frightened of acting without authorization from the Shah that he disappeared, and the Cairo police had to be called to find him so he could sign.
- The Gentlemen's Agreement reflected Perez Alfonzo's ideas: their governments should establish an Oil Consultative Commission, defend the price structure, and establish national oil companies. They urged jettisoning fifty-fifty and moving to at least sixty-forty in their favor. They should build up domestic refining, move downstream, and become more integrated. "In all its dimensions, the Gentlemen's Agreement, though secret, was a milestone in the changing dynamics of the petroleum industry." As for Wanda Jablonski — "she had just been the matchmaker for an alliance that would develop into the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries — OPEC."

## Chapter 26: OPEC and the Surge Pot

### The Soviet Threat and the Second Price Cut

- The surplus of oil continued to mount. Further discounts off the posted price followed, driven by aggressive Soviet marketing — slashing prices and making barter deals. Many in the West believed the Soviet petroleum campaign was not just commercial but "also a political assault, the purpose of which was to create dependence in Western Europe, weaken the unity of NATO, and subvert the Western oil position in the Middle East." Senator Kenneth Keating said: "Khrushchev has threatened to bury us on more than one occasion. It is now becoming increasingly evident that he would also like to drown us in a sea of oil."
- Russian oil could be picked up at about half the posted price of Middle Eastern oil. The most prominent buyer was Enrico Mattei's ENI.

### T Square Versus Slide Rule — The Jersey Board Debate

- In July 1960, fifteen months after the first price cut, the board of Standard Oil of New Jersey met to consider the vexing question of the posted price. The new chairman, Monroe Rathbone ("Jack"), was a no-nonsense man whose life was practically a textbook of the American oil industry. Both his father and uncle had been Jersey refiners in West Virginia. Rathbone studied chemical engineering and worked at Jersey's Baton Rouge refinery, where he developed political skills fending off Huey Long. "He was self-confident, decisive, unemotional, and uninterested in small talk. A colleague described him as 'an engineer with a T Square.'" The great drawback was that his entire career had been domestic — he did not intuitively grasp the changing mentality of foreign oil producers. "Money is heady wine for some of these poor countries, and some of these poor people," he once said.
- Jersey was managed by a committee system — the "Standard Committee Company of New Jersey" — intended to forestall precipitous decisions. But Rathbone had "the kind of determination that takes an awful lot of evidence to break down." Preoccupied with gaining markets amid the glut, Rathbone was determined to overwhelm the committee system and force a cut in the posted price.
- Howard Page, Jersey's expert Middle East negotiator and the man who had put together the Iranian consortium, vigorously disagreed. "He was a very tough man... He always had a slide rule on his lap, so that he could calculate down to the last half cent on a barrel. But he was also a man of some vision, and was very well able to understand other people's vision." Page grasped the explosive force of nationalism and feared his colleagues did not.
- Page arranged for Wanda Jablonski to brief the Jersey board. She told them there was "almost universal adulation for Nasser among all classes, and a hostility towards the west that had deepened markedly." She warned the existing structure of IPC and Aramco might prove to be "short-lived." At a separate meeting, Rathbone vehemently disagreed with her disquisition on nationalism. "You never got beneath the surface," Jablonski replied tartly. "Jack, do yourself a favor. You got the red carpet treatment, you were there only a few days. You'd be wiser if you didn't make those statements."
- Page argued against cutting the posted price — it would be cutting the countries' national revenues. He made a motion that any cut be effected only after consultation and agreement with the governments. The other directors backed the motion. But Rathbone was the chairman. He privately dismissed Page as "a know-it-all" and decided Jersey would go ahead unilaterally.
- On August 9, 1960, with no direct warning to the exporters, Jersey announced cuts of up to fourteen cents a barrel in posted prices of Middle Eastern crudes — about a 7% reduction. The other companies followed suit. John Loudon of Shell called it "the fatal move. You can't just be guided by market forces in an industry so essential to various governments. You had to be so terribly careful." BP, which had learned its lesson from its 1959 cut, complained it "heard the news with regret."
- The reactions went far beyond "regret." The producing countries were outraged. "All hell broke loose," recalled Howard Page. Another Jersey executive, in Baghdad when the cut was announced, was "glad to get out alive."

### "We've Done It!" — The Founding of OPEC

- Within hours of Jersey's August 1960 price cut, Abdullah Tariki telegraphed Perez Alfonzo, then hurriedly departed to Beirut. When asked what would happen, he replied: "Just wait." Tariki and Perez Alfonzo wanted to bring the signatories of the Cairo Gentlemen's Agreement together as quickly as possible.
- In the maelstrom of anger, the Iraqis recognized a political opportunity. The revolutionary government of Abdul Karim Kassem had no wish to subordinate itself to Nasser. By using the price cut as catalyst to establish a new organization of oil exporters (including non-Arab countries, Iran and Venezuela), they could isolate oil policy from Nasser. They also hoped such a grouping would bolster them in their confrontation with IPC. Iraq quickly dispatched invitations to meet in Baghdad.
- When the telegram from Baghdad reached Perez Alfonzo's office in Caracas, "he was exultant. Here were the makings of the international 'Texas association' that he so ardently advocated. 'We've done it!' he declared excitedly to his aides as he held the telegram aloft. 'We've achieved it!'"
- Shell tried to offer an olive branch on September 8, 1960, raising posted prices by two to four cents. Too little, too late. By September 10, representatives of Saudi Arabia, Venezuela, Kuwait, Iraq, and Iran had arrived in Baghdad. Qatar attended as an observer. The omens were not good — Perez Alfonzo had to delay his departure from Caracas owing to an attempted coup. Baghdad was filled with tanks and armed soldiers; armed guards were posted behind each delegate during discussions.
- Yet by September 14, the group had completed its work. A new entity was established: the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries — OPEC. It made its intention clear: to defend the price of oil (more precisely, to restore it to its precut level). Member countries would insist the companies consult them on pricing matters. They also called for "regulation of production" — Tariki's and Perez Alfonzo's dream of a worldwide Texas Railroad Commission. And they committed to solidarity in case the companies sought to impose "sanctions" on any one of them.
- OPEC's five founding members were the source of over 80% of the world's crude oil exports. OPEC's creation represented "the first collective act of sovereignty on the part of the oil exporters," in the words of Fadhil al-Chalabi, later OPEC Deputy Secretary General — "the first turning point in the international economic relations towards the states' control over natural resources."

### The Companies Dismiss OPEC

- Yet despite the rhetoric, the newly created OPEC did not seem very threatening or imposing. The companies did not take it seriously. "We attached little importance to it," said Howard Page. "We believed it would not work." Fuad Rouhani, the Iranian delegate to the founding conference and OPEC's first Secretary General, observed that the companies "initially pretended that 'OPEC did not exist.'" Western governments paid scant attention. In a secret forty-three-page CIA report on "Middle East Oil" in November 1960, two months after OPEC's founding, the CIA devoted a mere four lines to the new organization.

### OPEC in the 1960s — Limited Achievements

- OPEC could claim only two achievements in its early years. First, the companies would not dare cut the posted price again — they became cautious about any unilateral step without consultation. Second, OPEC had so little to show for its first decade because in all member countries (except Iran), the oil reserves actually belonged by contract to the concessionaires. The world oil market was overwhelmed with surplus, and the exporting countries were competitors who had to worry about holding on to markets.
- The 1960s saw decolonization and "Third World" questions. But sovereignty issues in oil subsided as companies met exporter demands for higher revenues by pushing up production. Broader political factors helped: in Saudi Arabia, King Faisal was now firmly in charge, oriented toward the West. Saudi Arabia and Egypt were engaged in a proxy war in Yemen. Venezuela was pursuing a stable relationship with the US through the Alliance for Progress.
- Political rivalries within OPEC were considerable. In 1961, when Kuwait became independent, Iraq claimed ownership and threatened invasion — backing off only after Britain dispatched a military squadron. Iraq temporarily suspended its OPEC membership. Iran and Saudi Arabia, the two major producers, looked upon each other with apprehension and envy — one was Arab, the other not; one Sunni, the other Shia. Each wanted to be the regional leader and the top oil producer. The Shah wanted revenues increased as fast as possible through selling more oil, not through holding back production and raising prices. He called international prorationing "nice in theory but unrealistic in practice."
- Abdullah Tariki had aligned himself with King Saud. When Faisal won the power struggle, Tariki was fired in 1962 and replaced by the young legal adviser Ahmed Zaki Yamani — who had no particular attachment to the idea of an international Texas Railroad Commission. Tariki spent the next decade and a half in itinerant exile, advising other petroleum countries and denouncing the oil companies as a journalist and polemicist.
- Perez Alfonzo, OPEC's other father, grew disillusioned with politics and with OPEC itself. He resigned in 1963, saying his mission had been to bring the oil producers together. He retreated to his villa in Caracas to read and study philosophy, managing his gardens. He no longer talked about "sowing the oil" — instead calling petroleum "the excrement of the devil." He kept the rusted Singer automobile in his garden as monument to the waste of oil wealth. "I am an ecologist first of all," he said shortly before his death in 1979. "Now I am not interested in oil any more. I live for my flowers. OPEC, as an ecological group, has really disappeared."
- The oil companies strenuously sought to avoid direct negotiations with OPEC. "Our position was that we owned the concessions, and we would deal with the countries in which the concessions were located," recalled a major company executive. OPEC continued through the 1960s to be, as another executive put it, a sideshow: "The reality of the oil world was U.S. import quotas, Russian oil exports, and competition." What loomed was the dizzying growth in demand and even dizzier growth in supply. "It seemed that OPEC's moment to mount an effective challenge to the power of the major oil companies had passed — or would never come."

### "The New Frontier" — Africa and More Elephants

- Almost as soon as OPEC was established, its member countries lost their near-total grip on world oil exports. New oil provinces were found and opened up in the 1960s, adding to supplies and swamping the market.
- Africa was the "new frontier." France took a lead, drawing on Clemenceau's WWI declaration that oil was "the blood of the earth." De Gaulle ordered a maximum drive to develop oil within the French empire. A new group of state companies, under the Bureau de Recherches Petroliers (BRP), explored in the empire. Oil was discovered in Gabon in West Africa.
- In North Africa, despite much skepticism (the Sorbonne's most prominent geology professor announced he was so sure there was no oil in the Sahara "that he would happily drink any drops of oil that happened to be found there"), Regie Autonome des Petroles (RAP) began exploration and in 1956 discovered oil in Algeria. The "Sahara" became a magical word in France — "France's answer to Germany's Ruhr." De Gaulle himself visited the Saharan oil fields in 1957: "Here is the great opportunity for our country that you have brought into the world. In our destiny, this can change everything."
- Getting the oil out was very difficult — fields deep in the desert, the simplest things trucked hundreds of miles across roadless wastelands. By 1958, oil was flowing from the Sahara. But Algeria was caught in a bloody war for independence (begun 1954). When Algeria won independence in 1962, the Evian Agreement guaranteed France's position in Saharan oil. By 1961, French state companies were producing oil equivalent to 94% of French demand. RAP and BRP merged in 1965, eventually becoming Elf-ERAP (later simply Elf) — one of the largest oil companies and one of the biggest industrial groups in the world.
- Shell and BP had begun exploration in Nigeria in 1937, finally hitting oil in the swampy Niger delta in 1956. But nothing anywhere would compare to the phenomenon that unfolded in Libya.

### The Libyan Jack-Pot

- During WWII, Rommel's forces had fought in Libya, chronically short of fuel — neither side knowing they were fighting at times only a hundred miles from one of the world's great reserves of oil.
- Libya after WWII was a very poor country whose leading exports were esparto (a grass for making currency paper) and scrap metal scavenged from rusting WWII tanks and weaponry. At the top of its rickety political system sat old King Idris, who "did not really like being king" — he actually wrote a resignation letter once. By the mid-1950s, growing geological suspicion led to the Libyan Petroleum Law of 1955, which provided for many smaller concessions (instead of the vast concession areas of the Persian Gulf). "I did not want Libya to begin as Iraq or as Saudi Arabia or as Kuwait... I didn't want my country to be in the hands of one oil company," explained the Libyan petroleum minister. Libya gave many concessions to independent companies that had no other oil production to protect, so they would explore and produce as fast as possible.
- Working conditions were primitive — no telephones to the outside world, and an estimated three million unexploded WWII land mines. Geologists and oil field workers were "not infrequently injured or killed." Some of the Germans who had laid the mines for Rommel were recruited to remove them.
- Early exploration results were disappointing. BP was beginning to dispose of its supplies and villas in preparation to exit. Then, in April 1959, at a spot called Zelten about a hundred miles south of the Mediterranean coast, Standard Oil of New Jersey made a big strike. The State Department summed it up: "Libya has hit the jack-pot." Ironically, Jersey had nearly given Libya a pass — it already owned 30% of Aramco, was in IPC, the Iranian consortium, and was the largest producer in Venezuela.
- By 1961, ten good fields had been discovered. Libyan crude was very high quality — "sweet" (low-sulfur), refining into a high proportion of gasoline and other light, "clean" products, perfect for Europe's growing automobile fleets and the dawning age of environmentalism. Libya was perfectly located — a quick, secure jaunt across the Mediterranean to European refineries, no need for the Suez Canal or the Horn of Africa route. By 1965, Libya was the world's sixth-largest oil exporter (10% of all petroleum exports). By the end of the 1960s, it was producing over three million barrels per day, and in 1969 its output exceeded Saudi Arabia's.
- Corruption pervaded the Libyan business environment — "nickeled and dimed to death," said one executive. "The pressures came from all kinds of places." The family running the royal household was "particularly well known for its interest in extremely large gratuities."
- The vast surge of Libyan oil dramatically affected world prices. In Libya, more than half the production was in the hands of independent companies that had no other outlets and no reason for restraint. Shut out of the American market by import quotas, they sold aggressively into Europe at whatever price they could get. Between 1960 and 1969, the market price of oil fell by 36 cents a barrel — a 22% drop (correcting for inflation, a 40% decline). "Oil was available for anybody, anytime, any place and always at a price as low as you were charging for it," recalled Howard Page. "I have never seen such a competitive market. The market was just falling on its face."

### Mattei's Last Flight

- As Mattei turned ENI into a world force — taking on the established companies, the US government, and NATO — he intended to link his Mediterranean-based pipeline system to the westward-marching Soviet system, bartering Italian pipe for Russian oil. But he was also working toward a compromise with Jersey and other majors, and preparing to meet President Kennedy.
- On October 27, 1962, Mattei took off from Sicily in his private jet. The only other passenger was Time magazine's Rome bureau chief, researching a cover story. They never made it to Milan — the plane crashed in a terrible thunderstorm, about seven miles short of the runway at Linate Airport.
- Much speculation followed — had Western intelligence services sabotaged his jet? The French Secret Army Organization? It is more likely his death was an accident — Mattei was always in a rush, his impatient personality "would not permit a storm to deter him from landing." He had often pushed his reluctant pilot through Milanese weather.
- At the time of his death, Mattei was fifty-six and at the height of his empire. The NYT foreign affairs columnist called him "the most important individual in Italy, more important than either the premier in Rome or the Pope in the Vatican." It was said he was more responsible than any other man for "the Italian Miracle." ENI's headquarters in Rome was named "Piazza Enrico Mattei." ENI and AGIP continued to grow — but without Mattei, "ENI's buccaneer days as the world's number-one maverick oil company were over."

### The New Competitors — Proliferation of Players

- Though Mattei was gone, he had instigated a revolution that would eventually overthrow the majors' global dominance. The history of international oil was one in which "newcomers" continually broke in on the established order. But up until the 1950s, newcomers could usually be accommodated. That possibility ended in 1957 with Mattei's deal, the Japanese Neutral Zone venture, and the frenetic activity in Libya.
- The explosion in the number of players was remarkable: in the Middle East, 9 oil companies operated in 1946; by 1956, 19; by 1970, 81. Between 1953 and 1972, more than 350 companies entered or significantly expanded their foreign oil activities. Among them: 15 large American oil companies, 20 medium-sized American companies, 10 large American natural gas/chemical/steel companies, and 25 non-American firms. By 1972, at least thirteen "new internationals" each owned more than 2 billion barrels of foreign reserves. Altogether, they owned 112 billion barrels — a quarter of the free world total — with daily output of 5.2 million barrels.
- One obvious result was a decline in profitability. Through the mid-1950s, foreign oil investment had earned above 20%. After the reopening of Suez in 1957, intense competition forced both prices and profits down. Through the 1960s, investment in foreign oil yielded 11 to 13% returns — about the same as manufacturing industries.

### Walking the Tightrope — Iran Versus Saudi Arabia (The "Surge Pot")

- The global battle of production intensified the Iran-Saudi Arabia rivalry. The major companies were in a political quandary — they had to balance supply against demand while newcomers kept adding production, which meant restraining output in the Persian Gulf. The Gulf became the stabilizer, the "swing area" or "surge pot," the control mechanism for balancing global supply and demand. Allocating growth between Iran and Saudi Arabia was hardly easy — "This is like a balloon... Push it in one place, it comes out in another, and so if we acceded to all those demands, we would get it in the neck," said Howard Page.
- The rivalry created enormous problems for the major companies, who were partners in both countries. Jersey, Mobil, Socal, and Texaco were the four Aramco partners — nothing should jeopardize the Saudi concession. But Iran was potentially the dominant power, and the Shah had to be placated. "Nobody could have lifted enough crude to satisfy all the governments in the Persian Gulf during this period," said George Parkhurst of Socal. The tightrope-walk meant allocating growth so neither government felt the other was getting a better deal.
- Between 1957 and 1970, Iranian production grew by 387%, compared to Saudi Arabia's 258%. But because Saudi Arabia had started from a larger base, the two countries' absolute outputs were within 5% of each other in 1970. The high-wire balancing act had, despite running controversies, succeeded.
- The Shah told LBJ at a 1964 White House luncheon that oil companies would give preferential treatment to Arab producers. OPEC, the Shah added, had become "an instrument of Arab imperialism." He told his old friend Kermit Roosevelt that he was "tired of being treated like a schoolboy" by the US. The special relationship between Iran and America, he warned, "is coming to an end." He made a gas deal with Moscow and threatened to reorient Iranian imports toward the Soviet Union. The Shah's tactics worked — both governments urged the oil companies to "do their best" to meet Iranian demands.
- For this achievement, the companies owed a considerable debt to radical Iraq. At the beginning of the 1960s, Iraq revoked 99.5% of the IPC concession, leaving it only the region where it was actually producing. IPC ceased investing in new exploration, and Iraqi output — which could have surged along with Iran's and Saudi Arabia's — only edged up gradually.
- Jersey's Howard Page recommended against entering Oman because he could imagine what Yamani would say if Aramco had to restrain Saudi output to make room for a new neighboring concession. Jersey stayed out; Shell took the lead. Oman became a significant producer. "I am absolutely sure we don't want to go into it," Page had said. "I might put some money in if I was sure we weren't going to get oil, but not if we are going to get oil because we are liable to lose the Aramco concession."

### "Us Independent Oil Suckers" — The Import Quota Fight

- Consumers worldwide welcomed cheap oil from Venezuela and the Middle East — except in the United States, where independent American producers saw the flood of imported oil as a dangerous threat. As early as 1949, an irate Texas geologist named "Tex" Willis wrote to his Senator Lyndon B. Johnson asking if he was "going to be able to do anything for us independent oil suckers on that foreign oil that has destroyed the market for two billion dollars worth of Texas independent oil this year?"
- Oil state representatives sought to raise tariffs tenfold and limit imports to 5% of domestic consumption. Truman opposed: "Something must be radically wrong with the reasoning of the people who would like to cut off our foreign trade for the benefit of the oil crowd." After the Korean War, petroleum imports made further inroads. Coal-producing and oil-producing states formed an unlikely coalition to limit imports.
- Eisenhower first tried "voluntary" restrictions, then a more formal system of voluntary controls in 1957. But companies with large foreign commitments were uncooperative — including Getty, who had embarked on a $600 million expansion program based on Neutral Zone oil. Eventually, in early 1959, Eisenhower reluctantly imposed mandatory import quotas through the Mandatory Oil Import Program (MOIP), the protectionist system that would endure for fourteen years.
- The program was defended as essential to national security — protecting domestic production capacity. Its critics called it a subsidy to domestic producers that cost consumers billions per year. Eisenhower himself had suggested an alternative: a government stockpile of cheap foreign oil in exhausted wells. Nobody supported the idea — the special committee he appointed rejected it as "simply too impractical." (This was essentially the concept of the Strategic Petroleum Reserve, which would be created nearly two decades later.)

### "A Very Healthy Domestic Industry" — The Import Quotas in Practice

- The quotas lasted for fourteen years. Under Eisenhower, imported oil could not exceed 9% of total consumption. The Kennedy Administration tightened them slightly in 1962. The Johnson Administration made some effort to relax them to counteract Vietnam War inflation. But essentially, the quota system remained intact.
- Oil import quotas sounded simple but became increasingly Byzantine — fights over allocations, searches for loopholes, and ever-more-intense hunts for exceptions and exemptions. A brisk market developed not in oil itself but in "tickets" or rights to bring in oil. One notorious loophole was the "Mexican Merry-Go-Round" or "Brownsville U-Turn": since "overland" oil from Mexico qualified for exemption, Mexican oil was shipped by tanker to Brownsville, Texas, put into trucks, driven across the bridge into Mexico, around a traffic circle, and back across the bridge into Brownsville — where it was reloaded onto tankers. Thus shipped "overland," it perfectly legally qualified for an exemption.
- The program made domestic US oil prices higher than they would have been without protection. The average wellhead price in 1959 was $2.90/barrel; a decade later, $2.94 — stable, but 60-70% above Middle Eastern crude in East Coast markets. Conversely, by closing off the American market, mandatory controls resulted in lower prices outside the US.
- Despite the complications, the quotas achieved their fundamental goal: ample protection for domestic production against cheaper foreign oil. By 1968, US crude output was 29% higher than in 1959. Without that protection, American production would surely have plateaued or declined. The majors, despite initial opposition, eventually came to see merit in a program that protected domestic profitability.
- The mandatory program taught the international companies a lesson about political power. Senator Russell Long of Louisiana delivered a homily to oil company executives: "We would like you fellows that produce oil overseas to realize this, that when problems come up with regard to your tax credit overseas or even your depletion allowance overseas, or the special tax treatment to your employees that you have overseas, the fellows you are going to rely upon to protect your activities in that respect are the same people who are interested in the domestic production of oil." In short: "It is very much to your advantage to have a very healthy domestic industry and do everything within your power to cooperate to that end." The international companies grudgingly absorbed the lesson.

## Chapter 27: Hydrocarbon Man

### The Explosion of Oil Consumption

- Whatever the twists of global politics, one trend following WWII progressed in a straight and rapidly ascending line — the consumption of oil. "If it can be said, in the abstract, that the sun energized the planet, it was oil that now powered its human population, both in its familiar forms as fuel and in the proliferation of new petrochemical products. Oil emerged triumphant, the undisputed King, a monarch garbed in a dazzling array of plastics." It was the Age of Hydrocarbon Man.
- Total world energy consumption more than tripled between 1949 and 1972. But oil demand increased more than five and a half times over. Between 1948 and 1972: US consumption tripled (5.8 to 16.4 million barrels/day); Western Europe increased fifteen times over (970,000 to 14.1 million barrels/day); Japan increased 137 times over (32,000 to 4.4 million barrels/day).
- The drivers: rapid economic growth and rising incomes. By the end of the 1960s, populations enjoyed a standard of living far beyond imagining just twenty years before. People bought houses, electrical appliances, central heating, air conditioning. Motor vehicles in the US increased from 45 million in 1949 to 119 million in 1972. Outside the US, the increase was even more monumental — from 18.9 million to 161 million vehicles.
- The petrochemical industry transformed oil and natural gas into plastics and a host of chemicals. In the 1967 film The Graduate, an older man confided the secret of success to a young man: "Plastics." The secret was already everywhere evident.
- Oil prices fell through the 1950s and 1960s, becoming very cheap, which encouraged consumption. Governments promoted oil use for economic growth and modernization. Exporting countries wanting higher revenues pushed production up, giving companies impetus to find new markets.
- The industry became elephantine in every aspect. New refineries were built ever larger for economies of scale. New technologies increased the yield of high-value products — gasoline, diesel, jet fuel, heating oil — from less than 50% to 90% of a barrel of crude. Tanker fleets multiplied; conventional tankers gave way to supertankers. Gasoline stations popped up everywhere. American automobiles, powered by huge engines and bedecked with chrome and extravagant tail fins, grew longer and wider, getting all of eight miles to a gallon of gas.

### Old King Coal Deposed

- In the buoyant decades after WWII, a war was being fought — buried in the day-to-day trade press — between coal and oil for the hearts, minds, and pocketbooks of the consumer. Paul Frankel called it "a war of movement." It was a great historical transformation.
- Coal had powered the Industrial Revolution. Economist W. S. Jevons wrote in the nineteenth century that coal "stands not beside but entirely above all other commodities. It is the material energy of the country, the universal aid, the factor in everything we do." King Coal held on through the first half of the twentieth century — but could not withstand the great tidal wave of petroleum.
- Oil was environmentally more attractive, easier to handle, and became cheaper than coal. In the US, the coal industry's own cost structure made it a sitting duck. There was also a compelling reason: labor strife. Strikes by coal miners, led by John L. Lewis's combative United Mine Workers, were virtually an annual ritual. Lewis's fiery rhetoric and militancy constituted "a powerful invitation to find a substitute for coal." A Venezuelan oil man mused: "We ought to take up a public subscription throughout Venezuela to erect a statue of John L. Lewis in the central square in Caracas — to honor him as one of the greatest benefactors and heroes to the Venezuelan oil industry."
- In Britain, the first postwar energy crisis (1947) was a severe coal shortfall. The government encouraged power plants to switch to oil as a stopgap — but oil was hardly a stopgap; it was a relentless competitor. After Suez, Britain pushed ahead with nuclear energy and discussed emergency oil stockpiles, but security concerns dissipated quickly. Part of oil's victory over coal in Britain was environmental — London's "Killer Fogs" from coal burning were so thick motorists could not find their streets. "Smokeless zones" were established; the 1957 Clean Air Act favored oil. From 1958 onward, oil was cheaper than coal as an industrial fuel. The coal industry responded with an advertising campaign around the "Living Fire" — but when it came to heating homes, "coal was a dying ember."
- By the mid-1960s, the British government concluded that rapid oil growth was needed for international competitiveness. "Oil has become the lifeblood of the economy, as of all other industrialized countries, and it affects every part of it."
- The pattern repeated across Western Europe. By 1960, France committed to rationalization and contraction of its coal industry and a wholesale switch to oil. Keynes had once said "the German empire was built more truly on coal and iron, than on blood and iron." But Germany too converted. In 1955, coal provided 75% of total energy in Western Europe, petroleum just 23%. By 1972, coal had shrunk to 22%, while oil had risen to 60% — almost a complete flip-flop.

### Japan: No Longer Poor

- Japan was somewhat slower to shift to oil. Coal was its traditional energy source. Refineries and oil infrastructure had been devastated in WWII. Not until 1949 did the American Occupation permit reestablishment of oil refining, under the tutelage of Western companies. In 1956, Japan made an epochal declaration: "We are no longer living in the days of postwar reconstruction."
- At the beginning of the 1950s, coal provided more than half of Japan's total energy, and oil only 7% — less than firewood. But oil prices kept falling. By the beginning of the 1960s, the government and industry bet on oil. MITI restructured the petroleum industry to ensure independent Japanese refiners gained substantial market share against Western majors. A 1962 oil law gave MITI authority over import permits and sales allocation.
- In the second half of the 1960s, while the Japanese economy grew at an extraordinary 11% per year, oil demand was growing at an even more extraordinary 18% per year. By the end of the 1960s, oil provided 70% of Japan's total energy, compared to 7% at the beginning of the 1950s.
- The Japanese automotive revolution also drove demand. In 1955, Japan produced only 69,000 cars; by 1968, 4.1 million — 85% bought and used domestically. The great auto export boom, which would establish Japan as a formidable global economic power, had yet to begin.
- The economic historian Alfred Chandler summed up the recipe for the postwar German and Japanese miracles: "improved institutional arrangements and cheap oil." On a global basis, coal had provided two-thirds of world energy in 1949. By 1971, oil, along with natural gas, was providing two-thirds. What Jevons had said in the nineteenth century about coal was now true for oil: "It stood above all other commodities; it was the universal aid, the factor in almost everything we did."

### The Struggle for Europe

- Because of rapid economic growth, the switch from coal to oil, and the advent of the popularly priced automobile, Europe was the most competitive oil market in the world in the 1950s and 1960s. With US protectionist quotas limiting imports, all American companies that had gone overseas to discover oil had to find other markets — which meant Europe. Producing nations kept up pressure to increase volume. "Each year our people made an annual pilgrimage to Kuwait City," said Gulf executive William King. "It was universally the same. It would be a difficult meeting, with lots of threats and blandishments on both sides."
- Companies could not simply buy land and put up a gasoline station in Europe — governments exerted tight controls on allocating locations. "Competition was horrendously intense in Europe because the amounts of money involved were so great," said King. "The people from the different companies would speak politely to each other and act friendly, and then we'd all go out and try to steal each other's markets."
- Shell, the leading European marketer, was on the defensive. Deutsche Shell trained 220 young salesmen in "American-style aggressive selling." Jersey tried to build its relative position; in Britain, stations would often carry pumps for six different brands — Jersey wanted Esso-only stations.
- Among the agile Davids was Continental Oil (later Conoco). Its president Leonard McCollum had been Jersey's worldwide production coordinator. He went overseas in the 1950s, drilled dry holes in Egypt and Africa, but struck it big in Libya with the Oasis Group (a partnership with Marathon and Amerada). Import quotas precluded bringing cheap Libyan oil into the US, so Continental built its own downstream refining and distribution system in Western Europe and Britain under the "Jet" brand name. By 1964, Continental was producing more overseas than in the US.
- The multiplication of such companies, each more or less autonomous, increased competitive pressures and pushed oil prices further down. Their success would also stir up nationalist sentiment in the countries that supplied their oil. "In short, the companies were most vulnerable at the extreme ends of the production chain, the wellhead and the pump."

### Courting the Consumer — Gasoline Wars and Gimmicks

- The American gasoline consumer was riding high in the 1950s and 1960s. Gasoline stations sprouted on every corner, and frequent "price wars" broke out — operators rushing hand-lettered signs to the edge of the property proclaiming prices half a cent less than across the street. The majors did not particularly like price wars — they adopted a "we were forced" attitude — but would sometimes initiate them when breaking into new markets.
- Never had motorists been better served. Tires and oil were checked, windows washed, drinking glasses and sweepstakes entry forms handed out — all for free. Credit cards were introduced in the early 1950s to tie customers to particular companies. Television provided a whole new medium for advertising. Texaco reached beyond its Metropolitan Opera radio fans with the Texaco Star Theater and Milton Berle, urging millions of viewers to "Trust Your Car to the Man Who Wears the Star." Texaco also proudly assured patrons it had gone so far as to "register" all its restrooms throughout the forty-eight states.
- There was the great hullabaloo over gasoline additives. In the mid-1950s, thirteen of the top fourteen marketers began selling new "premium" gasolines with extravagant claims. Richfield proclaimed its gasoline "uses hydrogen for peace" — technically true since all hydrocarbons contain hydrogen. Shell claimed its TCP (tricresyl phosphate), meant to reduce engine deposits, was such a wonder fuel that the Air Force had classified it a military secret — forcing Shell to offer its additive to all comers. Socony countered that TCP was actually harmful and would damage engines. An FTC investigation was launched. The "additives war" was not quite as serious as an oil war, but was a fair reflection of the intensity of competition.

## Chapter 28: The Hinge Years: Countries Versus Companies

### The Persepolis Celebration

- In October 1971, the Shah of Iran staged what Time magazine called "one of the biggest bashes in all history" at Persepolis, the ancient Persian capital sacked by Alexander the Great in 330 B.C. Three huge tents and fifty-nine smaller ones were erected on the desolate site. Assembled dignitaries included the President of the Soviet Union, the Vice-President of the United States, Marshal Tito of Yugoslavia, twenty kings and sheikhs, five queens, twenty-one princes and princesses, fourteen other presidents, three other vice-presidents, three premiers, and two foreign ministers.
- The Shah publicly communed with the ghost of Cyrus the Great, the empire's founder, and promised to continue his tradition. The jeweled guests were taken up the hill for a stunning son et lumiere under the stars — dramatizing, strangely enough, Alexander's destruction of Persepolis.
- The Iranian government urgently sought top-secret advice from Britain on seating protocol — the Foreign Office in London designed a specially constructed table with undulating curves so no one would be too far from a Pahlavi family member.
- The Shah invited Queen Elizabeth II, but she was committed to a state visit in neighboring Turkey, which infuriated him. He asked for Prince Charles — unavailable, on naval duty. London offered Prince Philip and Princess Anne. The Shah accepted but was not placated. He was, after all, ordering several hundred British-built Chieftain tanks, critically important to Britain's balance of payments.
- The event was catered by Maxim's of Paris — 165 chefs, bakers, and waiters flown in from Paris, along with twenty-five thousand bottles of wine. France's President Georges Pompidou was notably absent: "If I did go," he explained privately, "they would probably make me the head-waiter." Cost was estimated at $100-$200 million. The Shah dismissed criticism: "So what are people complaining about? That we are giving a couple of banquets for some 50 Heads of State? We can hardly offer them bread and radishes, can we?"
- After Persepolis, the British invited the Shah to spend Ascot Royal Weekend at Windsor Castle to reduce tensions. The visit was a great success, marred only by a near-diplomatic incident: the Shah, as an Iranian male, would not ride a mare or gelding, only a stallion. None was available until the Queen remembered Princess Anne had a stallion — named Cossack, a potentially offensive name since the Shah's father had been an officer of the Cossack Brigade. The Shah did ride Cossack, though the horse's name was kept from him. Thereafter the Shah wrote to the Queen as "My Dear Sovereign Cousin."
- The overall aim of the Persepolis celebration was to establish the Shah firmly in the line of Cyrus the Great, the Appointed One of God. He was no longer a puppet or pawn — he was a man of enormous wealth, power, and pride, stepping into pivotal new roles in the Middle East and on the international stage.

### The Anglo-American Retreat

- The postwar petroleum order in the Middle East had been developed under American-British ascendancy. By the latter half of the 1960s, the power of both nations was in political recession. The US was mired in Vietnam — costly, unpopular, and ultimately unsuccessful. Anti-Americanism had become a great fashion worldwide, organized around denunciations of imperialism, neocolonialism, and economic exploitation.
- For some in the developing world, the "lessons of Vietnam" were quite different from American interpretations: the dangers and costs of challenging the United States were less than in the past (not as high as for Mossadegh), while the gains could be considerable.
- Britain had been involved in the Persian Gulf since the early 1800s, establishing truces among the sheikhs and guaranteeing the independence of "trucial" states (Bahrain, Kuwait, Qatar). But Britain in the 1960s was preoccupied with economic decline, liquidating its empire. It bowed out of Aden (which became the Marxist-Leninist state of South Yemen), and in January 1968 Prime Minister Harold Wilson announced Britain would end defense commitments east of Suez and completely withdraw its military presence from the Persian Gulf by 1971, eliminating the last major remnant of Pax Britannica.
- Gulf rulers were dumbfounded — only three months earlier the Foreign Office had assured them Britain had no intention of leaving. "Who asked them to leave?" asked the Ruler of Dubai. The Amir of Bahrain said, "Britain could do with another Winston Churchill. Britain is weak now where she was once so strong."
- The British position actually involved only about six thousand ground troops plus air support, costing 12 million pounds a year — a small insurance premium given the vast British oil investments. Some sheikhs offered to pay the 12 million pounds themselves. Defense Secretary Denis Healey angrily rejected this, deriding "mercenaries for people who like to have British troops around." But similar offset payments were accepted for garrisoning troops in West Germany and Hong Kong.
- The British helped set up the United Arab Emirates to bind the small states together, then packed up and left in November 1971. Their departure marked the most fundamental change in the Gulf since World War II — the end of a century-old security system. It left a dangerous power vacuum in a region supplying 32 percent of the free world's petroleum and holding 58 percent of proven oil reserves.
- The Shah of Iran was eager to fill the vacuum: "The safety of the Persian Gulf had to be guaranteed, and who but Iran could fulfill this function?" This was the era of the Nixon Doctrine — depending on strong, friendly local powers as regional policemen. Nixon had developed a high regard for the Shah, whom he first met in 1953. When Nixon lost the 1962 California gubernatorial election, the Shah was one of the few heads of state to receive him cordially on a world trip — Nixon never forgot. Soviet arms were flowing into neighboring Iraq, which had its own ambitions for Gulf hegemony.

### The End of the Twenty-Year Surplus: To a Seller's Market

- The 1970s saw a dramatic shift in world oil. Demand was catching up with supply; the twenty-year surplus was over. The world was rapidly becoming more dependent on the Middle East and North Africa. Free world petroleum demand rose from almost 19 million barrels per day in 1960 to more than 44 million bpd in 1972. Cars were getting heavier, carrying more "extras" like air-conditioning. The cheap price of oil meant no incentive for fuel-efficient automobiles.
- The domestic US oil industry hit a watershed. For decades, the Texas Railroad Commission, Oklahoma Corporations Commission, and Louisiana Conservation Commission had prorationed output, keeping production below capacity. This inadvertently provided the entire Western world with a security reserve — surge capacity callable in crises (WWII, 1951, 1956, 1967).
- Rising demand, low investment, low discovery rates, and the end of import quotas erased the need to restrain production. American oil production reached 11.3 million barrels per day in 1970 — the peak, the highest level it would ever reach. From then on, it began its decline. In March 1971, for the first time in a quarter century, the Texas Railroad Commission allowed all-out production at 100 percent of capacity. "We feel this to be an historic occasion," declared the chairman. "Damned historic, and a sad one. Texas oil fields have been like a reliable old warrior that could rise to the task when needed. That old warrior can't rise anymore."
- US net imports rose rapidly from 2.2 million bpd in 1967 to six million bpd by 1973. Imports as a share of total consumption rose from 19 percent to 36 percent. The disappearance of surplus capacity meant the "security margin" upon which the Western world had depended was gone. In November 1968, the State Department warned European governments at an OECD meeting that American production would soon reach limits — there would be no security cushion. The other participants were taken by surprise.
- Between 1960 and 1970, free world oil demand had grown by 21 million bpd. Production in the Middle East (including North Africa) had grown by 13 million bpd — two-thirds of the huge increase was satisfied by Middle Eastern wells.

### Environmental Impact

- Beginning in the mid-1960s, environmental issues entered politics. Air pollution prompted utilities to shift from coal to less-polluting oil, adding stimulus to demand. In 1965, New York's mayor pledged to banish coal; after a smog crisis on Thanksgiving Day 1966, Consolidated Edison switched to oil. In 1967, a clean air bill passed the Senate 88-3. In 1970, federal environmental impact statements were enacted, and 100,000 people marched down Fifth Avenue for Earth Day.
- The Limits to Growth (1972), a Club of Rome report, argued that if trends in population, industrialization, pollution, food production, energy consumption, and resource depletion continued, industrial civilization would become unsustainable "sometime within the next hundred years." It warned of resource depletion, environmental consequences of hydrocarbon burning, CO2 buildup, and global warming. It became a lodestar in energy/environment debates, fueling fear and pessimism about impending shortages.
- The environmental movement had a paradoxical effect — both increasing demand for oil (as a cleaner-burning substitute for coal) and restricting its supply (blocking new exploration). In January 1969, an offshore well in the Santa Barbara Channel encountered an unexpected geological anomaly; an estimated six thousand barrels seeped out, washing crude oil onto thirty miles of beaches. The Nixon Administration imposed a moratorium on California offshore development. The Santa Barbara spill also increased opposition to development in other environmentally sensitive areas, including Alaska — the most promising area to stem the decline in American production.

### The Alaskan Elephant

- As early as 1923, President Harding had created a naval petroleum reserve on the Arctic coast of Alaska. Following the 1956 Suez Crisis, Shell and Standard Oil of New Jersey began exploring but drilled the most expensive dry hole to that date in 1959 and suspended operations. Sinclair Oil and BP drilled six expensive dry holes in succession on the North Slope and also suspended.
- Gulf Oil showed interest but top management refused: "It would cost $5 a barrel, and oil will never get to $5 a barrel in our lifetime."
- Richfield, a California-based independent attracted by thick marine sediments on the North Slope, partnered with Jersey's Humble subsidiary in 1964. In 1965, this joint venture won about two-thirds of the exploration leases on the Prudhoe Bay structure. The BP-Sinclair combination won the rest. That same year, Richfield merged with Atlantic Refining to form ARCO, headed by Robert O. Anderson.
- Anderson was one of the last great wildcatters and oil tycoons of the twentieth century — son of a Chicago banker who lent to Texas and Oklahoma independents in the 1930s. He grew up around the University of Chicago, considered becoming a philosophy professor, but was captured by the oil men who were his father's clients. In 1942, he took over a 1,500-barrel-per-day refinery in New Mexico and became one of the better-known independents. He had quick mental arithmetic rivaling Rockefeller and Deterding, and believed fervently in crude oil and reserves as "the absolute heart of the industry." "90 percent of what you drill are failures. You really have to take defeat regularly." The other 10 percent made him the largest individual landowner in the United States.
- In winter 1966, ARCO drilled a costly dry well sixty miles south of Alaska's north coast. One more wildcat was on the schedule at Prudhoe Bay. Anderson gave the okay reluctantly — it was more a decision not to cancel a well already scheduled than an enthusiastic go-ahead.
- In spring 1967, the ARCO-Humble venture began drilling Prudhoe Bay State Number 1. On December 26, 1967, a loud, vibrating sound drew about forty men in heavy clothes (thirty degrees below zero, thirty-knot wind). The roar of natural gas sounded like four jumbo jets. A gas flare shot thirty feet straight up. They had struck oil. In mid-1968, a "step-out well" seven miles away confirmed a world-class field. DeGolyer and McNaughton estimated Prudhoe Bay held as much as 10 billion barrels of recoverable reserves — the largest oil field ever discovered in North America, one-and-a-half times larger than Dad Joiner's East Texas field.
- ARCO acquired Sinclair, and the Big Three on the North Slope were ARCO, Jersey, and BP. The physical environment was fiercely hostile — "a mean, nasty, unforgiving place to work." Temperatures fell to sixty-five below zero, tundra thawed into spongy prairie in summer, no roads existed, permafrost extended a thousand feet deep. Normal steel piling crumbled in the permafrost.
- Transport options considered included icebreaker tankers through the Arctic, a monorail, an eight-lane highway of trucks across Alaska, nuclear-powered submarine tankers under the polar ice cap to a Greenland port created by nuclear explosion, and jumbo jet oil tankers. The decision was a pipeline — either an 800-mile trans-Alaskan pipeline south to Valdez, or an overland pipeline east through Canada to Chicago. The trans-Alaskan route was chosen because it was "all-American," more secure, and could be built more quickly.
- The pipeline group (ARCO, Jersey, BP) hurriedly bought 500,000 tons of forty-eight-inch pipe from a Japanese manufacturer and brought $75 million worth of Caterpillar trucks and trailers to the Yukon River. But a Federal court injunction won by environmentalists in 1970 — energized by the Santa Barbara spill — completely stopped the project. The trucks and tractors sat for five years, companies spending millions just keeping engines warm. The oil expected from Alaska in 1972 did not flow; American imports of foreign oil rose instead.
- North Sea oil was another promising alternative but highly uncertain — enormous scale, very expensive, harsh and treacherous environment requiring an entirely new generation of technology. Alaska and the North Sea shared a common bond: physically difficult but politically stable. Even so, neither could provide imminent relief for the tightening global supply-demand balance.

### The Doctor

- Just before dawn in late August 1970, a chartered French Falcon jet entered Libyan air space carrying Dr. Armand Hammer, chairman of Occidental Petroleum, age seventy-two. He feared losing his "shining star" — Occidental's richly prolific oil concession in Libya.
- Hammer was born in 1898 in New York City to an immigrant Jewish family with roots in Odessa on the Black Sea. His father, Dr. Julius Hammer, was a practicing physician, drug manufacturer, and partisan of the left who had met Lenin in 1907 and was one of the founders of the American Communist party. Armand did not share his father's socialist tendencies — he was interested in making money and doing deals.
- In 1921, having just graduated from medical school, Hammer went to Russia carrying relief medical supplies and aiming to collect $150,000 owed to the family drug business. Through his father's connections, he became known to Lenin, who was allowing some competition in the ruined Russian economy. Lenin commended Hammer to Stalin as "a little path leading to the American 'business' world." Hammer stayed in Russia doing business under Lenin's New Economic Policy — an asbestos concession, a Ford agency, the national concession for pencils, even fur stations in Siberia. When Stalin came to power, Hammer correctly read the portents, packed up, and carried out a load of Russian art, sold through department stores in the US. He went on to make millions in businesses from beer barrels and bourbon whiskey to bull semen.
- In 1956, at age fifty-eight, Hammer came to Los Angeles as a wealthy art gallery owner and collector, seeking a tax shelter. He made some oil investments, then bought a small, nearly bankrupt company called Occidental. By 1961, Occidental made its first significant discovery in California. By 1966, annual sales were almost $700 million. His political connections were unparalleled; his personal fortune vast. "The Doctor is one of the greatest actors in the world," was the acid comment of one who had mistakenly thought himself Hammer's heir apparent.
- Hammer renewed contacts with the Soviet Union under Khrushchev and became a go-between for five Soviet General Secretaries and seven US Presidents. As late as 1990, at age ninety-two, he was still the active chairman. He was in the line of the great buccaneer-creators of oil: Rockefeller, Samuel, Deterding, Gulbenkian, Getty, and Mattei.
- In 1965, Occidental won concessions in the second round of Libyan bidding. Oxy's thick bid stood out — done on sheepskin manuscripts wrapped in red, black, and green ribbons (the Libyan flag colors). As sweeteners, Oxy promised to establish an agricultural experimental farm at King Idris's childhood home and gave the King a gold chess set, plus the expected overrides and commissions to those who could help.
- Occidental's blocks (Numbers 102 and 103) covered almost two thousand square miles of desert in the Sirte Basin, more than a hundred miles from the Mediterranean coast. Initial drilling was dry and costly — Occidental's board grumbled about "Hammer's Folly." But in autumn 1966, Occidental struck oil on Number 102, and then spectacularly on Number 103 (the Idris field) — drilled right under the site of Mobil's former base camp, which Mobil had relinquished. The first well came in at 43,000 bpd; another at a phenomenal 75,000 bpd. It was newly developed seismic technology that enabled this "puny California producer to find what giant Mobil had missed." "All hell broke loose. We became one of the Big Boys."
- By another stroke of luck, the 1967 Six-Day War closed the Suez Canal, making Libyan oil (close to Europe) even more valuable. DeGolyer and McNaughton estimated Occidental alone held three billion barrels of recoverable reserves — almost a third of the reserves discovered on Alaska's North Slope. A 130-mile pipeline across the desert was force-built in less than a year. Occidental was soon producing upwards of 800,000 barrels per day in Libya, becoming the sixth-largest oil-producing company in the world.
- Yet Occidental was very vulnerable, owing to its lopsided dependence on Libya. Elderly King Idris could not last. Hammer sought to diversify by acquiring Island Creek Coal. Before agreeing, Island Creek's president William Bollano checked with the State Department, Chase Manhattan Bank, and Citibank — all assured him political stability in Libya for five or six years and an "orderly transfer of power when the King died." The merger went ahead in 1968. The experts were all dead wrong.

### The Libyan Squeeze

- On the night of August 31-September 1, 1969, a senior Libyan military officer was awakened in his bedroom by a junior officer telling him the coup was early — the senior officer's own coup was scheduled for a few days later. The whole Libyan military had been seething with conspiracies. A group of radical young officers led by the charismatic Muammar al-Qaddafi beat all others to the punch, staging their coup on September 1. Many military men participated without knowing who was in charge or even which coup it was.
- Qaddafi and his associates had begun conspiring a full decade earlier as teenagers in secondary school, inspired by Nasser, his Philosophy of Revolution, and the Voice of the Arabs radio station. They modeled their lives on Nasser and took the same route to power — through the military academy. In Qaddafi's mind, the revolutionary doctrines of Nasser were amalgamated "to the ideas of Islam at the time of Mohammed." He was a born conspirator, but also eccentric and erratic, with the mood swings of a manic-depressive. Equipped with huge oil revenues, he would become banker, sponsor, and paymaster for many terrorist groups around the world.
- Among Qaddafi's first acts: shutting down British and American military bases, expelling the large Italian population, closing all Catholic churches and ordering their crosses removed and contents auctioned off. After a December 1969 countercoup was aborted, Qaddafi was ready to deal with oil. In January 1970, officers of the Revolutionary Command Council demanded an increase in the posted price. Qaddafi warned the twenty-one oil companies he would shut down production if necessary: "People who have lived without oil for 5,000 years can live without it again for a few years in order to attain their legitimate rights."
- Initially, Libya pressured Esso-Libya, demanding a 43-cent-a-barrel increase. "43 cents in those days — Good God! That was out of the world," recalled Esso-Libya's head. Esso offered five cents. Stymied by the majors (which had alternative sources), Libya turned to the one company without alternatives — Occidental. "It had all its eggs in one basket." In late spring 1970, Occidental was ordered to cut production from 800,000 to around 500,000 bpd. Libyan policemen began to stop, search, and harass Occidental executives.
- Libya had chosen a propitious time. Libya was supplying 30 percent of Europe's oil. The Suez Canal was still closed. In May 1970, a tractor ruptured the Tapline inside Syria, cutting 500,000 bpd of Saudi oil to the Mediterranean. Tanker rates immediately tripled. There was no shortage of oil, but a shortage of transportation — putting Libya and Qaddafi, sitting directly across the Mediterranean from European markets, in a central position. Between the Tapline closure and the Libyan cutbacks, 1.3 million bpd had been abruptly taken off the market. Abdullah Tariki, the radical former Saudi oil minister fired eight years earlier, was now in Tripoli advising the revolutionary government.
- Hammer became agitated. He went to Egypt to ask Nasser to intercede with his "disciple." Concerned about Libya's subsidies to the Egyptian Army, Nasser advised Qaddafi to go easy and not to repeat Egypt's mistakes with nationalization and expulsion of foreign technicians. The advice was not heeded.
- Hammer tried to find other companies that would provide replacement oil at cost if Occidental stood firm against Qaddafi. He was unsuccessful. Even a visit to Kenneth Jamieson, chairman of Exxon, did not produce the oil Hammer wanted. Hammer was very bitter. "Here the President of Exxon, the world's largest corporation, was confronted with an art dealer, a non-fraternity type, coming out of the cold with a worldwide scheme."
- Desperate, Hammer tried a barter deal over dinner at Lyndon Johnson's ranch in Texas — he would be middleman trading McDonnell Douglas warplanes for Iranian oil. That too failed. Then in late August 1970, his Libya manager George Williamson warned that nationalization was imminent — sending Hammer streaking through the night skies to Tripoli.
- On the Libyan side, negotiations were led by Deputy Prime Minister Abdel Salaam Ahmed Jalloud — more fun-loving than the puritanical Qaddafi but a relentless negotiator. He once rolled up a Texaco/Standard of California proposal into a paper ball and threw it back in their faces. On another occasion, he charged into a room of oil executives with a submachine gun slung over his shoulder. At his first session with Hammer, after offering hot rolls and coffee, Jalloud unbuckled his belt and set his .45 revolver directly in front of Hammer. Hammer smiled but was disconcerted — he had never before negotiated across a gun barrel.
- Hammer commuted daily between Tripoli and Paris (flying back by 2 a.m., off again by 6 a.m.), telephoning his board from the Ritz Hotel. He worried about being "detained" and initially used a chartered French jet rather than his own Gulfstream II, fearing the Libyans might seize his private plane.
- Finally, Hammer and Jalloud reached agreement. The Libyans got a 20 percent increase in royalties and taxes — a thirty-cent posted price increase and a hike in Libya's profit share from 50 to 55 percent. By the end of September, virtually all other companies in Libya had given in with immense reluctance. The Libyans solemnly promised to stick to these new agreements for five years.
- The significance went far beyond the thirty-cent increase. The Libyan agreements decisively changed the balance of power between producing countries and oil companies. For the exporters, it was emboldening — it not only reversed the decline in the real price of oil but reopened the campaign for sovereignty and control over their resources. "The oil industry as we had known it would not exist much longer," said the Jersey director for Libya. George Williamson of Occidental had a premonition: "Everybody who drives a tractor, truck, or car in the Western world will be affected by this."

### Leapfrogging Prices

- The Shah of Iran would not be outdone by young Libyan military officers. In November 1970, he broke through the fifty-fifty profit-sharing barrier, winning 55 percent of consortium profits. The companies then offered 55 percent to all Gulf countries, triggering a leapfrog game. Venezuela introduced legislation raising its share to 60 percent with unilateral price-setting power. An OPEC conference endorsed 55 percent as the minimum and threatened a supply cutoff. At the beginning of 1971, Libya jumped over Iran with new demands.
- David Barran, chairman of Shell Transport and Trading, became the foremost advocate of a common company front: "Our Shell view was that the avalanche had begun. Without a united front, the companies would be picked off one by one." The companies formed a "Front Uni" of two dozen companies (American and non-American) representing about four-fifths of free world oil production, with an antitrust waiver from the US Justice Department. They also created the "Libyan Safety Net" — a secret understanding that if any company's production was cut back for standing up to Qaddafi, the others would provide replacement oil. This institutionalized the deal Hammer had tried and failed to get from Exxon six months earlier.
- On January 15, 1971, the companies sent a "Letter to OPEC" calling for an "all-embracing" settlement — negotiate with OPEC as a whole, not individual exporters or subgroups. But the Shah resolutely opposed this, saying "moderates" could not restrain the "radicals" (Libya and Venezuela). He promised a stable five-year agreement if the companies dealt with Gulf countries separately. "If the companies tried any tricks, the entire Gulf would be shut down and no oil would flow."
- The Front Uni negotiators — George Piercy of Exxon and Lord Strathalmond of BP — arrived in Tehran to find that Washington had acquiesced to the Shah's position, undercutting them. "It made the whole exercise look silly as hell," Piercy said. The OPEC Gulf committee (Iranian Finance Minister Jamshid Amouzegar, Saudi Oil Minister Zaki Yamani, Iraqi Oil Minister Saadoun Hammadi) was unbending. The Shah himself denounced the companies and brandished the possibility of an embargo, summoning the ghost of Mossadegh: "The conditions of the year 1951 do not exist anymore."
- Yamani privately warned Piercy that there was real talk of an embargo among exporting countries, and that the Saudis and the Shah supported it. "I don't think you realize the problem in OPEC. I must go along."
- The companies capitulated. On February 14, 1971, the Tehran Agreement was signed. The hallowed fifty-fifty principle was dead — 55 percent was the new minimum government take. The price of oil was raised by thirty-five cents a barrel, with further annual hikes. The exporters pledged no increases for five years. "It was the real turning point for OPEC," said one OPEC official. "After the Tehran Agreement, OPEC got muscles." David Barran of Shell was a better prophet: "There is no doubt that the buyer's market for oil is over."
- Negotiations then shifted to the Mediterranean committee (Libya, Algeria, Saudi Arabia, Iraq). Major Jalloud used all his now-familiar tactics — bluster, intimidation, revolutionary sermons, threats of embargo and nationalization. On April 2, 1971, agreement was announced: the posted price was raised by ninety cents — well beyond the Tehran Agreement's implications. Libya's oil revenues increased by almost 50 percent. The Shah was beside himself with rage — he had been leapfrogged again.

### Participation: "Indissoluble, like a Catholic Marriage"

- The five-year stability pledged in the Tehran and Tripoli agreements proved illusory. OPEC soon sought further increases to compensate for dollar devaluation. But a more momentous conflict was over "participation" — the acquisition by exporting countries of partial ownership of oil resources within their borders. If exporters won this battle, it would mean a radical restructuring of the industry.
- The concession system, dating back to William Knox D'Arcy's 1901 venture in Persia, was now seen by exporters as a holdover from the defunct age of colonialism, wholly inappropriate to the new age of decolonization, self-determination, and nationalism.
