Countries did not want to be mere tax collectors — the greater question was sovereignty over their own natural resources.
- "Participation" was consciously devised as an alternative to outright nationalization. Outright nationalization would disrupt relations with international companies and put producing countries directly into the business of selling oil — leading to a battle royal among exporters for markets, a dramatic collapse of the price structure, and financial/political instability. Yamani insisted participation — joint ownership with the majors — was the way to meet exporters' objectives while maintaining the system that held up the price. It would create "a bond that would be indissoluble, like a Catholic marriage."
- Participation fit Saudi Arabia's situation — gradual change rather than radical overturning. But for other exporters, gradual participation was insufficient. Algeria took 51 percent of French oil operations outright. Venezuela passed legislation under which all concessions would revert to the government upon expiration in the early 1980s. Libya nationalized BP's holdings (punishing Britain for Iran's seizure of islands near the Strait of Hormuz). Iraq nationalized the Iraq Petroleum Company's last remnant — the Kirkuk concession, the great producing field discovered in the 1920s. "There is a worldwide trend toward nationalization and Saudis cannot stand against it alone," Yamani warned.
- In October 1972, a "participation agreement" was reached between the Gulf states and the companies: an immediate 25 percent participation share, rising to 51 percent by 1983. But the agreement was less popular than Yamani hoped — Algeria, Libya, and Iran all stood outside it. Kuwait's parliament rejected it, so Kuwait was also out.
- At a New York meeting of oil executives chaired by John McCloy, Aramco announced its decision to accept participation. Ed Guinn of Bunker Hunt was upset — concessions in the Gulf would only goad Libya into greater demands. The Aramco plan reminded him of "two skeletons hanging in a closet. One skeleton said to the other, 'How did we get here?' The other replied, 'I don't know, but if we had any guts we'd get out.'" "Meeting adjourned!" McCloy shouted.
- After Yamani's deal with Aramco, Libya took over 50 percent of ENI's operations, then expropriated Bunker Hunt entirely. Qaddafi proudly announced — standing with Uganda's Idi Amin Dada — that by taking over Bunker Hunt, he had given the United States "a big hard blow on its cold insolent face." He then nationalized 51 percent of all other companies in Libya, including Occidental.
- The Shah insisted on a better deal than Saudi Arabia. Owing to the 1951 nationalization, Iran already owned the oil and facilities, but the consortium — not NIOC — actually operated the industry. The Shah demanded NIOC become not only owner but also operator; the 1954 consortium companies would set up a new company to act as a service contractor to NIOC. He got what he wanted. "Finally I won out," the Shah declared. "Seventy-two years of foreign control of the operations of our industry was ended."

### The Hinge Years

- In gaining greater control over oil companies, the exporting countries also gained greater control over prices. Where they had previously sought income by emphasizing volume (pushing prices down), they now sought higher prices, supported by the tight supply-demand balance. The companies had been unable to put up a new Front Uni, nor had their governments backed them. Western governments were distracted by other matters and some thought price increases were justified and would stimulate conservation and new energy development.
- Both Britain and the US had powerful incentives to cooperate with rather than confront Iran and Saudi Arabia — by the early 1970s, both were heeding the Sultan of Oman's appeal to put down a radical rebellion and were taking up roles as regional policemen. Arms purchases were increasing rapidly, calibrating the interaction of rising oil prices with the new security calculus for the Gulf.
- The supply-demand balance was sending a critical message: cheap oil had been a tremendous boon to economic growth, but it could not be sustained. New supplies were needed. As late as 1970, there were still about 3 million bpd of excess capacity outside the US, mostly in the Middle East. By 1973, this had been cut in half to about 1.5 million bpd (~3 percent of total demand). Middle Eastern countries had been instituting cutbacks; by 1973, surplus "available" production capacity was just 500,000 bpd — one percent of free world consumption. A 99 percent utilization rate with a one percent security margin was extraordinarily precarious. Politics was adding to the dangers.
- James Placke, an American diplomat in Tripoli who had watched the Libyan squeeze unfold from his broom-closet office overlooking an Occidental building, wrote a prescient dispatch to the State Department in late November 1970. He argued that the international petroleum order had been irrevocably changed. The producing countries "will be able to overcome their divisions to cooperate in controlling production and raising prices." The extent of Western dependence on oil as a source of energy had been exposed, and the "rationale of those who call for the use of Arab oil as a weapon in Middle East conflict has also been strengthened." He added: "Control of the flow of resources has been of strategic concern throughout history. Asserting control over a vital source of energy would permit Middle Eastern states to regain the power position vis-a-vis the West, which this area lost long ago." The greatest sin was inattention.
- The US ambassador was so impressed he sent the dispatch over his own name to give it added weight. But to Placke's knowledge, no one in Washington paid any serious attention. He certainly never heard a word back on it.

## Chapter 29: The Oil Weapon

- Just moments before 2:00 p.m. on October 6, 1973 — Yom Kippur, the holiest of Jewish holidays — 222 Egyptian jets roared into the sky targeting Israeli command posts and positions on the eastern bank of the Suez Canal and in the Sinai. Minutes later, 3,000 field guns opened fire along the entire front. Simultaneously, Syrian aircraft attacked Israel's northern border, followed by a barrage from 700 pieces of artillery. This began the October War (Yom Kippur War), the fourth Arab-Israeli war — the most destructive, intense, and with the most far-reaching consequences. One of the most potent weapons was unique to the Middle East: the oil weapon, wielded as an embargo, production cutbacks, and export restrictions that, in Henry Kissinger's words, "altered irrevocably the world as it had grown up in the postwar period."
- By 1973, oil had become the lifeblood of the world's industrial economies, pumped and circulated with very little to spare. Never before had the supply-demand equation been so tight while the relationships between oil-exporting countries and oil companies continued to unravel.

### The United States Joins the World Market

- Under the Nixon Administration, oil and energy were rising on the political agenda. The Mandatory Oil Import Program (Eisenhower's quotas) was laboring under mounting strain. Nixon established a Cabinet Task Force on Oil Import Control, headed by Labor Secretary George Shultz, to review the quota program. Shultz's committee recommended scrapping the quotas and replacing them with a tariff. The political response was overwhelmingly negative — a hundred Congressmen signed a letter denouncing it as a threat to the domestic industry. Nixon shelved the report and kept the quotas.
- The Shah of Iran wrote to Nixon, arguing Iran's security required surmounting quota barriers. The Administration was sympathetic to the "power vacuum in the Persian Gulf" but would not tear down restrictions to please the Shah. Nixon apologized in a letter: "Your disappointment that it has not been possible to find a way to provide for increased sales of Iranian oil in the United States is understandable."
- Signs of stress were multiplying in the US energy supply system. During the 1969-70 winter (the coldest in thirty years), both oil and natural gas were in short supply. Electric utilities switched from coal to oil. The following summer, capacity constraints led to brownouts up and down the Atlantic Coast. Surplus oil production capacity in the US had disappeared. Price controls on oil, imposed by Nixon in 1971 as part of his anti-inflation program, were discouraging domestic production while stimulating consumption. Natural gas supplies were tightening due to a regulatory system that controlled prices. Utilities were ordering new nuclear power plants.
- In April 1973, Nixon delivered the first-ever Presidential address on energy, announcing he was abolishing the quota system. Domestic production could no longer keep up with America's voracious appetite. The Administration immediately followed with a "voluntary" allocation system to distribute whatever supplies were available. The shift perfectly symbolized how circumstances had changed: quotas managed surplus; allocations distributed shortage.

### "The Wolf Is Here"

- James Akins, a tall, somber Foreign Service officer and State Department oil expert detailed to the White House, had directed a secret study concluding the world petroleum industry was passing through "the last gasp in the buyers market." By 1975, "we will have entered a permanent sellers' market, with any one of several major suppliers being able to create a supply crisis by cutting off oil supplies." He called for reducing consumption, raising domestic production, and importing from "secure sources." These actions "will be as unpopular as they will be costly." None were taken.
- In April 1973, Akins prepared a secret report from his White House post with proposals including expanded coal use, synthetic fuels, a stiff gasoline tax, and much-increased research and development. His ideas were met with incredulity. "Conservation is not the Republican ethic," Nixon's chief domestic adviser John Ehrlichman told him flatly. That same month, Akins published an article in Foreign Affairs: "The Oil Crisis: This Time the Wolf Is Here." It was widely read but controversial. Foreign Policy magazine simultaneously published "Is the Oil Shortage Real?" emphatically answering no and suggesting Akins was part of a State Department/oil exporter/company cabal.
- Without import barriers, the US was now a full-fledged, very thirsty member of the world oil market. Companies were buying any oil they could find. Petroleum Intelligence Weekly reported in August 1973 "near-panic buying by U.S. and European independents as well as the Japanese" sending "oil prices sky-rocketing." By summer 1973, US imports were 6.2 million bpd, compared to 3.2 million in 1970.
- As demand exceeded supply, market prices exceeded official posted prices for the first time — a decisive change marking the end of the twenty-year surplus. The exporters did not want to see the growing gap between posted price and market price going to the companies. On September 1, 1973, Libya nationalized 51 percent of those company operations not already taken over. Nixon warned: "Oil without a market, as Mr. Mossadegh learned many, many years ago, does not do a country much good." But the stern admonition fell flat — in 1973, there was no spare capacity; the market was hungry. Libya had no trouble selling its oil.
- The radicals in OPEC — Iraq, Algeria, Libya — pushed for revision of the Tehran and Tripoli agreements. By September, Yamani told Aramco's president the Tehran Agreement was "now out of whack," "either dead or dying." If the companies did not cooperate in framing a new price agreement, the exporters would "exercise our rights on our own."

### The Secret: Sadat's Gamble

- Upon coming to power after Nasser's death in 1970, Anwar Sadat had been regarded as a nonentity, judged likely to last only a few months. He was much underestimated. "The legacy Nasser left me was in a pitiable condition," he later said. Egypt was pouring over 20 percent of its GNP into military expenditures (Israel was close behind at 18 percent). Sadat wanted to break out of the cycle of conflict with Israel and out of stalemated diplomacy, but concluded this could not occur while Israel sat on the eastern bank of the Suez Canal and held the entire Sinai.
- He purged the pro-Soviet Egyptians and in July 1972 threw out twenty thousand arrogant Soviet military advisers, though he continued receiving Soviet military supplies. Still, he did not get the response he expected from the West. In late 1972 and early 1973, Sadat came to his fateful decision: war was the only way to obtain his political objectives. "What literally no one understood beforehand was the mind of the man," Kissinger later said. "Sadat aimed not so much for territorial gain but for a crisis that would alter the attitudes into which the parties were then frozen — and thereby open the way to negotiations." His purpose was "psychological and diplomatic, much more than military."
- Sadat's decision was calculated — he was operating on Clausewitz's dictum that war was the continuation of politics by other means. By April 1973, he had begun formulating joint Egyptian-Syrian attack plans with Syria's President Hafez al-Assad. His secret was tightly kept. One of the few people outside the high commands with whom he shared it was King Faisal of Saudi Arabia — meaning oil would be central to the coming conflict.

### The Oil Weapon Unsheathed: Faisal Changes His Mind

- Ever since the 1950s, the Arab world had talked about using the "oil weapon." But it had always been deflected by the fact that Arab oil was not the supply of last resort — Texas, Louisiana, Oklahoma could always put additional oil into the world market. Once the US hit 100 percent production rates, that old warrior could not rise again to defend against the oil weapon.
- King Faisal had gone out of his way to reject use of the oil weapon. In summer 1972, when Sadat called for manipulation of oil supplies, Faisal disagreed strongly: "It was not only useless, but dangerous even to think of that. Politics and oil should not be mixed." Saudi Arabia had discovered during the 1967 war that cutting exports availed nothing except loss of revenues and markets. Faisal believed the US would not need Arab Gulf oil before 1985. "Therefore my opinion is that this proposal should be ruled out."
- But by early 1973, Faisal was changing his mind. Saudi Arabia had become the marginal supplier for the entire world, graduating to the position once held by Texas — the swing producer. Saudi Arabia's share of world exports had risen from 13 percent in 1970 to 21 percent in 1973. Its average output in July 1973 was 8.4 million bpd, 62 percent higher than July 1972. Aramco was producing at capacity. There was also a growing view that Saudi Arabia was earning revenues in excess of what it could spend, while two devaluations of the dollar had abruptly cut the worth of its financial holdings. "What is the point of producing more oil and selling it for an unguaranteed paper currency?" the Kuwaiti oil minister asked.
- The changing marketplace coincided with significant political developments. Faisal had been estranged from Nasser but was sympathetic to Sadat, who was seeking to dismantle Nasser's legacy. Without Saudi support, Sadat might revert to the Soviet connection. By spring 1973, Sadat was pressing Faisal to use the oil weapon. Faisal also felt growing pressure from within his kingdom and the Arab world — guerrillas attacked the Tapline terminal at Sidon, destroying one tank.
- In early May 1973, Faisal met with Aramco executives. Yes, he was a staunch friend of the US, but it was "absolutely mandatory" that the US "do something to change the direction that events were taking in the Middle East today." Aramco's president reported the King's warning: it was "most unsafe for American interests in the Middle East." "A simple disavowal of Israeli policies and actions" would "go a long way toward overcoming the current anti-American feeling." There was "extreme urgency."
- A few weeks later at the Geneva Intercontinental Hotel, Yamani mentioned to Aramco executives that the King had just had a "bad time" in Cairo; Sadat had put intense pressure on him. "Time is running out with respect to U.S. interest in Middle East," the King told the oil men. "Saudi Arabia is in danger of being isolated among its Arab friends, because of failure of the U.S. Government to give Saudi Arabia positive support." Faisal was emphatic: "You will lose everything."
- The company executives went to Washington. "Action must be taken urgently; otherwise, everything will be lost." US government officials expressed "a large degree of disbelief that any drastic action was imminent." "Some believe" the King "is calling wolf when no wolf exists except in his imagination," the oil men were told. Three of the companies — Texaco, Chevron, and Mobil — publicly called for a change in American Middle East policy. Faisal himself gave interviews to the Washington Post, Christian Science Monitor, Newsweek, and NBC Television: "We have no wish to restrict our oil exports to the United States in any way, but America's complete support for Zionism and against the Arabs makes it extremely difficult for us to continue to supply the United States with oil, or even to remain friends with the United States."

### Nervous Leaders

- In June 1973, at a summit meeting at San Clemente, an agitated Brezhnev, unable to sleep, suddenly demanded an unscheduled late-night meeting with Nixon. For three hours, in front of a little fire overlooking the dark Pacific, Brezhnev argued in rough and emotional terms that the Middle East was explosive and a war might soon start. He insisted the only way to avoid war was a new Soviet diplomatic initiative. Nixon and Kissinger thought it was a heavy-handed ploy and dismissed it.
- On August 23, 1973, Sadat made an unannounced trip to Riyadh and told King Faisal he was considering war against Israel. It would begin with a surprise attack. Faisal allegedly promised half a billion dollars for Sadat's war chest and pledged to use the oil weapon. "But give us time. We don't want to use our oil as a weapon in a battle which only goes on for two or three days, and then stops. We want to see a battle which goes on for long enough time for world opinion to be mobilized."
- Less than a week later, on August 27, Yamani told an Aramco executive that the King had suddenly taken to asking for detailed reports on Aramco's production, expansion plans, and the consequences of curtailment on consuming countries, especially the US. "This is a completely new phenomenon," Yamani explained. Yamani warned that elements led by Kissinger "are misleading Nixon as to the seriousness of Saudi Arabia's intentions." The King was "one hundred percent determined to effect a change in U.S. policy and to use oil for that purpose." He was "additionally under constant pressure from Arab public opinion and Arab leaders, particularly Sadat. He is losing patience."

### September 1973: "Pressure All Around"

- By September 1973, talk of an impending energy crisis was widespread. The Middle East Economic Survey headlined: "The Oil Scene: Pressure All Around." The administration decided to impose mandatory allocations for some oil products in tight domestic supply. Joseph Sisco, American Assistant Secretary of State, told Israeli television: "While our interests are parallel to the interests of Israel, they are not synonymous with the state of Israel."
- Nixon at a press conference acknowledged the possibility of Arabs using oil as a club: "We are all in the same bag when you really come down to it. Both sides need to start negotiating." In Germany, the Bonn government unveiled its first energy program focused on security of supply — Ulf Lantzke had needed five years to overcome "the deep-seated political belief that energy supplies were no problem."
- In Japan, MITI released a September 1973 White Paper: "The oil-supply management system, until now run by the international oil companies, has crumbled." Japan pursued "resource diplomacy" — MITI Minister Yasuhiro Nakasone called for Japan to follow "her own independent direction." On September 26, Prime Minister Kakuei Tanaka told a television interviewer, "Regarding the energy crisis, an oil crisis ten years from now is clearly seen." It was more like ten days. Sadat was beginning his countdown to war.

### Nothing Further to Negotiate

- At a mid-September Vienna meeting, OPEC called for a new deal with oil companies. The Tehran and Tripoli agreements were dead. Oil company representatives were summoned to meet in Vienna on October 8 with Yamani's team. The companies' joint lawyer John McCloy requested an antitrust waiver from the Justice Department on September 21. Justice Department attorneys, citing a book by an MIT professor, insisted oil prices were going up because of the machinations of large integrated companies, not OPEC. Kenneth Jamieson of Exxon stared in disbelief. Finally, on October 5, three days before the Vienna meeting, the Antitrust Division reluctantly gave clearance.
- American intelligence had been dismissing the likelihood of war for months. It seemed irrational for the Arabs to start a war they would badly lose. A CIA analysis on October 5: "The military preparations that have occurred do not indicate that any party intends to initiate hostilities." At 5:30 p.m. on October 5, the latest Israeli estimate: "We consider the opening of military operations against Israel by the two armies as of low probability." The Watch Committee representing the entire American intelligence community agreed. War, it said, was unlikely.
- On that same day, as Yom Kippur began, the Saudi delegation to OPEC boarded their jet for Vienna. Only when they arrived on October 6 did they learn that Egypt and Syria had launched surprise attacks against Israel. Senior American officials awoke to find the Middle East at war.

### Sadat's Surprise

- The choice of Yom Kippur was designed to catch Israel when it was least prepared — the entire defense strategy depended on rapid mobilization, impossible on the day of meditation and prayer. Sadat had feinted at least twice before, causing costly Israeli mobilizations that bred skepticism and complacency. Assad had also joined in the deception. A Syrian source had provided the US with astonishingly exact intelligence, including Syria's order of battle, but it was lost among hundreds of other pieces of information. On October 3, Assad ordered preparation of large graveyards. Egyptian hospital beds were suddenly emptied. A young Israeli lieutenant submitted reports on October 1 and October 3 pointing to imminent war — they were ignored. The Israeli military was in thrall to "the conception" — a particular view that by definition precluded an Egyptian attack under current circumstances.
- A key Israeli source in Egypt sent an urgent signal in the first days of October — he was hurriedly extricated and debriefed in Europe. There could be no doubt about what he said. But inexplicably, there was a delay of a day in transmitting his warning to Tel Aviv. By then it was too late.
- The Americans made the same fundamental error — not thinking the way Sadat thought, not putting themselves in his shoes, and not taking him seriously. Until October 1973, Kissinger admitted, he thought of Sadat as "more actor than statesman." The surprise of the Arab attack was for the Israelis what Pearl Harbor had been for Americans thirty-two years earlier.
- When finally, nine and a half hours before the attack, the Israelis got confirmation, they were still hamstrung — they could not go first, could not take preemptive action. They thought the war would start four hours later than it actually did. In the initial days, the Israelis fell back, disordered, while the Egyptians and Syrians scored massive victories.

### "The Third Temple Is Going Under"

- America's initial objective was to arrange a truce with a pullback to prehostilities lines, then pursue a diplomatic solution. The US wanted to keep a low profile — to avoid supplying the Israelis against Soviet-supplied Arabs, believing Israel would quickly win. The best outcome, in the words of one senior official, was one in which "Israel won, but had its nose bloodied in the process," making it more amenable to negotiation.
- But Israel's second big miscalculation (after assuming there would be no war) was that it had enough supplies for three weeks of war, based on the 1967 experience. Now, immediately thrown on the defensive against two richly Soviet-equipped enemies, the Israelis were devouring materiel at an alarming pace. A distraught Moshe Dayan told Premier Golda Meir that "the Third Temple is going under," and Meir herself prepared a secret letter to Nixon warning Israel might be destroyed.
- On October 9, the US realized the Israelis were in deep trouble and desperately short of supplies. On October 10, the Soviet Union began a massive resupply to Syria and then Egypt, put airborne troops on alert, and encouraged other Arab states to join. The US started discussions about more unmarked El Al planes and pressed commercial carriers for charters. Kissinger wanted a low profile. But on Thursday, October 11, the Americans realized Israel could lose the war without resupply. The US could not allow an American ally to be defeated by Soviet arms.
- On Friday, October 12, two private letters went to Nixon. One from the four Aramco chairmen (Exxon, Mobil, Texaco, Standard of California) via John McCloy, warning that the OPEC 100 percent price increase demand was "unacceptable" but that increased US military support for Israel could produce a "snowballing effect" retaliation and a "major petroleum supply crisis." "The whole position of the United States in the Middle East is on the way to being seriously impaired." The second letter was from Golda Meir — her nation's survival hung in the balance. Israel might run out of critical munitions within days.
- Kissinger learned from Defense Secretary Schlesinger that all efforts to arrange commercial charters had failed — airlines feared an Arab embargo or terrorist attacks. "If you want supplies there, we are going to have to use U.S. airlift all the way. There's no alternative." Kissinger asked Schlesinger to have the Israelis promise that Air Force planes could land under cover of darkness and be gone by daybreak. Before daylight Saturday, October 13, the Military Airlift Command began moving supplies.
- But powerful crosswinds at Lajes airfield in the Azores (the C-5As needed a refueling stop) delayed the transports half a day. Instead of arriving Saturday night, they came lumbering out of the sky Sunday during the day, October 14, their immense white stars visible for all to see. The US, instead of keeping a low profile as honest broker, was now portrayed as an active ally of Israel.
- The Israelis stopped the Egyptian offensive and on October 15 launched successful counteroffensives. Meanwhile, in Vienna on October 14, OPEC announced the failure of negotiations with the companies. The Gulf OPEC countries scheduled a meeting in Kuwait City. Most delegates were stranded in Vienna due to war-cancelled flights until they discovered one Air India flight through Geneva with a stop at Kuwait City — many delegations rushed to the airport on October 15.

### The October 16 Price Decision and the Oil Weapon

- On October 16, Gulf state delegates (five Arabs and an Iranian) met in Kuwait City. They would not wait for the companies to reply. They raised the posted price of oil by 70 percent, to $5.11 a barrel, bringing it in line with the panicky spot market. The significance was twofold — the price increase itself, and the unilateral way it was imposed. The pretense of negotiating with companies was over. "This is a moment for which I have been waiting a long time," Yamani told a fellow delegate. "We are masters of our own commodity."
- The exporters argued consuming governments took 66 percent of retail oil prices in taxes, while they received only 9 percent. Iranian oil minister Amouzegar said the exporters were merely keying to market forces and would set prices unilaterally in the future. Piercy of Exxon learned about it from the newspapers.
- At the White House on October 17, Nixon told his senior advisers: "No one is more keenly aware of the stakes: oil and our strategic position." That same day in Kuwait City, the Arab oil ministers (the Iranian had left) gathered for an exclusively Arab conclave. Their subject: the oil weapon. "Now the atmosphere is more propitious than in 1967," declared the Kuwaiti oil minister.

### Embargo

- Despite Sadat's importuning, King Faisal was reluctant to take action against the US without more contact with Washington. He sent a letter to Nixon on October 16 warning that continued American support for Israel would make Saudi-American relations only "lukewarm."
- On October 17, Kissinger and Nixon received four Arab foreign ministers, led by Saudi Omar Saqqaf, whom Kissinger characterized as "gentle and wise." Discussions were cordial. Nixon pledged to "work within the framework of Resolution 242" to return Israel to 1967 borders. Saqqaf seemed to affirm Israel's right to exist within those borders. Nixon promised Kissinger as negotiator and assured Saqqaf that Kissinger, despite his Jewish origins, "was not subject to domestic, that is to say Jewish, pressures." Kissinger was writhing with embarrassment. Saqqaf deftly replied, "We are all Semites together." Saqqaf told reporters the talks had been "constructive and friendly."
- After the meetings, Kissinger told his staff he was surprised that there had been no mention of oil, and that it was unlikely the Arabs would use the oil weapon. But that was exactly what the Arab oil ministers in Kuwait City were contemplating. Early in 1973, Sadat had discussed the oil weapon in a "thinking aloud" speech. Experts from Egypt and other Arab countries had drawn up a plan for using oil, taking into account the growing US energy crisis.
- At the October 17 meeting, radical Iraq called on Arab states to nationalize all American business, withdraw all Arab funds from American banks, and institute a total oil embargo. The Algerian chairman dismissed this as impractical. Yamani, on instructions from his King, also resisted all-out economic warfare. The angry Iraqi delegates withdrew.
- Instead, the Arab ministers agreed to cut production 5 percent from September levels, with further 5 percent cuts each succeeding month until their objectives were met. Oil at previous levels would be maintained to "friendly states." A secret resolution recommended the US be "subjected to the most severe cuts" aiming at "the total halt of oil supplies to the United States from every individual country party to the resolution." Several countries immediately announced 10 percent rather than 5 percent cutbacks. The cutbacks were shrewder than a single-country export ban — they would reduce the absolute level of available supplies, maximize uncertainty, tension, and rivalry among importing countries, and split the industrial countries from the start.
- Events moved rapidly. On October 18, Nixon told his Cabinet that fighting might be prolonged and the Soviets had begun a massive resupply, so "we had to act to prevent the Soviets from tilting the military balance against Israel." On October 19, Nixon publicly proposed a $2.2 billion military aid package for Israel.
- The $2.2 billion aid package was designed so neither Egypt nor Israel ended up in a position of ascendancy, so both would have reason to negotiate. That same day (October 19), Libya announced it was embargoing all oil shipments to the United States.
- At two o'clock Saturday morning, October 20, Kissinger departed for Moscow to devise a cease-fire formula. On the plane, he learned stunning news: in retaliation for the Israeli aid proposal, Saudi Arabia had gone beyond rolling cutbacks — it would now cut off all shipments, every last barrel, to the United States. The other Arab states had done or were doing the same. The oil weapon was now fully in battle — "a weapon, in Kissinger's words, of political blackmail." The three-decade-old postwar petroleum order had died its final death.
- The embargo came as an almost complete surprise. "The possibility of an embargo didn't even enter my mind," said a senior Aramco executive. Despite two decades of discussion about the "oil weapon," the failed 1967 attempt, the threats during the Tehran negotiations, and the exceedingly tight market, neither the companies nor the US government had given serious thought to the prospect. What transformed the situation and galvanized the production cuts and embargo was the very public nature of the American resupply — the result of crosswinds at Lajes airfield in the Azores — and then the $2.2 billion aid package.
- Even the embargo was not the end of stunning events on October 20. In Moscow, Kissinger learned of the Saturday Night Massacre — Nixon had fired special prosecutor Archibald Cox. Attorney General Elliot Richardson and his chief deputy William Ruckelshaus resigned in protest. White House Chief of Staff Alexander Haig told Kissinger, "all hell has broken loose."

### The Third-Rate Burglary

- Throughout the war and the oil crisis, Nixon was preoccupied with Watergate. The unfolding of Watergate during the October War created a strange, surrealistic dimension. On October 9, the day Golda Meir signaled she wanted to fly to Washington to plead for aid, Nixon was working out the resignation of Vice-President Spiro Agnew. On October 12, the day officials realized Israel might lose the war, they were summoned to the White House for what Kissinger called an "eerie ceremony" in which Nixon introduced Gerald Ford as his new Vice-President.
- Effective control over American policy was lodged in the hands of Kissinger, who was now both Special Assistant for National Security and Secretary of State. His original base was Harvard's Center for International Affairs, housed in space borrowed from Harvard Semitic Museum, and his service to Nixon's great rival Nelson Rockefeller. Through the strange twists of Watergate, this former professor and Jewish refugee became the very embodiment of the legitimacy of the American government. His public personality expanded to fill the vacuum created by a discredited Presidency.
- Watergate had direct and major consequences for the Middle East and for oil. Sadat might never have gone to war had a strong President been able to open a dialogue between Egypt and Israel after the 1972 election. Watergate gave a lasting cast to the energy problems of the 1970s — the coincidence of the embargo and the Saturday Night Massacre fed conspiracy theories and obstructed rational responses. Some argued Kissinger had masterminded the crisis; some believed Nixon had deliberately encouraged the embargo to distract from Watergate. Illegal oil company campaign contributions, part of the loot extracted by the Committee to Reelect the President, flowed together in the public mind with the embargo, expanding the traditional distrust of the oil industry.

### Alert

- On October 21, the day after the Saturday Night Massacre, Yamani met with Aramco president Frank Jungers in Riyadh. Using computer data on exports and destinations, Yamani laid out the ground rules for the cutbacks and embargo. "Any deviations by the Aramco offtakers from the ground rules would be harshly dealt with." Yamani asked Jungers if he would be "surprised at the next move if this one didn't produce some results." "No," said Jungers. He guessed the subsequent move would be "complete nationalization of American interests if not a break in diplomatic relations." Yamani's final ominous comment: "The next step would not just be more of the same."
- In Moscow, Kissinger and the Russians completed a cease-fire plan, but implementation ran into serious snags — neither the Israelis nor Egyptians observed the cease-fire, and Egypt's Third Army faced capture or annihilation. Brezhnev sent a blunt, provocative letter to Nixon: the Soviet Union would not allow the Third Army to be destroyed. He demanded a joint American-Soviet force to separate the sides, or the Soviets would intervene unilaterally. "I shall say it straight," he menacingly wrote. Soviet airborne troops were on alert, ships in the Mediterranean were proceeding in a belligerent fashion, and neutron emissions from what might be nuclear weapons had been detected on a Soviet freighter passing through the Dardanelles.
- A half dozen senior national security officials were summoned to a late-night emergency meeting in the White House Situation Room. Nixon was not awakened — Haig told Kissinger the President was "too distraught." The officials concluded the risks had suddenly escalated; the US had to respond resolutely to Brezhnev's challenge. American forces were raised to DefCon 3, and in some cases even higher — the American military went on a worldwide nuclear alert in the early morning of October 25. The US and Soviet Union were squaring off directly, something that had not happened since the Cuban Missile Crisis. Miscalculation could lead to nuclear confrontation.
- But the following day, the fighting stopped, Egypt's Third Army was resupplied, and the cease-fire went into effect. The superpowers pulled back from their alerts. Two days later, Egyptian and Israeli military representatives met for direct talks for the first time in a quarter century. Both had been objectives of Sadat's gamble. The nuclear weapons were sheathed. But the Arabs continued to wield the oil weapon. The oil embargo remained in place, with consequences that would extend far beyond the October War.

## Chapter 30: "Bidding for Our Life"

- The embargo signaled a new era for world oil. Oil was now too important to be left to the oil men — it had become the province of presidents and premiers, of foreign and finance and energy ministers, of congressmen and parliamentarians. Kissinger, who by his own admission had known nothing about oil before 1973, would tell aides, "Don't talk to me about barrels of oil. They might as well be bottles of Coca Cola. I don't understand!" Yet once the oil weapon had been brought into play, this diplomatic acrobat would do more than anybody else to get the sword back into its scabbard.

### "The Loss"

- The "Arab oil embargo" had two elements: the rolling production cutbacks (5 percent initially, then additional 5 percent each month) affecting the entire market, and the total ban on exports to the US and the Netherlands (subsequently extended to Portugal, South Africa, and Rhodesia). In a bizarre twist, the embargo also extended to US military forces in the Eastern Hemisphere, including the Sixth Fleet — whose responsibilities included protecting some of the states imposing the embargo. Congress hurriedly made it a criminal act to discriminate against the Department of Defense.
- At the beginning of November, the Arab ministers increased the size of cuts. Available Arab oil in the first part of October had totaled 20.8 million bpd. In December, at the most severe point, it was 15.8 million bpd — a gross loss of 5 million bpd. America's spare capacity was gone. Other producers (led by Iran) increased output by 600,000 bpd. Iraq, whose proposal for total economic warfare had been rejected, sulked and actually increased production. Iraq's Saddam Hussein blasted Saudi Arabia and Kuwait as "reactionary ruling circles well known for their links with America." Net loss in December was 4.4 million bpd, or about 9 percent of the 50.8 million bpd "free world" total — amounting to 14 percent of internationally traded oil. With world consumption growing at 7.5 percent a year, the effects were amplified.
- Great uncertainty compounded the loss — contradictory information, massive disruption of supply channels, violent emotions. Would further cutbacks be imposed each month? Would new countries be embargoed? Would countries be shifted from the "neutral" to the "preferential" list? There was one other very large uncertainty: with per-barrel prices skyrocketing, the exporters could cut back on volumes and still increase their total income. They could sell less and earn more. They might decide to make the cuts permanent, meaning a chronic shortage, permanent fear, and even higher prices.

### Panic at the Pump

- The ingredients for panic prices: war, cutbacks, embargoes, shortages, desperate consumers, the specter of further cutbacks, and the possibility the Arabs would never restore production. Fear and uncertainty had a self-fulfilling effect — both companies and consumers frantically sought additional supplies not just for current use but for storage against future shortages. "We weren't bidding just for oil. We were bidding for our life."
- The posted price for Iranian oil per the October 16 agreement was $5.40 a barrel. In November, Nigerian oil sold for over $16. In mid-December, Iran held a major auction — bids came in over $17 a barrel, 600 percent greater than the pre-October 16 price. A Japanese trading company, inexperienced in buying oil and under pressure to assure Japan's supply, competing with eighty other companies, bid $22.60 a barrel (though the deal was never consummated). There were reports of even higher bids.
- E. F. Schumacher's pessimistic Limits to Growth outlook seemed vindicated. His timely publication of Small is Beautiful in 1973 made him a spokesman for those who challenged "the bigger is better" philosophy. "The party is over," he declared. "Whose party was it anyway?"
- The age of shortage was at hand. The prospect was gloomy: lost economic growth, recession, inflation, possible dislocation of the international monetary system. The US, the world's foremost superpower, had been thrown on the defensive by a handful of small nations. Western Europeans and Japanese were transported back to the bitter postwar years of deprivation and shortages.
- In Japan, the oil shortage was accompanied by a toilet paper shortage — housewives rushed out to hoard laundry detergent and toilet paper (in some cases, a two-year supply). The price of toilet paper would have quadrupled without government controls. Taxicab drivers staged angry demonstrations.
- In the United States, the shortfall struck at fundamental beliefs in the endless abundance of resources. A large part of the public did not even know, until October 1973, that the US imported any oil at all. Gasoline prices climbed by 40 percent. The shortage became vivid with the emergence of "gas lines." The allocation system, introduced just before the embargo, now perversely assured that gasoline could not be shifted from well-supplied areas to where it was needed. Motorists who had been content to drive until the gauge was on "empty" now hastened to "top off" their tanks with even a one-dollar purchase, lengthening lines further. Stations sprouted "Sorry, No Gas Today" signs. The experience would severely undermine Americans' confidence in the future.

### "Beef Prices"

- On November 7, 1973, Nixon made a major Presidential address on energy to an alarmed nation. He called for Project Independence — "Let us set as our national goal, in the spirit of Apollo, with the determination of the Manhattan Project, that by the end of this decade we will have developed the potential to meet our own energy needs without depending on any foreign energy source." His staff had told him the goal was impossible and silly to proclaim. Nixon overruled them — energy was now both a crisis and high politics.
- Nixon fired energy czar John Love and replaced him with Deputy Treasury Secretary William Simon. Nixon likened Simon's new post to Albert Speer's position as armaments overlord in the Third Reich — Simon was "somewhat discomfited by the comparison." The new energy czar found himself in never-ending Congressional hearings. Once, rushing to a hearing, Simon was walking backward to finish a conversation and cracked his head on his car, cutting his scalp. Though he needed stitches, the committee chairman would not postpone; Simon sat through five hours of interrogation with blood oozing from the gash. His wife gave up using any charge cards that carried her husband's name.
- Nixon resisted calls for gasoline rationing. He ordered rationing stamps printed and held in reserve. "Maybe that will shut them up." Aide Roy Ash counseled caution: "In a few months, I suspect, we will look back on the energy crisis somewhat like we now view beef prices — a continuing and routine governmental problem — but not a Presidential crisis." Nixon agreed: "absolutely right" and "makes great sense." But to the public, what had happened to gasoline prices far transcended beef. Their birthright as Americans seemed in jeopardy.
- Public opinion analyst Daniel Yankelovich warned of "incipient signs of panic" — people were "growing fearful that the country has run out of energy." Public confidence that "things are going well in the country" plummeted from 62 percent in May 1973 to 27 percent in late November 1973. "Because of Watergate, there was a general sense of paralysis," recalled Steven Bosworth of the State Department's Office of Fuels and Energy. "Congress was mesmerized by Watergate, the executive branch was mired in it, and the White House was circling the wagons."

### "Equal Misery"

- How was the shortfall to be allocated? The companies adopted a policy of "equal suffering" and "equal misery" — the same percentage of cutbacks from total supplies to all countries, by moving both Arab and non-Arab oil around the world. Non-Arab oil was diverted to embargoed or neutral countries; Arab oil went to the preferred lists. The five American majors ended up diverting about a third of their oil. Adjusting for different energy and oil growth rates, the loss over the embargo period was: Japan 17 percent, United States 18 percent, Western Europe 16 percent. The Federal Energy Administration later concluded that "it is difficult to imagine that any other allocation plan would have achieved a more equitable allocation of reduced supplies."
- The companies were caught in a vise: on one side, deadly serious pressure from Arab governments threatening to nationalize and throw them out; on the other, consuming governments demanding the oil their publics needed. "The only alternative was not to ship the oil at all," said Chevron's George Keller. "Obviously it was in the best interests of the United States to move 5, 6, 7 million barrels per day to our friends around the world rather than to have that cut off."
- Washington gave little direct guidance. Kissinger asked the companies "to take care of Holland," which had been particularly targeted by the Arabs. Japan felt a special vulnerability — little indigenous energy, highly dependent on American majors. A senior MITI bureaucrat warned the majors not to divert non-Arab oil from Japan to the US. The companies replied they would be happy to hand the whole thankless job over to governments. Japan backed off.
- The most turbulent reaction came from Britain. Though placed among "friendly nations" supposed to receive 100 percent of September supplies, Prime Minister Edward Heath summoned BP chairman Sir Eric Drake and Shell chairman Sir Frank McFadzean to Chequers and demanded special treatment — 100 percent of normal requirements. A confrontation between coal miners and Heath's government was brewing, threatening to cut coal supplies just as oil was short.
- Both chairmen pointed out they had been sucked into a vacuum from the failure of governments to plan. McFadzean noted Shell was 60 percent Dutch-owned, so even if he agreed with Heath, which he "most decidedly did not," it would not be possible to ride roughshod over Dutch interests. Drake, who had stood up to Mossadegh in Iran in 1951 and later to BP's autocratic chairman William Fraser, was certainly not going to give way to Edward Heath and allow him "to destroy my company." Having lived through one nationalization in Iran, Drake did not intend to be party to any more.
- Drake asked: "Are you asking me to do this as a shareholder or as a government? If you're telling me to do it as a government, then I must have it in writing. Then we can plead force majeure to the other governments." Heath lost his temper: "You know perfectly well that I can't put it in writing." "Then I won't do it," Drake replied with absolute firmness. After a few days of reflection, Heath gave up on his insistence for special treatment. The civil servants in Whitehall were more deft — they reminded companies that the British government decided who got North Sea exploration licenses, ensuring Britain received what they defined as "fair share" — and a bit more.

### A New World of Prices

- Amid the feverish spot-market bidding, OPEC oil ministers met in Tehran in late December 1973 to discuss the official price. The proposed range went from the OPEC Economic Commission's $23 a barrel down to Saudi Arabia's $8. Saudi Arabia feared a skyrocketing price could result in a depression. "If you went down," Yamani said of the industrial world, "we would go down." King Faisal also wanted to maintain the "political character" of the embargo — he did not want it to look like a money grab.
- Iran was by far the most aggressive. The Shah argued for $11.65 as the new posted price (meaning a $7 government take), based on what he called "a new concept" — the cost of alternative energy sources (liquids and gas from coal and shale oil). He cited an Arthur D. Little study. But Shell's chief Middle East adviser dismissed it: "The alternative source existed, in the volume required, only in economic theory, not in reality."
- After much hard discussion, the Shah's position was accepted. The new posted price would be $11.65. The price had been raised from $1.80 in 1970 to $2.18 in 1971 to $2.90 in mid-1973 to $5.12 in October 1973 — and now to $11.65. The two price increases since the war began constituted a fourfold increase. The Shah graciously said it was a price fixed out of "kindness and generosity."
- Nixon wrote a strong private letter asking that the price be reconsidered — "This drastic price increase is particularly unreasonable coming as it does when oil supplies are artificially restrained." The Shah's reply was brief and totally ungiving: "We also know that for us this source of wealth might be finished in thirty years."
- The Shah had found a new role as moralist for world oil: "Really oil is almost a noble product. We were almost careless to use oil for heating houses or even generating electricity. Why finish this noble product in, say, 30 years' time when thousands of billions of tons of coals remain in the ground." He lectured the industrial nations: "The era of their terrific progress and even more terrific income and wealth based on cheap oil is finished. They will have to find new sources of energy. Eventually they will have to tighten their belts." His haughty stance would cost him dearly only a few years later, when he desperately needed friends.

### Alliance Strained

- The embargo was a political act that took advantage of economic circumstances, and it had to be met with political action on three interrelated fronts: between Israel and its Arab neighbors; between America and its allies; and between the industrial countries and the Arab oil exporters.
- On the first front, Kissinger made himself the center of a vortex. "Shuttle diplomacy" became the hallmark of his tireless, dramatic, and virtuoso performance. Landmarks included an Egyptian-Israeli disengagement agreement in mid-January 1974, and a Syrian-Israeli disengagement at the end of May. Throughout, Kissinger had a particular partner in Sadat, who knew his own objectives. "The United States holds 99 percent of the cards in this game," Sadat publicly declared (though he privately admitted it was really only 60 percent — "but it sounds better if I say 99 percent"). Less than a month after the war, Sadat signaled to Kissinger that he was ready to begin a peace process.
- The oil embargo precipitated one of the gravest splits in the Western alliance since its foundation after World War II — the worst since Suez in 1956. Once the embargo began, the European allies, led by France, hastened to disassociate themselves from the United States and assume positions more agreeable to the Arabs.

### European Capitulation and the Netherlands Crisis

- The Arabs pressed Europeans to oppose the United States and support Arab Middle East policy. Yamani applied his personal touch: "We are extremely sorry for the inconvenience caused in Europe by the Arab oil cut," he said apologetically, while making clear exactly what was expected — distance from America and support for the Arab position.
- As Europeans shifted to "dialogue" and "cooperation" with Arab countries, senior American officials talked caustically about Europeans "being soft on OPEC and falling all over themselves in their haste to accommodate and appease." Europeans countered that the US was too confrontational. French President Georges Pompidou told Kissinger bluntly: "You only rely on the Arabs for about a tenth of your consumption. We are entirely dependent upon them."
- The French had long resented being excluded from Middle Eastern oil by "Anglo-Americans," especially since the postwar Red Line agreement renunciation. Suez in 1956 — when America undercut France and Britain — remained a deep wound. Prime Minister Edward Heath privately told the Americans, "I don't want to raise the issue of Suez but it's there for many people." Heath had been Anthony Eden's loyal whip during Suez.
- In mid-November 1973, the European Community passed a resolution supportive of the Arab position — described by one Arab official as "a kiss blown from afar — which is all very nice but we would prefer something warmer and closer." The resolution won Europeans a suspension of the December 5 percent cut. But the Arab oil ministers warned cuts would be imposed if Europeans did not keep "putting pressure on the United States or Israel."
- The Netherlands posed an awkward problem for the European Community. While most EC members were on the "friendly" list, the Netherlands was still embargoed. Other members were inclined to embargo transshipments to the Netherlands — violating a core EC principle of free commodity flow. The Netherlands reminded them, forcefully, that it was the major source of natural gas for Europe, including 40 percent of France's total supply and most of the gas used for heating and cooking in Paris. A quiet compromise emerged: an unspecified "common position" and expedited non-Arab supplies from international companies.

### Japan's Capitulation

- Japan was distressed to find itself on the "unfriendly" list. Forty-four percent of Japan's oil came from Arab Gulf states, and of all industrial nations it was the most dependent on oil — 77 percent of total energy versus 46 percent for the United States. Yamani spelled out Arab policy to Japan: "If you are hostile to us you get no oil. If you are neutral you get oil but not as much as before. If you are friendly you get the same as before."
- Before the embargo, a "resource faction" in Japanese government and business circles had pushed for reorientation toward the Middle East with little progress. Vice-Minister for Foreign Affairs Fumihiko Togo recalled: "before 1973, we could always buy the oil if only we had the money." After the crisis, the faction intensified efforts. On November 14, the same day Kissinger was in Tokyo trying to keep Japan allied, worried business leaders met privately with Prime Minister Kakuei Tanaka to make a "direct request" for a major policy change.
- Days later, Arab exporters exempted European countries that had issued pro-Arab statements — tangible proof that policy changes were rewarded. Japanese emissaries sent on secret trips to the Middle East reported that Arabs regarded "neutrality" as insufficient, treating it as opposition. On November 22, Tokyo issued its own statement endorsing the Arab position.
- This represented Japan's first major split on foreign policy with the United States in the postwar era — a profound step since the U.S.-Japan alliance was the basis of Japanese foreign policy. Four days after the statement, Japan was exempted from December cutbacks. Tokyo sent a host of high-level representatives to the Middle East with economic assistance, loans, new projects, joint ventures, bilateral deals. The majors, through their allocations and cutbacks, had effectively told Japan they could not supply it as before — so Japan pursued its own security of supply. However, Japan refused Arab demands to break all diplomatic and economic relations with Israel. Those who pushed for such a break, argued Vice-Foreign Minister Togo, were suffering from "oil on the brain."

### The Scramble for Bilateral Deals

- Even as allies gave way to Arab demands, the US tried to promote a coordinated response among industrial countries. Washington feared bilateral state-to-state deals would create a rigid, permanently politicized oil market. But the rush was already on: "Bilateral Deals: Everybody's Doing It" headlined the Middle East Economic Survey in January 1974.
- The oil industry viewed with skepticism politicians scrambling for national supplies. Frank McFadzean of Shell marveled at "the spectacle of two senior Cabinet Ministers flying off with the melodrama normally associated with the relief of a beleaguered fortress, to sign a barter agreement involving a quantity of crude oil equal to less than four weeks of the country's requirements."
- Delegations and emissaries "descended on the Middle East like a latter day plague of near Biblical proportions" — most with little knowledge of the oil business. Kissinger feared these bilateral deals would undermine his Arab-Israeli peace negotiations. Competitive bidding based on panic, lack of information, mercantilism, sauve quipeut ("every man for himself") — all would make everyone worse off.

### The Washington Energy Conference and the Birth of the IEA

- The United States called for an energy conference in Washington in February 1974 to heal rifts, assuage fears about supply competition, and prevent oil from becoming a permanent source of Western division.
- Britain, despite being on the "friendly" list, found it bought them little — they still faced the same price increases. The oil shortage was magnified by a coal miners' confrontation with Heath's government that turned into a full-scale economic war. Electricity was interrupted, industry went on a three-day workweek, domestic hot water was in short supply, and BBC clergymen debated whether families should share bath water for the national weal. In what turned out to be Heath's last weeks in government, Britain was a supportive participant in the Washington Conference.
- Japan also supported a coordinated response, keen to mute what they saw as America's "tendency to become very confrontational." Germany wanted multilateral talks. France remained unreconstructed. Foreign Minister Michel Jobert opened a caucus of European Community participants in Washington with the bitter salutation: "Bonjour les traitres" ("Hello traitors").
- American officials hinted that the overall security relationship, including US troops in Europe, might be jeopardized by energy discord. Most conference participants agreed on developing consensus and common policies. The conference led to establishment of an emergency sharing program for the "next" crisis and the creation of the International Energy Agency (IEA) — charged with managing the program and harmonizing Western energy policies. Before 1974 was out, the IEA was set up in a leafy section of Paris's sixteenth arrondissement, in an annex to the OECD. One major industrial country refused to join — France. Foreign Minister Jobert called the IEA a "machine de guerre," an instrument of war.

### Sheathing the Oil Weapon

- When and how would the embargo end? No one knew — not even the Arabs. In late December 1973, Arab producers relaxed the embargo slightly as progress was made on the Arab-Israeli dispute. Kissinger called the embargo, by early January, "increasingly less appropriate."
- Kissinger visited King Faisal twice in Saudi Arabia. Walking through a tremendous hall where distinguished men in black robes and white headdresses sat against the walls, Kissinger — a Jewish refugee from Nazi persecution — "found himself reflecting in some wonder what strange twists of fate had caused a refugee from Nazi persecution to wind up in Arabia as the representative of American democracy."
- Faisal spoke in a gentle voice with elliptical comments "capable of many interpretations." He sat at Kissinger's right, looking straight ahead, "occasionally peeking from around his headdress to make sure I had understood the drift of some particular conundrum." Whether discussing the conspiracy of Jews and communists or the practical politics of ending the embargo, the King was apologetic but firm — the oil weapon was a joint Arab decision and its withdrawal must also be joint. His fundamental condition: Jerusalem must become an Arab Islamic city. When asked about the Wailing Wall, Faisal replied another wall could be built somewhere else "against which the Jews could wail."
- Washington turned to Sadat, who had become the chief advocate of ending the embargo he had championed. Like the war itself, Sadat said, the embargo had served its purpose and should end. Continuation could damage relations with Saudi Arabia and Kuwait, and a superpower could not afford to remain "under the gun" indefinitely.
- But Arab exporters were in no hurry to give up their trump card or appear to capitulate to American pressure. As the embargo dragged on, oil seeped back into the market and the cutoffs became less effective. The Saudis indicated the embargo could not end without movement on the Syrian front and tacit acquiescence of Syria's Hafez al-Assad, who held an effective veto because of his fury at Egyptian diplomatic progress.
- In mid-February 1974, Faisal met in Algiers with Sadat, Assad, and the president of Algeria. Sadat made clear the embargo had outlived its usefulness and might work against Arab interests. Faisal agreed to end it provided there was "constructive effort" toward Syrian-Israeli disengagement. Assad maintained a hard line for weeks, but ultimately could not resist the warning that the US peace effort required lifting the embargo. On March 18, 1974, the Arab oil ministers agreed to end the embargo. Syria and Libya dissented.

### Aftermath: The Oil Weapon's Impact

- After two decades of talk and failed attempts, the oil weapon had been used successfully — with impact "not merely convincing, but overwhelming, and far greater than even its proponents might have dared to expect." It recast alignments and geopolitics of the Middle East and the entire world, transformed relations between producers and consumers, and remade the international economy. The threat would remain.
- In May, Kissinger secured Syrian-Israeli disengagement. In June, Nixon visited Israel, Egypt, Syria, and Saudi Arabia. The embargo was history for the US (though still in place against the Netherlands).
- Nixon's behavior on the trip was bizarre. Sitting with the Israeli Cabinet in Tel Aviv, he announced he knew the best way to deal with terrorists, sprang to his feet, and with an imaginary submachine gun pretended to gun down the entire Cabinet, Chicago-style, making a "brrrrr" sound. The Israelis were "perplexed and a little concerned." In Damascus, he told Assad that the Israelis should be pushed back until they fell off the cliff, making strange chopping gestures. Assad would insist on recalling this exposition in subsequent meetings with Americans.
- Nixon's great moment was Egypt, where millions of enthusiastic Egyptians turned out to cheer — his last hurrah. The irony: the land of Nasser, master of anti-American crowds, now cheered the American president, while hostile crowds awaited Nixon at home. For Egyptians it was a celebration of Sadat's restoration of national prestige. For Nixon, it could have been a celebration of the embargo's end and his diplomacy, but he was in poor health, his leg swollen with phlebitis, spending free time listening to incriminating Oval Office tapes that would compel his resignation.

## Chapter 31: OPEC's Imperium

### The OPEC Building

- Times change, empires rise and fall. The modern office building on Vienna's Karl Lueger Ring, with a small bookstore on the ground floor, had customarily been called "the Texaco Building" in honor of its lead tenant. But by the mid-1970s it had abruptly become known as "the OPEC Building." The change symbolized a profound global upheaval: the suddenness with which oil-exporting countries had assumed the position formerly held by the international companies.
- OPEC ended up in Vienna only by accident. Originally established in Geneva, the Swiss doubted its significance and refused diplomatic status. The Austrians, eager for international prestige, were more accommodating — so in 1965 OPEC moved to Vienna. Its lodgment in the Texaco Building showed how little account had been taken of this "rather mysterious and peculiar organization" which, despite initial clamor, had fallen well short of its central political objective — "the assertion of 'sovereignty' by the oil exporters over their resources."
- Now, in the mid-1970s, all had changed. The international order was upside down. OPEC's members were courted, flattered, railed against, and denounced. Oil prices were at the heart of world commerce, and those who controlled them were regarded as the new masters of the global economy. OPEC's membership was virtually synonymous with all the world's petroleum exporters, excepting the Soviet Union. OPEC's members would determine if there was inflation or recession, would be the world's new bankers, and would seek a new international economic order — a wholesale redistribution of economic and political power.
- A former Secretary General of OPEC would look back on 1974-1978 as "OPEC's Golden Age." Yet the OPEC countries completed the acquisition of control over their own resources during these years, settling once and for all who owned their oil. But those years were dominated by a bitter battle not only with consumers but also within OPEC about the price — and that one question would dominate the economic policies and international politics of the entire decade.

### Oil and the World Economy

- The quadrupling of prices brought massive changes to every corner of the world economy. Combined petroleum earnings of oil exporters rose from $23 billion in 1972 to $140 billion by 1977. Exporters built up huge financial surpluses, and the fear that unspent tens of billions sitting idle in bank accounts could spell serious contraction created grave concern for the world's bankers.
- They need not have worried. The exporters, suddenly wealthy, embarked on a dizzying spending program: industrialization, infrastructure, subsidies, services, necessities, luxuries, weapons, waste, and corruption. Ports were clogged far beyond capacity, ships waited weeks to unload, vendors scrambled for rooms in overbooked hotels and elbowed into government ministry waiting rooms. Everything was for sale, and the oil producers now had the money to buy.
- Arms transactions became huge business. To Western industrial nations, securing access to oil became a strategic concern of the first order. Weapons sales were a way to enhance that security and gain influence. The 1973 events had demonstrated the region's volatility — deep rivalries, large ambitions, and the two superpowers had squared off in a nuclear alert over the Middle East.
- The proliferation of Datsun pickup trucks in Saudi Arabia was a sign of the times. "It is very expensive to maintain camels," said a Nissan executive, "it is cheaper to keep a Datsun." A Datsun cost $3,100 while a camel cost only $760, but at twelve cents a gallon for gasoline it was much cheaper to fuel a Datsun than feed a camel. Nissan became the number one vehicle purveyor to Saudi Arabia, and the Datsun pickup was the favorite among sheep-tending Bedouins whose fathers and grandfathers had been the camel-riding backbone of Ibn Saud's armies.
- The massive spending, combined with galloping inflation, ensured that OPEC's financial surpluses soon disappeared. In 1974, OPEC had a $67 billion surplus on balance of payments. By 1978, the surplus had turned into a $2 billion deficit.

### The "OPEC Tax" and Global Recession

- For the industrial West, oil rents flooding into exporter treasuries amounted to a huge withdrawal of purchasing power — the "OPEC tax." This tax sent industrial countries into deep recession. US gross national product plunged 6 percent between 1973 and 1975, while unemployment doubled to 9 percent. Japan's GNP declined in 1974 for the first time since World War II. Sobered Japanese students in Tokyo stopped chanting "Goddamn GNP" at demonstrations and instead found new virtue in hard work and the promise of lifetime employment.
- The price increases delivered a powerful inflationary shock to economies where inflation had already taken hold. While economic growth resumed in 1976, inflation became so embedded in the fabric of the West that it came to be seen as the intractable problem of the modern age.
- The group that suffered most were non-oil developing countries. The price shock was the most devastating blow to economic development in the 1970s — not only the same recessionary and inflationary shocks, but crippled balance of payments that constrained or prevented growth altogether. Many coped by borrowing, and OPEC surplus dollars were "recycled" through the banking system to them. A new category — the "fourth world" — was invented to cover the lower tier of developing countries knocked flat on their backs.
- The oil exporters' new wealth put them in an awkward position vis-a-vis the developing world, since they too proclaimed themselves the vanguard of the "South" fighting Northern "exploitation." Other developing countries initially cheered OPEC, but OPEC's new prices were a huge setback for them. The exporters' main response was to champion a broad "North-South dialogue" tying oil prices to other development issues, with the stated aim of global wealth redistribution.
- The Conference on International Economic Cooperation, meant to embody the North-South dialogue, convened in Paris in 1977. France promoted it as an alternative to American policies. Though it proceeded for two years absorbing much effort, there was little to show — participants could not even agree on a communique. What mattered for the developing world was not the high-minded rhetoric but the reality of depressed markets in the industrial world for their commodities.

### The Saudis Versus the Shah

- OPEC became an international spectacle of the first order in the mid-1970s. Oil talk — "differentials," "seasonal swings," "inventory build" — entered the parlance of policymakers, journalists, and financial speculators. Though usually described as a "cartel," OPEC was not one, properly speaking. Howard Page of Exxon observed in 1975: "You can call OPEC a club or an association but not, properly speaking, a cartel." He cited the Funk & Wagnall's definition — "a combination of producers to regulate the prices and the output of a commodity." OPEC set price but not output. There were no quotas or assigned production levels. The market was really dominated by "a somewhat unruly oligopoly." Most exporters produced at capacity; only Saudi Arabia set production to achieve price objectives.
- OPEC exporters responded to price criticism by noting that Western governments were taking more in tax per barrel than OPEC received in its sales price. In Western Europe in 1975, about 45 percent of what consumers paid went to their government, 35 percent to OPEC, 20 percent to shipping/refining/margins. In the US, the tax component was only 18 percent while OPEC's share was about 50 percent. In Japan, government took 28 percent, OPEC 45 percent. Consumer governments retorted that domestic taxes and the "OPEC tax" were strikingly different in macroeconomic consequences.
- The central concern from 1974 to 1978: would the price of oil go up or stay steady and be eroded by inflation? This was the focus of the running battle between Saudi Arabia and Iran — the two largest producers. In the 1960s they had competed over who would produce more; now they struggled over price and primacy.
- For the Shah, the December 1973 price increase was a great personal victory. He saw it as his moment and opportunity — seemingly endless revenues, as if by divine intervention, to fulfill his ambitions to create "Iran's Great Civilization." "One of the only things my husband likes in life," said the Empress, "is flying, driving, driving boats — speed!" The Shah applied his passion for speed to hurtle Iran into the twenty-first century, ignoring the agitation, disorientation, resentment, and unhappiness among those who did not share his obsession with modernity. Iran would become the world's fifth largest industrial power, a second Japan. "Iran will be one of the serious countries in the world," he boasted. "Everything you can dream of can be achieved here."
- Cut off from reality by oil money, the Shah became consumed by ambitions and dreams and began to believe all the imperial trappings. Who would dare disagree, counsel caution, or deliver bad news? He dismissed Western inflation as justifying still higher prices and discounted the notion that higher oil prices stoked inflation. "The day has passed when the big industrial countries can get away with political and economic pressure tactics," he told the US ambassador. Iran's more limited reserves, compared to Saudi Arabia's, argued for higher prices sooner rather than later. And there was the Shah's pride: "There are some people who thought — and perhaps some who still think — that I am a toy in the Americans' hands. Why would I accept to be a toy? There are reasons for our power which will make us stronger, so why would we be content to be someone else's catspaw?"

### Saudi Opposition to Price Increases

- The Saudis had never approved of the December 1973 price increase — too large, too dangerous to their own position. They feared the economic consequences and were alarmed at losing control over OPEC and the basic decisions about oil so central to the kingdom's future. Owing to their vast reserves, the Saudis had a decisive stake in long-term oil markets. Higher prices could trigger conservation and a shift to alternative fuels that would change and contract the long-term market, diminishing the value of their reserves.
- Saudi Arabia was large in territory but small in population — not much bigger than Hong Kong. Rapid oil revenue buildup could create social and political tensions, weakening ties that held the kingdom together. Nor did the Saudis want higher prices to interfere with Arab-Israeli conflict objectives. They worried about political stability in the industrial and developing world — economic difficulties in mid-1970s Europe seemed to be opening the door to communists, particularly in Italy, deeply unsettling to a Saudi government fearful of Soviet designs.
- The Saudis were also alarmed about Iran. The Shah was too short-sighted, too fired by ambition. Higher prices would give Iran more money and power, enabling more weapons purchases, shifting the strategic balance and encouraging the Shah to claim hegemony over the Gulf. In August 1975, the US ambassador reported from Riyadh that Yamani had said "the talk of eternal friendship between Iran and the United States was nauseating to him and other Saudis. They knew the Shah was a megalomaniac, that he was highly unstable mentally, and that if we didn't recognize this there must be something wrong with our powers of observation." Yamani sounded a warning: "If the Shah departs from the stage, we could also have a violent, anti-American regime in Teheran."
- The Saudis purposefully and forcefully pursued their line against further price increases at meeting after meeting. Their firmness at one point forced OPEC to accept two different prices — a lower one for Saudi Arabia and the UAE, and a higher one for the eleven other members. When others sought justification for price raises, the Saudis would push up production to weaken the market. But in doing so, they made a disconcerting discovery: their sustainable production capacity was not as high as assumed.

### Yamani: The Symbol of the New Age of Oil

- In all these Saudi maneuvers, the spotlight fell on one man — Ahmed Zaki Yamani. To the global oil industry, politicians, journalists, and the world at large, Yamani became the representative and symbol of the new age of oil. His visage, with large, limpid, seemingly unblinking brown eyes and clipped Van Dyke beard, became familiar the planet over. But the world sometimes confused his role and ascribed greater power than he had. He was the representative of Saudi Arabia, albeit enormously important — he could not dictate or solely determine policy, but he could shape it. His longevity augmented his power — he ended up being "there" longer than anyone else.
- Yamani was widely known as "Sheikh," though the title was an honorific assumed by prominent commoners. By background he was a Hijazi, an urban man from the worldly Red Sea coast, rather than a Nejdi from the desert principalities that had provided the original base of Ibn Saud's support. Born in Mecca in 1930, the year St. John Philby convinced King Ibn Saud to allow oil prospecting, Yamani grew up when camels still thronged the streets. He read at night either by oil lamp — "electreeks" — or at the mosque, where electricity had been hooked up.
- His grandfather and father were religious teachers and Islamic lawyers; his father had been grand mufti in the Dutch East Indies and Malaya. This combination of learning and piety shaped Yamani's outlook. After his father's return, the family house in Mecca became a meeting place for students: "Many of them were famous jurists and they would discuss the law with my father and argue cases. I started to join them and often after they left, my father and I would stay up for hours and he would teach me and he would criticize my arguments."
- Yamani studied at university in Cairo, then New York University Law School, followed by a year at Harvard Law School. He developed an intuitive grasp of the West and of how to communicate with Americans. Returning to Saudi Arabia, he set up the first law office in the country, advised government ministries, and wrote the contract for the 1957 concession with Arabian Oil, the Japanese consortium that broke in on the majors in the Middle East.
- Yamani's legal commentaries attracted the attention of Prince Faisal, second son of Ibn Saud, who invited Yamani to become his legal counselor. In 1962, when Faisal emerged triumphant from the power struggle with his brother Saud, one of his first acts was to fire the nationalist oil minister Abdullah Tariki. He appointed the thirty-two-year-old Yamani as successor, whose initial task was to end Tariki's confrontation with Aramco and begin with more subtlety and skill — driving toward the same objectives.
