"Give me the rantings and ravings of Tariki any day," an Aramco executive complained. "Yamani drives you to the wall with sweet reasonableness."
- By the 1973 embargo, Yamani had been oil minister for eleven years with considerable experience and superb negotiating talents. His voice was soft, forcing adversaries to strain to hear. He almost never lost his temper; the angrier he got, the more quiet he became. He went logically from point to point, dwelling on each long enough to draw out the essence. "It was all so simple and persuasive and so overwhelmingly obvious and irrefutable that only a maniac or a simpleton could disagree. It was a manner of presentation that was mesmerizingly irresistible to many, and absolutely infuriating to others."
- Yamani crafted his mystique: master of patience and of the unblinking stare, always fingering his worry beads, playing chess, pondering his opponent's position. He thought long-term: "In my public life, in my personal life, in everything I do I think long term. Once you start thinking short term, you are in trouble because short-term thinking is only a tactic for immediate benefit." He believed the Western world was afflicted by the curse of short-term thinking, "the inevitable result of democracy." He never gambled: "I can't bear gambling. Yes, I hate it. It rots the soul. I've never been a gambler. Never." In oil politics, "It's always a calculated risk. Oh, I calculate my risks well."
- Many thought him brilliant, a diplomat of high order. "He was a consummate strategist," said one who dealt with him for twenty-five years. "He never went directly to his goal, but he never lost sight of where he wanted to go." In the West he embodied the OPEC Imperium. Within OPEC and the Arab world, some hated him — resenting his prominence or regarding him as too close to the West. Iranian newspapers castigated him as a "stooge of capitalist circles, and a traitor not only to his own King and country but also to the Arab world and the Third World as a whole." The Iraqi oil minister declared Yamani was acting "in the service of imperialism and Zionism." To such rhetoric, the unflappable Yamani reacted with his enigmatic smile and unblinking stare.
- Kissinger commended Yamani in backhanded fashion: "I found him extraordinarily intelligent and well read; he could speak penetratingly on many subjects, including sociology and psychology. His watchful eyes and little Van Dyke beard made him look like a priggish young don playing at oil policy but not really meaning the apocalyptic message he was bringing... In his country at that time, barred by birth from the political leadership reserved for princes and by talent from an ordinary existence, he emerged in a position as essential as it was peripheral to the exercise of real political power within the Kingdom. He became the technician par excellence."
- Yamani was deeply devoted to King Faisal, who regarded him as a favored protege and rewarded him with extensive grants of real estate that skyrocketed during the oil boom, becoming the basis of his personal fortune. Yamani's close relationship with the King gave him carte blanche on oil policy, though always under Faisal's final control and within lines defined by the Royal Family, whose most prominent member on oil after the King was Prince Fahd.

### The Assassination of King Faisal

- In March 1975, Yamani accompanied the visiting Kuwaiti oil minister to an audience with King Faisal. A nephew of Faisal followed the party into the reception room, and as the Kuwaiti knelt before the King, the nephew stepped forward and fired several bullets into Faisal's head, killing him almost instantaneously. The murder was variously attributed to revenge (the nephew's brother had been killed a decade earlier leading a fundamentalist attack on a broadcasting station to protest television), the extreme left, mental derangement, and drugs (the nephew had been prosecuted for selling LSD in Colorado).
- In December 1975, the international terrorist "Carlos" — a fanatic Marxist from Venezuela — led five other terrorists in an attack on the OPEC ministerial meeting in Vienna's Karl Lueger Ring. Three people were killed in the first minutes. The terrorists took the oil ministers and aides hostage and embarked on a harrowing air journey — flying to Algiers, Tripoli, and back to Algiers — threatening to kill the ministers throughout. Two had been sentenced to death: Jamshid Amouzegar of Iran and Yamani, their number-one quarry. On the tense flights, Yamani spent the time playing with his worry beads and reciting Koran verses, convinced he would be a dead man. After forty-four hours, the ordeal ended in Algiers with the suspension of death sentences and release of everyone, including Yamani. Some believed a faction in one of the Arab governments had assisted the terrorists.
- After 1975, Yamani became obsessive about security. And after Faisal's assassination, he never had the same independence on oil. Faisal's successor was his half-brother Khalid, who had a heart condition and did not project himself as a strong leader. Fahd became Crown Prince and Deputy Prime Minister — the chief policymaker on oil and the person to whom Yamani now reported. To the outside world Yamani remained the number-one figure, but within Saudi Arabia it was the careful, cautious Prince Fahd who had the final say. Fahd made clear the opposition to higher prices was Saudi policy, not Yamani's alone. Hiking the price further would spell "economic disaster." In 1977, Fahd vigorously urged President Carter to put pressure on Iran and Venezuela to prevent further price increases.

### America's Strategy

- Whatever the internal OPEC rivalries, there was a general meeting of minds between Riyadh and Washington on oil prices. Through Nixon, Ford, and Carter administrations, the US consistently opposed higher prices. But Washington did not want to aggressively force prices down. "The only chance to bring oil prices down immediately would be massive political warfare against countries like Saudi Arabia and Iran to make them risk their political stability and maybe their security," Kissinger explained in 1975. There was even concern that exporters might suddenly slash prices, undermining expensive new developments like the North Sea, leading to IEA discussions about a "minimum safeguard price" to protect Western energy investment.
- Washington's central objective was stability — building stability into the price and letting inflation erode it. Before every OPEC meeting, the US dispatched emissaries worldwide, armed with statistics on inflation and energy use. At times the Saudis suspected the US was playing a trick, secretly colluding with the Shah to raise prices. Nixon, Ford, and Kissinger were reluctant to push too hard on the Shah, given strategic considerations.
- In an effort to cap prices, Washington flirted with the idea of going into the oil business in partnership with the Soviet Union. Kissinger pursued a "barrels-for-bushels" deal — Soviet oil for American wheat. A preliminary letter of understanding was signed in Moscow in October 1975. Senior Soviet officials came to Washington for intense negotiations. On a weekend break, Gulf Oil whisked the Russian delegation to Disney World. The head of the Soviet delegation explained that the Russians would love to sell their oil but the transaction had to be kept secret — they could not be seen as undercutting OPEC and Third World nationalism. There was also a pricing problem: Kissinger wanted American wheat valued at world prices but Russian oil at 12 percent below world prices. The Russians asked why the disparity — the Americans explained wheat was sold into an established market while Russian oil was entering a new one. Ultimately, the deal fell through. But the Soviet officials "did have a fabulous time at Disney World."
- The American commitment to stable prices put it on a collision course with Iran. When Ford criticized higher prices, the Shah shot back: "No one can dictate to us. No one can wave a finger at us, because we will wave a finger back." Iran was tied politically and economically to the US — government ministers, businessmen, and weapons merchants trouped to Tehran — yet the Shah continued to lecture the West on its weaknesses, and some in Washington questioned "exactly who was whose client."

### The "Blank Check" and the Shah's Shift

- At the beginning of the 1970s, Nixon and Kissinger had established a "blank check" policy — giving the Shah a free hand to buy any American weapons he wanted, even the most technologically advanced, so long as they were not nuclear. This was part of the "twin pillars strategy" for regional security after British withdrawal from the Gulf. Iran and Saudi Arabia were the two pillars, but Iran was clearly the "Big Pillar" — by the mid-1970s, Iran was responsible for fully half of total American arms sales abroad. The Defense Department was alarmed; Defense Secretary Schlesinger cautioned the Shah that Iran lacked the technical resources to assimilate so many complex weapons. "He fell in love with the F-15," said Schlesinger — though the Shah normally brushed aside such warnings, in this case he heeded and did not purchase the F-15.
- Treasury Secretary William Simon called the Shah "a nut." The Shah took strong exception; Simon apologized and explained he meant the Shah was "a nut on oil prices in the way one might say somebody was a nut about tennis or golf." The American ambassador was away from Tehran when this blew up; the Minister of Court replied: "Simon may be a good bond salesman, but he does not know a whole lot about oil." The Shah was reported to have said he knew English and understood "exactly what Mr. Simon meant."
- Despite the friction, a consensus held through Nixon and Ford administrations: Iran was an essential ally, and nothing should undercut the Shah's prestige. Nixon, Ford, and Kissinger had strategic and personal predilections for the Shah — he had not embargoed oil in 1973, and Iran could play a key geopolitical role. Kissinger told colleagues the Saudis were "pussy cats," but with the Shah he could talk geopolitics — Iran shared a border with the Soviet Union.
- The Shah worried about the new Carter Administration in 1977. Carter's policies on human rights and arms sales restrictions were directly threatening. But as Gary Sick of the NSC later wrote, "The United States had no visible strategic alternative to a close relationship with Iran."
- Relations were facilitated by the Shah's shift on oil prices. By the time Carter took office, the Shah was having second thoughts about pushing for higher prices. The freneticism, euphoria, and the oil boom itself were wrecking Iran's social fabric — chaos, waste, inflation, temptation, corruption, and deepening political and social tensions broadening opposition to the regime. Increasing numbers of subjects wanted no part of the Shah's Great Civilization.
- At the end of 1976, the Shah ruefully summed up: "We acquired money we could not spend." Money was not the remedy but the cause of many of Iran's ailments. Higher oil prices would not help. The Carter Administration launched a "price freeze offensive" as central US policy. After Secretary of State Cyrus Vance reassured the Shah about continued support in May 1977, the Iranian government surprised everyone — including its own officials — by calling for moderation on oil prices. The Shah privately told Treasury Secretary Blumenthal that Iran "does not want to be known as a price hawk." Had the number-one price hawk become a dove?
- In November 1977, the Shah came to Washington to meet Carter. At the very moment the Shah arrived at the White House, a battle erupted on the nearby Ellipse among anti- and pro-Shah demonstrators.

### The Shah-Carter Meeting and Price Stabilization

- Police broke up the demonstrators with tear gas. The fumes wafted over the South Lawn where Carter was greeting the Shah. Carter began blinking and rubbing his eyes; the Shah wiped away tears with a handkerchief. The footage was broadcast not only in America but in Iran, owing to new liberalization — giving Iranians a less-than-grand view of their monarch they had never before been permitted. That scene convinced some Iranians the US was about to disengage from the Shah. Why else, not understanding the American system, would Carter have "allowed" such demonstrations?
- In private meetings, Carter lobbied for both human rights and oil price stability. The Shah saw a trade: moderation on oil prices in exchange for continued arms flow and relief from human rights pressure. Carter stressed "the punishing impact of increased oil prices on the industrial economies." Contradicting what he had said since 1973, the Shah agreed to urge other OPEC countries to "give Western nations a break."
- Iran had now joined Saudi Arabia on the side of moderation. With those two countries representing 48 percent of OPEC production, they could dictate to the others and oil prices would be held in check. The battle between the Shah and the Saudis was ended — the Shah had been won over. Through the half-decade from 1974 to 1978, there were only two rather small OPEC-wide price increases: from $10.84 set at Tehran in December 1973 to $11.46 in 1975 and to $12.70 at the end of 1977.
- But inflation was increasing faster than anticipated, eroding the real price. By 1978, the oil price adjusted for inflation was about 10 percent below what it had been in 1974, immediately after the embargo. By restricting increases to two small ones, the real oil price had been lowered somewhat. Oil was not cheap, but neither had the price, as many feared, gone through the roof.

### Kuwait and "Our Friends": The End of Concessions

- If the exporters no longer needed to negotiate over price with anyone except each other, there were still the oil concessions — relics of the days when companies held sway and exporters were poor. The very existence of concessions was now said to be degrading. Mossadegh's nationalization in Iran (1951), Iraq's nationalization of IPC (1972) — while some concessions remained, the termination of the last great ones in Kuwait, Venezuela, and Saudi Arabia would mark the final demise of the concessional system that began with William Knox D'Arcy's bold commitment to Persia in 1901.
- The Kuwait Oil Company, established in 1934 by BP and Gulf, had been the arena for Major Frank Holmes and Ambassador Andrew Mellon's determination. Forty years later, in early 1974, Kuwait acquired 60 percent participation. In early March 1975, Kuwait announced it would take the last 40 percent from BP and Gulf with no special links maintained. "We will just say thank you very much and good bye," said oil minister Abdel Mattaleb Kazemi. "Oil is everything in Kuwait."
- James Lee of Gulf and John Sutcliffe of BP were summoned to Kuwait City. Sutcliffe pleaded: "There should be consideration for the old relationship." The Kuwaiti reply was emphatic: "No compensation was due." They were told firmly that Kuwait intended to take over 100 percent — a matter of sovereignty, not open to debate. The profit split had shifted "from the 50/50 split concept in the early 1960s to the present split of about 98 percent for the Government and 2 percent for the companies."
- BP negotiator P. I. Walters half-jokingly suggested the Kuwaitis invest their new oil wealth in BP shares rather than acquiring the physical assets. The Kuwaitis were not interested — at least not at that time. In December 1975, the two sides came to agreement on Kuwait's terms. Gulf and BP had asked $2 billion in compensation. The Kuwaitis laughed. The companies got $50 million.
- Herbert Goodman of Gulf Oil Trading Company was dispatched to put finishing touches on the new relationship. Goodman was one of the most experienced oil traders in the world — a former Foreign Service officer who had sold over a billion barrels in Japan and Korea in the 1960s. He recalled the glory days: "There was a respect for your credibility, clout, power. Why? It was trade following the flag — the enormous credibility and respect enjoyed by the United States. The American passport was truly a laissez-passer — a safeguard. Then that began to fade. I could feel it everywhere. It was the ebbing of American power — the Romans retreating from Hadrian's Wall."
- Goodman expected Gulf would get some preference reflecting a half-century relationship. But no — Gulf would be treated like any other customer, and would only get oil for its own refineries, not for third-party customers in Japan and Korea. "But those were the markets that Gulf had sweated blood to develop," Goodman protested. The Kuwaitis replied: "It was his blood that had been sweated. Those were their own markets, based upon their oil, and they would sell their oil in those markets."
- The Gulf men noticed how differently they were treated: "We would go from our hotel to the ministry, day after day, and wait. Sometimes, a junior person would come. Sometimes not." When Goodman tried to remind a Kuwaiti official of all Gulf had done for Kuwait, the Kuwaiti became very angry: "Whatever you did, you got paid for. You never did us any favors." Then he walked out. Gulf got a very small discount on oil for its own system, none on oil sold to others. "For the Kuwaitis, it was the overthrow of the colonial power," Goodman reflected. "There was this misunderstanding. Here was the conceit of the Americans that we were loved because we had done so much for these people. This was the American naivete... They saw it from a different point of view. They had always felt patronized. They remembered it."

### Venezuela: The Kitty Cat Died

- Venezuela had passed a "law of reversion" in 1971 stipulating all oil company concessions and assets would revert to Venezuela when concession terms ended, with restricted compensation. The first concessions would start expiring in 1983. Companies slowed investment, causing production to decline. "It was chicken and egg," recalled Robert Dolph of Creole (Exxon's subsidiary). "The policy was that there were no new areas to explore. So we were not feeding the kitty cat, and then they were complaining that the kitty cat is dying."
- By 1972, the government had effective administrative control over every phase of the industry and a 96 percent effective tax rate — nationalization's objectives without nationalization. The 1973 price increase strengthened nationalism and self-confidence, making 1983 too long to wait. Virtually all political factions agreed.
- Two sets of negotiations ensued: one with the international companies (Exxon, Shell, Gulf, and others), and one among Venezuelans themselves. The internal debate was fiercer: "The field had clearly split between those who advocated a violent confrontation with the foreign oil companies and those who preferred a nonviolent, negotiated settlement." Juan Pablo Perez Alfonzo, OPEC's cofounder, weighed in from his garden demanding immediate nationalization of not only oil but all foreign investment.
- The settlement proceeded with less rancor than expected, partly because of the companies' realism. Venezuela had been the source of a substantial part of their profits — at one point half of Exxon's entire global income. "We couldn't win," said Dolph of Creole. "Prices were strong; circumstances in the market were emboldening all the countries." Companies negotiated service contracts (fourteen to fifteen cents a barrel) and long-term supply deals. The first year after nationalization, Exxon signed with Venezuela what was considered the largest single oil supply contract ever — 900,000 barrels per day.
- The harder negotiation was between Venezuelan politicians and Venezuelan oil men. The key question: Would the nationalized industry be a political entity with its agenda set by politicians, or a business run by oil men with a longer time horizon? There was widespread fear of creating "another Pemex" — a bloated, politicized state-within-the-state. President Carlos Andres Perez opted for a "moderate" and pragmatic solution: Petroleos de Venezuela (PDVSA) was established as a state holding company — a buffer between politicians and oil men. Operating companies based on pre-nationalization organizations were created and eventually consolidated to three, each fully integrated down to its own gasoline stations. This quasi-competition would assure efficiency and prevent the growth of another bureaucratic state company. On the first day of 1976, the nationalization took effect. President Perez called it "an act of faith."

### Saudi Arabia: The Concession Surrendered

- What remained was the greatest concession of all — Aramco's in Saudi Arabia. From the bleak 1930s when King Ibn Saud wanted water more than oil, Aramco had grown into a vast enterprise. In June 1974, Saudi Arabia took 60 percent participation. But by year's end, the Saudis told the four Aramco companies — Exxon, Mobil, Texaco, and Chevron — that 60 percent was not enough. They wanted 100 percent. Anything less was humiliating in the new era of oil nationalism.
- The companies dug in — their number-one dictum was "never to give up the concession." It was the most valuable in the world. But even if that rule could not withstand the political pressures of the mid-1970s, the companies would try to make the best deal possible. Negotiations to determine ownership of fully one-third of the free world's oil reserves were arduous and nomadic. For a month in 1975, Aramco representatives encamped with Yamani in Beit Meri, a hill town above Beirut. Each morning, oil men walked from their hotel to an old monastery Yamani had converted into one of his homes. Then word came that a terrorist group might attack, and suddenly the quaint little street looked not quaint but dangerous. They promptly cleared out and thereafter trailed along with Yamani on his global peregrinations.
- Late one night in the spring of 1976, they reached agreement in Yamani's suite at the Al-Yamama Hotel in Riyadh. Forty-three years earlier, Standard of California had reluctantly agreed to make an up-front payment of $175,000 for the right to wildcat in the trackless desert. By 1976, proven reserves in that desert were estimated at 149 billion barrels — more than a quarter of total free world reserves. "It was truly the end of the era," said one of the Americans present at the Al-Yamama Hotel that night.
- Under the new arrangement, Saudi Arabia took ownership of all Aramco assets and rights within the country. Aramco could continue as the operator and provide services for twenty-one cents a barrel. It would market 80 percent of Saudi production. In 1980, Saudi Arabia paid compensation based on net book value. The sun had finally set on the great concessions. The oil producers had achieved their grand objective — they controlled their own oil. These nation-states had become synonymous with petroleum.
- There was one odd thing: the Saudis did not sign the agreement until 1990, fourteen years after it was agreed upon. "It was very practical," said a company negotiator. "They got what they wanted — full control — but they didn't want to disrupt Aramco." About 33 billion barrels and over $700 billion of business was conducted for fourteen years "in limbo."
- While companies retained links through supply contracts in Saudi Arabia, Venezuela, and Kuwait, those connections would weaken over time due to diversification by both sides. At the same time, a new relationship was emerging: instead of "concessionaires" with ownership rights, companies were becoming "contractors" with "production sharing" contracts — pioneered by Indonesia and Caltex in the late 1960s. The shift in terminology reflected an all-important political change: the sovereignty of the country was recognized by both parties. The lingering aura of a colonial past was banished — the companies were there merely as hired hands. By the mid-1970s, production sharing contracts were becoming common worldwide.

### The Changing Global Oil Industry

- Meanwhile, the amount of oil sold directly by exporters into the market — without the companies as middlemen — quintupled from 8 percent of total OPEC output in 1973 to 42 percent by 1979. The state-owned companies of oil-producing countries were moving downstream, beyond production, into the international oil business outside their own borders. In little more than half a decade, the global oil industry had taken on a completely new form under the OPEC Imperium. Even more dramatic changes were ahead.

## Chapter 32: The Adjustment

### The End of Cheap Oil and the Western Response

- Did the end of cheap oil mean the end of the road for Hydrocarbon Man? In the 1950s and 1960s, cheap and easy oil had fueled economic growth and promoted social peace. Now, expensive and insecure oil was going to constrain, stunt, or even eradicate economic growth. One of the great lessons of the interwar decades was how central economic growth was to the vitality of democratic institutions. After 1973, the industrial nations found their sovereignty diminished, their security threatened, and their foreign policies constrained. "The very substance of power in international politics seemed to have been transmuted by its oleaginous reaction with petroleum." The 1970s became a time of "rancor, tension, unease, and gritty pessimism."
- Yet Hydrocarbon Man would not easily surrender his postwar legacy. A massive adjustment began. The IEA turned out to be not the agent of confrontation the French had predicted, but an instrument for coordination — bringing Western energy policies into parallel. Emergency sharing procedures were worked out, along with targets for government-controlled strategic reserves of oil. The IEA also provided a forum for research on conventional and new energy sources.
- The central goal came down to changing what Kissinger described as the "objective conditions" from which oil power derived — the supply-demand balance and overall reliance on oil. Each major consuming country proceeded in its own characteristic way: Japan with a public-private consensus, France with dirigisme and state direction, the United States with its usual fractious political debate. But the elements were the same: alternative fuels, diversified oil sources, and conservation.

### Nations Respond: Japan

- After the initial panic, Japan began to marshal its responses. MITI made a "personal statement" by curtailing elevator service in its own headquarters. To reduce the need for air conditioning, Japan promoted the shoene rukku or "energy conservation look" — business suits with short-sleeve jackets. While the elevator service remained curtailed, the new suits, though advocated by Prime Minister Masayoshi Ohira himself, never caught on.
- Japan was committed to drastically altering its energy position, which since the early 1960s had been based on cheap Middle Eastern oil — no longer cheap or secure. Responses included conversion of electric generation and industrial production from oil to other fuels, acceleration of nuclear power development, expansion of coal and liquefied natural gas imports, and diversification of oil imports away from the Middle East toward the Pacific Rim. "Resource diplomacy" came to the fore in Japan's international relations.
- The most concentrated effort was the government-business drive to promote energy conservation in industry and reduce oil use. The campaign's success far exceeded expectations and was of key importance to Japan's renewed international competitiveness. "Both workers and business leaders were very apprehensive after 1973," recalled Naohiro Amaya, then Vice-Minister of MITI. "They feared for the survival of their companies, and so everybody worked together."
- In 1971, MITI had conducted a study on the need to shift from "energy-intensive" to "knowledge-intensive" industry. Heavy industries had not liked the study, which implied they were lame ducks. But the 1973 crisis provided the force to implement the strategy at breakneck speed. "Instead of using the resources in the ground, we would use the resources in our head," said Amaya. "The Japanese people are accustomed to crises like earthquakes and typhoons. The energy shock was a kind of earthquake, and so even though it was a great shock, we were prepared to adjust." He added: "In a way, it was a kind of blessing, because it forced the rapid change of Japanese industry."

### Nations Respond: France

- In France, the most senior energy official was Jean Blancard, an engineer from the elite Corps des Mines. Even as Paris pursued conciliatory bilateral policies toward oil producers, Blancard was arguing to President Pompidou: "The period from here on will be quite different — a transformation, not a crisis... It is not reasonable for such a country as ours to be hanging on the Arabs' decisions. We must pursue a policy of diversification of energy and try to decrease the need for oil — or at least not allow it to increase."
- Pompidou, seriously ill with swollen features from his treatment, convened his senior advisers in early 1974. Despite great pain during the long meeting, the discussion confirmed three basic planks of French energy policy: rapid development of nuclear power, a return to coal, and heavy emphasis on energy conservation — all designed to restore France's autonomy. Pompidou died less than a month later, but his successor Valery Giscard d'Estaing pushed ahead on all three. With a government system much more impervious to outside interveners like environmentalists, France would within a few years outstrip all other Western countries in its commitment to nuclear power.
- France also developed the most aggressive government policy on energy conservation. Inspectors would swoop down on banks, department stores, and offices to do "le check up" — measuring inside temperature with special thermometers. If the temperature exceeded the officially approved twenty-degree-centigrade level, fines would be levied on the building management.
- Perhaps the most striking aspect of France's conservation program was a ban on any advertising that "encouraged" energy consumption. Exxon's tiger was tamed in France — no longer in the tank, he was judiciously advising motorists to check their tires and tune their engines. Gasoline stations could not give away the usual trinkets — mugs, glasses, spoons, decals — as such gifts would encourage consumption. The only permitted giveaway: cheap tool kits, but only if they contained a brush for cleaning spark plugs to promote higher efficiency.
- The French national oil company Total tried putting up billboards showing green French countryside with the legend "This is France" signed "Total." The ad was banned. A stunned Total asked why. Jean Syrota of the Energy Conservation Agency explained: "Consumers look at this ad and say, 'Oil companies are wasting a great deal of money on such ads, therefore the companies must be rich, therefore there must not be any energy problem, therefore it is all right to waste energy.'"

### "Obscene Profits" and the Jackson Hearings

- In the halls of Congress, Senators and Congressmen held hearings on the energy crisis and the oil companies' role. The most dramatic were held by the Senate Permanent Subcommittee on Investigations, chaired by Senator Henry "Scoop" Jackson — a hard-headed Truman Democrat who, as he liked to say, had his head "screwed on straight." Nixon would fume in private at what he called "Scoop Jackson's demagoguery." A White House aide explained to the irate Nixon that "our troops in the Interior Committee have a major inferiority complex when it comes to Jackson, because, frankly, he Scoops the hell out of them."
- Jackson's hearings were one of the greatest political scoops of his career. Senior executives of the seven largest oil companies were lined up at one table and made to testify under oath. Flooded by television lights, they were subjected to withering questioning about operations and profits. The executives, whatever their skills at geology or chemical engineering, were no match for Jackson and the other Senators at political theater. "They came across as inept, insulated, self-satisfied, and out of touch."
- The timing was exquisite: oil companies were reporting huge profit increases while the embargo was still in effect. In an atmosphere of distrust and hostility, Jackson announced his subcommittee would find out if there was really an oil shortage. "The American people want to know if this so-called energy crisis is only a pretext; a cover to eliminate the major source of price competition — the independents, to raise prices, to repeal environmental laws, and to force adoption of new tax subsidies... Gentlemen, I am hopeful that we will receive the answers to these and other questions before we leave here today. If not, I can assure you we will get the answers one way or another in the days ahead."
- The company executives tried to defend themselves. The president of Gulf U.S. protested: "The contrivance theory is absolute nonsense... I know people are a bit mystified by the rapid turn of events." A Texaco vice-president declared: "We have not cheated or misled anyone." When an Exxon vice-president could not recall the amount of his company's 1973 dividend, Jackson scathingly called him "childish."
- The oil men were humiliated, sobered, and infuriated. Jackson's line, though "perhaps not quite up to Eugene O'Neill, nevertheless won thunderous applause across the country." The companies were guilty of making "obscene profits." "We didn't have a chance," complained Gulf's bloodied president. Jackson knew he spoke for many Americans — the two gasoline stations near his house were always closed by the time he headed home. He was outraged by what he saw as "the arrogance and greed of the oil companies" and proposed they be chartered by the Federal government. Jackson succeeded in turning "obscene profits" into a national catch phrase. When Exxon released its 1973 earnings — up 59 percent over 1972 — on the third day of hearings, chairman Kenneth Jamieson felt constrained to declare, "I am not embarrassed." Many thought otherwise.
- The oil companies became among the most unpopular institutions in America. Some Japanese publications ran articles about how American companies had planned the crisis to raise profits. A confidential planning document at one of the largest oil companies warned in 1976: "The future for private oil companies is much less certain. The trend for upstream operations to pass into government hands will continue, with companies filling a contractor role." In 1977, a senior Shell executive opined: "Paradoxically, the threat to the viability of an oil company may today come more from the importing than the exporting governments."
- The companies had been nationalized in producing nations, no longer owned the oil or set prices. Now consuming governments were hammering them too. The hallowed depletion allowance was sharply curtailed; so was the foreign tax credit, the "golden gimmick" used since WWII to facilitate oil development in Venezuela and the Middle East. There were continuing efforts to roll back oil prices, political pressure to keep down natural gas prices, and a movement for "divestiture" — breaking up integrated companies into separate firms for each segment. At one point, forty-five out of one hundred Senators voted in favor of divestiture. The industry called it "dismemberment."

### The Facts Behind "Obscene Profits"

- Profits of the largest oil companies had been almost perfectly flat for five years through 1972, despite explosive demand growth. Profits then rose from $6.9 billion in 1972 to $11.7 in 1973 to $16.4 billion in the banner year of 1974. Much of the increase came from foreign operations — as exporting countries pushed prices up, companies got a free ride on non-US equity oil they still owned, plus gains from buying oil at lower prices and selling at $11.65 after inventory. Their chemical operations had also done well, helped by a weak dollar.
- But profits dropped back to $11.5 billion in 1975, lower than 1973. Demand was down from recession, and exporting countries raised taxes to capture rents from the companies. Over the next few years, profits rose to $15 billion in 1978, which in real terms meant they were just about keeping up with inflation. Rates of return were, except for 1974, somewhat below the average rate for all American industry.
- Profits were concentrated in the upstream — crude oil and natural gas production. The value of reserves in the US and North Sea had increased with the oil price. The downstream — refineries, tankers, gasoline stations — had been built before 1973 on expectations of 7-8 percent annual oil demand growth. Actual demand was much lower, so downstream capacity was bloated far beyond requirements. A third of the tanker fleet was in surplus. This overcapacity, combined with loss of equity crude in the Middle East, made international oil companies question the rationale of the large downstream systems built in Europe in the 1950s-1960s for the disposal of Middle Eastern oil now taken away from them.

### The United States Energy Policy: "Chinese Water Torture"

- Despite strong consensus internationally through Nixon, Ford, and Carter Administrations on energy policy, no similar agreement existed domestically. Energy policy was marked by divisive, angry, bitter, confused debate about price controls and company practices. Nixon's resignation in August 1974 and Watergate had left a crippling crisis of confidence and a pervasive suspicion about the energy crisis itself.
- Oil and energy became the hottest cauldron in national politics. Nixon had imposed economy-wide price controls in August 1971; most expired by 1974, but not those on oil. The politics gave rise to "an awesome Rube Goldberg system of price controls, entitlements, and allocations that made the mandatory oil import program of the 1960s appear, by comparison, to have the simplicity of a haiku."
- The public wanted Washington to do "something" — return prices to the good old days while assuring adequate supplies. Markets were confused and distorted. "For every problem you solve, it seems you create two more," moaned one government regulator. Acquiring entitlements to crude supplies became a big business, leading to the return of hopelessly inefficient "tea kettle" refineries. "For the oil industry, the Federal Register became more important than the geologist's report." The costs were enormous — the Federal Energy Administration alone involved 200,000 respondents committing an estimated five million man-hours annually. Direct regulatory costs ran to several billion dollars in the mid-1970s.

### Ford's Grand Plan and Two Key Achievements

- In January 1975, President Ford proposed a grand ten-year plan to build 200 nuclear power plants, 250 major coal mines, 150 coal-fired power plants, 30 major oil refineries, and 20 major synthetic fuel plants. Vice-President Nelson Rockefeller — grandson of the man who personified the monolith of oil — championed an even grander $100 billion program to subsidize synthetic fuels. But opponents challenged the costs, and the Rockefeller initiatives came to naught.
- There were two highly significant achievements during the Nixon-Ford years. Congress gave a green light to the Alaskan oil pipeline (TAPS), which ended up costing ten billion dollars. TAPS — the Trans-Alaskan Pipeline — made possible what proved to be the single most important new contribution to American energy supply since Dad Joiner's discovery of the East Texas field in the 1930s.
- The other landmark was the 1975 fuel efficiency standards for the automobile industry. Average fuel efficiency would have to double over ten years, from 13 miles per gallon to 27.5 miles per gallon. Since one out of every seven barrels of oil used in the world daily was burned as motor fuel on America's roads, this change would have major impact on the world's oil balance. The legislation also established a strategic petroleum reserve — the same idea Eisenhower had proposed after the 1956 Suez crisis. In practice, the rate of fill proved fatally slow.

### Carter, Schlesinger, and the "Moral Equivalent of War"

- In 1977, Jimmy Carter became President, having campaigned as an outsider promising moral renewal. Energy had engaged his attention for years — as a former Navy submariner, he remembered Admiral Hyman Rickover's warning about exhausting nature's oil stock. He had promised a national energy policy within ninety days and was dedicated to keeping his word.
- He gave the job to James Schlesinger, a Ph.D. economist who combined powerful analytic intelligence with "intellectual zeal and moral fervor." Nixon had plucked Schlesinger from the Rand Corporation to head the Bureau of the Budget, then the Atomic Energy Commission, then the CIA, and then made him Secretary of Defense. On weekends, Schlesinger could be found bird-watching around Washington with binoculars. His tenure at Defense ended under Ford when he took exception to Kissinger's detente policy.
- Carter phoned Schlesinger after the Democratic Convention and invited him to Plains, Georgia. Schlesinger was close to Senator Henry Jackson — the single most important Senator on energy — and Carter's rival for the nomination. Jackson pressed Carter to make Schlesinger the energy champion. Carter was more than willing. "It was kind of convenient if the chairman of the Senate Energy Committee was a pal of your proto-energy secretary," Schlesinger noted.
- Carter read a late-1976 CIA report predicting future oil shortages and found it compelling. Schlesinger shared Carter's deep concern about foreign policy implications of a tight oil market. As Carter wrote in his memoirs, many Americans "deeply resented that the greatest nation on earth was being jerked around by a few desert states."
- In 1972, while at the Atomic Energy Commission, Schlesinger had expressed a then-heretical idea: that the US should promote energy conservation on grounds of national security, economic policy, and environmental improvement. "We can do somewhat better than automobiles that move at 10 miles to the gallon and badly insulated buildings that are simultaneously heated and cooled." By 1977, he was more convinced than ever that conservation should be central to energy policy. Unfortunately, "that premise was considerably more obvious to him than to many others."
- The Administration kept its energy program development secret, leaving no time to build consensus. Schlesinger asked Carter to relent on the ninety-day deadline. "I said 90 days," Carter firmly replied. "I made the pledge and I intend to keep it." Carter himself complained to Schlesinger: "Our basic & most difficult question is how to raise the price of scarce energy with minimum disruption of our economic system and greater equity in bearing the financial burden. I am not satisfied with your approach. It is extremely complicated." Then plaintively: "I can't understand it."
- The plan was unveiled in early April in a fireside Presidential address. On a TV interview the Sunday before, Schlesinger quoted William James — "the moral equivalent of war" — to capture the magnitude of the energy challenge. Carter was impressed and put it into his speech. He introduced his energy program as "the moral equivalent of war," and by that sobriquet it would often be known. Its detractors preferred the acronym: "Meow."
- The Carter program included a host of initiatives: introducing economic rationality into pricing, reducing the need for imported oil. Schlesinger's number-one priority was to let domestic oil under price controls rise to world market prices so consumers would react to correct price signals. The Carter program proposed ending price controls through a "crude oil equalization tax." Ironically, it had been the Republican Nixon who originally imposed price controls in 1971, and now a Democratic Administration was trying to lift them. Carter and Schlesinger also sought to extract the country from the straitjacket of natural gas price controls, put greater emphasis on conservation and coal, introduce competition into the electricity sector, and encourage alternative energy sources including solar power.
- Carter received a firsthand education in how special interests operate: liberals, conservatives, oil producers, consumer groups, automobile companies, pro- and anti-nuclear activists, coal producers, utility companies, environmentalists — all with conflicting agendas. To Schlesinger, the issue was clear: "a substantial long-run national problem." But he was unprepared for the storm of debate and the bitterness. "There are three kinds of lies — lies, damn lies, and energy lies," an old veteran at the Atomic Energy Commission had told him. Schlesinger later said: "I have a sort of World War II mentality. If the President says something is in the national interest, I had assumed he would get a more supportive response than we found. As Secretary of Defense, everyone not against you is with you. Here, on energy, you had interest groups against interest groups. You couldn't put a consensus together. It was distressing."
- Natural gas proved the most contentious issue — a decades-old theological struggle over whether prices should be controlled by government or set by the market. Schlesinger said of the Senate-House conferences: "I understand now what Hell is. Hell is endless and eternal sessions of the natural gas conference." A very intricate compromise was eventually arranged.
- A number of different categories for pricing purposes were created for a commodity that was composed of the same standard molecules of one carbon atom and four hydrogen atoms. Despite the bloody political battles and exhaustion of much political capital, the Carter Administration could claim important accomplishments. "Passage of the National Energy Act represents a watershed in that it starts the adjustment of our demands to the means available," Schlesinger told a London audience. But he ruefully observed: "The response was less close to William James' moral equivalent of war than to the political equivalent of the Chinese water torture."

### Boom Times: The Exploration Rush

- By end of 1978, post-embargo policies were just beginning to have their influence felt. But one reaction to the embargo was virtually instantaneous: the price hikes, expectation of future increases, expanded cash flows, and investor eagerness combined to ignite a frenetic global hunt for oil. "It's just wild," said Exxon's deputy exploration manager. What had been a depressed exploration business through 1972 was now running at capacity. The cost of everything — from semi-submersible drilling rigs to old-fashioned Oklahoma land crews — doubled from 1973 levels.
- The flow of investment was redirected in a very substantial way. The number-one commandment was to avoid nationalism in the Third World. Exploration in most OPEC countries was foreclosed by nationalization, and there was a strong presupposition that success in other developing countries would be seized. Companies redirected exploration spending to the industrial world: the United States (despite pessimism about its oil potential), Canada, and the British and Norwegian sectors of the North Sea. In 1975, Gulf undertook a complete global budget review — every investment dollar not nailed down was "quietly yanked out of the Third World and brought back to North America and the North Sea." By 1976, Royal Dutch/Shell was concentrating 80 percent of its worldwide non-US production expenditures in the North Sea. "After 1973 and nationalization, you had to go hunt your rabbits in a different field," recalled an Exxon executive.
- Oil companies also began to diversify into totally different businesses — reflecting the view that the business and political environment might grow "more and more narrow, more and more harsh, more and more constrained by government intervention and regulation." There was also a nagging fear that the days of oil itself might be numbered by geological depletion — between 1970 and 1976, proven American oil reserves dropped by 27 percent and gas reserves by 24 percent. Mobil bought the Montgomery Ward department store chain, Exxon went into office automation, ARCO into copper. But nothing aroused so much mirth and derision as Gulf's bid for Ringling Bros. and Barnum & Bailey Circus — seeming to prove that the new era of OPEC's imperium, high oil prices, confusion, bitter debates, and energy wars in Washington "really was a circus."

### New Supplies: Alaska and Mexico

- OPEC continued to dominate the world oil market throughout the 1970s — 65 percent of total "free world" oil production in 1973 and 62 percent in 1978. But its grip was beginning to loosen. The incentive of price and the motive of security were stimulating oil development outside OPEC. Three new oil provinces would have overwhelming influence: Alaska, Mexico, and the North Sea. Ironically, each had been identified before the 1973 price hike but not yet developed due to various mixtures of politics, economics, environmental opposition, technical obstacles, and the long leads required by major energy projects.
- With the emergency go-ahead on the Alaskan pipeline after the embargo, work proceeded rapidly. The steel pipe and tractors, purchased in 1968, had sat on the frozen banks of the Yukon River for five years, engines dutifully turned over on schedule. By 1977, the eight-hundred-mile pipeline was completed — some parts suspended on stilts above the tundra — and the first oil made the journey from the North Slope to the shipping port at Valdez. By 1978, over a million barrels per day were flowing. Within a few years, the flow would be two million barrels per day — a quarter of America's total crude oil production.
- In Mexico, Pemex had been the national oil company and embodiment of Mexican nationalism since the fiery 1930s nationalization. It devoted itself to supplying the domestic market, subject to a struggle for control between the government and the powerful oil workers' union. Pemex did relatively little to expand its reserve base; as the "Mexican economic miracle" grew, Mexico ceased being an exporter and actually became a minor oil importer, though effort went into disguising that fact.
- In 1972, Pemex launched a deep-drilling program in Tabasco's rolling savannas and struck an extraordinary structure called Reforma. The highly productive wells led to the region being dubbed "Little Kuwait," followed by further very large discoveries in the adjacent offshore Bay of Campeche. Mexico possessed world-class oil reserves.
- New President Jose Lopez Portillo, taking over in 1976 from the radical Echeverria, inherited Mexico's worst economic crisis since the Great Depression — the peso had collapsed, the economy stalled, and one out of every two Mexicans was under fifteen while 40 percent of the workforce was unemployed or underemployed. There were even rumors of a possible military coup.
- Lopez Portillo appointed his old friend Jorge Diaz Serrano to head Pemex. Unlike his predecessor (a bridge construction engineer), Diaz Serrano knew the oil industry — he had become a millionaire supplying services. Oil would provide Mexico with foreign earnings, remove the constraint on balance of payments, provide collateral for international borrowing, and put Mexico at the center of the new oil-based international economy. Lopez Portillo cautioned: "The capacity for monetary digestion is like that of a human body. You can't eat more than you can digest or you become ill." But his actions spoke louder — investment poured in, reserves were expanded rapidly, production surged from 500,000 barrels in 1972 to 830,000 in 1976 to 1.9 million barrels in 1980, almost a fourfold increase in less than a decade.
- Mexico went from being avoided by international lenders to becoming one of the most active borrowers. "Why the Bankers Suddenly Love Mexico" headlined Fortune. "Every Tom, Dick, and Harry in banking is knocking on their door," said the vice-chairman of Manufacturers Hanover Trust. One Mexican official was chosen "borrower of the year" in 1978 by a New York financial newsletter. Mexico had become a major new force in the world oil market, as it had not been since the 1920s, providing yet another alternative source of supply undermining OPEC's imperium.

### The North Sea: The Biggest Play of All

- For centuries, fishermen had the North Sea to themselves. By the mid-1970s, a helicopter view revealed floating drilling rigs, supply boats, platforms, pipe-laying barges — singly at first, then in such profusion as almost to crowd the sea. Here was the biggest new play for the world oil industry and its single greatest concentration of capital investment and effort. No major company dared to be left out, and many new players were drawn in — from Edinburgh investment trusts to Lord Thomson, the newspaper tycoon who owned the Times of London and was a partner of Armand Hammer.
- Ever since 1920, thousands of onshore wells in Western Europe had been drilled with disappointing results — total production never exceeded 250,000 barrels per day. The Suez crisis of 1956 boosted the search for secure European oil and gas. In 1959, Shell and Esso discovered the vast Groningen gas field in Holland, the largest then known outside the USSR. Realizing the North Sea's geology matched Holland's, companies began exploring adjacent waters. In 1965, Britain and Norway agreed to divide the North Sea right down the middle.
- Phillips Petroleum from Bartlesville, Oklahoma, began exploring in 1962 after its vice-chairman noticed a drilling derrick near Groningen during a Netherlands vacation. By 1969, after a string of dry holes — some thirty-two wells on the Norwegian shelf, not one commercial — Bartlesville's message was clear: "Don't drill any more wells." But Phillips had already paid for the rig Ocean Viking and could not sublease it. In the grand tradition stretching back to Colonel Drake, they decided on one more go. The weather was terrible — the rig broke from its anchors, and one night a capsize seemed so likely that emergency evacuation was initiated. But in November 1969, the Ocean Viking made a major find on Block 2/4 — the Ekofisk field, on the Norwegian side of the median line. The drilling superintendent examined the oil sample: "What the astronauts have done is great, but how about this?" It had a golden sheen, "almost transparent, but definitely almost like gold."
- Phillips's discovery set all companies to re-evaluating their data and stepping up activity. When asked what methods Phillips used to diagnose the geology, a senior Phillips executive replied: "Luck." Toward end of 1970, BP announced the discovery of oil in the Forties field on the British side, one hundred miles northwest of Ekofisk — a huge reservoir. Major strikes followed in 1971, including Shell and Exxon's discovery of the huge Brent field. The North Sea oil rush was on, and the 1973 crisis turned it into a roar.
- The venture was risky and dangerous. Drilling rigs had to work through water depths much greater than anything tried before, then drill another four miles under the seabed. "There's nothing quite as vile as the North Sea when she's in a temper," lamented one skipper. Weather could change three or four times a day; waves of fifty feet and winds of seventy miles an hour were not uncommon. Platforms — really small industrial cities set on man-made islands — had to stand on mud, quicksand, clay, and sand wave bottoms and withstand the fury of a "100-year wave" ninety feet high, as well as winds of 130 miles per hour.
- On June 18, 1975, British Secretary of State for Energy Anthony Wedgwood Benn turned a valve on an oil tanker in the Thames estuary. The first North Sea oil flowed ashore. Publicly, Benn enthusiastically declared June 18 should be a day of national celebration. Personally, he did not enjoy the inaugural at all — a leader of the Labour left with a passion for nationalization and inbred detestation of capitalism, especially as represented by the oil industry. He sourly noted in his diary that he had been forced to participate "in the company of a complete cross-section of the international capitalist and British Tory establishment." When he turned the valve, the oil "allegedly went on shore," he added with great suspicion.
- Benn played a leading role in replicating the traditional battle between governments and oil companies. Now that reserves had been proved and risks reduced, the British government wanted a larger share of the rents and more control. "Oil companies can jump over national boundaries to save on taxes more easily than a kangaroo pursued by a dingo can jump over a fence," complained Lord Balogh, Benn's Minister of State. The result was a special tax on oil revenues and the establishment of the British National Oil Corporation (BNOC) — with 51 percent participation in North Sea production, meant to be the national champion. One company head finally exploded: "I don't see any difference any more between those OPEC countries and Britain."
- Prime Minister Harold Wilson, puffing his pipe at 10 Downing Street in the summer of 1975, was ruminating on how Britain's oil production could grow to perhaps two and a half million barrels per day, transforming Britain's prospects and the balance of oil power. He was already thinking like the prime minister of an oil country. At the time, the Ford Administration was campaigning against higher oil prices. "We do have an interest in seeing that the price of oil doesn't fall too much," Wilson said. "If America wanted to really bring down the price, not many people here would necessarily agree." Wilson was sitting in the same room that Anthony Eden had used two decades earlier during the Suez crisis. Wilson confessed an ambition that might have shocked Eden: as leader of a newly emerging major oil power, Wilson genially said he hoped he might be chairman of OPEC by 1980.

### "The Crunch": Oil Price Forecasting and the Consensus of 1978

- One peculiar result of the 1973 price shock was the rise of oil price forecasting as a new line of work. Before 1973, it had not been necessary — prices changed in cents, not dollars, and were more or less flat. After 1973, oil price movements were decisive not only for energy industries but for a multitude of businesses from airlines to agricultural cooperatives, for governments, and for the international economy. "Birds do it, bees do it, even educated fleas do it" — everyone was in the forecasting business. Oil companies, governments, central banks, international organizations, brokerage houses, banks all did it.
- This forecasting was as much art as science. Judgments and assumptions governed predictions. Moreover, forecasting was much affected by the "community" in which it was done — a psychological and sociological phenomenon reflecting peer influence, the groping for certainty and mutual comfort in an uncertain world. The result was a strong tendency toward consensus, even when the consensus completely changed its tune every couple of years.
- By 1978, a clear consensus existed: while Alaska, Mexico, and the North Sea would together add six to seven million barrels per day by the early-to-mid 1980s, these new sources would serve only as a supplement, holding off and postponing but not decisively banishing the inevitable day of shortage and reckoning. Most forecasters agreed another oil crisis was highly probable a decade hence — in the second half of the 1980s — when demand would once again be at the very edge of available supply. The result would be an "energy gap," a shortage, resolved by another major price increase. There was considerable unanimity on this central theme, whether from the CIA, oil companies, Western governments, international agencies, or OPEC itself.
- The single most important assumption was the "Iron Law" — that there was an inevitably close relationship between economic growth rates and oil use. If the economy grew at 3-4 percent, oil demand would also grow at 3-4 percent. The facts of 1976-1978 seemed to confirm this: economic growth had rebounded, averaging 4.2 percent, and oil demand had grown at about 4 percent. The future picture was a projection of these current circumstances. The future effects of conservation were discounted. The stage was set for a repetition of 1973.
- Yamani, departing from his customary advocacy of price stability, began to argue for regular, small increases that would encourage conservation and alternative sources — much preferable to the wrenching increase everyone now expected. "From our own studies and from all the reliable studies I have read," he said in June 1978, "there are very strong indications that there will be a shortage of supply of oil sometime around the mid-1980s if not before... No matter what we do, that date is coming."
- It was also generally agreed that conditions did not point to any major price increase in the near term. That was a view based on economics; politics, however, was never easy to fit into models dealing with growth rates and elasticities of demand. And politics was not about to allow anyone the luxury of a long-term strategy.

### Carter's New Year's Eve Toast in Tehran

- On the last day of 1977, President Carter, en route from Warsaw to New Delhi, arrived in Tehran. He said he had asked Mrs. Carter where she wanted to spend New Year's Eve, and she had said with the Shah and his wife — so delightful a time the Carters had had when the royal couple visited Washington six weeks earlier. There were reasons of realpolitik behind the sentiment. Carter had been impressed by the Shah, who was taking steps toward liberalization and talking about human rights. Iran was a fulcrum country, essential to regional stability, critical in counterbalancing Soviet power, and central to the security of the world's oil supplies.
- Carter also wanted to show gratitude for the Shah's shift on human rights and oil prices, and was regretful and embarrassed about the tear gas incident at the White House. At a New Year's Eve banquet, Carter rose to offer a memorable toast: "Iran, because of the great leadership of the Shah, is an island of stability in one of the more troubled areas of the world. This is a great tribute to you, Your Majesty, and to the respect and the admiration and the love which your people give you." On that strong and hopeful note, the President and the Shah welcomed the momentous New Year of 1978.
- Not everyone saw the same island of stability. Shortly after Carter's visit, the president of one of the independent American oil companies active in Iran came back from Tehran with an urgent confidential message: "The Shah is in big trouble."

## Chapter 33: The Second Shock: The Great Panic

### The Newspaper Article That Triggered Revolution

- A week after Carter's departure from Iran, a Tehran newspaper published a savage attack on Ayatollah Ruhollah Khomeini, an implacable opponent of the Shah living in exile in Iraq. The article, though anonymous, appeared to be the work of a regime official. Perhaps the Carter visit had bolstered flagging confidence. But the article was already in the works — exasperation was increasing with Khomeini's harsh attacks on the Shah's government, which were being circulated clandestinely on cassettes throughout Iran.
- Animosity between the royal house and the Shiite clergy dated back to Reza Shah's fierce battle for power with the Shiite clergy in the 1920s and 1930s — part of the larger struggle between secular and religious forces. But that newspaper article of January 7, 1978, triggered a wholly new stage in the struggle.

### Disillusion and Opposition

- It had become evident in the mid-1970s that Iran simply could not absorb the vast increase in oil revenues flooding in. The petrodollars, megalomaniacally misspent on extravagant modernization programs or lost to waste and corruption, were generating economic chaos and social and political tension throughout the nation. The rural populace poured from villages into already-overcrowded towns and cities; agricultural output was declining while food imports rose. Inflation had seized control of the country. A middle manager or civil servant in Tehran spent up to 70 percent of his salary on rent. Iran's infrastructure could not cope — the backward railway system was overwhelmed, Tehran's streets were jammed with traffic, the national electricity grid broke down. Parts of Tehran and other cities were regularly blacked out, sometimes for four or five hours a day — a disaster for industrial production and domestic life.
- Iranians from every sector of national life were losing patience with the Shah's regime and the pell-mell rush to modernization. Grasping for some certitude in the melee, they increasingly heeded the call of traditional Islam and of an ever more fervent fundamentalism. The beneficiary was Ayatollah Khomeini, whose religious rectitude and unyielding resistance made him the embodiment of opposition to the Shah and his regime, and indeed to the very character and times of Iran in the mid-1970s.
- Khomeini was born around 1900 in a small town 180 miles from Tehran, into a family of religious teachers. His father was killed by a government official a few months after his birth; his mother died when he was in his teens. He turned to religious studies and by the 1930s-1940s was a popular lecturer on Islamic philosophy and law, promulgating the concept of an Islamic Republic under the stern control of the clergy.
- Khomeini had regarded the Pahlavi regime as corrupt and illegitimate, but did not become politically active until about sixty. In 1962, he emerged as a leading figure in the opposition to the Shah's "White Revolution" reform program — opposing, among other things, the proposal that places in local assemblies no longer be restricted exclusively to male Moslems. When the government redistributed large estates including vast Shiite clergy holdings, Khomeini came forward as one of the most unyielding opponents, landing in jail more than once and eventually ending up in exile in Iraq. His hatred of the Shah was matched only by his detestation of the United States. His denunciations from exile were cast in the rhetoric of blood and vengeance; he seemed driven by unadulterated anger of extraordinary intensity, and he himself became the rallying point for the growing discontent.

### The Human Rights Dimension

- Another dimension of opposition had emerged. With Carter's election, human rights became a major issue in US foreign policy. The Shah's human rights record was not good — typical of much of the Third World, and better than some other countries in the region. A member of the International Commission of Jurists concluded in 1976 that the Shah was "way down the list of tyrants. He would not even make the A-list." Still, Savak, the Iranian secret police, was "brutal, quick, and particularly nasty in its torture; it was also callous, stupid, intrusive, pervasive, and arbitrary." None of this fit the image of the Great Civilization whose Shah was lecturing the industrial world on its character flaws. Thus, Iran's human rights record became more visible and much more reported upon, contributing to growing hostility both inside Iran and abroad.

### "Doing the 40-40"

- Khomeini's words took on new fury in late 1977 when his eldest son was murdered under mysterious circumstances — attributed to Savak. Then came the Tehran newspaper article of January 7, 1978. It ridiculed Khomeini, questioned his religious credentials, challenged his Iranian nationality, and accused him of various acts of immorality including authoring risque love sonnets as a young man. This journalistic assault set off riots in the holy city of Qom, Khomeini's spiritual home. Troops were called in and demonstrators killed.
- The disturbance in Qom ignited a new confrontation between the Moslem religious leadership and the government. The Shiite branch of Islam provided for a forty-day mourning period. By plan, the end of the forty-day period for the slain at Qom became the occasion for new demonstrations, more deaths, more mourning — and then after forty days, more demonstrations and still more deaths. One leader of this relentless cycle later called it "doing the 40-40." The riots and demonstrations spread across the country with further dramatic clashes, more killed, more martyrs.
- Attacks by police and army on critics of the regime only served to swell and broaden the ranks of those antagonistic to the Shah. The withdrawal of subsidies to the Shia religious establishment alienated the clergy further. Yet all through the first half of 1978, the opposition's significance was discounted. The Shah told the British ambassador the situation was serious but he was determined to press ahead with liberalization. His most implacable enemies were the mullahs, with their hold on the minds of the masses. "There could be no compromise with them. It was a straightforward confrontation and one side had to lose." The Shah made clear he could not imagine being on the losing side.
- In the US government, hardly anyone could imagine the Shah might fail. For Washington, any alternative was virtually unthinkable. He had sat on his throne for thirty-seven years, was courted throughout the world, was modernizing his country. Iran was one of the world's two great oil powers, with wealth far beyond anything it had known. The Shah was a critical ally, a regional policeman in a crucial area, the "Big Pillar." How could he possibly be toppled?
- American intelligence on Iran was constrained. As the US became more dependent on the Shah, there was less willingness to risk his ire by investigating the opposition he despised.
- There was not great demand among the "consumers" of intelligence for analyses of the Shah's stability — either thought unnecessary or the conclusions might be too unpalatable. "You couldn't give away intelligence on Iran," said one frustrated analyst. The intelligence community struggled throughout 1978 to assemble a National Intelligence Estimate but could never complete it. As late as September 28, 1978, the Defense Intelligence Agency's prognosis was that the Shah "is expected to remain actively in power over the next ten years."

### The Shah's Illness and the Escalating Crisis

- There were grisly signs of the fury against the Shah. Over two weeks in August 1978, half a dozen movie theaters were set afire by fundamentalists opposed to "sinful" movies. In mid-August, in Abadan, about five hundred people were crowded into a theater when someone locked the doors and incinerated the trapped moviegoers. In early September, bloody demonstrations took place in Tehran itself — the turning point. From then on, the Shah's government began to collapse as an effective ruling force.
- Something seemed wrong with the Shah himself. He appeared distant and more isolated. The British ambassador went to see him on September 16: "I was worried by the change in his appearance and manner. He looked shrunken; his face was yellow and he moved slowly. He seemed exhausted and drained of spirit." The Shah did have cancer — specifically leukemia, first diagnosed by French doctors in 1974. The seriousness was kept secret for several years from both the Shah and his wife. The British government and most certainly the Americans did not know. Had they been informed, calculations on many accounts might have been different. As time went on, the Shah began to feel the effects of the illness more and more and to fear its consequences — which might help explain the indecisiveness, strange detachment, malaise, and fatalism that seemed to take hold.

### "Like Snow in Water"

- As the political situation deteriorated, the Shah vacillated. He would not wage total war against the growing rebellion — "world public opinion" was watching too closely, and these were his people. But neither would he concede. He was befuddled by contradictory advice from the US government, and expressed his suspicion that the American CIA, British intelligence, and the BBC were conspiring against him.
- More of the country went on strike, including oil industry technicians. In early October 1978, at Iran's urging, Khomeini was expelled from Iraq (the Ba'thist regime in Baghdad had to worry about its own Shiite population). Denied refuge in Kuwait, Khomeini went to France and established himself in a suburb of Paris. The Iranian government may have thought: out of sight, out of mind. But it was mistaken — France provided Khomeini access to direct-dial international phone service that the Shah had installed in Tehran, greatly facilitating communication. The elderly, irate cleric proved himself a master of propaganda in front of the media that camped at his door.
- The Shah proceeded with liberalization — academic freedom, freedom of the press, freedom of assembly — but such Western-style rights were of little interest to a population rising against the monarch and the whole process of modernization. At the end of October, the Shah could only say, "We are melting away daily like snow in water." Strikes immobilized the economy and government; students, demonstrations, and riots went on unchecked.

### The Oil Industry in Chaos

- The main production area was known as "The Fields," located in the southeast, including Masjid-i-Suleiman where Anglo-Persian had made its original 1908 discovery. Seventy years later, operations were in the hands of the Oil Service Company of Iran (Osco), the descendant of the 1954 consortium. Staffed by expatriate oil men mostly from the member companies, Osco's headquarters was in Ahwaz. In October, striking Iranian laborers from The Fields moved into Osco's main headquarters, and by November a couple hundred were living in the corridors, eating and sleeping there. The Western oil men went about their jobs, carefully trying to avoid stepping on the workers. Meanwhile, impromptu prayer meetings in the courtyard grew from half a dozen to several hundred.
- Iran was the second-largest exporter after Saudi Arabia. Of 5.5 million barrels produced daily, about 4.5 million were exported. By early November, exports had been reduced to less than a million barrels per day, and thirty tankers were waiting in line at Kharg Island loading facilities — at a time when the international market's winter demand surge was beginning. Petroleum companies, responding to the general market softness, had been letting inventories fall.
- The head of the National Iranian Oil Company went south to The Fields to seek dialogue with the striking oil workers. He was mobbed by angry strikers, immediately decided to forgo negotiations, and fled the country. The Shah took a critical step: he installed a military government. But the general put in charge immediately suffered a heart attack and never asserted authority. The new government temporarily restored some order in the oil industry, and soldiers moved into Osco's headquarters in Ahwaz, coexisting uneasily with striking workers still camping in the corridors.

### US Policy in Disarray

- As events tumbled toward their conclusion, US policy was in confusion, disarray, and shock. During most of 1978, senior officials had been distracted by other developments — Camp David peace accords with Egypt and Israel, SALT negotiations with the Soviets, normalization of relations with China. American policy had been based on the premise that Iran was a reliable ally and the Big Pillar. Out of deference to the Shah, officials had kept their distance from his opponents, meaning they lacked channels to the emerging opposition. There was not even reporting to Washington on what Khomeini was actually saying on his famous tapes. Some insisted the unrest was a Soviet-orchestrated plot.
- The last months of 1978 saw a fierce bureaucratic battle over policy in Washington: How to bolster the Shah or assure continuity? How to support him without being so committed that relations with successors would be antagonistic? The indecision produced contradictory signals to Iran. "The United States never sent a clear, consistent signal," one senior official recalled. "Instead of oscillating back and forth between one course of action and the other and never deciding, we would have done better to have flipped a coin and then stuck to a policy." The cacophony from Washington confused the Shah and his senior officials, undermined their calculations, and "drastically weakened their resolve." No one in Washington knew how sick the Shah was.
- Efforts to construct an American position were complicated by a romantic and unrealistic view of Khomeini. A prominent professor wrote in the New York Times of Khomeini's tolerance, of how "his entourage of close advisers is uniformly composed of moderate, progressive individuals," and how Khomeini would provide "a desperately-needed model of humane governance for a third-world country." The American UN ambassador Andrew Young went even further: Khomeini would eventually be hailed as "a saint." An embarrassed Carter felt the need to make clear "that the United States is not in the canonization business."
- On November 9, Ambassador William Sullivan in Tehran sent a dramatic cable entitled "Thinking the Unthinkable" — perhaps the Shah would not survive; the US should begin to consider contingencies. Carter sent hand-written notes to his Secretary of State, National Security Adviser, Secretary of Defense, and Director of Central Intelligence to ask why he had not been previously informed of the situation. Sullivan concluded the US faced the situation in Iran "with no policy whatsoever."

### "Torrents of Blood"

- December 1978 was a month of mourning, processions, and self-flagellation in the Shiite creed. The high point was the holiday of Ashura, marking the martyrdom of the imam Hussein and symbolizing unremitting resistance to a tyrant. Khomeini promised it would be a month of vengeance and "torrents of blood." He called for new martyrs: "Let them kill five thousand, ten thousand, twenty thousand. We will prove that blood is more powerful than the sword." Huge demonstrations were held across the country, some truly awe-inspiring in size. The Army was crumbling away. "A dictator may survive by slaughtering his people, a king cannot act in such a way," the Shah said privately. And among his humiliations was a telephone prank: told that Senator Edward Kennedy was phoning from Washington, the Shah picked up — only to hear a quiet voice repeating: "Mohammed, abdicate. Mohammed, abdicate."
- A task force from Osco had begun preparing an evacuation plan for the twelve hundred expatriate oil men and their families in The Fields. The effort was not taken seriously until one afternoon George Link, an Exxon man who was Osco's general manager, had a car bomb thrown at him as he was being driven back to work after lunch. Link reflexively threw open his door and jumped out moments before the car exploded. Evacuation planning took on a new seriousness.
- Strikes once more gripped The Fields and Iranian production fell away rapidly. Paul Grimm, Osco's assistant general manager on loan from Texaco, was in direct confrontation with the workers. He bluntly warned some blue-collar expatriates who were joining the strikes that they would be dismissed. He was singled out as the man trying to break the walkout.
