In mid-December, as Grimm was driving to work, a shot was fired at him from a following car. He died instantly from a bullet in the back of his head. Evacuation of dependents now hurriedly began.
- By December 25, Christmas Day, Iranian petroleum exports had ceased altogether — a pivotal event in the world oil market. Spot prices in Europe surged 10 to 20 percent above official prices. The cutbacks deprived Iran of domestic oil supplies too — long lines formed in Tehran for gasoline and kerosene (the standard cooking fuel). Soldiers maintained order by firing shots into the air. Oil workers refused to provide petroleum products to the military, helping immobilize it. In an ironic reversal, an American tanker was diverted to Iran to supply badly needed fuel, but it was never able to unload because sufficiently secure arrangements could not be made.

### "I Am Feeling Tired": The Shah's Departure

- At the end of December, there was reluctant agreement in the ruling circles that a coalition government would be formed, the Shah would leave Iran ostensibly for medical treatment, and the Pahlavi dynasty appeared to be finished. So, virtually, was petroleum production in The Fields. In the week after Christmas, Osco decided to evacuate all Western employees. The expatriates assumed their exit was temporary — weeks or months at most. They were strictly limited to two suitcases. They left their houses intact. They faced a quandary similar to their predecessors who had been forced by Mossadegh to leave Abadan in 1951 — what to do with their dogs. Since they did not know how long they would be gone, they did what their predecessors had done: took their dogs out back and either shot them or clubbed them to death.
- They gathered at the airport at Ahwaz. Once again, the heirs of William D'Arcy Knox and George Reynolds were ignominiously leaving Iran. Unlike the "farewell to Abadan" in 1951, there were no honor guards, no bands, no singing of verses to "Colonel Bogey's March." Ahwaz had once been a very busy airport — now the oil industry was shut down, and the sky over the deserted airport was empty and silent and foreboding.
- On January 8, the British ambassador went to say farewell to the Shah. The monarchy that had survived all sorts of vicissitudes for almost half a century was at its end. The pomp of the celebration at Persepolis of the twenty-five hundredth anniversary of the Persian monarchy was gone. So was the power. Alexander the Great had captured Persepolis in 330 B.C. and burned the royal palace; now Khomeini was making a mockery of the self-proclaimed heir to Persepolis. "Like the Wizard of Oz, Mohammed Pahlavi was revealed to be, after all, but a mere mortal. The show was over."
- The Shah was calm and detached, speaking about events as though they had no relevance to him personally. The ambassador, despite all his years of self-discipline, found himself crying. The Shah said, "Never mind, I know how you feel." Then, in an odd gesture, he looked at his watch: "If it was up to me, I would leave — in ten minutes."
- At midday on January 16, the Shah appeared at Tehran airport. "I am feeling tired and need a rest," he said to a small group, maintaining the pathetic fiction that he was only going on vacation. He boarded his plane, carrying with his luggage a casket of Iranian soil, and left Tehran for the last time. His first stop was Egypt.
- With the Shah's departure, all of Tehran erupted into jubilation not seen since the Shah's own return in triumph in 1953. Car horns blared, headlights flashed, windshield wipers decorated with pictures of Khomeini swished back and forth. Newspapers were handed out with the unforgettable banner headline "The Shah Is Gone." Throughout the country, the great equestrian statues of his father and himself were pulled from their pedestals by wild crowds, and the Pahlavi dynasty crumbled into dust.

### Khomeini's Return and the End of the Pahlavi Dynasty

- On February 1, Khomeini arrived back in Tehran in a chartered Air France 747. Seats had been sold to Western reporters to finance the flight; Khomeini himself spent the trip resting on a carpet on the floor of first class. He brought with him a second government, a revolutionary council headed by Mehdi Bazargan, whose credentials as an opponent of the Shah were impeccable — in 1951, twenty-eight years earlier, Bazargan had been Mossadegh's choice to head the nationalized oil industry, had gone to the oil fields in person with stamps and a wooden sign reading "National Iranian Oil Company." He had served time in jail under the Shah.
- For a brief time, there were two rival governments in Tehran. But there could only be one. In the second week of February, fighting broke out at an air force base between "homafars" (non-commissioned officers sympathetic to the revolution) and troops of the Imperial Guard. The military support for the coalition government collapsed, and Bazargan was in. The American defense attache provided a succinct summary to Washington: "Army surrenders; Khomeini wins. Destroying all classified."

### The Last Man Out

- Not quite all oil men had departed from The Fields. About twenty stayed on to maintain Osco's legal presence. Among them was Jeremy Gilbert, an Irish mathematician turned petroleum engineer assigned by BP. Stricken with severe hepatitis, Gilbert was not allowed on the evacuation plane. He stayed in the hospital through the tumultuous days of January, hearing chanting and gunfire from his bed. On the day the Shah left, he heard the clangorous sounds of a giant celebration. His only contact with the outside world was the BBC and a huge arrangement of flowers from Osco.
- Very weak, Gilbert was mistaken by Iranians for an American. Nurses gathered outside his window to chant "Death to Americans." Another patient began beating him with crutches, ranting against Americans. Gilbert's actual Irish nationality posed another problem — Irish troops in Lebanon had recently gotten into a shooting match with Iraqis, so Gilbert was denied an Iraqi visa. He had to go on his knees before an agent at the Iraqi consulate and apologize for all the sins of the Irish.
- At the end of January, Gilbert made the trip out through Iraq. Iranian officials at the border waved him across; Iraqi guards detained him for hours suspecting he was a spy. The only taxi to Basra had left. "How do I get to Basra?" Gilbert asked. "You walk," a guard replied. Tired, weak, carrying two bags, he dragged himself along the dirt road until a van picked him up for a fee. His Iranian money was worthless — the driver chortled. Gilbert used his last dollars to get to the Basra airport. Broke, he remembered a new American Express card in his wallet and got a flight to Baghdad, then to London.
- Arriving late Friday at Heathrow, Gilbert phoned BP's personnel department to announce that the last Western oil man from The Fields was finally out. The personnel officer, distracted by weekend plans, misheard and thought the caller was phoning with news of the missing engineer: "Jerry Gilbert — we've been wondering where he was. Have you been in contact?" That was the final indignity. From the open pay phone at Heathrow, summoning whatever fragments of strength he had left, Gilbert loudly and abusively cursed not only the hapless personnel man but everyone connected with the world oil industry.

### Panic Begins

- The old regime was gone in Iran, the new one in power but struggling with bitter internal fights. From Iran, as if shaken by a violent earthquake, a giant tidal wave surged around the world. All were swept up in it; nothing and no one escaped. When the wave finally spent its fury two years later, the survivors would find themselves on totally new terrain. Everything was different; relations among all of them were altered. The wave would generate the Second Oil Shock, carrying prices from thirteen to thirty-four dollars a barrel, and bringing massive changes not only in the petroleum industry but in the world economy and global politics.
- The new oil shock passed through several stages. The first stretched from the end of December 1978 (when Iranian exports ceased) to autumn 1979. The loss of Iranian production was partly offset by increases elsewhere. Saudi Arabia pushed up production from its self-imposed ceiling of 8.5 million barrels per day to 10.5 by the end of 1978, lowering to 10.1 million in the first quarter of 1979 — still well above its 8.5 million ceiling. Other OPEC countries also boosted output. But overall, free world production in the critical first quarter of 1979 was about 2 million barrels per day below the last quarter of 1978.
- There was an actual shortage — but against world demand of 50 million barrels per day, the shortage was no more than 4 to 5 percent. Why should a 4-5 percent loss of supplies have resulted in a 150 percent increase in price? The answer was the panic, triggered by five circumstances:
- (1) The apparent continued growth of oil consumption and the signal it gave to the market. Demand had risen smartly from 1976 onward; the impact of conservation and non-OPEC oil was not yet clear, and it was assumed demand would continue to rise.
- (2) The disruption of contractual arrangements within the oil industry. Despite major upheavals, world oil had remained integrated through long-term contracts. The Iranian interruption hit companies unevenly, depending on their dependence on Iran, and led to disruptions of contractual flow. Hosts of new buyers hurtled into the marketplace, scrambling to secure supplies. Here was the real end of the classic integrated oil industry — links between upstream and downstream were finally severed. What had been the fringe, the spot market, became the center. What had been a somewhat disreputable activity, trading, became a central preoccupation.
- (3) The contradictory and conflicting policies of consumer governments. The IEA emergency sharing system was still in development, with many aspects untested. Actions by governments for domestic reasons were read as major international policies, adding stress and tension.
- (4) The upheaval presented oil exporters with the opportunity to capture additional, enormously large rents. Most, though not all, kept pushing the price up at every opportunity, and some manipulated supplies to further agitate the market.
- (5) The sheer power of emotion — uncertainty, anxiety, confusion, fear, pessimism. These sentiments fueled and governed actions during the panic. After the fact, when numbers were sorted out, such emotions seemed irrational. But at the time they were indubitably real. The whole international oil system seemed to have broken down. And what gave the emotions additional force was the conviction that a prophecy had been fulfilled: the oil crisis expected for the mid-1980s had arrived in 1979. This was not a temporary disruption, but the early arrival of a deeper crisis which would mean permanently high prices. Would the Iranian Revolution spread to Kuwait, Riyadh, Cairo, and beyond? Religious fundamentalism had caught the Western world by surprise, and one of its driving forces was obvious: a rejection of the West and the modern world. That recognition led to "an icy, pervasive fear."

### Panic Buying and Inventory Hoarding

- Buyers, gripped by panic and fearing a repetition of 1973, inadvertently made the shortage worse by building up inventories. The world oil industry maintains billions of barrels in inventories — stocks necessary for the smooth operations of the "machine" that extended from wellhead to gasoline station. A particular barrel could take ninety days to travel from a Gulf wellhead through refining and marketing to a gasoline station.
- Companies did not want to hold more inventories than needed. If prices were expected to fall, they reduced inventories; if prices were expected to rise, they bought more of today's cheaper barrels. In the panic of 1979-1980, companies bought well in excess of anticipated consumption — not only because of price but because they were not sure they could get any oil later. This extra buying, combined with hoarding, dizzily drove up the price. The panic of 1979-80 was self-fulfilling and ultimately self-defeating, prophecy on a truly colossal scale.
- Down the consumption chain, industrial users and utilities also furiously built inventories as insurance against rising prices and possible shortages. So did the motorist. Before 1979, the typical Western motorist drove with his tank one-quarter full. Suddenly, worried about gasoline shortages, he kept his tank three-quarters full. Almost overnight, upwards of a billion gallons of motor fuel were sucked out of gasoline station tanks by frightened motorists.
- The rush to build inventories by companies, reinforced by consumers, resulted in an additional three million barrels per day of "demand" above actual consumption. Added to the two million barrels per day of net lost supplies, the total shortfall was five million barrels per day — about 10 percent of consumption. The panic buying more than doubled the actual shortage and further fueled the panic. That was the mechanism that drove the price from thirteen to thirty-four dollars a barrel.

### Force Majeure: The Domino Effect

- The panic might have been contained if the shortfall had been distributed evenly. But BP, as a result of its historical position, was far more dependent on Iran than any other company — fully 40 percent of its supplies came from Iran. BP was "crude long" — a wholesaler selling much of its oil through long-term contracts to third parties including other majors like Exxon and independent refiners, particularly in Japan. Having lost Iranian supply, BP invoked force majeure and cut back on its buyers. It canceled its supply contract with Exxon altogether, while scrambling to buy oil elsewhere. Neither BP nor Shell were Aramco members, so they had no direct access to the increased Saudi production.
- The dominoes began to fall. Other companies, deprived of oil by BP's cutbacks, also invoked force majeure. In March, Exxon, faced with an April 1 renewal date for its contracts with Japanese buyers, announced it was going to phase out many of its third-party contracts. Exxon had been warning buyers since 1974 to diversify and "not to count on Exxon." Chairman Clifton Garvin explained: "The handwriting had been on the wall. Venezuela had been taken away from us. We no longer had the concession in Saudi Arabia. We couldn't see a role in being the middleman between the Saudis and the Japanese consumer." Exxon had already begun winding down third-party contracts, but in the crisis mood, its March 1979 message took on unexpected significance.
- Japan was hit hard. After the first crisis, it had carved a niche in Iran — by 1978, Iran supplied almost 20 percent of Japan's total oil needs. Now the majors could no longer be counted upon either. Japanese refineries could not go idle. The government ordered the Ginza lights dimmed as an energy-saving measure and urged Japanese buyers to go directly into the world marketplace. The formidable Japanese trading companies scoured the world for supplies. One trading house discovered that giving gloves as presents to secretaries was an excellent way to get in to see the right officials; to cultivate the Iraqi oil minister, this same house provided the services of a world-class acupuncturist.
- Other independent refiners from many nations joined the Japanese in the frantic quest. So did state oil companies like India's. Suddenly, where there had been relatively few buyers, now there were many — a highly desirable situation from the sellers' point of view. All the action moved to the spot markets, which had comprised only about 8 percent of total supplies as a balancing mechanism. But as buyers rushed in, prices were bid up and up. By late February 1979, spot prices were double official prices. People called it the "Rotterdam Market" after Europe's huge oil port, but in fact it was a global market, connected by a fevered network of telephones and telexes.
- Exporters also invoked force majeure, canceling contracts. One morning Shell received a telex from an exporting country announcing a contracted supply was no longer available due to force majeure. That same afternoon, Shell received another telex from the same country saying crude oil was available on a spot basis — miraculously the same volume that had just been cut off. The only difference? The price was 50 percent higher. Shell took the offer.

### Iranian Oil Returns — But the Panic Continues

- At the beginning of March 1979, far more rapidly than expected, Iranian exports began to return to the world market, albeit at much lower levels than before the Shah's fall. Spot prices began falling back toward official prices. This was the moment when order might have been reestablished. IEA member countries pledged to cut demand by 5 percent. But the panic had its own momentum. Who could be confident Iranian oil would remain available? Khomeini had asserted control, but The Fields were actually controlled by a radical leftist "Committee of 60" — militant white-collar workers functioning as a virtual government of their own, jailing oil administrators at will. Moreover, other OPEC countries had begun to announce cutbacks in their own output — with prices rising, it was more valuable to keep oil in the ground and sell it later.

### Leapfrog and Scramble

- At the end of March, OPEC met. Spot crude prices had risen by 30 percent, products by as much as 60 percent. OPEC decided members could add whatever surcharges and premiums "they deem justifiable in light of their own circumstances." What this really meant, as Yamani bluntly allowed, was a "free-for-all." The exporters were abandoning any notion of an official price structure. They would charge whatever the market would bear.
- Now there would be two games: "leapfrog" — producers vying with each other to raise prices; and "scramble" — a bruising competition for supply among anxious buyers. Companies that had been cut off, refiners, governments, traders, and of course the majors trampled one another in their rush to court the various exporters. None of this fevered activity brought forth any new supplies; all it did was intensify competition for existing supply and drive up the price. "Nobody controlled anything," said Shell's supply coordinator. "You just fought for it. At every level, you felt you had to buy now; whatever the price, it was good compared to what it would cost you tomorrow. You had to say 'yes' or you would lose out. That was the psychology of the buyer. Horrible as the terms might be from your point of view, they would be worse tomorrow."
- Only one exporter stood clearly in opposition to the surcharges, premiums, and rapid price increases — Saudi Arabia. Having fought further increases ever since the 1973 quadrupling, the Saudis now objected to the leapfrog because short-term winnings would be followed by large and perhaps ruinous losses. Oil would price itself out of its competitiveness in energy markets; Middle Eastern producers would again become the residual supplier, shunned on grounds of energy security.
- The Saudis issued what became known as the "Yamani Edict" — Saudi Arabia would keep to official prices with no surcharges. The four Aramco companies were required to sell at those official prices to both their own affiliates and third-party buyers. If Saudi Arabia found companies adding premiums, there would be hell to pay — they would risk having their access to Saudi oil cut off. Saudi Arabia was virtually alone among exporters in this position, with only the UAE as an ally. There was much behind-the-scenes pressure and outright imploring from Western nations.

### Saudi Production Cuts and Three Mile Island

- Yet in Q2 1979, the Saudis cut production back to the pre-crisis "ceiling" of 8.5 million barrels per day. Despite their insistence on keeping to official prices, that cutback helped send spot prices soaring. Multiple reasons were proffered: sending a conciliatory signal to Khomeini's new Islamic regime by making room for returning Iranian production; expressing dissatisfaction with the Camp David peace accords between Israel and Egypt (signed March 26); focusing on conservation of oil reserves and "the whole question of production in excess of actual revenue needs"; or simply assuming the crisis was easing. Whatever the reason, only Saudi Arabia had the kind of spare capacity that could quell the panic — the role once held by the United States.
- Hours after the latest OPEC meeting adjourned (early morning March 28), a pump failed and a valve malfunctioned at the Three Mile Island nuclear plant near Harrisburg, Pennsylvania. Hundreds of thousands of gallons of radioactive water poured into the reactor building. Days of near-panic followed. The event raised large doubts about nuclear power's future and threatened the Western assumption that nuclear would be a major line of response to the 1973 oil crisis. Did Three Mile Island mean the industrial world would find itself more dependent on oil than expected? The accident contributed to gloom, pessimism, and fatalism across the Western world.
- David Howell, British Secretary of State for Energy, declared: "Friends, we are living dangerously." Western governments' efforts to mobilize demand cutbacks were proving insufficient, but they were loath to invoke the IEA's emergency oil sharing system for fear of introducing more rigidity. Governments were torn between two contradictory objectives: obtaining low-priced oil and guaranteeing secure supplies at any price. Energy questions had become "short, short, short-run politics." Western governments became champions of aggressive worldwide acquisition hunts through companies and state-to-state deals, producing suspicion and finger-pointing among supposedly allied nations.

### The Return of Gas Lines in America

- For the American public, gas lines snaking for blocks around stations became the embodiment of the panic — the nightmare of 1973 had returned. There was a genuine shortage of gasoline: refineries geared to Iranian light crude couldn't produce as much gasoline from the heavier crudes they were forced to substitute. Inventories were low in California, and after news reports of spot shortages, all 12 million vehicles in the state seemed to show up at once.
- Emergency regulations made matters worse. Some states prohibited buying more than $5 of gasoline at a time, forcing motorists to return more frequently. Price controls limited the conservation response — decontrolled prices might have ended the lines quickly. The federal allocation system froze distribution patterns on a historical basis, denying the market flexibility to move supplies to match demand. Gasoline was in short supply in major urban areas but abundant in rural and vacation areas "where the only shortage was of tourists."
- The nation was rationing gasoline through the mechanism of gas lines themselves. And gas lines begat more gas lines: a typical car used seven-tenths of a gallon an hour idling in line. One estimate: America's motorists in spring and summer 1979 wasted 150,000 barrels of oil a day waiting in line to fill their tanks.
- Oil companies were once again public enemy number one. Charges flew thick and fast — companies withholding oil, tankers held offshore to drive up prices, deliberate hoarding. Clifton Garvin, chairman of Exxon, decided to "go public" to refute the accusations. A cool, measured chemical engineer and passionate bird watcher, Garvin appeared on the Phil Donahue Show — a first for the chief executive of the world's largest oil company. But whenever he explained the basic logistics of inventories, the interviewers' eyes glazed over. Garvin on the public mood: "The American is a funny person. He worships the result of things that are big, economies of scale, mass production, but he hates anything that is big and powerful, and the oil industry is seen as the biggest and most powerful industry." One day at his local Exxon station in Greenwich, Connecticut, the dealer offered to let Garvin skip to the front of the line. "I'm sitting right here," Garvin said firmly.

### Petroleum and the President — Carter's Crisis

- The gas lines marked the beginning of the end of Jimmy Carter's Presidency. Carter was both the Preacher (moral rehabilitation of post-Watergate America) and the Engineer (micro-managing the political machine). Energy and oil had been his number-one domestic focus, and now he confronted the very crisis he had warned against. By mid-March 1979, his chief White House energy adviser Eliot Cutler warned of "darts and arrows coming at us from all directions."
- In April, Carter delivered a major speech announcing decontrol of oil prices (infuriating liberals) coupled with a "windfall profits tax" on "excess" oil company earnings (infuriating conservatives). A special Presidential task force met repeatedly to find a solution to the gasoline shortage — the only quick fix was to get the Saudis to increase output again. In June, Carter sent the American ambassador to Riyadh with an official letter and a personal handwritten note imploring increased output. The ambassador met for hours with Prince Fahd, head of the Supreme Petroleum Council.
- Carter went to Vienna to sign SALT II with Brezhnev — normally a landmark achievement through three administrations. But it simply did not count. The only thing that mattered was the gas lines, and they were Carter's fault.

### "The Worst of Times"

- A survey by the American Automobile Association of 6,286 service stations showed 58% were closed Saturday June 23 and 70% closed Sunday June 24 — leaving Americans with very little gasoline on the first weekend of summer. Independent truckers conducted a rowdy, violent nationwide strike (three weeks running) to protest fuel shortages and rising prices. A hundred truckers snarled rush-hour traffic for thirty miles on the Long Island Expressway. Inflation was moving to unprecedented levels.
- Support grew for a huge "synthetic fuels" program to reduce dependence on imported oil. Three Mile Island had closed the door on nuclear. The alternative: produce several million barrels per day of synthetic fuels through hydrogenation of coal (as the Germans had done in WWII) and heating of shale rocks in the Rockies to 900 degrees Fahrenheit. Cost: tens of billions of dollars, years to implement, major environmental consequences, uncertain whether it would actually work at scale. Politically, the concept seemed increasingly irresistible.
- At the Tokyo economic summit (July 1979), Carter found the conference "harsh and acrimonious." German Chancellor Helmut Schmidt "got personally abusive toward me... He alleged that American interference in the Middle East trying to work for a peace treaty was what had caused the problems with oil all over the world." On Margaret Thatcher: Carter found her "a tough lady, highly opinionated, strong-willed, cannot admit that she doesn't know something."
- Stuart Eizenstat, chief White House domestic policy adviser, dispatched a grim memorandum from Tokyo: "Nothing else has so frustrated, confused, angered the American people — or so targeted their distress at you personally... In many respects, this would appear to be the worst of times. But I honestly believe that we can change this to a time of opportunity." Carter's approval rating had plummeted to 25%, matched only by Nixon's in his final days. He retreated to Camp David with a 107-page dissection of the national mind by pollster Patrick Caddell.
- In July, the Saudis pushed output from 8.5 to 9.5 million barrels per day, heeding the implorings from the US and responding to their own security assessment. But it was not a long-term solution and could not immediately cool the hot temper of the American public.
- Carter embraced the synthetic fuels plan: 2.5 million barrels per day by 1990, primarily from coal and shale oil (originally wanted 5 million bpd but was talked down). In his July "crisis of confidence" speech, though he did not use the word, it became known as Carter's "malaise" speech.
- Carter forced the resignations of five Cabinet members — including Treasury Secretary Michael Blumenthal and Health Secretary Joseph Califano. His political advisers Hamilton Jordan and Jody Powell had convinced him they were disloyal. Eizenstat argued strenuously against firing Califano and Blumenthal. Just before a Cabinet meeting, Carter told staff he wanted all Cabinet members to submit resignations; some staff tried to dissuade him, warning it could create a panic. Robert Strauss, walking in late and not knowing what had transpired, jokingly said everyone ought to resign. "Bob, shut up," whispered a colleague. "They had all just resigned." The aim was to bolster Presidential leadership; it had the opposite effect. The Washington Post's national editor muttered that "America's central government had just collapsed."

### The Cat-and-Mouse Dialectic

- Spot prices eased off slightly in summer 1979, but OPEC countries continued to cut output. Iraq extended its embargo to Egypt to punish Anwar Sadat for the Camp David accords. Nigeria, in the most dramatic use of the "oil weapon" since 1973, nationalized BP's extensive holdings in retaliation for alleged indirect sales to South Africa, then auctioned off its newly nationalized supply at higher prices.
- Buyers continued their quest to build up inventories and fill storage tanks to the brim, driven by fear and the assumption that demand was continuing to grow. This was a fatal miscalculation — a decline had already set in, reflecting conservation and an economic downturn, but the fall was almost imperceptible at first. Shell's supply coordinator: "Every negotiation with a producer government was a finger-biting exercise: there was one dominant thought in the mind of company presidents and negotiators alike — to hold on to term oil and limit the need for spot acquisition." A cat-and-mouse dialectic began, with contract terms and prices becoming "continuously worse."

### Information Failure and the Rise of the Traders

- As in most panics, information — or the lack of it — was the key. If timely, credible, widely accepted data had existed, companies would have recognized earlier that they were increasing stocks to unnecessarily high levels and that underlying demand was weakening. But there was not much in the way of such statistics, and what early indicators did exist were not given much attention. So the stock build continued unabated, as did rising prices.
- Price increases were not limited to OPEC. Britain's new state oil company BNOC raised prices on its desirable North Sea crudes and was for a moment even leading the market. "If BNOC, and by implication the British Government, were behaving like OPEC, who could expect OPEC to put an end to the oil price spiral?" With the exception of Saudi Arabia, OPEC countries lost no time catching up.
- The market was further churned by traders, for whom the volatility, disarray, and confusion was a heyday. Some were from established commodity trading houses, some had entered the business after 1973, and some were johnny-come-latelies whose only capital requirement was a telephone and a telex. They were everywhere, vying with traditional oil companies for ownership, as cargoes still on the high seas were sold and resold — one cargo fifty-six times over. A single cargo on a supertanker could be worth $50 million.
- The raison d'etre for the traders was the breakdown of the integrated systems of the majors. In the old days, oil stayed within a company's integrated channels or was swapped among companies. Now state oil companies accounted for larger and larger shares of total production, had no downstreams of their own, and sold to a wide spectrum of buyers. Traders exploited the enormous arbitrage between lower term-contract prices and higher, more volatile spot prices. "The trader could be in a superb position. All he had to do was manage to get himself a term contract of some sort. He could then turn around and sell it for eight dollars a barrel more on the spot market." How did the trader get his contract? "A ridiculously small commission to the appropriate parties. And sometimes the required brown envelopes would be passed."
- By summer and early fall 1979, the world oil market was in a state of anarchy whose global effects far exceeded those of the early 1930s (Dad Joiner's East Texas discovery) and the earliest days of the industry. While producers and traders swelled with money, consumers were forced to dip ever deeper into their own pockets. For the exultant exporters, "there was no limit, they thought, to what the market would bear." Some in the Western world feared what was at stake was "not only the price of the world's most important commodity, not only economic growth and the integrity of the world economy, but perhaps even the international order and world society."

### "The World Crisis" — Schlesinger's Farewell

- Among those leaving Carter's Cabinet that summer was James Schlesinger. Depressed by the unfolding situation in energy markets and international politics, Schlesinger gave a farewell speech invoking Winston Churchill's The World Crisis (his WWI history). Churchill had written of his efforts to convert the British Navy from coal to oil, despite the risk of depending on oil from Iran. Now, six decades later, "eerily and uncannily, that risk had become reality."
- Schlesinger warned: "Today we face a world crisis of vaster dimensions than Churchill described half a century ago — made more ominous by the problems of oil. There is little, if any, relief in prospect. Any major interruption — stemming from political decision, political instability, terrorist acts or major technical problems — would entail severe disruptions... The energy future is bleak and is likely to grow bleaker in the decade ahead." He would later say of himself, "I'm not a brooder," but "there was nothing more that he could do about the situation." With that foreboding valedictory, he departed public life. Very shortly after, "the pessimism reflected in his remarks, and the dark anxieties about what he saw as growing Western vulnerabilities and what others were now calling the decline of the West, were to take on yet more bizarre and devastating significance."

## Chapter 34: "We're Going Down"

### The Embassy Seizure — November 4, 1979

- Shortly after 3:00 a.m. Washington time, November 4, 1979, Elizabeth Ann Swift, the political officer in the US embassy in Tehran, got through by phone to the Operations Center on the seventh floor of the State Department. She reported that a large mob of young Iranians had broken into the embassy compound, surrounded the chancery building, and were forcing their way into other buildings. An hour and a half later, she reported the attackers had set fire to part of the embassy. Still another half hour later, some invaders had threatened to kill two unarmed Americans. The table and sofa blocking the door had been pulled aside. Swift continued in a professional, almost matter-of-fact way as the Americans were being tied. "We're going down" were her last words, before a young Iranian with a picture of Khomeini pinned to his shirt grabbed the phone. Swift and the other Americans, all blindfolded, were led into captivity. The line remained open for a long time, though no one was there. Then it went dead.
- Some 63 Americans — the skeleton staff remaining after the embassy was scaled down from 1,400 officials in the Shah's time — were taken hostage by a large, rowdy, violent band of zealots thereafter known as "students." Some were soon released, leaving 50 in captivity. The Iranian Hostage Crisis had begun, and the Second Oil Shock entered an even more dire geopolitical phase.

### The Shah as Pariah and the Trigger for the Hostage Crisis

- The specific grievance of the hostage takers focused on Mohammed Pahlavi and America's relation to him. His father, Reza Shah, had found exile in South Africa. But the son became a modern Flying Dutchman — he could find refuge in no port. He went to Egypt, Morocco, the Bahamas, Mexico, but no one wanted him. He was a reject, a pariah, and virtually no government wanted to risk the ire of the new Iran. All the courting, flattery, ingratiation, the respectful premiers and supplicating cabinet ministers — "it was as if none of that had ever happened."
- Cancer and related illnesses were ravaging the Shah's body. Remarkably, it was only at the end of September 1979, more than eight months after he left Iran, that senior American officials first learned the Shah was seriously ill. Only on October 18 did they discover it was cancer. Carter had adamantly refused to allow the Shah into the US for treatment. But after months of controversy, compounded by a vigorous campaign by Henry Kissinger, John McCloy, David Rockefeller, and others, the Shah was admitted. He arrived in New York October 23 and checked into the New York Hospital, Cornell Medical Center, under a pseudonym that happened to be the real name of US Undersecretary of State David Newsom. His presence was immediately known and widely reported.
- A few days later, Zbigniew Brzezinski attended the 25th anniversary of the Algerian revolution in Algiers, where he met with the new Iranian Prime Minister Mehdi Bazargan. Bazargan protested the Shah's admission to the US. The news of this meeting, combined with the Shah's arrival, alarmed Bazargan's theocratic rivals and young militant fundamentalists. The Shah's presence in the US stoked memories of 1953 — Mossadegh's fall, the Shah's flight to Rome, his triumphant return. It aroused fear that the US was about to stage another coup. And here was Bazargan trucking with Brzezinski, "one of the chief agents of the Great Satan," just a week and a half after the Shah's arrival. This provided the impetus and pretext for the embassy invasion.

### "Death to America" and the Hostage Crisis Unfolds

- The embassy seizure was perhaps originally intended as a sit-in, but it soon turned into an occupation and mass kidnapping, as well as a bizarre circus — vendors in front of the embassy sold revolutionary tape cassettes, shoes, sweatshirts, hats, and boiled sugar beets. The occupiers answered the embassy phone: "Nest of spies." Khomeini and his circle took advantage of the crisis to dispose of Bazargan, consolidate power, eliminate opponents, and put in place the elements of the theocratic regime. The hostage crisis would grind on for almost fifteen months — 444 days. Every day, Americans read about "America in Captivity." Each night, they were subjected to the televised spectacle of "America Held Hostage" with its repetitive chorus of "Death to America." (ABC, with its late-night hostage programs, finally found a way to compete against Johnny Carson and the Tonight Show.)
- The hostage crisis transmitted a powerful message: the shift of power in the world oil market in the 1970s was only part of a larger drama. The US and the West "seemed to say, were truly in decline, on the defensive, and, it appeared, unable to do anything to protect their interests." Carter: "They have us by the balls."
- Iran was not the only scene of unrest. In late November 1979, some seven hundred armed fundamentalists seized the Great Mosque in Mecca, intending it as the first stage of an uprising against the Saudi government. They were dislodged only with difficulty. The larger uprising never materialized, but it sent shock waves through the Islamic world. In early December, there was a Shia protest in al-Hasa, in the heart of the Saudi oil region. Then, in December, the Soviet Union invaded Afghanistan — the first large-scale use of Soviet military forces beyond the communist bloc since WWII. Russia "was still intent on fulfilling its century-and-a-half-old ambitions to drive toward the Gulf."
- President Carter responded in January 1980 with the Carter Doctrine: "An attempt by any outside force to gain control of the Persian Gulf region will be regarded as an assault on the vital interests of the United States of America, and such an assault will be repelled by any means necessary, including military force." This echoed Harry Truman's 1950 pledge to Ibn Saud and bore striking similarities to the Lansdowne Declaration of 1903, by which Britain had warned off Russia and Germany from the Persian Gulf.

### Carter's Fall and the Shah's Death

- Carter had earned great respect in the oil world in 1977 as the man who forced the Shah to bend on prices. He had been "the magician who had tamed the Shah." But now the Shah was an outcast, the Iranian Revolution had sparked the oil panic, and Carter's Presidency was held hostage by events in Iran. After the hostages were taken, the dying Shah quickly and apologetically left the US, spending his final hours before departure in pathetic isolation in a psychiatric ward, with barred windows, on an American Air Force base. He went to Panama and then back to Egypt, where he finally died in July 1980, "a year and a half after his flight from Tehran. No one really cared." Mohammed Pahlavi, son of an officer from the Cossack brigade, had become irrelevant to the hostage crisis, to the panic in the oil market, and to the international game of nations.
- Carter responded to the hostage taking by placing an embargo on Iranian oil imports and freezing Iranian assets. The Iranians counterattacked by prohibiting oil exports to any American firm. The freezing of assets hurt Iran; the oil import ban did not. But it necessitated a redistribution of supplies around the world, further disrupting supply channels and sending more frantic buyers into the spot market, bidding prices to new heights. Some cargoes went for $45/barrel; Iranians quoted $50 to worried Japanese trading companies. The dislocation amplified panic buying. As a major executive remarked four days after the hostage taking: "In this environment companies feel a need for higher stocks than previously considered normal." The inventory build was, in industry language, "supply protection" — in other words, insurance.
- The hostage crisis demonstrated the apparent weakness, "even nakedness," of the consuming countries, particularly the United States. It seemed to establish that world mastery really did lie in the hands of the oil exporters. But there were forces at work in the oil market even more powerful than governments — and now it was the turn of the exporters to make fatal miscalculations of their own.

### The Bazaar — Caracas OPEC Meeting, December 1979

- Rising oil prices were the object of constant attention by presidents and prime ministers — but also of intense dismay for the Saudi leaders. They were alarmed by their loss of control and the fact that power had passed to militants like Libya and Iran. They thought wildly rising prices threatened the world economy with recession, depression, or even ruin — thus threatening their own well-being. The days when Saudi Arabia's economic future was determined by the number of pilgrims coming to Mecca were long past; now the "rates" that mattered to Riyadh were world interest rates, exchange rates, inflation rates, growth rates. The Saudis also feared that price increases would destroy consumers' confidence in oil and stimulate long-lasting competition from alternative fuels — particularly threatening to a country sitting on reserves extending far into the twenty-first century.
- The Saudis responded with jawboning: Yamani became more of a hawk on conservation than almost any Western leader. They held down their own official prices, even if it meant leaving money on the table. They also sought to counteract rising prices by pushing production up. "We're losing control over everything," Yamani declared plaintively in mid-October 1979. "We feel so unhappy. We don't like to see it happening like this." Then came the hostage drama, and prices spiked again and again.
- At the Caracas OPEC meeting (late December 1979), OPEC's pricing structure was in total disarray: Saudi Arabia's official price was $18/barrel; others were as high as $28; spot prices were in the $40-$50 range. The Saudis announced a $6 increase to $24, hoping others would bring prices down into line. It didn't work — the Iranians immediately leapfrogged by another $5. Once again the sharpest fissure was between Saudi Arabia and Iran.
- In 1979, OPEC production was back up to 31 million barrels per day — even with the Iranian shutdown, 3 million bpd higher than 1978. Where was the extra oil going? Not into actual consumption, Yamani was sure, but into the inventories of companies fearful of future interruptions. "Political decisions cannot permanently negate the divine laws of supply and demand," Yamani explained. "Prices go up, and demand goes down, it's simple, it's ABC."
- At the Tamanaco hotel, Yamani moved into the top-floor Presidential suite and campaigned privately with other oil ministers. For eleven hours they argued in his suite. He warned that demand was already showing signs of weakening, that continuing leapfrogging would bring a "catastrophe in the world economy," and that they would eventually have to cut production to protect prices — then prices would collapse anyway. One oil minister said Yamani must be joking; another that he was obviously on drugs. There was no agreement whatsoever — indeed, there was no official price at all. "OPEC and the oil market," Yamani said dismissively, "had turned into a bazaar." He also had a warning for the other producers and a promise to consumers: "There will be a glut in the market. It is coming." Prices would fall.
- The other exporters ignored the admonition. "In the name of almighty God, there will be no surplus and prices will not fall," intoned the Iranian oil minister. Most exporters assumed demand was so inflexible they could dictate whatever prices they wanted. Immediately after the meeting, Libya, Algeria, and Nigeria raised prices once again.

### The "Miniglut" and the Failed Rescue Mission

- Caracas in late December 1979 was the moment when exporters lost touch with reality. Demand was indeed weakening, new supplies were being developed, inventories were being built up, spot prices were declining. And the Saudis continued their steady overproduction. Other producers continued to push up prices while some cut back on production — helping buoy prices. There was now talk of a "miniglut," but that was more than offset by a new "minipanic" as Washington sought a general embargo against Iran, accentuating nervousness.
- In April 1980, frustrated to the extreme by the hostage impasse, Carter mounted a military rescue operation. Eight helicopters were dispatched from the carrier Nimitz to a desolate spot dubbed Desert One in Iran, to rendezvous with six C-130s carrying assault teams. The squads were to retake the embassy, free the hostages, and get them to a secured airfield near Tehran. But things went awry: one helicopter dropped out for navigational problems, another for mechanical malfunction. Three Iranian vehicles, including a bus with forty-four people, passed close enough to observe the Americans. In a blinding sandstorm, one of the remaining helicopters collided with a C-130 and burst into flames, killing several servicemen. Only five helicopters remained; the mission required a minimum of six. It was aborted on direct orders of President Carter. The Iranians dispersed the hostages throughout Tehran.
- The general outlook was bleak. The "mini-glut" was expected to be over by spring 1981. OPEC's Long Range Strategy Committee planned for 10-15% annual price increases — meaning $60/barrel within five years. The CIA director, testifying five days after the failed rescue, warned: "Politically, the cardinal issue is how vicious the struggle for energy supplies will become." A Foreign Affairs article in summer 1980 was titled "Oil and the Decline of the West."
- OPEC met again in Algiers in June 1980. The Saudis, now joined by the Kuwaitis, again tried to end the bazaar — to no avail. The average oil price was $32/barrel, almost three times what it had been a year and a half earlier. Yamani, sitting in the coffee shop of the Algiers hotel, being shunned by the other delegates, unburdened himself to a friend: "They're too greedy, they're too greedy. They'll pay for it."
- The oil market was sagging under the distress Yamani had been predicting. Inventories were very high; a pronounced recession was emerging; both product prices and demand were falling; the inventory surplus was continuing to swell. Companies were even beginning to store oil in supertankers rather than sell it at a loss. Now it was the buyers' turn to walk away from contracts. In mid-September, a number of OPEC countries agreed to voluntarily cut back production by 10% to firm prices.

### OPEC's Twentieth Anniversary and the Outbreak of War

- The twentieth anniversary of OPEC was approaching. A grand celebration was being planned for a November summit in Baghdad, where OPEC had been founded in 1960. An official history was commissioned, a film made, 1,500 journalists invited. On the morning of September 22, 1980, OPEC ministers assembled at the Hapsburg Palace in Vienna to continue planning. But within minutes of the meeting's opening, it erupted into confusion, anger, and chaos — and was turned into a closed session. Something else had already been planned in Baghdad.

### The Second Battle of Qadisiyah: Iraq versus Iran

- On September 22, 1980, squadrons of Iraqi warplanes attacked a dozen targets in Iran without warning, and Iraqi troops began pushing into Iran along a broad front with heavy artillery. The outbreak of war shook the Persian Gulf yet again and threw the oil supply system into jeopardy, threatening a third oil shock.
- The war was sparked by a host of rivalries: ethnic, religious, political, economic, ideological, personal; a struggle for primacy in the Gulf; the insecurities of national cohesion; and the arbitrary way "nations" had been created and borders overlaid on the map of the defunct Ottoman Empire. Geography was decidedly at the heart of the conflict.
- The critical geographic issue was the Shatt-al-Arab, a meandering river and delta formed by the confluence of the Tigris and Euphrates with several Iranian rivers, serving as the boundary for 120 miles between the two countries. It was crucial to Iran as its avenue to the Gulf — the Abadan refinery was built on a mud flat in the river delta. It was critical to Iraq as virtually its only avenue to the high seas — Iraq's entire coastline was only twenty-six miles long, compared to Iran's fourteen hundred miles. A considerable part of both countries' oil infrastructure was concentrated around the Shatt-al-Arab.
- The Shah and the Ba'thists had sorted out their claims in an Algiers agreement in 1975, signed by Saddam Hussein. Iran got the better deal on sovereignty — Iraq gave up its forty-year insistence that the boundary was on the Iranian side, accepting the midpoint of the navigational channel instead. In return, the Shah cynically agreed to cut off his considerable aid to the Kurds (20% of Iraq's population), who were fighting the Ba'thists for autonomy in a region where much of Iraq's oil was found. The Shah's jettisoning of the Kurds was a major quid pro quo, perhaps a requisite for the Ba'thist regime's survival. Baghdad launched a decisive anti-Kurdish offensive just six hours after the Algiers communique. Three years later, in 1978, at the Shah's request, Iraq expelled Ayatollah Khomeini, who had been living in exile in Iraq for fourteen years — an act that "hardly turned out to be a favor at all."

### Saddam Hussein's Rise

- The Ba'th ("renaissance") movement grew out of an Arab Students Union formed by two Syrian intellectuals in 1930s Paris. It was militantly pan-Arabic, fervent in its denunciation of Western imperialism, all-embracing in ideology, contemptuous and hostile to opponents. It celebrated violence and absolutism. The party split into two branches — one took power in Syria, the other in Iraq — which became irreconcilable rivals despite their common origins.
- Saddam Hussein's father died just before his birth in 1937. The decisive influence on him was his uncle Khayr Allah Talfah, a fervent Sunni Arab nationalist who hated and despised European culture. For both uncle and nephew, the lodestar event was the pro-Nazi Rashid Ali coup of 1941, in which German planes attacked British forces in Iraq. Talfah participated in the coup; when it failed he was imprisoned for five years, leaving him with lasting bitterness and hatred that he communicated to his fatherless nephew. The Rashid Ali coup became a central myth of the Ba'thist movement.
- Saddam was shaped by the culture of his hometown Tikrit — remote from national life, oriented to the harsh desert. Tikrit's values of "suspicion, stealth, surprise, and the unalloyed use of force" were the ones Saddam absorbed. He was recruited into the Ba'th party as a teenager during the Suez crisis (1956). Shortly after joining, he reportedly carried out his first assassination — of a local political figure in Tikrit. In 1959, he was one of the assailants in the assassination attempt on Iraq's ruler Abdul Karim Kassem on Baghdad's main street. The attack failed; Hussein, wounded, fled to Egypt under a death sentence, not returning until 1963. He took charge of the Ba'th party's underground militia and became the strongman behind the Ba'thist regime that took power in 1968.
- He assumed the Presidency in 1979, replacing Ahmad Hasan al-Bakr (cousin of his uncle) — only in the course of a purge in which many Ba'thist party members were executed. To ensure the imprisoned Ba'thists provided appropriate confessions, Saddam took some of their families hostage. By 1979 he was known as a shaqawah — an implacable tough, "ruthless and emotionless when it came to those he considered as enemies, threats, obstacles to his objectives, or simply useful or convenient to run over."
- The new Iraqi regime was dominated by Tikritis, many related to Hussein. The government banned the use of names indicating clan, tribe, or locality. At the top sat members of Hussein's Talfah family and two other closely related families. His cousin and brother-in-law Adnan Khayr Alah Talfah was Minister of Defense. His cousin and son-in-law Hussein Kamil al-Majid became chief weapons buyer, responsible for nuclear and chemical weapons development.
- The influence of uncle Khayr Allah Talfah continued to be felt. In 1981, the government printing house distributed a pamphlet by Talfah titled Three Whom God Should Not Have Invented: Persians, Jews, and Flies.

### Khomeini vs. Hussein — The Road to War

- Khomeini held Hussein personally responsible for his troubles, ranking him among his preeminent enemies. When asked to list his enemies, Khomeini replied: "First, the Shah, then the American Satan, then Saddam Hussein and his infidel Ba'th Party." Khomeini's circle saw the Ba'thists as implacable enemies of their own creed and attacked Ba'thism as "the racist ideology of Arabism." Khomeini denounced Hussein as a "dwarf Pharaoh."
- Saddam had good reason to fear Khomeini's diatribes. Around half of Iraq's population was estimated to be Shia, while the Ba'thist regime was secular and based on the minority Arab Sunnis. Iraq was also the site of the holiest shrines of the Shia faith, and Shia agitation, fed from Iran, was growing. After an assassination attempt against his Deputy Prime Minister in April 1980, Hussein ordered the execution of the most prominent Shia ayatollah in Iraq and, for good measure, the ayatollah's sister. He denounced Khomeini as "Khomeini the rotten" and "a Shah dressed in religious garb."
- Iraq thought it saw its opportunity. Iran appeared disorganized and chaotic — "There is a government on every street corner in Iran." Iran's army was demoralized and in the grip of a bloody purge. Iraq could strike hard, topple Khomeini, end the Shiite revolutionary threat, assert sovereignty over the Shatt-al-Arab, and protect Iraq's oil position. There were even more enticing plums: Hussein could appeal to ethnic Arabs in Iran's Khuzistan (though less than half the population was of Arab descent) as their "liberator" and perhaps incorporate that territory, which the Iraqis called Arabistan. The prize was not merely fraternal reunification — 90% of Iran's oil reserves happened to be in Khuzistan. On top of all this, the Ba'thists could eradicate the wound of pride from the humiliating 1975 Algiers agreement. The Shah was gone; Hussein could assert Iraq's primacy, emerge as the new leader of the Arab world (with Egypt alienated by Camp David), and become one of the dominant oil powers. The opportunities were irresistible.
- Hussein billed the war as the "Second Battle of Qadisiyah" — the first having occurred in A.D. 636/637 near Najaf, when the Arabs defeated the Persians. That victory led to a further triumph in 642 celebrated as the "Victory of Victories," which sealed the doom of the Sassanid Persian Empire.

### Iraq's Blitzkrieg Fails — The Human Wave Assaults

- Hussein targeted the heart of the Iranian oil industry — Abadan and Ahwaz. He thought he could achieve all his objectives with a blitzkrieg of hard, sharp blows. In Vienna, at the disrupted OPEC meeting, the virtually universal assumption was that the war would be over in one or two weeks. But the Iraqi strategy proved a grave miscalculation: the Iranians withstood the first blow and struck back immediately at Iraqi targets. The assault actually enabled Khomeini to further consolidate his power, silence his critics, dispose of nonclerics in his government, and proceed with fashioning the Islamic Republic while mobilizing the population to resist.
- Iranians from virtually all political camps rushed to the common defense. The Arabs of Khuzistan showed no desire to be liberated by Iraq and did not welcome the Iraqis as "brothers." The Iraqis were unprepared for the "human wave" assaults — hundreds of thousands of young people, drawn by the Shiite vision of martyrdom, advanced on Iraqi positions in front of regular Iranian troops. Some arrived carrying their own coffins, exhorted by Khomeini that "the purest joy in Islam is to kill and be killed for God." They were given plastic keys to heaven to wear around their necks. Children were even used to clear minefields for the far more valuable tanks, and thousands of them died.

### The End of the Road — The Third Shock That Wasn't

- The war shook the oil market. On September 23, 1980, the second day of the war, Iraqi warplanes began a sustained assault against the Abadan refinery (the world's largest), proceeding over the next month to damage it severely. They also attacked every Iranian oil port and oil city. The Iranians counterattacked against Iraqi facilities, completely choking off Iraqi exports through the Gulf. Iran persuaded Syria (ruled by a rival Ba'thist party) to cut off Iraqi pipeline exports through Syria, leaving Iraq with only one limited pipeline through Turkey. Iranian exports were reduced, but Iraq's almost ceased — something Hussein had not counted on.
- In its initial stages, the Iran-Iraq war abruptly removed almost 4 million daily barrels — 15% of total OPEC output and 8% of free world demand. Spot prices jumped again. Arab light reached its highest price ever: $42/barrel. Was this the Third Shock? One reading of economic indicators pointed to a new shock; the other pointed in the opposite direction. Oil demand was certainly weakening — whether from recession (temporary) or conservation (lasting) was unclear. Economic contraction had already begun, compounded by a new resolve to fight inflation at all costs, even if it meant deep recession.
- Governments, working through the IEA, had learned the lessons of 1979 and concerted efforts to persuade companies not to buy in panic but to draw down inventories. The IEA message: things were manageable, this was not 1979, go easy, avoid "undesirable purchases." The message made more sense this time because since early 1979, companies had spent a great deal buying up supplies far in excess of demand. Those extra barrels had gone not into engines, furnaces, or power plants, but into storage. The Great Panic had turned into the Great Inventory Build, and when the war broke out, storage tanks around the world were brimming over, with supertankers chartered as additional floating storage.
- But the war reignited panic buying. "No matter what restraint we show," complained one refiner in November 1980, "there is still someone else out there who is willing to buy at higher prices and thereby pushes up the market." The all-important question was how companies would manage inventories. An American company executive: "Commercial firms can't afford such moves. Suggestions that we draw down inventories imply some knowledge of when the crisis will end. If I knew Iraq and Iran output would be back to prewar levels by July, sure I would draw down stocks." But he couldn't know.
- In December 1980, OPEC ministers met in Bali to discuss price. The Iranian oil minister had been captured by the Iraqis while touring the battlefield near Abadan — no one had bothered to tell him the area was in enemy hands. OPEC or no OPEC, Iraq would not free him. The Iranians were so angry they threatened to boycott all OPEC meetings. Indonesia's oil minister Dr. Subroto worked out a satisfactory compromise on seating (normally alphabetical, which would put Iran and Iraq next to each other): he inserted Indonesia between them. The Iranian delegation entered carrying a large portrait of their captured oil minister, which was placed in his designated chair. The conference concluded with another rise in OPEC prices, to a $36/barrel marker for all except the Saudis. A Third Shock was at hand — or so it seemed.

### The IEA Confrontation with Japan — D'Avignon's Threat

- At almost the same moment, energy ministers of the industrial nations gathered in Paris at the IEA. The mood was gloomy; the IEA's efforts to encourage inventory drawdown instead of panic buying were not meeting with great success. Japanese trading companies' frenetic purchasing had emerged as a particular sore subject. Near midnight, Count Etienne D'Avignon of Belgium, a forceful Commissioner of the European Community, lost his patience with the Japanese representative: "If you don't get your trading companies under control, you can forget about any more Toyotas and Sonys coming into Europe." The Japanese official paused: "You are a great international civil servant," he said at last. And he said no more.
- But MITI reinforced its "administrative guidance" to companies to go easy. By the end of 1980, the picture was becoming clearer: inventories remained very high, demand was continuing its precipitous fall, and market prices were weakening. It was becoming more and more uneconomic to hold on to stocks, creating a growing incentive to use inventories instead of buying additional oil.

### Saudi Overproduction and the Market Turns

- Not only was consumption declining, but supply from other sources was making up for the lost output from Iran and Iraq. The Saudis had been cranking out barrel after barrel to stifle the price surge. "We engineered the glut," Yamani once said, "and we want to see it in order to stabilize the price." Within days of the first battles, they pushed production up another 900,000 bpd, to the very limit of sustainable capacity — an increase equivalent to almost a quarter of the lost production from the two belligerents. Other OPEC producers also upped output, and even some oil from Iraq and Iran was coming back. Production from Mexico, Britain, Norway, Alaska, and other non-OPEC countries was continuing to increase. It was no longer just a "miniglut." Buyers were beginning to revolt against high prices. Non-OPEC producers cut their official prices to increase market share — their gain was OPEC's loss. OPEC's output in 1981 was 27% lower than 1979 output, the lowest it had been since 1970. Yamani's prophecy was at last coming true.

### The October 1981 Compromise and the End of Chapter 34

- OPEC was nearing the end of the road, though neither exporters, the industry, nor Western consuming countries had any idea of what lay ahead. Carter's Presidency had also ended: the hostages were not released until the very day he left office, succeeded by Ronald Reagan, whose buoyant confidence was more palatable to the electorate than Carter's "malaise."
- The oil market was responding to the phenomenal rise in prices over the 1970s and consumers' fears for the future. Yet the exporters were still unwilling to face up to the shifting "objective conditions" of the marketplace. They would not contemplate a price cut. Finally, in October 1981, they came to a new agreement: Saudi Arabia would raise its price from $32 to $34/barrel, while the others agreed to bring their price down from $36 to $34. Prices would be reunified. For the other producers, the compromise did mean a price cut. Saudi Arabia agreed, as part of the deal, to go back down to its old 8.5-million-barrel-per-day ceiling.
- Iraq and Iran remained locked in bitter battle. Yet even a war between two of the most important exporters could only retard, not cancel, the powerful forces set in motion by the two oil shocks. October 1981 represented the last time that the OPEC price would go up, at least for a decade. The "divine laws of supply and demand" were already in motion to drive prices down, "though not yet with the thunderous vengeance that was still to come. It was, as Yamani had said, as simple as ABC."

## Chapter 35: Just Another Commodity?

### The Greatest Boom of Them All

- None of the previous booms in an industry characterized by booms could rival the magnitude and madness of the fever at the end of the 1970s with the Second Oil Shock. It was the greatest boom of them all. With oil at $34/barrel, sums of money dwarfing anything ever before seen were involved. Oil companies plowed earnings back into new development, borrowed from banks, raised money from eager investors, leveraged themselves to the hilt. It was the golden age of the independent oil men — slapping backs, hiring drilling rigs, exploring at greater depths.
- To celebrate it all, at the very end of the 1970s, the television show Dallas went on the air, introducing the rapacious J.R. Ewing — replacing the lovable Clampetts of The Beverly Hillbillies — fashioning the image of the independent American oil man for years to come.
- In the US, the industry surged to a dizzy and unprecedented level of activity. Costs went out of control. Acreage, real estate in oil cities (Houston, Dallas, Denver), and drilling crew costs skyrocketed. Young geology graduates were wined, dined, and paid $50,000/year for their first job. Geologists with twenty years' experience quit the majors to put together deals, dreaming of becoming the next H.L. Hunt or J. Paul Getty. Doctors and dentists poured money into drilling funds, told their savings could be devastated by inflation and rising oil prices if they didn't have oil in their portfolios.

### The "Oil Mountain" and Exxon's Colony Shale Oil Project

- The industry was thought to be standing at the perilous edge of the "oil mountain" — supplies would begin precipitously falling away. Depletion combined with OPEC militancy would guarantee high and rising prices for an increasingly scarce commodity. Technology would have to create alternatives, setting a ceiling price for oil. This meant shale oil locked in the rock formations of the Western Slope of the Rockies in Colorado and Utah would finally be brought to market — exactly what Carter had proposed in 1979.
- Occidental, Unocal, and others were already working on shale oil technology. In 1980, Exxon hastily bought its way into the Colony Shale Oil Project on the Western Slope, spending fully a billion dollars on development, getting ready for the "new era" of energy. "Exxon had had a love affair with shale oil for a long time," recalled Clifton Garvin.
- But over the subsequent two years, the economic outlook changed quickly and drastically. In real terms, the oil price was going down; so was demand. Surplus production capacity was building. Colony Project cost estimates kept going up — "$6 or $8 billion for 50,000 barrels per day." One night Garvin told himself: "I can't spend shareholders' money that way." On May 2, 1982, Exxon announced tersely that it was terminating the Colony Project.
- The boom on Colorado's Western Slope ended literally in hours. The towns of Rifle, Battlement Mesa, and Parachute fell prey to the great tradition established at Pithole, Pennsylvania (1865-1866) — from dense forest to boom town of 15,000, to eerie ghost town. In Colorado, newly built homes were empty, apartments unrented, construction workers packed up and left, teenagers vandalized partly built homes and offices. "My business just died," said the owner of an office supply shop in Rifle. The boom to end all booms could not last.

### The Fundamentals — Recession, Conservation, and Non-OPEC Supply

- The US Federal Reserve responded to virulent inflation with exceedingly restrictive monetary policy — the prime rate reached 21.5% at one point. Tight money combined with the drain of spending power from oil price increases produced the deepest recession since the Great Depression, with two bottoms (1980 and the more severe 1982). The developing world, which was supposed to be a major source of new oil demand, instead went into steep economic decline — debt-laden, hit by recession in the industrial world — choking off their oil demand.
- Fundamental changes were taking place in the energy economy. Earlier fears of shortage (1920s, mid-1940s) had ended in surplus and glut because rising prices stimulated new technology and development of new areas. The pattern was now repeated at $34/barrel. Huge new developments were taking place outside OPEC: Mexico, Alaska, the North Sea all coincided with the Second Oil Shock. Egypt was also becoming a significant exporter. So were Malaysia, Angola, and China. Major innovations were improving exploration, production, and transportation technologies. The Alaskan pipeline's initial capacity of 1.7 million bpd was boosted to 2.1 million bpd with the addition of a chemical nicknamed "slickem" that reduced drag. Many things in exploration and production at $34/barrel that were not economical at $13 became viable. American oil production actually increased in the first half of the 1980s.
- The massive twentieth-century march toward higher dependence on oil within the total energy mix was reversed by higher prices, security considerations, and government policies. Coal staged a massive comeback in electricity generation and industry. Nuclear power made a rapid entry into electricity generation. In Japan, liquefied natural gas increased its share. Oil's share of the market for total energy in industrial countries declined from 53% in 1978 to 43% by 1985.
- Conservation had turned out to have massive impact. Energy conservation meant not deprivation but "greater efficiency and technological innovation." The 1975 legislation mandating a doubling of average fuel efficiency of new automobile fleets to 27.5 mpg by 1985 would reduce US oil consumption by 2 million bpd — just about equivalent to the 2 million bpd of additional production from Alaska. By 1985, the US was 25% more energy efficient and 32% more oil efficient than in 1973. If the US had stayed at 1973 efficiency levels, it would have used 13 million barrels of oil more than it actually did in 1985. Japan became 31% more energy efficient and 51% more oil efficient over the same period.
- By 1983, oil consumption in the noncommunist world was 45.7 million bpd — about 6 million bpd less than the 1979 high of 51.6 million. So while demand fell 6 million bpd between 1979 and 1983, non-OPEC production increased by 4 million bpd. On top of that, companies were eagerly disposing of the tremendous inventories built up in anticipation of demand that never materialized. Those three trends — the collapse in demand, the relentless buildup of non-OPEC supply, and the Great Inventory Dump — reduced the call on OPEC by about 13 million bpd, a fall of 43% from 1979 levels.

### Finally — The Cartel

- As late as 1977, OPEC had produced two-thirds of total free world crude. In 1982, for the first time, non-OPEC overtook OPEC production — and was still rising. Even Soviet oil exports to the West were substantially increasing. Much of the new oil, particularly from the North Sea, was sold on spot markets, making it very responsive to market conditions. Spot prices for a particular quality oil could be as much as $8/barrel lower than term contract prices — the difference between "a profit margin" and "formidable losses." Any buyer doing the simplest arithmetic was going to "go spot." New non-OPEC producers offered the most "market-responsive" (cheapest) prices to win market share.
- OPEC was confronted with an unpalatable choice: cut prices to regain markets, or cut production to maintain price. They did not want to reduce prices for fear of undermining their whole pricing structure and their newly acquired power and influence. They also feared that if they cut prices, industrial countries would raise excise and gasoline taxes, transferring oil rents from OPEC's treasuries to their own.
- In March 1982, OPEC (which had produced 31 million bpd in 1979) set an output limit of 18 million bpd, with individual quotas for each country except Saudi Arabia, which would adjust its production to support the system. OPEC had finally done what it had talked about — it had taken on the part played by the Texas Railroad Commission, turning itself into a true cartel, managing and allocating production as well as setting a price.
- New factors added to uncertainties: Iran was gaining the upper hand in the war with Iraq, becoming more belligerent toward Saudi Arabia and the Gulf states. In June 1982, Israel intervened in Lebanon. There was discussion of another embargo against the US as "punishment," but it was quickly scotched as irrelevant and dangerous in a distressed market. Meanwhile, King Khalid of Saudi Arabia died in June 1982 and was succeeded by Prince Fahd, who had already been the country's chief administrator and was the Royal Family's oil expert.
- By autumn 1982, the new quotas were already failing: demand was not recovering, non-OPEC production was still rising, and spot prices were plunging again. OPEC oil was still overabundant and overpriced.

### "Our Price Is Too High..." — The March 1983 London Meeting

- In 1983, competition mounted rapidly. The British North Sea sector alone was now producing more than Algeria, Libya, and Nigeria combined, with more coming on stream. Unofficial discounting and price cutting became the norm among OPEC countries. Saudi Arabia alone maintained the $34 marker — but buyers were forsaking Saudi Arabia in favor of discounted oil, even including the Aramco partners. Saudi production fell to its lowest level since 1970.
- Yamani offered a philosophical disquisition on the crisis: "Please excuse the comparison, but the history of the crisis is similar to that of a pregnant wife... The crisis started just like a normal pregnancy — with passion and joy. At this moment other members wanted us to raise the price of oil even higher despite our warnings... We were consumed with our moments of pleasure. But now the consequences had to be faced. Our price is too high in relation to the world market."
- By late February 1983, complete collapse seemed at hand. BNOC cut North Sea oil by $3 to $30/barrel — devastating to Nigeria, a country of 100 million whose economy was dangerously overdependent on oil. Nigerian oil competed directly with North Sea crudes, and Nigeria's normal buyers deserted the African country for cheaper North Sea oil. Nigeria virtually stopped exporting oil. "We are ready for a price war," Yahaya Dikko, the Nigerian oil minister, said firmly.
- In early March 1983, the oil ministers convened hurriedly at the Intercontinental Hotel at Hyde Park Corner, London — ironically the home court of their leading non-OPEC competitor, Great Britain. For twelve interminable, frustrating days they wrangled. OPEC slashed its prices by about 15% — from $34 to $29/barrel. It was the first time in OPEC's history that it had ever done such a thing. They also agreed on a 17.5-million-barrel-per-day quota for the whole group. OPEC had reset its price to meet the market — not a rising market price as in the past, but a falling one.
- One country had no official quota: Saudi Arabia. Yamani insisted the number would have been well below the 6 million bpd minimum acceptable to Riyadh. Instead, Saudi Arabia would "act as the swing producer to supply the balancing quantities to meet market requirements." For the first time, Saudi Arabia was explicitly charged with raising and lowering its output to balance the market and maintain the price. But OPEC's new system depended on twelve members not cheating and on Saudi Arabia's willingness and ability to play that pivotal role of swing producer.

### The Commodity Market — Oil's Transformation

- Behind OPEC's mutation into a true cartel was a far-reaching transformation of the oil industry itself. No longer would it be dominated by large, highly integrated companies. Instead, it would turn into a free-for-all, a clanging world of multitudinous buyers and sellers. Oil was becoming "just another commodity."
- Oil had always been a commodity — prices fluctuated wildly in the 1860s and 1870s. But the constant thrust toward integration had internalized price volatilities within the workings of a company, tying everything from the wellhead to the gasoline pump. Yamani warned: "Oil is not an ordinary commodity like tea or coffee. Oil is a strategic commodity. Oil is too important a commodity to be left to the vagaries of the spot or the futures markets, or any other type of speculative endeavor."
- But that was exactly what began to occur. Two forces drove the transformation: (1) The buildup of a huge surplus meant producers now had to worry about their access to markets, rather than consumers worrying about access to supplies. Buyers expected discounts, not security premiums. Security was hardly an issue anymore — what mattered was to be competitive in a glutted market. (2) The changing structure of the industry — exporting countries had assumed ownership of oil resources and international marketing, breaking the links that had tied their reserves to particular companies, refineries, and markets overseas. Companies, shorn of direct access to supplies, sought new resources elsewhere — but also had to find a new identity or become obsolete.
- Companies' focus shifted from long-term contracts to the spot market. Until the end of the 1970s, no more than 10% of internationally traded oil was found in spot markets — a fringe activity, a way to sop up excess refinery output. By the end of 1982, after the upheaval of the Second Oil Shock, more than half of internationally traded crude was either in the spot market or sold at prices keyed to spot.

### BP Leads the Way — Deintegration

- BP led the way into the new trading world, by no choice of its own. As a result of the upheaval in Iran and nationalization in Nigeria — on top of previous nationalizations in Kuwait, Iraq, and Libya — BP lost 40% of its supply.
