Desperately exposed, it went out into spot markets and began buying and trading oil on a larger and larger scale. The new BP could shop around for the cheapest crude, push efficiency throughout its operating units, and beat the competition. It became much more decentralized, with individual units responsible for their own profitability. The corporate culture changed from one dominated by the supply planner to one dominated by "traders and commercial people." BP's new chairman P.I. Walters: "It is nice to have some integration, obviously, but it is not something we would pay a premium for. We see ourselves as being much more opportunistic."
- Walters had long concluded that traditional integration, increasingly governed by computer models, did not make sense. The revelation came in June 1967, during the Six-Day War, when Aristotle Onassis abruptly canceled all chartering arrangements and offered BP his entire fleet at double rates. BP had until noon to decide. Walters realized "no computer program could help him now, only commercial judgment." He accepted Onassis's offer and returned to mowing his lawn. By Monday, tanker rates were four times what they had been on Friday. From that day, Walters became a campaigner for deintegrating BP's operations: "The proponents of further integration were moving in the wrong direction. They were handing over to machines what should be management judgments." He became famous for telling managers "there are no sacred cows in BP."

### The Aramco Partners Retreat and US Decontrol

- The four Aramco partners — Exxon, Mobil, Texaco, and Chevron — though somewhat cutting back, had continued taking large volumes of Saudi oil at "official prices" far above competitive crudes. The fundamental precept had always been to preserve access to Saudi oil. But in 1983-1984 they had to acknowledge the price for access was too high. George Keller, chairman of Chevron: "We couldn't keep pouring money down a hole. We had to back away, and ultimately we had to tell Yamani that we couldn't continue to do this." The Aramco links were not broken but significantly reduced. The alteration in the Aramco-Saudi Arabia commercial relationship was "one of the great symbols of how the oil industry was being transformed."
- The shift toward the commodity market was facilitated by the decontrol of US oil prices and the elimination of various controls. The US was no longer insulated from the world oil market — it was now tightly tied into the rest of the market. American output accounted for almost a quarter of total free world production, and one particular stream of American crude would become the new bellwether for the global industry.

### From Eggs to Oil — The Birth of NYMEX Crude Futures

- The emergence of West Texas Intermediate (WTI) as the benchmark reflected another momentous innovation: in 1983, the New York Mercantile Exchange (Nymex) introduced a futures contract in crude oil. This took place on the eighth floor of the World Trade Center, not in Vienna, Riyadh, or Houston.
- Futures markets allow buyers and sellers to minimize risk: a buyer acquires the right to buy at a specific future price (locking in costs), and a producer can sell forward (locking in revenue). Both are hedgers. "Liquidity" is provided by speculators hoping to profit from market psychology and supply-demand swings. Futures had existed for commodities like grain and pork bellies for years. In the 1970s, as the world economy became more volatile, futures emerged for gold, interest rates, currencies, and finally oil.
- The Nymex had not exactly enjoyed a distinguished career. Founded in 1872 (the same year Rockefeller launched "our plan" to take over the industry), it began as the Butter and Cheese Exchange for sixty-two New York City merchants. Eggs were added; it became the Butter, Cheese, and Egg Exchange, then the New York Mercantile Exchange. The Maine potato was the mainstay until the supply-demand balance changed and potato scandals hit (including the "mortifying failure of delivered stocks of potatoes to pass inspection"). The exchange abruptly terminated trading in Maine potatoes and was itself threatened with extinction.
- Just in time, the Nymex introduced a home heating oil contract, then gasoline futures (1981), and on March 30, 1983 — just two weeks after OPEC concluded its marathon London meeting — the exchange introduced futures in crude oil. "The juxtaposition was ironic, for the crude oil futures contract would resolutely undermine OPEC's price-setting powers."
- Floor traders took enthusiastically to crude futures. The initial reaction from established oil companies was "skepticism and outright hostility." A senior executive of one of the majors dismissed oil futures "as a way for dentists to lose money." But within a few years, most major oil companies, some exporting countries, and large financial houses were all participating. As volume built up astronomically, "Maine potatoes became a distant, quaint, and embarrassing memory."
- The evolution of price-setting: First it had been Standard Oil. Then the Texas Railroad Commission system and the majors. Then OPEC. Now price was being established every day, instantaneously, on the open market, in the interaction of floor traders on the Nymex with buyers and sellers glued to computer screens worldwide. "It was like the late-nineteenth-century oil exchanges of western Pennsylvania, but reborn with modern technology." The benchmark was WTI — a plentiful, easily traded crude stream and a good proxy for the world oil price. Two decades earlier, Arab light had supplanted Texas Gulf Coast oil as the world's marker crude. Now, in almost full circle, it was back to Texas. The price of WTI joined the gold price, interest rates, and the Dow Jones as among the most vital measures of the daily beat of the world economy.

### New Oil Wars: The Shootout at Value Gap

- With restructuring of global markets, the oil industry went through wholesale corporate reorganization. Deregulation removes protection and increases competitive pressure, typically resulting in consolidations, spinoffs, takeovers. Oil was completely deregulated by 1981. Overcapacity and weakening prices encouraged consolidation. Institutional investors (pension, mutual fund, money managers) — who controlled three-quarters of the stock of major American corporations — were becoming more aggressive, insisting on higher returns and unwilling to wait around for the long term. The oil industry was losing its luster after the boom.
- At the heart of the restructuring was the "value gap" — when a company's shares did not fully reflect what its oil and gas reserves would fetch. Companies with the greatest gap between stock price and asset value were most vulnerable. New management could enhance "shareholders' value" — and it could cost two or three times more to add a barrel by exploration than by buying another company's assets. The obvious implication: it was cheaper to "explore for oil on the floor of the New York Stock Exchange" — buy undervalued companies — than to explore under the topsoil of West Texas or the seabed of the Gulf of Mexico. Many companies had taken the huge cash flows from the two oil shocks and put them right back into exploration in the US, seeking secure alternatives to OPEC. The results were very disappointing — reserves were still declining, the expenditure proved inefficient and wasteful.
- The value gap facilitated a great upheaval — a series of corporate battles pitting company against company, with Wall Street warriors mixed in and sometimes in command. It was an entirely new kind of oil war.

### The Trigger — T. Boone Pickens

- The trigger for the upheaval was found in Amarillo, Texas — a town of 150,000 on the high, flat, dry Panhandle plateau. Oil and gas was the biggest business, run mostly by small independents. Cattle was another major business. So was nuclear weaponry — Amarillo was the nation's only center for final fabrication of nuclear bombs, producing four warheads a day.
- T. Boone Pickens, more than anyone else, detonated the explosions that remade the corporate landscape of oil. In the financial community, Pickens was widely applauded — he made things happen, enhanced shareholders' value. In the oil industry, while admired by some, he was loathed by others. He said he was pushing the industry "back to basics, fighting its self-indulgent waste." His adversaries said he was "merely a clever opportunist, with a gift for salesmanship, who wrapped old-fashioned greed in the mantle of shareholders' rights." Pickens saw the vulnerabilities of the oil industry on the backside of the Second Oil Shock earlier and with more clarity than most. On one level, his campaign "represented the revenge of the independent oil man on the hated majors."
- Born in 1928, Pickens grew up in the oil patch near Seminole, Oklahoma (site of one of the greatest Oklahoma discoveries). His father was a landman; his mother was in charge of gasoline rationing for three counties during WWII. He was "brash, self-confident, independent-minded, sharp-tongued, and outspoken." He did not readily accept the established order and "hated to lose." He went to work for Phillips after studying geology, but could not stand the bureaucracy. A boss told him: "If you're ever going to make it big with this company, you've got to learn to keep your mouth shut." In 1954, after three and a half years, he quit Phillips to become an independent, hustling around the oil states, using phone booths as offices, shaving in gas station restrooms.
- By 1964, Pickens had rolled up his various drilling deals into Mesa Petroleum. After Mesa went public, he became fascinated by the gap between stock value and the value of underlying oil and gas assets. He fastened his eyes on Hugoton Production — a sleepy but considerably larger company with extensive gas reserves in the nation's largest gas field in southwestern Kansas. Its stock price was much lower than what its reserves would have fetched. In 1969, he completed a hostile takeover of Hugoton and merged it into Mesa, creating a significant independent.
- Pickens was an inveterate trader, a veteran of futures long before most in the oil industry had even heard of them. His early speciality was cattle futures — at one point he took Mesa into the cattle-feeding business, making it the second-largest cattle feeder in the nation (the venture ended poorly). Even at the height of the oil wars of the mid-1980s, with billions at stake, Pickens would look out the window as his plane descended over the Texas range and start counting cattle to decide whether to go long or short on cattle futures. "It was sport."

### Pickens's Corporate Raids Begin

- Pickens had been an intense basketball then racketball player — speed, fast breaks, unexpected moves, quick reflexes, constant improvisation. That was the way he did business. "We used to crowd into Boone's office every Saturday morning, some of us sitting on the floor, and Boone would ask us how we were going to make money the next week." His style — game-planned, attentive to detail, but also highly improvisational — made him a tough rival for the big bureaucratic companies. His standard retort when hearing a competitor had done something he didn't like: "Tell them to go kiss a fat man's ass."
- By the early 1980s, Pickens was seeing the weaknesses: the US was a declining producer with increasingly poor prospects and disappointing discovery records. Stock prices of oil companies did not reflect the sale value of their proven reserves. It was Hugoton Production all over.
- His initial target (1982) was Cities Service — the nineteenth largest US oil company, thirty-eighth largest industrial corporation in the Fortune 500, three times the size of Mesa. But its stock sold for only a third of the appraised value of its oil and gas reserves. Mesa acquired a block of stock. Cities Service counter-tendered for Mesa. Gulf intervened with an offer for Cities at almost double its pre-brouhaha price, but then backed out. Finally, Armand Hammer's Occidental acquired all of Cities' stock. Mesa made a $30 million profit. That was the first move.

### Megamergers and the Boom Turns Sour

- Restructuring and megamergers were already spreading. Shell acquired Belridge (1979) for $3.6 billion — the largest corporate acquisition to that point. In 1981, Conoco escaped a Dome Petroleum takeover by falling into DuPont's arms for $7.8 billion. Mobil took a run at Marathon, which sold itself to US Steel for $5.9 billion (a way to diversify out of the disaster of the American steel industry). Mesa made a bid for General American, but Phillips picked it up for $1.1 billion.
- The global oil boom was turning sour. Exploration in the US fell away. Refinancings and bankruptcies among smaller companies jumped. Major companies started belt-tightening — cutbacks, hiring freezes, early retirements. Investors began forsaking the oil patch for the stock market; mutual funds and red-hot money managers were more interesting than oil, drilling programs, and geologists.

### The Mexican Weekend — Oil and the Global Financial Crisis

- Mexico had run up a huge international debt (over $84 billion by 1982) on the basis of its sudden emergence as a world oil power. Jesus Silva Herzog became Finance Minister in April 1982. To his shock, the country was on the edge of a grave economic crisis — the result of weakening oil prices, high interest rates, a severely overvalued peso, unrestrained government spending, and the drying up of markets for Mexico's non-oil exports. There was also immense capital flight. Mexico was unable to service its enormous debt.
- Silva Herzog began making secret Thursday-night trips from Mexico City to Washington to see Federal Reserve Chairman Paul Volcker. He arranged a $900 million emergency loan, but it was dissipated in a week by capital flight. On August 12, 1982, he concluded improvisation would not work. Mexico could default — but that might cause the international financial system to collapse. The loan exposure of the nine largest American money center banks to Mexico was equivalent to 44% of their total capital.
- On August 13, Silva Herzog flew to Washington — the episode that became "the Mexican Weekend." He told Treasury Secretary Donald Regan: "We have to organize something, or otherwise there will be very serious international consequences." Regan: "You really have a problem." Silva Herzog: "No, Mr. Secretary, we have a problem." They worked nonstop through Sunday morning, assembling a multi-billion-dollar package of loans, credits, and advance purchases of Mexican oil for the Strategic Petroleum Reserve. Near 3:00 a.m. Sunday, negotiations nearly broke down when Silva Herzog discovered a $100 million service fee buried in the agreement. He was furious: "This is not a business transaction. Sorry, I cannot accept this." He called President Lopez Portillo, who said to terminate discussions and return to Mexico City. Later that day, the US Treasury rescinded the fee. The Mexican Weekend concluded with the first part of the emergency package in place. What was devised was a debt moratorium — politely called a "rollover." Mexico had, at least in part, defaulted.
- Silva Herzog reflected: "I thought of my father and the role he played in the oil expropriation... And now here I was in Mexico's worst crisis since the one of 1938, and it was also a crisis involving oil. We had just committed terrible mistakes on the basis of oil. But there had been this great mood of victory... We thought we were rich. We had oil."
- The Mexican debt drama brought home the reality that the global oil boom was over, and that "oil power" was less powerful than assumed. Oil could mean not only wealth but also weakness. The world oil crisis was now giving way to the international debt crisis — many world-class debtors would turn out to be oil nations.

### Penn Square and Continental Illinois — The Banking Fallout

- At the same time Mexico was teetering, Penn Square — a tiny go-go energy bank in an Oklahoma City shopping center — was on the edge of insolvency. Its senior energy lending officer liked to drink Amaretto and soda out of his Gucci loafers. Penn Square had generated a huge volume of questionable oil and gas loans, then sold some $2 billion worth "upstream" to major money center banks: Continental Illinois, Bank of America, Chase Manhattan. The loan portfolio Penn Square retained was worthless; regulators closed it down.
- But that was not the end. Continental Illinois — largest bank in the Midwest, seventh largest in the nation, the fastest-growing lender, winner of awards for management, its chairman named "Banker of the Year" — had been the most aggressive energy lender. As a competitor put it, Continental was "eating our lunch" in oil and gas loans. When oil prices weakened, Continental Illinois was "walking on nothing more solid than thin air." In 1984, it was hit by the largest bank run in history — all around the globe, banks and companies yanked their money. The Federal government intervened with a huge bailout: $5.5 billion of new capital, $8 billion in emergency loans, new management. Continental Illinois had, at least temporarily, been nationalized. With its collapse, energy lending "instantaneously went out of fashion."

### Dr. Drill — The Mukluk Wildcat

- Half of the undiscovered oil and gas in the US was thought to be in Alaska or adjacent waters. All eyes focused on Mukluk — a vast underground structure fourteen miles off the north coast of Alaska, sixty-five miles northwest of Prudhoe Bay. It was billed as "the most exciting prospect to come around for a generation." BP geologists said the odds were one in three instead of the normal one in eight. The effort to drill cost over $2 billion — companies had to build their own gravel island from which to drill in the frigid Arctic waters.
- In the first week of December 1983, "Dr. Drill spoke" — at eight thousand feet below the seabed, where the pay sands were supposed to begin, the drill had struck salt water. Mukluk was dry. Oil had once been trapped there, but either the structure was breached (a gigantic oil spill with no environmental gauging) or regional tilting caused it to migrate into the Prudhoe Bay structure. "We drilled in the right place," said the president of Sohio's production company. "We were simply 30 million years too late."
- Mukluk was not only the most expensive dry hole in history; it was also the turning point for exploration in the US. Betting so heavily on exploration was too risky and too expensive. Managements would face penalties for continuing to risk and lose money on such a scale. In the minds of many senior executives, Mukluk sent a bracing message: they ought to shift from exploring for oil to acquiring proven reserves — buying individual properties or entire companies. After Mukluk, they were much more in a buying mood.

### Family Matters — Getty Oil and Texaco

- Not only economics and geology drove restructuring — so did hatreds, resentments, and feuds inside families. A war among the Keck family heirs resulted in Mobil's acquiring Superior Oil ($5.7 billion). But the most prominent family troubles were at Getty Oil. J. Paul Getty had died in 1976, and by the 1980s Getty Oil was not replacing its reserves and its stock was selling at a very low price relative to its holdings. Gordon Getty, one of J. Paul's sons (more interested in composing music — a song cycle based on Emily Dickinson's poems — than in searching for oil), wondered where all the value had gone. That put him at odds with the professional managers running Getty.
- Two knocks came. First, from Pennzoil — a large independent run by Hugh Liedtke, an early partner of George Bush and friend of Boone Pickens. In some manner, Getty assented to the Pennzoil offer. The second knock came from Texaco, whose chairman appeared at the Pierre Hotel (once owned by the elder Getty) to make a counteroffer. Gordon Getty definitely accepted. So Texaco got Getty Oil for $10.2 billion. It also got sued by Pennzoil.

### The Death of a Major — Pickens Goes After Gulf Oil

- In July 1983, Mesa was committed to spending $300 million on an exploration program — fifty-one drilling rigs, five very expensive ones in the Gulf of Mexico. "Boys," Pickens announced at a board meeting, "this is it. We've got to figure out a way to make $300 million, and we've got to make it fast. We've lost too much money in the Gulf of Mexico. We can't drill our way out of this one. A field goal won't do it — we need a touchdown."
- Pickens' eyes fastened on Gulf Oil — one of the Seven Sisters, built by the Mellon family on the basis of Guffey and Galey's discovery at Spindletop in 1901, a major American institution and global company. It had firmly planted the American flag in Kuwait. The Mellons had long since stepped aside from active management; the family had fragmented and sold off much of its holdings. Gulf was the most vulnerable of the majors — its stock was selling at little more than a third of its appraised value.
- Pickens had a close-in view of Gulf management during the Cities Service struggle and decided it was "ineffective and indecisive," with a heavy bureaucratic structure that would be slow to respond. Gulf had suffered ten years of internal problems and deep splits — illegal political contributions (some tied to Watergate), controversial foreign payments, a purge of senior management and replacement by managers whose "appearance of probity was among their most important qualifications." The chairman who took over was nicknamed "Mr. Clean" and "the Boy Scout." Gulf was the only major oil company to place a nun on its board of directors. "These problems had meant six years of no decision... during a crucial time in the oil industry, during the OPEC turmoil, and at a time when everything was up in the air in the Far East, and we were losing our ass in Europe."
- Gulf's woes were long. In 1975, its Kuwait concession (a substantial part of earnings) had been nationalized. It lost a costly antitrust case on uranium marketing. Despite vast sums spent searching for politically secure oil in the US, domestic reserves were falling rapidly — declining 40% between 1978 and 1982. It was not well-positioned and had lost "a great deal of its old raison d'etre as a global company undertaking a massive international exploration and production effort. It had not yet found a new one."
- The new chairman, Jimmy Lee (successor to "Mr. Clean"), embodied the evolution of the major oil companies. He had worked in Gulf's Philadelphia refinery in the late 1940s, built refineries and marketing systems in the Philippines and Korea, headed the entire Far Eastern operation, was Gulf's man in Kuwait during the Middle Eastern battles, and ran Gulf's entire Eastern Hemisphere from London. Now he was back in Pittsburgh to rebuild the battered company. But he wouldn't have much time.

### The Battle for Gulf Oil

- In August 1983, Mesa started accumulating Gulf stock through numbered bank accounts with transfer codes known only to one or two people. In October, Mesa formed the Gulf Investors Group (GIG), providing financial clout for the offensive. GIG's objective was to push Gulf into transferring half its US oil and gas reserves into a royalty trust owned directly by stockholders (eliminating double taxation on dividends).
- Gulf initiated a counterattack targeting its 400,000 shareholders. But Gulf's senior management was sharply divided on how to proceed, making it seem indecisive — just as Pickens had predicted. In contrast, Pickens was "quick, flexible, continually improvising, and imaginative." He knew how to court institutional shareholders, play to the public, and handle the press. He presented himself as "the champion of shareholders, an authentic oil man, a populist, not a faceless bureaucrat from 'the good ol' boys club' of 'Big Oil.'"
- Gulf managed to squeak to victory in the proxy vote in December 1983, by a bare 52% to 48%. But it was only a reprieve. Pickens submitted a written proposal to spin off oil and gas reserves directly to shareholders; the board turned him down flat. He went to see the junk bond king Michael Milken at Drexel Burnham in Beverly Hills to explore raising money for an outright takeover bid.
- Jimmy Lee knew time was short. In late January 1984, he took a call from Robert O. Anderson, chairman of ARCO, who wanted to discuss things of "material interest." They met for dinner at the Brown Palace Hotel in Denver. Anderson wanted Gulf's foreign production — he believed the future of the major oil companies lay in overseas reserves and "the international circuit." He was willing to pay $62/share for Gulf (a big increase over its $41 pre-Pickens price). Lee replied by suggesting the two companies merge their US oil operations, which would have given Gulf half of ARCO's immensely valuable North Slope reserves. Anderson said "no thank you."
- Then Anderson called Lee again: "I think I should tell you that I had dinner last night in Denver with Boone Pickens. I told him that we were prepared to pay $62 a share for Gulf." Lee: "Thanks for telling me this," restraining his sarcasm. Anderson's objective in meeting Pickens was to understand what Pickens was trying to do and to ascertain that he would not block any deal. But Lee saw it differently — as soon as he hung up, he summoned his crisis team: "Bob Anderson has just cut our legs out from under us. For all practical purposes, we're in play."
- Anderson's second call ended whatever hopes Lee had that Gulf could remain independent. "The game was up." There had been a long-observed aversion among major oil companies against making hostile offers for one another. But Anderson's proposition, following Mobil's run at Marathon, made clear that the rule was no longer valid. Now there was a price on Gulf's head. Lee decided to go for the best price, telephoning CEOs of the other large companies with the same message: "We're vulnerable... If you're interested in taking a look, start doing your financials."
- Pickens played the next card, topping ARCO's $62 offer with a $65 tender offer. Lee: "I knew that $65 was a low ball. If someone's going to take your company, you might as well get the most you can."

### Chevron Wins Gulf — $13.2 Billion

- George Keller, chairman of Chevron (descended from Standard Oil Trust's western operations, headquartered in San Francisco), had already become interested in Gulf. Chevron had an admirable record of taking risks and finding oil, including in Saudi Arabia in the 1930s. But like other top executives, Keller had been shaken by the Mukluk failure: "After that, almost everybody decided to put more money into better bets."
- On New Year's Eve 1984, the chairman of Getty Oil had called Keller asking if Chevron would look at Getty. Keller's team analyzed Getty against other companies: Superior, Unocal, Sun — and Gulf. Getty was soon acquired by Texaco, but Chevron was still looking closely at Gulf. After Jimmy Lee's second phone call, Chevron frantically set about valuing Gulf. By March 3, they had a range: Gulf was worth $62/share at the most pessimistic end; at the most optimistic, $105 — anywhere from $10.2 to $17.3 billion. "It was a hell of a range," said Keller. Chevron's board authorized up to $78/share, with the actual proposal at Keller's discretion. "For God's sake, put a ceiling on it," begged Keller, unnerved by the thought of sole responsibility — "Every dollar higher on the stock means another $135 million."
- On March 5, the Gulf board met at its ornate Pittsburgh headquarters (virtually deserted, as most operations had moved to Houston). There were three serious offers: ARCO's ($72/share), Kohlberg Kravis Roberts' ($87.50/share, 56% cash, 44% securities — a leveraged buyout), and Chevron's (unknown amount). ARCO's was dismissed as too low. KKR's offer was more money in theory but riskier — Gulf's financial advisers (Merrill Lynch, Salomon Brothers) could not figure out what the "paper" would be worth, and the outside directors worried that accepting a management buyout could appear self-serving.
- Keller, waiting his turn, had an offer letter with just one blank spot — the price. He knew the two great risks: crude prices going down and interest rates going up. He picked up his pen and filled in $80/share — $13.2 billion, all cash. The reception from the Gulf board seemed chilly. He returned to the Chevron floor to await the decision.
- After seven hours of debate, the phone rang. It was Jimmy Lee. "Hello, George. You just bought yourself an oil company." All Keller could think was that he felt like someone who had bid on a house for the first time and discovered, to his shock, that he now owned it. It was a $13.2 billion house. That was the end of Gulf Oil — Spindletop, Guffey and Galey, the Mellons, Kuwait, Major Holmes — it was all over. It was history.
- Anderson (ARCO) was philosophical about losing: his absolute limit was $75. "At least if you like to lose a merger, you like to lose by a wide margin rather than by a narrow one. You hate to lose by a dollar a share." As far as Pickens was concerned, it was a great victory for stockholders — during his campaign, Gulf's stock price went from $41 to $80, and total market capitalization was raised from $6.8 billion to $13.2 billion, a $6.5 billion profit for Gulf stockholders. "That was $6.5 billion that would have never been made if Mesa and GIG hadn't come on the scene." His Gulf Investors Group made a $760 million profit, of which about $500 million went to Mesa — after tax, just about the $300 million Mesa had so desperately craved. Pickens was the highest-paid corporate executive in America that year ($18.6 million deferred bonus).
- Jimmy Lee's first reaction was relief. He set out to talk to employees around the country, but "over the next several days, exhaustion took over. So did sadness, and he sometimes broke into tears. 'I never had any intention but that Gulf would be here for ever and ever... It had been my whole life, my whole career. To think that it wasn't going to be there any more really got to me.'" Keller: "It was a good buy... We were able to acquire assets on a scale that would never be available otherwise." Why was Gulf in trouble? "It had ignored its solid existing position. It had decided it had to have one great big elephant. It was as though, instead of risking its future in the town in which it lived, it decided to go to Las Vegas. It missed everywhere."

### Shareholders' Value — Phillips, Unocal, ARCO, and Exxon

- Pickens was not yet through. In rapid fire, he made bids for both Phillips (Bartlesville, Oklahoma) and Unocal (Los Angeles). On Phillips, he was trailed by Carl Icahn, who had already bagged Trans World Airlines. Both companies successfully fought off the takeover attempts through the courts and by assuming a great deal of debt, buying back stock at much higher prices — increasing the payout to shareholders. Mesa made significant profits in both cases. After Unocal survived, its chairman Fred Hartley received a call from Armand Hammer of Occidental, who told him "he deserved a Nobel Prize for his valor."
- ARCO itself, seeing it might be vulnerable "to a Pickens, or to the Pickens," carried out a sort of self-acquisition — leveraging to buy back its stock at a higher price while sharply consolidating its activities and employment. Robert O. Anderson: "We were a sitting duck unless we got our share values up more closely to the values of our company."
- Royal Dutch/Shell paid $5.7 billion for the 31% of Shell Oil U.S.A. it did not already own. BP, disillusioned with Sohio's management (including the Mukluk fiasco), paid $7.6 billion for the rest of Sohio's shareholders to own the company completely and control the huge cash flow from Alaska.
- Exxon, at least until the beginning of the 1990s, stayed away from the high-visibility arena of mergers. It had been badly stung by its poor acquisitions record (Fortune once named two of its purchases among the five worst acquisitions of the 1970s). Clifton Garvin: Exxon had "a phobia about acquisition." Instead, Exxon spent $16 billion on share buy-backs between 1983 and mid-1990, guaranteeing stockholders a rising stock price and good yield, and ensuring nobody could claim Exxon shareholders were getting the short end of the stick. It also cut the number of its employees by 40%.
- Restructuring meant a smaller and more consolidated petroleum industry. Beginning geologists were no longer hired at $50,000/year — they were not being hired at all. The biggest losers were the people whose jobs were wiped out. "I thought I was working for a great social institution," said one executive whose job disappeared in Chevron's acquisition of Gulf. "I didn't think I was giving 25 years of my life, with all the costs for my family, for some pieces of paper." The great beneficiaries were the shareholders — all the mergers, acquisitions, recapitalizations, and stock buy-backs propelled well over $100 billion into the pockets of institutional and individual investors, pension funds, arbitrageurs. The shareholders did, eventually, win out.

### The New Security — 1985 Bonn Summit

- In May 1985, the seven major Western powers met in Bonn. The themes were free market politics, deregulation, and privatization. Reagan had been reelected by an enormous margin. Thatcher was well embarked on her reconstruction of British society. Even Mitterrand had jettisoned nationalization in favor of free markets. The Western world was in its third year of relatively vibrant economic growth — but this recovery was fundamentally different from previous ones: it was not being fueled by a rise in oil demand. The economies had adapted to high oil prices, and oil use was flat.
- The only serious energy question that leaders had grappled with recently was a divisive early-1980s battle over European plans to increase imports of Soviet gas. The Europeans wanted energy diversification and reduced dependence on oil, plus employment in engineering and steel. The Reagan Administration opposed the plan, fearing Soviet political leverage over Europe and hard currency earnings strengthening the Soviet economy and military. Washington banned export of American equipment, then sought to prohibit European equipment containing American technology — setting off "the most severe European-American conflict since the October War and the embargo in 1973." Margaret Thatcher personally called Reagan: "John Brown is going under, Ron." She flew to Scotland to be present when John Brown began exporting equipment in explicit defiance of the American ban. A compromise was finally reached: Europeans would restrict Soviet imports to 30% of total gas, and Norway's huge Troll field would be promoted as an alternative source within NATO.
- The 1985 Bonn summit issues revealed how the world had changed: they were "West-West" issues — protectionism, the dollar, accommodating Japan's economic challenge. Oil and energy, the preeminent "North-South" issue, was not on the table at all. Oil was available in abundance and was not a constraint on economic growth. Excess oil capacity exceeded demand by 10 million bpd (20% of the free world's consumption). The US, Germany, and Japan were putting significant amounts into strategic petroleum reserves — restoring the "security margin" absent during the 1970s.
- Iran and Iraq were breaking all the supposed "taboos" in their continuing conflict — attacking each other's cities, refineries, oil fields, and tankers, including tankers bearing the flags of other nations. But the bombing of a tanker, which earlier would have sent oil prices shooting up, could now just as easily cause the price to go down on spot and futures markets.
- For the first time since these summits had been instituted a decade earlier, the leaders issued a communique in which there was nothing about oil and energy. Not a single word. The omission itself was a powerful statement — the world economy had adjusted and accommodated itself to the upheavals of the 1970s. Oil did not seem to need any special tending; it was indeed "just another commodity." Half of the equation — secure oil supplies — appeared to be coming back into place. The other half, however, was not there. Oil was still not cheap — not yet.

## Chapter 36: The Good Sweating: How Low Can It Go?

### The Price Question

- The price of oil was balanced precariously in the mid-1980s. As the president of Esso Europe said in 1984: "Today, the price of oil is the chief variable in our equation and the greatest single source of uncertainty about the future." The answer would have a profound impact not only on oil companies but would determine the future vigor of "oil power" in world politics and mightily affect global economic prospects and the shifting balance of world economic and political strength.
- High prices would favor oil exporters — from Saudi Arabia to Libya to Mexico to the Soviet Union (which depended on oil, along with natural gas, for most of the hard currency it used to purchase Western technology). Low prices would favor the oil-importing countries — Germany, Japan. In between was the United States, with interests on both sides: the world's largest importer and consumer, yet also the world's second-largest producer, and a good part of its financial system had cast its fate with high oil prices.

### OPEC Quotas Fail — Cheating and Barter

- Despite becoming even more restrictive in 1984, OPEC's quota system was not working. Non-OPEC production continued to grow; coal, nuclear power, and natural gas were still taking markets away from oil; and conservation still shrinking demand. As revenues dwindled, quota cheating became more obvious. OPEC retained an international accounting firm to police the quotas — but the accountants had great difficulty gaining entree to some OPEC countries and were "completely denied entrance to key facilities." Various exporters turned to barter and countertrade — exchanging oil directly for weapons, planes, and industrial goods — which had the effect of increasing the oversupply.

### High or Low? — BNOC Abolished

- In the 1970s, BNOC (British National Oil Company) bought up to 1.3 million barrels/day from North Sea producers and resold it to refiners, giving it an important price-setting role. But with weakening prices, BNOC found itself buying at one price and selling at a lower one — a significant loss for BNOC and the British Treasury. Margaret Thatcher, who saw "no security reason to maintain BNOC," simply abolished it in the spring of 1985. The elimination of BNOC "removed one more important prop from the OPEC price; it was another victory for the market."
- In the oil industry, the general view was that while the price might sink a few dollars, it would recover and start rising toward the end of the 1980s or beginning of the 1990s. Yet weak demand, growing supply capacity, and the shift to the commodity market all pointed much more strongly in one direction — down. But how far?

### OPEC's Deepening Dilemma — Saudi Arabia's Impossible Position

- By the mid-1980s, OPEC faced a critical choice: cut price (but where would a falling price stop?), or continue propping up price — which would be holding up an umbrella under which non-OPEC oil, competing energy sources, and conservation would all flourish, guaranteeing OPEC a shrinking market share. Even as the Iran-Iraq War dragged on, exports from both belligerents were recovering. Nigeria adopted a "Nigeria first" policy aimed at maximizing exports.
- Saudi Arabia, as swing producer since 1983, was bearing disproportionate costs. The high point of Saudi earnings was $119 billion in 1981. By 1984, revenues had fallen to $36 billion, and would fall further to $26 billion in 1985. The country had embarked on a great spending and development program, now had to cut back dramatically, was running a large budget deficit, and its foreign reserves were being drawn down. The promulgation of the national budget was indefinitely postponed.
- The loss of market was reducing Saudi Arabia to a more marginal role on the world stage — at a time when the Iran-Iraq War was threatening the region and Khomeini continued his vendetta against Saudi Arabia. Yamani on Saudi television: "Crude oil is a political power and no one can deny that Arab political power in 1973 was based on oil and that its influence reached its peak in the Western world in 1979... At present we are suffering because of the weakness of Arab political power based on oil. These are elementary facts which are known even to the man in the street."
- The Saudis sent warning after warning: they would not continue to accept loss of market share, would not indefinitely tolerate quota violations and increased non-OPEC production, could not be counted on to be the swing producer. If need be, Saudi Arabia would flood the market. Were these warnings serious? Without a change, Saudi production might fall to a million barrels per day or less — and Saudi Arabia "would on the world stage almost cease being Saudi Arabia."

### Market Share — King Fahd's Letter and Production Collapse

- In June 1985, OPEC ministers congregated at Taif in Saudi Arabia. Yamani read them a letter from King Fahd sharply criticizing the cheating that had led "to a loss of markets for Saudi Arabia." The King: "If Member countries feel they have a free hand to act, then all should enjoy this situation and Saudi Arabia would certainly secure its own interests." The Nigerian oil minister hoped "this wise message had sunk in." But in the following weeks Saudi production sagged to as low as 2.2 million barrels per day — half its quota, little more than a fifth of what it had been producing half a decade earlier. Exports to the US, which had been 1.4 million bpd in 1979, slumped to a mere 26,000 bpd in June 1985 — "essentially nothing."
- At times, Saudi output fell below that of the British North Sea sector. "Here was the last outrage" — the Saudis were propping up the price so the British could produce more while Thatcher celebrated her indifference to whether oil prices were high or low. The Iraqis were also reconstructing export capacity and adding new pipelines through Saudi Arabia. Something had to give.

### The "Good Sweating" — Netback Deals

- A shade from the past: in the late 19th and early 20th centuries, Rockefeller and his colleagues had instituted a "good sweating" against competitors by flooding the market and cutting prices. In the mid-1980s, circumstances were wholly different, yet not so different after all. "Once again, a 'good sweating' was at hand."
- The Saudis moved from a defense of price to a defense of volume — choosing an ingenious weapon: netback deals with the Aramco partners and other oil companies. Under these deals, Saudi Arabia would not charge a fixed price. Rather, it would be paid based on what the refined products earned in the marketplace. The refiner was guaranteed a predetermined profit off the top — say $2/barrel. No matter whether the final selling price of the products was $29 or $19 or $9, the refiner got his $2 and the Saudis got the rest. The refiner's profit was locked in, so he had no compulsion to strive for a higher price — he would simply want to move as much product as he could. "Increasing volume and reduced concern about the selling price would add up to a perfect recipe for falling prices."
- The Saudis were also careful not to be too confrontational — their aim was to regain their quota level, capping the volume covered by netback deals. They were aiming their new policy as much at other cheating OPEC members as at non-OPEC countries.
- As word circulated about the Saudi netback deals in late September/early October 1985, nervousness and anxiety rose. Other exporters started to follow suit out of pure competitive self-defense. For the long-suffering downstream part of the industry, netback deals were "a godsend, an opportunity at last to make money in refining, which had seemed almost beyond human ingenuity since the early 1970s."
- Most exporters expected no more than a drop to $18-$20/barrel, below which they thought North Sea production would not be economical. They were wrong — the tax rates in the North Sea were so high that in one field called Ninian, a drop from $20 to $10 would cost the companies only 85 cents; the British Treasury was taking most of the rents. Actual operating costs in Ninian were only $6/barrel. George Keller: "There is not much of a price floor."

### OPEC Declares War for Market Share — November/December 1985

- From the beginning of November 1985, the WTI futures price continued to climb, reaching its then-highest point ever — $31.75 on November 20, 1985 — belying the threat of a price collapse. Many thought the Saudis did not really mean what they were saying; it was merely an elaborate warning.
- A week and a half later, OPEC met. By its actions, Saudi Arabia had already declared a war for market share against the other OPEC countries. Now OPEC as a group announced its intention to battle non-OPEC to regain lost markets. The new formula: OPEC was no longer protecting price; its objective was to "secure and defend for OPEC a fair share in the world oil market consistent with the necessary income for member countries' development."
- When the communique was carried into a room of senior OPEC country planners, one said dismissively: "Oh, it's just another OPEC winter communique." Then the price began to collapse.

### The Third Oil Shock — Prices Plunge 70%

- WTI plummeted by 70% over the next few months — from its peak of $31.75 in late November 1985 to $10. Some Persian Gulf cargoes sold for around $6/barrel. The actual variation in OPEC output was marginal — 17.8 million bpd in early 1986 (only 9% above 1985, about the same level as the 1983 quota), and the additional production meant not much more than a 3% increase in total free world supply. But that, combined with the commitment to market share, was enough to drive prices to levels virtually unimaginable months earlier.
- It was the Third Oil Shock, but with all consequences running in the opposite direction. Now exporters were scrambling for markets rather than buyers for supplies. And buyers rather than sellers were playing leapfrog, each jumping over the other in pursuit of the lowest price. Security now had new dimensions: "security of demand" for the exporters — guaranteed access to markets. For consumers, it seemed all the concerns of the 1970s about security of supply were being made irrelevant. But would cheap imported oil undermine the energy security so laboriously rebuilt over the preceding thirteen years?
- Prices were also out of control. For the first time in memory, there was no price-setting structure at all — no official OPEC price, no standard. The market was victorious, at least for the time being. Price was set through thousands of individual transactions — net-back deals, spot deals, "spotback deals," barter deals, processing deals, "topping off" deals. OPEC countries were battling each other over customers. "Everybody gets tired of endless negotiations for each cargo or each quarter," said the head of the Iraq State Marketing Commission. "In the end, the lazy negotiator for the crude exporter simply gives across-the-board discounts... he undercuts all other OPEC barrels." It was not netbacks or any specific deal that caused the price collapse, but the fundamental fact that more oil was seeking markets than there were markets for oil, and the hand of the regulator (OPEC and Saudi Arabia) was removed.

### OPEC Division and the Non-OPEC "Dialogue"

- OPEC was bitterly divided. Iran, Algeria, and Libya wanted a new and much lower quota to restore price to $29/barrel. Saudi Arabia and Kuwait remained committed to regaining market share. Yamani told one of the major companies: "I never sold a barrel someone didn't want." Iran and Iraq were still locked in a death struggle, and Iran's hostility to the Arab exporters was unmitigated.
- Non-OPEC countries were suffering no less. Belatedly, they started a "dialogue" — Mexico, Egypt, Oman, Malaysia, and Angola attended an OPEC meeting as observers in spring 1986. Norway's conservative government had initially declared it would not negotiate with OPEC, but oil supplied 20% of government revenues and the budget couldn't be met. The ruling party fell and was replaced by its labor opposition, whose new prime minister immediately announced Norway would take steps to help stabilize the oil price. The new oil minister boarded Zaki Yamani's yacht in Venice for a cruise and a discussion of oil prices. Yet the dialogue between OPEC and non-OPEC yielded little of substance.

### "A Little Action" — Oil Companies Unprepared

- Many oil companies were unprepared for the crisis, having been convinced that OPEC would not do something so silly as to eradicate a large part of their own revenues. But planners at Shell in London, reading the fundamentals carefully, had geared up an "OCS" — an Oil Collapse Scenario. Shell insisted senior managers take it seriously, discuss their responses, and start taking prophylactic action. When the collapse struck, there was "an eerie calm and orderliness at Shell Centre on the south bank of the Thames. Managers there, as well as in the field, went about their jobs as though carrying out a civil defense emergency operation for which they had already practiced."
- In general, the industry responded with quick and massive cuts in expenditures. Particularly hard hit was exploration and production in the US — one of the highest-cost and most disappointing oil provinces. Companies had the most flexibility to respond in the US, where they didn't have to worry about jeopardizing long-negotiated arrangements with national governments.
- Consumers were jubilant. The fears of permanent shortage were laid to rest. "Gas wars" at filling stations were back, writ large as a global oil war. The irreducible minimum was established at Billy Jack Mason's Exxon station on the north side of Austin, Texas — a one-day promotion in early April 1986, sponsored by a local country music station: unleaded for zero cents a gallon. By nine o'clock, the line of cars stretched for six miles. Billy Jack's view on the future of prices: "That's overseas. Nothing we can do about that until the Arabs get the prices right."

### George Bush — From Oil Patch to Vice-Presidency

- George Bush, Vice-President and an adopted Texan, was preparing to leave on an overseas mission to the Middle East. As he later said: "They got a President of the United States that came out of the oil and gas industry, that knows it and knows it well." He knew the risk-taking, deal-making world of the independent oil men — "the backbone of exploration in the United States and who were the ones knocked flat on their backs by the price collapse."
- On graduating from Yale in 1948, Bush passed up Wall Street jobs (his father Prescott had been a partner at Brown Brothers, Harriman, before becoming Senator from Connecticut). He packed up his red 1947 Studebaker and set off for Texas — first Odessa, then Midland, "the oil capital of West Texas." He started as a trainee painting pumping equipment, then graduated to itinerant salesman, driving from rig to rig selling drill bits.
- Bush was part of what Fortune magazine called "the swarm of young Ivy Leaguers" who descended on Midland — creating "a most unlikely outpost of the working rich" and "a union between the cactus and the Ivy." He formed an independent oil company called Zapata (named after the film Viva Zapata!, with Marlon Brando, then playing in Midland). He mastered the skills of the independent oil man — combing courthouse records for mineral rights, arranging drilling rig crews, making the pilgrimage back East to round up investor money. He even closed a deal with Eugene Meyer, publisher of the Washington Post, in the back seat of Meyer's limousine near Union Station.
- The partners amicably split Zapata in two; Bush took the offshore oil services side, becoming one of the pioneers of offshore drilling and production in the Gulf of Mexico and around the world. He also served as part-time investor relations officer. By the mid-1960s, he had made sufficient money and turned to politics — Congressman from Houston, chairman of the Republican National Committee during Watergate, US envoy to China, head of the CIA, and then four years of unsuccessfully campaigning for the Republican nomination before Reagan chose him as running mate in 1980.
- Reagan's approach to energy was to make it a footnote — the crisis resulted from "regulation and the misguided policies of the United States government." He sped up Carter's decontrol. Rising oil prices, "Jimmy Carter's misfortune," had been transmuted into "Ronald Reagan's good fortune" — the inflation-adjusted price of oil actually started its long slide just around the time Reagan moved into the White House. The "free market" approach rested on a contradiction: a cartel (OPEC) was preventing a big fall in the price, thus providing the incentives for conservation and energy development in the US. But this contradiction remained latent until the price collapse of 1986.
- What was unleashed was "absolute competition," devastating to the American oil industry. Pink slips flew at an awesome rate; drilling rigs stacked up; the financial infrastructure of the Southwest was headed into an economic depression. If prices stayed down, US oil demand would shoot up, domestic production would plummet, and imports would flood in again. There were calls for an oil import tariff, but none came from the Reagan Administration. Another option: jawbone OPEC into getting its act together. Thus did George Bush's long official inattention to oil abruptly come to an end.

### "I Know I'm Correct" — Bush's Saudi Arabia Trip

- In early April 1986, Bush said he would "be selling very hard" to persuade the Saudis "of our own domestic interest and thus the interest of national security... I think it is essential that we talk about stability and that we not just have a continued free fall like a parachutist jumping out without a parachute." He ritualistically acknowledged the free market precept: "Our answer is market, market — let the market forces work." But then added: "I happen to believe, and always have, that a strong domestic U.S. industry is in the national security interests, vital interests of this country." Bush was clearly saying that market forces had gone too far. He was quickly and embarrassingly disavowed by the Reagan White House: "The way to address price stability is to let the free market work."
- Bush's first stop was Riyadh, where at a dinner with several ministers including Yamani, he commented that if prices remained too low, pressure for a tariff would build in Congress and it would be increasingly difficult to resist. The Saudis took his remark very seriously. In Dhahran, at a late-night session with King Fahd (lasting past 2:00 a.m.), the prime subject was Persian Gulf security and US weapons supplies — oil was mentioned only glancingly, but Fahd expressed his hope for "stability in the market." The King "felt Saudi Arabia was, in non-royal language, being given a bum rap."
- Bush stuck to his position despite domestic criticism. "I know I'm correct... Some things you're sure of. This I'm absolutely sure of — that low prices would cripple the domestic American energy industries, with serious consequences for the nation." At a breakfast with American businessmen in Dhahran: "There is some point at which the national security interest of the United States says, 'Hey, we must have a strong, viable domestic industry.'" A senior White House official disparagingly called it "not Administration policy." But Bush refused to retreat: "I don't know that I'm defending the [U.S. oil] industry. What I'm doing is defending a position that I feel very, very strongly... Whether that's a help politically or whether it proves a detriment politically I couldn't care less."
- The general view was that Bush was making an enormous political blunder. But the Washington Post editorial page said he was right: "A steadily increasing dependence on imported oil is, as the man suggests, not a happy prospect."
- The Saudis interpreted Bush's remarks as the VP saying that the price collapse was destabilizing and threatening US security — American petroleum imports would rise substantially, the US would be weakened militarily and strategically vis-a-vis the Soviet Union. The Bush mission, on top of the turmoil and other difficulties, provided reason for the Saudis to reconsider the fierce battle for market share. Moreover, the other exporters had finally learned that cheating had a cost.

### "Hara-Kiri" and $18 a Barrel — Finding the Right Price

- Alirio Parra, a senior official of Petroleos de Venezuela who had begun his career as an assistant to Juan Pablo Perez Alfonzo during the formation of OPEC, struggled to find historical context. He recalled a 1926 book, The United States Oil Policy, by John Ise: "The unfortunate features of the oil history of Pennsylvania have been repeated in the later history of almost every other producing region... the same instability, the same recurrent or chronic over-production, the same wide fluctuations in prices." Ise described the 1920s as a "spectacle of a vast overproduction of this limited natural resource, growing stocks, overflowing tanks, and declining prices." Oil producers were committing "hara-kiri" by producing so much oil. The remedy: "a reduction in the production." Though written sixty years earlier, the language and diagnosis sounded "all too familiar to Parra."
- Parra and others began working out a new pricing system that took into account the fact that oil and energy markets were competitive. They focused on $17-$19 a barrel — $11 less than the previous official price of $29. Correcting for inflation, $18 took oil prices back to where they had been in the mid-1970s, on the eve of the Second Oil Shock. It seemed to be the price at which oil was again competitive with other energy sources and with conservation — "the highest level that the exporters could attain and still achieve their goals of stimulating economic growth in the rest of the world and thus energy demand." An OPEC official: "Eighteen dollars is inconvenient for my country, but don't you think it's the best we can do?"
- In late May 1986, six oil ministers met in Taif. Yamani stated Saudi Arabia's position: "We want to see a correction in the trends in the market. Once we regain control of the market by increasing our share, we will be able to act accordingly. We want to regain our market power." The ministers confirmed their support for $17-$19 and agreed on the need for a new quota system. This "consensus" of $18 emerged "out of the wreckage of the old" through "a process of osmosis," said Parra.
- The Japanese, importing more than 99% of their oil, might have been expected to prefer the lowest possible price. But they did not — prices too low would undercut their large commitment to alternative energy sources, leading back to higher oil dependence and renewed vulnerability. Also, very low oil prices would swell Japan's already-huge trade surplus. Japan's industry and government believed in "reasonable prices" — which came out to about $18.
- The new US consensus: gains from falling oil prices (higher growth, lower inflation) would outweigh the losses (the Southwest, energy industries) — but only up to a point. The pain and dislocation at some level would cancel the benefits. That point fell somewhere between $15 and $18. The Reagan Administration was rooting for $18 — it would boost growth, restrain inflation, and be a price at which the domestic oil industry could scrape by.

### Revenue Pain and the End of the Market Share Strategy

- The market share strategy was, at least in the short term, a failure for OPEC. But who was hurt most? The Arab Gulf states (Saudi Arabia, Kuwait), which had greatly increased volumes, were least hurt: Kuwait's revenues down only 4%, Saudi Arabia's down 11%. The price hawks — Iran, Libya, Algeria (the most belligerent and hostile to the West) — were hit hardest: Iran's and Libya's oil earnings in H1 1986 were down 42% from 1985. Iran was the country most disadvantaged — its revenues were plummeting even as it was having to finance the war with Iraq, which had entered a more intense phase.
- The market share strategy was acknowledged as a failure. Yamani: "Not on your life. We all swing together or not at all. On this point, I am as stubborn as Mrs. Thatcher." Some exporters insisted Saudi Arabia resume its swing role; Yamani refused. In July 1986, Persian Gulf crudes were going for $7/barrel or less.
- By July, OPEC experts had worked out a detailed rationale: $17-$19 would lead to improved world economic outlook, stimulate oil demand, slow and arrest fuel substitution, and "will definitely discourage future developments of high cost oil." But if prices went any lower, exporters would face "strong protectionist measures in the major consuming countries, including the imposition of an oil imports tariff in both the U.S. and Japan."
- At the Geneva OPEC meeting (late July/early August 1986), Iran's oil minister Gholam Reza Aghazadeh appeared in Yamani's suite for a private discussion through an interpreter. His message so startled Yamani that he had the interpreter repeat it. Iran was willing to accept the temporary, voluntary quotas pushed by Yamani. Iran had backed down — its oil policy was more pragmatic than its foreign policy.
- The market share strategy was over. In announcing new quotas, OPEC insisted it would not carry the burden alone — non-OPEC would have to cooperate. Mexico would cut its output. Norway promised not to cut back but to reduce the growth of its output. The Soviet Union, which had stood aside (a senior Soviet energy official had derided the idea of cooperating with OPEC: "We are not a producer of bananas"), finally promised a 100,000-barrel-per-day cutback. The pledge was vague, but in the immediate turmoil, the symbolism was important.

### Playing It by Ear — Yamani at Harvard, Then Fired

- In September 1986, Harvard University was celebrating its 350th anniversary. From among the planet's five billion citizens, Harvard chose two people to give major speeches: Prince Charles, heir to the British throne, and Ahmed Zaki Yamani, the Saudi oil minister. A delegation from Harvard even flew to Geneva to personally extend the invitation.
- Prince Charles delivered a lively, entertaining speech. Yamani chose a very substantive, dense discourse filled with numbers to the second decimal place. He recalled his battles over price with the oil companies in the early 1970s and with his OPEC brethren in the late 1970s and early 1980s. He called for stability and recognition that oil was a "special commodity." He held out the hope of oil priced at $15/barrel with a gradual rise in both price and OPEC output. "It was a vision of a very orderly world. Did he really believe it?"
- When asked how oil policy was made inside Saudi Arabia, Yamani replied smoothly and without hesitation: "We play it by ear." The audience roared with laughter. Unbeknownst at the time, those words would be among the last of Yamani's official utterances.

### Yamani Fired

- A month later, in October, Yamani was in Geneva for the next stage of OPEC's reconstruction. The kingdom wanted not only to protect its quota and assure its volumes, but also to receive a higher price — the consensus $18, quite different from the $15 Yamani had talked about at Harvard. Yamani went so far as to suggest, semipublicly, that it was contradictory to try to pursue higher volume and higher price at the same time — which seemed to go explicitly against the policy King Fahd had enunciated.
- One evening, a week after the meeting, Yamani was back in Riyadh at dinner with friends when he received a phone call advising him to turn on the television news. An item at the end of the broadcast reported tersely that Ahmed Zaki Yamani had been "relieved" of his post as oil minister. That was the way he learned he had been fired. Yamani had been in the job twenty-four years — "a good, long run in any position anywhere. Still, it was an abrupt, embarrassing, and disconcerting end to a quarter-century career."
- The reasons for his firing were many and somewhat contradictory: he had embarrassed the Royal Family by failing to pursue their instructions vigorously and by criticizing them; he had made powerful enemies by opposing barter deals; his firing reflected the jettisoning of policies with which he had been associated; there was resentment of his arrogance, patronizing manner, high profile, and celebrity status outside the country. Yamani had been King Faisal's man, but Faisal had been dead for almost a dozen years; Fahd was King now and was the maker of oil policy. By 1986, Yamani had precious few allies, and many other ministers and advisers believed he had usurped their own authority. "And, when it came down to it, some said, King Fahd just plain didn't like Yamani."
- Perhaps in the long run it was the decline and then the precipitous collapse in the price of oil that led to Yamani's own downfall. There was also the specific matter of the Harvard speech — some in Riyadh had expected merely a few general words, not a seventeen-page speech that was not everyone's idea of an impromptu talk. Moreover, the policy it espoused was not exactly identical with the official policy of Saudi Arabia. And the "play it by ear" remark was interpreted in Riyadh as a tart criticism of the Saudi government. So Yamani was returned to private life — to manage his fortune, try to acquire a Swiss watchmaker, preside over his perfume factory in Taif, teach part-time at the Harvard Law School, and comment from time to time on world oil.

### Price Restored — The December 1986 Geneva Meeting

- The OPEC countries finally brought the "good sweating" to an end at a meeting in Geneva in December 1986. It was the first major OPEC meeting for the new Saudi oil minister, Hisham Nazir — among the initial generation of Saudi technocrats, just two years younger than Yamani, educated at UCLA, had been a deputy to Abdullah Tariki, then served many years as Minister of Planning. He was particularly alert to the links between oil and the national economy and to the overall revenue question. He carried no responsibility for the repudiated market share strategy.
- The exporters agreed to a "reference price" of $18/barrel, based on a composite of several different crudes. They also agreed to a quota that would support the price. No agreement was possible between Iran and Iraq on Iraq's quota — so the quota only applied to twelve countries; Iraq was outside it, free to do what it could. A "notional" quota of 1.5 million bpd was assigned to Iraq, bringing the total to 17.3 million bpd.
- To the surprise of many, the framework of agreement managed, with considerable reconstruction, to hold up through 1987, 1988, and 1989 — though with recurrent and sometimes intense pressure. The OPEC price was not exactly $18, but rather $15-$18. Prices were volatile, and at times the quota system seemed about to fall apart. But the producers, faced with the alternative, rallied. "After all, the OPEC countries themselves had felt the full brunt of the 'good sweating,' and they had had enough of it."
- The new oil prices completely wiped out the increases of the Second Oil Shock of 1979-81. The economic benefits to consumers were enormous. If the two oil price shocks of the 1970s constituted the "OPEC tax" — an immense transfer of wealth from consumers to producers — then the price collapse was the "OPEC tax cut," a transfer of $50 billion in 1986 alone back to the consuming countries. This tax cut stimulated and prolonged the economic growth that had begun four years earlier, while driving down inflation. In economic terms, the long crisis was certainly over.

### Iran Versus Iraq: The Tide Turns — The Tanker War and Cease-Fire

- Politically and strategically, the threat remained — the Iran-Iraq War, in its seventh year in 1987, broke through the barriers that had restricted it to the two belligerents and became internationalized for the first time, drawing in the other Arab Gulf states and the two superpowers. Iran had captured the Fao Peninsula, the southernmost extreme of Iraq bordering Kuwait — a potential gateway to the conquest of Basra. But the Iranians bogged down in marshy sands. Thereafter, the war turned against them. Iraqi successes in the air and its "tanker war" on Iranian shipping led to stepped-up Iranian attacks on third-country tankers. Iran zeroed in on Kuwait (which was assisting Iraq), launching at least five missile attacks directly on Kuwait itself.
- Kuwait asked the US to protect its shipping. Washington was jolted to learn that the Kuwaitis had also asked the Russians for protection. The potential significance of a Soviet approach caused the Kuwaiti request to "not linger" at the most senior levels of the Reagan Administration. To exclude the Russians, the US took on the whole job of reflagging — eleven Kuwaiti tankers were reflagged with the Stars and Stripes. US naval vessels were patrolling the Gulf. British and French naval units, along with ships from Italy, Belgium, and the Netherlands, also entered the Gulf. The Japanese, forbidden by their constitution from sending ships, chipped in by increasing the funds they provided to offset the cost of maintaining American forces in Japan and by investing in a precision locator system in the Strait of Hormuz.
- By spring 1988, Iraq was manifestly winning, making use of chemical weapons. Iran's ability and will to fight were fading. Its economy was in shambles. War weariness gripped the country; in one month, 140 Iraqi missiles fell on Tehran alone. Volunteers were no longer forthcoming.
- Ali Akbar Hashemi Rafsanjani — speaker of Parliament, deputy commander of the Army, nicknamed "Kuseh" (the Shark) — concluded it was time to seek an end to the war. He was a member of a wealthy pistachio-growing family, a Khomeini student and disciple since 1962. Iran was diplomatically and politically isolated, while Iraq was growing stronger.
- The American naval presence led to a major tragic confrontation: in early July 1988, the USS Vincennes, engaged in an exchange with Iranian warships, mistook an Iranian Airbus carrying 290 passengers for a hostile aircraft and shot it down. To the Iranian leadership, this was a sign that the US was preparing to bring its great power to bear in direct military confrontation with Iran. Iran could no longer afford to go against the United States.
- On July 17, 1988, Iran informed the United Nations of its willingness to countenance a cease-fire. "Taking this decision was more deadly than taking poison," Khomeini declared. "I submitted myself to God's will and drank this drink for his satisfaction." But vengeance was still his ambition: "God willing, we will empty our hearts' anguish at the appropriate time by taking revenge on the Al Saud and America." The Ayatollah would not live to see such a day; within a year, he would be dead.
- The cease-fire took effect August 20, 1988. Iraq immediately began symbolic oil shipments from its Gulf ports. Iran announced its intention to rebuild the great refinery at Abadan. One month short of eight years, the war ended in a stalemate favoring Iraq. As far as Baghdad was concerned, it had won the war and was now "the dominant political power in the Gulf, and one of the world's major oil powers." But the significance of the end of the war was much more far-reaching — it appeared that the threat to the free flow of Middle Eastern oil had been removed; the era of continuing crisis that had begun with the October War fifteen years earlier along the banks of the Suez Canal "finally seemed to be at an end."

### Reintegration and a New Era

- The end of the war pointed to a new era. The great contentious question of sovereignty had been resolved — the exporters owned the oil. But what came to matter over the 1980s was sure access to markets. The producing countries discovered that consumers had more flexibility and wider choices than had been imagined. "Security of demand" was no less important to them than "security of supply" was to consumers. Most exporters now wanted to establish themselves as reliable suppliers and that oil was a secure fuel. With socialism in bad repute and the North-South confrontation a fading memory, exporters could act more on economic than political concerns. Some were reopening the doors to exploration by private companies — doors that had been slammed shut in the 1970s.
- Others went further, following the logic of integration — state-owned companies of the exporters went downstream to acquire outlets. Petroleos de Venezuela built up a large refining and marketing system in the US and Western Europe. Kuwait turned itself into an integrated oil company, with refineries in Western Europe and thousands of gasoline stations operating under the brand name "Q-8." Kuwait did not stop there: in 1987, Margaret Thatcher reversed Winston Churchill's historic 1914 decision and sold off the government's 51% stake in British Petroleum. Kuwait thereupon snapped up 22% of BP — the very company that, along with Gulf, had developed and owned Kuwait's oil until 1975. The British government was infuriated and forced Kuwait to reduce its holdings to 10%.
- Saudi Arabia and Texaco (one of the original Aramco partners) announced a new joint venture — Saudi Arabia acquired a half interest in Texaco's refineries and gasoline stations in 33 states in the eastern and southern US. The transaction guaranteed the Saudis, if they wanted it, 600,000 barrels per day of sales in the US, compared to the trickle of 26,000 bpd they had fallen to in 1985. Such "reintegration" represented one effort to put greater long-term stability back into the industry and to manage the risks faced by producers and consumers.

### The Epilogue — Lessons Learned, Lessons Forgotten

- A few months after the cease-fire, George Bush, the former oil man, succeeded Ronald Reagan as President. With the astonishing disintegration of the barriers dividing the Soviet bloc from the Western democracies, unprecedented prospects for global peace appeared at hand. Oil would certainly remain a vital commodity in the economies of both industrialized and developing nations — and as a bargaining chip among producers and consumers, it would remain of paramount importance in the politics of world power.
- Yet out of the tumult of the 1970s and 1980s, important lessons had emerged. Consumers had learned that they could not regard oil, the fundament of their lives, so easily as a given. Producers had learned that they could not take their markets and customers for granted. The result was "a priority of economics over politics, an emphasis on cooperation over confrontation, or at least so it appeared." But would those critical lessons be recalled?
- At a discussion in New York City in the late spring of 1989, the oil minister from one of the major exporting countries spoke at length about the new realism of producers and consumers and the lessons learned by both. Afterwards, he was asked how long such lessons would be remembered. The question took him by surprise. He thought for a moment. "About three years, without reminding," he said. Within a year of that exchange, he himself was no longer minister. And a month later, his country was invaded.
